## Page 1

FIRST REPORT

FROM THE SELECT COMMITTEE APPOINTED TO CONSIDER OF THE MEANS OF IMPROVING AND MAINTAINING THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Ordered, by The House of Commons, to be Printed, 9 March 1821.

---
## Page 3

REPORT.

THE SELECT COMMITTEE appointed to consider of the Means of maintaining and improving the Foreign Trade of the Country, and to report their opinion and observations thereupon from time to time to The House; and to whom the Report relative to the Timber Trade, which was communicated from the Lords in the last Session of Parliament, and the several Petitions respecting the Duties on Timber, presented to the House in the present Session, were severally referred;—HAVE, pursuant to the Order of the House, considered the Matters to them referred; and have agreed to the following REPORT:

YOUR Committee have deviated from the course which their former Report appeared to prescribe, and instead of proceeding to examine some of the burthens that were stated to press with considerable weight upon the Foreign Trade of the Country generally, have applied their consideration to that particular branch of it, which embraces the importation of Timber from the Northern states of Europe, and the British colonies in North America. This they have done, as well on account of that branch having (as appears by a Report referred to them) already occupied the attention of a Committee of the other House of Parliament, appointed for similar purposes; as of the anxiety they understand to prevail among the Commercial and Shipping interests, connected with the trade in question, and the inconvenience of a continued suspense in respect to the system which Parliament may deem it expedient to adopt, on the expiration of the existing law, which according to the latest extension of it, will terminate on the 25th of March in the present year.

In the imposition of the several duties, at present in force, on the importation of timber, the consideration of the Legislature appears to have been directed to two distinct objects; first, to the protection and encouragement of the wood trade in the British American colonies; and secondly, to the augmentation of the Revenue.

Regarding them in this point-of-view, the first question that obviously presented itself was, to the maintenance of what part of these duties, if of any, the public faith might be supposed to be committed; a short reference to the laws which imposed the respective duties, and to the circumstances attending the periods at which they were imposed, has been sufficient to satisfy Your Committee on this head.

Although the policy of giving encouragement to the trade in timber from the British American colonies, may be inferred to have been previously entertained, from the Acts 3 & 4 Anne, and 5 Geo.3, by which Bounties upon the export of it were granted; it does not appear to have been acted upon with much effect before the year 1809; at that time the course of events had placed our relations with the Northern States (from whose territories our supplies of timber, as well for domestic as for naval purposes, had been chiefly derived) in a situation which gave rise to a well-founded apprehension, lest the resources in that quarter might entirely cease to be available for the demands of this country.

186. Under

---
## Page 4

Under the influence of this apprehension, it was deemed advisable by Parliament to resort to the hitherto neglected though abundant supplies to be found in our American colonies, and by adequate protection to encourage the transport of them to meet the exigency with which we were threatened; to accomplish this object, a virtual exemption from duty was granted to the timber imported from our North American possessions, while a large addition was made to that levied on timber from the north of Europe, first, by the 49th Geo. 3, c. 98, and in the ensuing year by the 50th, c. 77, by which the duties of the preceding year were doubled, making the whole duty on northern timber, including the temporary duty imposed in the same years, for the support and during the continuance of the war, amount to £1. 14s. 8d. per load. These duties were again augmented by an addition of 25 per cent to the permanent duties on timber, in common with all other duties of customs, for the express purpose of assisting the revenue. The whole of these duties were consolidated by the 59th of the late King, and now amount to £1. 5s. (per load,) when imported in British ships.

From this statement it will appear, that of these duties (however they may all alike have operated in the way of protection to the colonial timber trade,) a part only can be said to have been intended for that purpose; viz. those which were imposed by the acts passed avowedly with the object of giving encouragement to that trade, amounting to £1. 1s. per load, and which may be contended to have led to its extension by the application of capital, which except for such inducement would never have been so invested. With respect to the exemption from duty in favour of colonial timber, that advantage was originally temporary, and has been since continued from time to time for limited periods; and although the persons concerned in the colonial timber trade may have had a just expectation that they should enjoy for a considerable period the advantages afforded them, as well by the exemption granted as by the duties imposed by the 49th and 50th of Geo. 3; so far from any expectation being held out that the encouragement so given had been considered by government as permanent or was intended to be indefinitely continued to them, that means seem to have been studiously taken to produce by explanation a conviction of a contrary tendency, and to impress them with the assurance that previously to the expiration of the existing law, the timber trade would be brought under consideration of Parliament with the view of introducing an alteration into the scale of the present duties, that should render them more equal and more favourable to our intercourse with the foreign states with whom it was carried on. Your Committee are therefore of opinion that there is nothing which precludes the consideration of these duties, nor any part of them which in strictness may not be open to any modification, either in respect to the rate at which they shall continue, or the mode in which they shall be levied, that Parliament under a sense of the public interest, may deem it prudent to introduce.

The policy most advantageous to the country, as far as the mere supply of timber is concerned, would be, to obtain it of the best quality, and at the lowest price, without reference to the quarter from whence it might be derived; and the course of Your Committee has been to inquire, first, to what extent the operation of this policy is infringed by the system of duties now in force; in the next place, to examine how far the limits imposed on its operation are sustained by adequate considerations of expediency; and lastly, to determine whether, by the adoption of any and what alterations, the duties might be rendered, as far as circumstances allowed, more consistent with the regard due to the principle on which this policy proceeds, and generally more beneficial to the commercial interests of the United Kingdom.

---
## Page 5

THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

It appears that previously to the imposition of the duties in 1809-10, the supplies of wood required for the consumption of the country were principally furnished by the Northern states of Europe; that subsequently to that period, a great and gradually increasing proportion of its supplies has been drawn from the British North American colonies; that at present the use of the timber from the North of Europe, owing to the price it bears in comparison to American timber, is in a great measure confined to the higher and more valuable description of buildings, and to purposes for which increased strength in bearing is necessary or desirable; that for less substantial buildings, and for the inferior purposes to which wood is applicable, the American timber and deals have been generally brought into consumption, and although the Red Pine of America (of which the quantity is relatively small), is said by several witnesses to be equal in quality to the Fir from the North of Europe, yet the Yellow Pine, of which the great importation consists, is stated, when used in this country, to be inferior to it, except for particular purposes and in particular situations, from its supposed greater liability to dry rot, and comparative deficiency in strength and durability. At the same time there is reason to believe, from other evidence, that much prejudice subsists on this head, and that in Lancashire, where the Yellow Pine has been a longer time in general use than in any other part of the kingdom, as well as in the neighbourhood of Shields, its qualities are considered as more valuable than they are generally esteemed; and there is repeated testimony, that when used in America, both in the construction of ships and buildings, it has been found to be free from the particular defect alluded to, and of a durability equal to that of the best timber of Europe.

The scale of comparative value attached by different witnesses to the wood drawn from each particular country, will be seen in the evidence of Sir R. Seppings, Mr. Hollund, Mr. White, Mr. Copland, Mr. Churchill, Mr. Smith, Mr. Haigh, Mr. Bellhouse and others, to which Your Committee think it sufficient to refer.

That the supply of wood to meet the demands of the British and Irish market might be obtained with greater facility and cheapness to the consumer, (if the means of purchasing and transporting it at the lowest rate were the only considerations to be attended to,) a reference to the account of the charges of obtaining and transporting it from the Northern ports of Europe, independent of the duty, will leave no doubt; and although under the pressure of the duty, the demands of the country for superior purposes may have been such as to lead to the importation to a certain amount of timber from the Baltic, it yet must be obvious, that while this duty bears upon it with its present weight, it is to those higher purposes alone to which that species of timber can be applicable; and that a great proportion of wood of an inferior quality must be forced into consumption, both in avowed substitution for the superior timber in buildings, which thereby become less solid and lasting; and in a fraudulent application of it, when that of a superior kind has been contracted for, which, according to the evidence, if practised, can be with difficulty detected; expedients of this nature would probably not be resorted to, if the difference of price was reduced, and the inducement to prefer the American wood were less powerful. It appears, too, by the admission of some of the principal dealers, that the difference is at present such as to be prejudicial to the trade itself, and to bring into the market from the colonies an excessive quantity of timber of a very inferior description, both in point of quality and preparation, and that some alteration of the duty, calculated to approximate the relative prices of the timber from the North and from the American colonies, would be desirable, if only to confine the supply of the market to a more carefully selected and better prepared commodity. In addition to these inconveniences, the amount of the duty levied on Baltic timber, and the increased price which,

---
## Page 6

under the operation of that duty, the American timber must have borne, may be considered as a bounty paid by the consumers of the United Kingdom for the benefit of the North American colonies, and the support of the superfluous shipping, to which the transport of their wood is said to afford the only employment.

The prudential considerations by which the application of the same principle appear to have been limited, are, The danger incident to want of competition, from the exclusion of colonial timber, and from a reliance for our supplies on a single source: The possible failure of supply from the north of Europe, in a moment of necessity; The maintenance and employment of our shipping, and the effect that might be produced on the various interests connected with our American trade, and the capital embarked in the establishments for carrying it on. The same prudential considerations, in the opinion of Your Committee, at present forbid any recommendation on their part, tending entirely to take away the legislative protection hitherto enjoyed by the colonial trade; but, as the extent of that protection is admitted on almost all hands to exceed the necessary bounds, They have directed their attention to ascertain to what amount that protection, and in what mode, should be prospectively continued.

In so far as any alteration introduced is favourable to foreign trade, it must have a tendency to produce an increased importation from the north of Europe, and thereby possibly to induce an increased demand from that quarter for the manufactures of Great Britain; and Your Committee are inclined to believe that an increased demand would be the result, as well from the desire for British manufactures that is said strongly to prevail in those countries, as from the extent to which the export of them has been maintained, notwithstanding the burdens imposed on the importation of this important branch of their produce into the United Kingdom. Your Committee do not think it improbable, that a diminution of the export of wood from the British North American colonies might, on the other hand, be experienced; some diminution, as far as the trade is concerned, would be desirable, and indeed can hardly fail to take place, even independent of any alteration of duties, owing to the excess beyond the consumption of the country, to which the importation has been, by peculiar circumstances, recently enlarged. The causes to which we may attribute this excess, are, in part, the prolonged expectation of an alteration in the rate of duties, combined with the desire to take advantage of the time the present law may continue, and to anticipate the impending change by the greatest possible previous importation; and in part, to the amount of shipping (greatly exceeding the actual demands of our commerce,) which has been thrown out of employment by the conclusion of the war, and other circumstances, and which has been since engaged in this branch of trade, not so much because the employment was attended with advantage, as because it was preferable to the vessels lying entirely idle, incurring expense, and deteriorating in value.

As our intercourse with the Northern states must be liable to be influenced by the fluctuations of political events; and as the exclusion from their ports, which has been once experienced, may at some future period recur, Your Committee are apprehensive, that the consequences of any measure that might have the effect of placing our dependence for a supply of timber exclusively on those countries, might become eventually the occasion of serious political inconvenience and danger; and by the exclusion of competition, possibly defeat the expectation of comparative cheapness to the consumers of this country.

The alarms represented in the Petitions from the shipping interest in various quarters, which have been referred by the House to Your Committee, appear to proceed on a presumption of the necessarily destructive effects

---
## Page 7

effects of any alteration made in the existing duties, a measure which is accordingly deprecated with corresponding earnestness. Your Committee feel the respect due to an interest so important to the power and safety of the country; and if all the weight is not given to the representations of the Petitioners which they may expect, it is because the alarms expressed in them appear to be carried to excess, and the objects sought, not conducive to the general commercial interests of the kingdom, in which their own must be inseparably involved.

Your Committee see no reason whatever to imagine, that the alteration which they have in contemplation, would be attended with the effect of depriving the American colonies of their due participation in the benefits of the wood trade, although it might have a moderate and temporary tendency to the reduction of the importations from thence, and so far may, in the first instance, and for a given period, affect the interests of the ship-owners. In fact, the interest of the ship-owners is the one most concerned in the present question; while that of the colonies themselves, important as it is, is still a comparatively subordinate one. Your Committee fully concur in the wisdom of that policy which has rendered the British shipping a favourite object of the attention and vigilance of Parliament; but they cannot feel that, beyond the extent which may be rationally deemed essential to the safety and defence of the country, every other consideration is to be sacrificed to that object, or that it is consistent with the public welfare, that the care due to the interests of our merchants and manufacturers, and every regard to our foreign commercial relations, should be foregone, for the purpose of supporting by artificial means a mercantile marine in a state of magnitude, at which it has arrived from accidental causes alone, and which is neither conducive to the commercial prosperity, or essential to the political security of the nation; and in which (unless by the opening of new sources of employment, or the extension of those existing,) the regular trade of the country is incapable of maintaining it. Of this, the great depreciation which all property in shipping is represented to have undergone, appears to Your Committee to furnish sufficient proof.

The degree in which the shipping may be affected by any change that is adopted, must depend upon the influence which such change is likely to have in reducing the export of wood from the colonies. From the evidence of persons conversant in the uses to which wood is applied, Your Committee collect, that for many of those uses, the wood imported from America is either indispensable, or preferable, or as good, or nearly so, as that brought from the north of Europe. In reference to the first description, are mentioned masts for ships of large dimensions, both ships of war, and the more valuable description of merchant-men, which can only be found in our North American colonies, and which must therefore form a considerable article of import into this country under almost any state of duties. To the second, all articles in which facility of working, and an extensive surface and freedom from knots is required, and to whatever extent pine timber for these purposes is employed, the American wood even at equal prices would have a preference. To the last, the application to all inferior purposes, as for packing chests, and various objects in the interior of houses, or in situations where it has the benefit of a free circulation of air: for such uses it may be fairly supposed, and indeed it is more than once admitted, that the consideration of mere cheapness would cast the balance in favour of the produce of our American provinces.

The aggregate of these applications of timber, form a very considerable proportion of the general consumption of the country; and it appears to Your Committee, that it would require something little short of equality in point of price with those of the Baltic, to exclude the timber and deals of the British colonies from importation for these purposes. Nor must

---
## Page 8

it be forgotten that the experience, obtained within the last few years, of the qualities and value of the American wood, which has had the effect of removing much of the prejudice that prevailed against it, in so far must probably have contributed to induce a permanent extension of its consumption.

By the estimate of some of the witnesses, the excess of supply of American pine beyond the demand, is stated to be at present considerable, insomuch that a heavy loss is incurred by the importers. This circumstance, even under the existing duties, cannot fail to lead to a reduction in its future importation, and of course to a diminution of employment, in the same degree, of the shipping engaged in the trade. If the effect of an alteration of duty should for a time increase the demand for northern timber, it must also undoubtedly tend to a similar consequence, not likely to be more than partially counterbalanced by a corresponding increase of demand for British shipping in the trade with the north of Europe, from the ports of which a smaller number of vessels may be sufficient for an equal amount of importation.

By a diminution of the demand from America, the capital invested in sawmills and establishments in those colonies, stated at £150,000l., may be liable to some injury. It must be recollected however, that the advantage given to the American colonial trade, on which these establishments were founded, has already extended beyond the period on which those engaged in it had any right to calculate; and having speculated on their own views of public policy, they can have no just ground of complaint, in the event of Parliament taking a different view of what that policy requires, and subjecting these duties to some modification beyond the expectation which they had formed.

To the Canadian proprietor, the principal value of the timber trade appears to consist in the employment it affords to the persons concerned in agriculture and their servants, during a certain period of the year, in conveying the wood from the places where it has been felled, to the places from which it is to be conveyed to the ports of export; while the part of the business which belongs to cutting and rafting, is in many instances performed by axemen passing from the United States for this purpose, who are understood to be more expert in these operations than the labourers of the British territories.

If what has been stated by Your Committee leads them to expect some diminution of the export of the American colonies, from a change in the scale of existing duties, they have also reason to believe that it is only by a temporary restraint of that export that the character of the wood is likely to be improved, and its value eventually increased; such a diminution, therefore, is in itself by no means, in the contemplation of Your Committee, a sufficient ground of objection on the part of the colonies to any alteration that may be proposed, unless it be such an alteration as shall be calculated extensively to exclude from consumption the timber of the North American colonies, and transfer the trade to foreigners. Within certain limits, the trade of the colonies of Great Britain have a just claim to encouragement and support from the mother country; and to such claim Your Committee are anxious to give full weight. It is not, however, a question whether this encouragement and support should be given or withheld; but admitting it to be due, to what extent it should be carried, in justice to other interests, which have also their peculiar claims to attention, and which are, in the opinion of Your Committee, also deeply involved in this discussion. On the fair regard shown to foreign countries, the extent of our commerce with them may depend, and in providing with too much partiality for the interests connected with the trade to and from our American colonies, we may put in hazard all those still more extensive interests that are engaged in the export to those countries which are directly concerned in the timber trade, (if not of our foreign trade generally,) by such a proof

---
## Page 9

of deliberate preference of a principle of restriction, as the rule of our commercial policy.

In maintaining the original duty imposed expressly for the purpose of encouragement to the North American trade, it cannot be contended that every claim on public faith is not fully satisfied. In point of expediency, however, and in consideration of the interests involved, Your Committee are disposed to think it may be allowed to go even something further in favour of the colonies. The difference created by duty on timber amounts at present to 3l. 5s. per load; if, by the effect of the alteration, that should be reduced to 2l. 5s. which would leave a protection, after providing for the ordinary difference in freight, in the actual selling price of the respective descriptions of timber, of 1l. 10s. per load in favour of the imports from our North American provinces, Your Committee cannot but think, in recommending such a difference, they shall at least be free from the charge of not having sufficiently listened to the pretensions of the parties whose interests are involved in the colonial trade, and tendered as great sacrifice on the part of the country, both to the shipping and the colonies, as they can persuade themselves the House will be disposed to sanction. At this rate of difference, it appears to Your Committee, a fairer competition will be given to foreign produce, and a freedom of choice (which, under the present relative prices can hardly be said to exist) will be secured to the consumers, between the descriptions of wood brought from the respective points of supply, while a certain and large proportion of the consumption of the United Kingdom will be assured to the American colonies, in the applications of their timber to those uses for which its qualities and comparative price must give it a preference.

However, the tendency of the evidence generally may be, to recommend an alteration in the duties, to such an amount as may prove a corrective to the trade, without impairing materially the consumption of the American timber; a considerable variety of opinion was expressed by the witnesses examined, as to the extent to which an alteration of the existing duty might be carried, without danger to the fair demand for the produce of our American colonies. Amidst the different opinions given, it was difficult for Your Committee to determine the precise amount by which the relative difference between the colonial timber and that from the northern states should be reduced, and in fixing upon 20s. they have not only taken that sum as a point between the extremes, but have been influenced by a reference to the accounts of the market in several years, and particularly in 1816, 1817, 1818, and 1819, as it is given in a paper added in the Appendix, and in the evidence of two of the witnesses; when it appears to have been, according to the remarks of one of them, in a natural and healthy state; when a fair competition existed, when the prejudice entertained against American timber seemed to be on the decline, and the demand for it augmenting. The relative price was, at this period, about or nearly three to four, which has recently been reduced to one-half, owing to the unnatural situation into which the market has been brought by excessive importations, produced by the various circumstances which, at the present moment, have contributed to disturb the channels, and change the character of the trade.

Your Committee next proceeded to consider, in what mode that alteration of duty should be effected, whether by reduction of duty on Baltic timber, by an imposition of duty on American, or by a combination of both; and the result of their consideration has been, a preference of the last mode of producing the relative approximation they have recommended, in the prices of the respective descriptions of timber, by the imposition of 10s. on the American timber, and a corresponding reduction from the duty on that imported from the north of Europe; this they conceive

---
## Page 10

conceive to be most effectual to produce the advantages they have in contemplation; by removing the excessive inequality of the present system, facilitating our intercourse with foreign nations, and marking our desire, as far as circumstances will permit, to adopt more liberal principles than those by which our commerce with them has been hitherto governed.

The state of the duty on deals will not, in the opinion of Your Committee, allow the same degree of relative reduction to be applied to it, which has been recommended for that on timber.

The rate of duty on long deals at present falls considerably below that on timber, whilst on those of short lengths, it rather exceeds it. In what principle this distinction in favour of deals, as compared with timber in the log, originated, Your Committee are at a loss to discover, and are averse to recommend a continuance of it, at least to its present extent. They feel, however, considerable difficulty in proposing to equalize the duty on timber and deals, which, if effected by a reduction of the duty on timber, must be attended with a large sacrifice of revenue, and if by an addition to that on deals, might tend in some measure rather to impair than assist the foreign trade of the kingdom, by the effect it would have on the exports of wood from those states, of which deals form the greatest proportion. This, in the opinion of Your Committee, precludes the application of a rule of strict equality to deals and to timber; but it appears to them, that while the amount of duty on timber is reduced in the degree proposed, a small increase on deals of large dimensions, will in some measure lessen the distinction, at least as far as that class of deals is concerned. On the shorter deals, they recommend some reduction of duty, less with reference to the manner in which the duty at present bears upon this description of deals in comparison with timber, than in consideration of the difference in the quantity of wood contained in a given number of deals of the larger and smaller dimensions, which seems to call, in respect of the latter, for a more favourable assessment. Another alteration which has suggested itself to Your Committee, is one that has reference to deal ends, on which a comparative low duty has been hitherto levied, in order to accommodate the ship-owner in broken stowage; this indulgence has been found to lead to great abuse, in covering the introduction of timber of this description as cargo, (a practice never contemplated) to an extent most injurious to the revenue. They therefore submit the propriety of confining the length of this class of deals to six feet, and making a moderate reduction in duty to which they are at present liable.

In consequence of the Report referred to them, the mode of levying the duty on deals and wood of the other denominations, under which it is imported into this country, according to the cubic measure, has been an object of Your Committee's examination; and although the reduction of them all to their cubical contents in assessing the duty, seems, on the first view of it, the most easy as well as the most equitable principle that could be adopted, Your Committee have found reasons in support of continuing the existing mode, (both as a matter of convenience and as producing a degree of equality between the countries by which our importations of wood are furnished,) sufficient to prevent their proposing to the House to relinquish it; in preserving the mode, however, they are of opinion, that an improvement may be introduced into the scale now in use, by admitting a gradation of duty, between the deal ends and deals of the largest class, which, it appears to Your Committee, would attain more effectually that advantage by which the existing mode of levying the duty is chiefly recommended.

Your Committee have abstained from entering, in this Report, into details upon the subject of battens, oak-plank, staves, deck-plank, paling-boards, masts, spars, and the other various denominations under which timber is imported,

---
## Page 11

imported, to which their attention has been directed. The duties on these will be influenced by those on the more important articles, and will make a necessary part of any measure that may hereafter be submitted to the House.

The policy of a legislative preference being given to the importation of timber in the log, and the discouragement of the importation of deals, seems to Your Committee very doubtful, both because they are of opinion that any advantage to be expected from the conversion of timber into deals in this country, will not be sufficient to compensate for the corresponding disadvantage to the general consumer, (to whom the deals would come with a considerable increase of cost,) and because it is founded on a principle of exclusion, which they are most averse to see brought into operation in any new instance, without the warrant of some evident and great political expediency.

Your Committee have discovered in the Accounts before them, that the protective duty in favour of British shipping, has been made to operate in different degrees on the importation of wood of different descriptions, varying from 2½ to 5 per cent, and in some unimportant instances falling below, as in others considerably exceeding these rates, on the value of the particular article imported; for this inequality, which introduces much perplexity into the collection of the duty, there does not appear to be any sufficient reason, and they therefore submit to the consideration of the House, the propriety of making the same duty attach on all importations of wood in foreign ships alike, and that the amount of difference between the importation in the foreign ship and that in the British ship, should be fixed for the future at 5 per cent.

One only further recommendation has suggested itself to Your Committee, which, in concluding their Report, they are desirous of offering to the House. It has appeared in the evidence, that a great proportion of the timber which is imported from the province of Canada, is the growth of the United States, and has been permitted to be received into that province free from duty, and has from thence been exported to the United Kingdom, with all the benefits and immunities conceded to the produce of the British territory. To obviate the objection to which this practice appears to Your Committee to be liable, they are of opinion, that with every exportation of timber from the British provinces in North America, a Certificate of its being the produce of those provinces should be required, and that timber imported without such certificate should be hereafter charged with the same rate of duty as would be payable on it, if imported directly from a foreign state.

In submitting the result of what has occurred to them in the course of their inquiry into this important subject, Your Committee have only to add, that in the recommendations which they have tendered, it has been their endeavour, to the utmost of their power, to conciliate the claims of adverse interests and the contending considerations of policy that demanded their attention. If what they propose falls far short of a recurrence to those sound principles by which all commerce ought to be regulated, they trust it will appear to the House, that they have proceeded as far as, under present circumstances, is consistent with an equitable regard to the protection due to extensive interests that have grown up under an established system, and which must be deeply affected by any material and sudden change to which that system is subjected.

9 March 1821.

**APPENDIX**

- p. 169

---
## Page 13

MINUTES OF EVIDENCE.

Lunæ, 12° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

Sir Robert Seppings,

Called in; and Examined.

YOU are one of the surveyors of the navy?—I am.

How long have you been connected with the naval department?—Ever since 1782.

Your attention has been officially directed to the construction of ships in the King's dock yards?—Yes.

In the course of the time that you have been so employed, your observation has been particularly directed to the different kinds of timber that are used in the dock yards?—It has.

You are therefore acquainted with the timber that is brought from the north of Europe, as well as that brought from America?—Yes, I am.

Are both kinds used in the dock yards?—The timber of the growth of North America is not at this time used in the construction of ships, excepting for partitions or bulk-heads; also for masts, yards and bowsprits.

Was it at any time used in the construction of ships in the planking?—It was, very generally I believe, from the year 1807.

How long has it ceased to be so used?—About five or six years, I should think.

State the reasons why it has ceased to be so used?—It was from experience found to be very subject to decay, particularly in generating fungi, which is the forerunner of dry rot.

Is the fir from the north of Europe now used in the construction of ships?—It is, that is to say in certain parts, not generally.

In those parts to which fir was applied about the year 1807?—Not so generally, but since that period (1807) ships were built with American fir; this took place in 1812 and 1813.

You speak of King's ships?—Yes, of the red and yellow pine, and the pitch pine.

Are ships now built with Baltic fir?—We have lately built a frigate with Baltic fir by way of experiment, to try the comparative durability of that and larch.

What larch?—Scotch larch from the Duke of Atholl's estate.

Have you had any opportunity of ascertaining whether ships built of Baltic fir are subject to the same defects as those you have attributed to the Canada timber?—I believe in the year 1796 or thereabouts, there were a number of frigates built with Baltic fir, and their average durability was eight years. In the years 1812 and 1813 there was a certain number of frigates built with the pine of North America, and their durability averaged about four years.

In speaking of the North American timber, do you speak of that of Canada, or do you speak generally of all which is brought from North America?—I speak of that

---
## Page 14

that of Canada generally; I include in my answer every description of fir and oak said to be of the growth of North America.

Do you happen to have made any comparison, with a view of ascertaining the different qualities of fir brought from the different parts of North America?—No, I have not made any particular comparison between the timber of Canada and that of New Brunswick.

Have you at any time made any experiments with a view to ascertaining the comparative qualities of the Baltic and American timber, or do you give your answer solely from the experience you have had in shipbuilding?—I give my answer of course from my experience; in point of strength I have made comparisons.

What have been the results?—The yellow pine we find to be weaker than the Baltic timber; the red pine possesses greater tenacity in the first instance, but tenacity has nothing to do with durability.

Have you ever made any experiments, or has your observation been directed to the different species of fir timber brought from the north of Europe, and the comparative qualities of that brought from different countries of the north of Europe?—Yes; I have made a comparison of the Riga fir with the Dantzig and the Memel fir.

Be so good as to state, what the result of that comparison is?—I should rank a certain description of Dantzig fir as possessing the greatest durability; the second Riga, the third Memel.

What is the reason, that in enumerating the timber from the north of Europe, you do not mention that which is grown in Sweden and Norway?—The reason is, that it is not of sufficient dimensions; it is not used in His Majesty's yards in consequence of its not being of sufficient scantlings.

Is it used for spars or yards?—Yes; small spars.

Then you mean, that it is not used in the construction of ships?—Yes.

As compared with these, how do you rank the American timber?—I should rank the American timber for shipbuilding inferior to them all, from my positive experience.

In speaking of the comparative durability of the different kinds of timber, do you include the comparative subjectness to dry rot?—I spoke of the American timber being most prone to the growth of fungi, much more than any other timber that I am acquainted with.

Have you in many instances found the Baltic timber subject to the dry rot at all?—Oh yes, certainly; every description of timber is subject to the dry rot, if put into a situation that is friendly to it.

What particular situation do you allude to?—In the confined parts of the ship's hold, such as in the wake of the bread-room, the magazines, and such other parts where there is not a free circulation of air.

In what other respect is the American timber inferior to the Baltic?—I can speak of no other respects, but its not possessing durability, in the same proportion that the timber of the north of Europe does.

Does the dry rot apply to the planking?—Yes; in the oak of the growth of North America particularly, I have seen a four-inch plank of American oak on the bottom of a ship, nearly decomposed to its surface.

In the account you have delivered in, there are several ships built with red pine, the average lasting of which appears to have been about four years?—Yes; I think that was the extent of it.

Did all those ships come under your observation?—Yes; I believe I may say generally.

Did you superintend the building of any of them?—Yes, I did, by occasional visits.

What proportion of them were built in the King's yards and what in the merchants yards?—None of the ships built with American pine were built in the King's yards, but the government provided the materials.

The materials therefore came under your immediate observation?—Yes; the government supplied the timber.

Was the timber supplied by the government for the construction of those ships, of the same description and in the same state in which it would have been applied to the construction of ships in the King's dock yards?—Certainly, of the same description; it was of the best quality of the kind.

Do you conceive, that the ships were as well built in the merchants yards as they would have been in the King's dock yards?—I think in some instances that they were equally so; but ships at that time were not built with the same care as they

---
## Page 15

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

are at present, namely, they were exposed to the vicissitudes of the weather, and now they are excluded from them in the King's yards.

It appears that there were seventeen ships built in the year 1813; were not they built upon a sudden emergency, and in a great hurry?—Yes, they were built on an emergency; that is always the case with fir ships.

Was that the case with the ships built in 1796, and 1804, 1805, and 1806, which appear to have been built in the King's yards?—I cannot answer that question exactly, the period is so long ago; but I can, by referring to documents, supply the period when each ship was commenced, and when she was finished.

[The witness was directed to furnish this information]

Is it not generally the case, that ships built in the merchants yards have proved less durable than those which have been turned out of the King's yards?—Generally speaking, they are.

Have you reason to think that those ships, if they had been turned out of the King's yards, would have lasted longer than they appear to have done, by the account which has been delivered in?—No; I do not believe that their durability would have been much increased.

Then you attribute their having lasted so short a time to the wood with which they were constructed, and to that only?—Yes, to that cause only.

Do you recollect whether the wood with which they were constructed had undergone the seasoning which wood applied to the construction of ships usually does, before it was employed?—In some instances the timber had been received, I believe, some years (two or three) before it was applied for the purpose of building the frigates.

Can you point out the ships that were built with that seasoned timber?—I believe I can, by referring to documents.

[The witness was directed to furnish this information.]

Were there not several line of battle ships broken up, or obliged to undergo a considerable repair during the last war, in consequence of the dry rot?—Yes, there were ships that were built both in the King's and merchants yards, that after a few years service required very considerable repair, after a comparatively short service.

Were those ships built of English or Foreign timber?—Built of English timber.

To what do you attribute ships built of English timber having been so particularly subject to the dry rot?—In many instances they were ships built in great haste, exposed during their building to the vicissitudes of the weather, and the introduction of unseasoned materials with seasoned; it also frequently arose from new ships being sent into hot climates, and thereby deprived of what is technically termed the second caulking, so highly necessary to new ships.

Could any of the same circumstances have been the cause of the dry rot among those frigates built of the American timber?—Those causes, no doubt, in some degree facilitated their decay; but the frigates that were built of the timber of the north of Europe, were equally exposed to those causes.

You have stated as one of the causes of the dry rot in the line of battle ships, that in the building they were exposed to the vicissitudes of the weather; was not that formerly the case with all ships that were built, that they were exposed for many years in the dock yards to the vicissitudes of the weather?—They were so.

Were there frequent instances of the dry rot at that time?—I believe the dry rot was always known in the navy from its earliest period, more or less; but a long war, and the trying services which our fleet has had to contend with, have caused the defects of ships to be examined into considerably more than they were at former periods; but I might say, if we had examined ships at an early part of the late war as we have done within the last ten years, we should have had very few ships to have fitted out a fleet with, for although a ship may have many defects, she still is sea-worthy.

Is there any difference in the weight of American and Baltic fir?—I believe the weight of the red pine is nearly equal to that of timber of the Baltic; the yellow pine lighter.

Does the American timber appear to have grown quicker than it grows on the shores of the Baltic, or the contrary?—I am not able to answer that question.

Could you furnish the Committee with an account of the times when the timber used in the construction of the ships in 1795 and 1796, was received into the dock yards, and when the timber used in the construction of the ships in 1813, was received into the dock yards?—I dare say we can.

[The witness was directed to furnish this information.]

---
## Page 16

Can you state to the Committee, from your own knowledge, that the timber used in 1813 was at all times properly piled after it was received, and placed in proper situations for seasoning?—I believe it was, agreeably to the usages of the service.

Are the Committee to understand that all the timber used in the construction of those ships in 1813, was received into the dock yards, and issued from thence?—Yes.

None of the timber, so piled, was within the reach of the spring tide?—No, I think not; but all the timber of course was transported from the dock yards to the merchants yards, and of course floated across the river to the merchants yards; those ships were built generally on the opposite side of the river, and the timber was floated from the dock yards at Deptford, Woolwich, or Chatham.

Do you know whether it was piled carefully in a dry place in the merchants' yards, before it was used in the merchants yards?—I cannot tell.

Can you furnish the names of the contractors who built those ships?—I can.

[The witness was directed to furnish this information.]

Has any instance occurred, of a frigate being built entirely in the King's yards of American timber?—None, that I am aware of: it will be right to say, though we call them fir frigates, there is a proportion of oak.

You say, that the American pine timber is peculiarly subject to the dry rot; do you consider the American oak so?—Yes; the North American oak I have seen equally so.

Were not the contractors for building the frigates in the merchants yards obliged to furnish them within a limited period?—They were.

Was not that period much shorter than the time that is generally allowed for building frigates in the King's yards?—The period for building ships in the merchants yards is generally shorter than that at which the same work is performed in the King's yards; they have no limited time in the King's yards, in the merchants yards they have.

Is not that rapid building unfavourable to the durability of the timber?—Rapid building with unseasoned materials is very unfavourable to the durability of the ship; but rapid building with seasoned timber is favourable to the durability of the ship, if the ship be exposed.

Is timber that has been floated up the river to the merchants yards, in a fit state to be immediately used in the construction of frigates?—To use it immediately would be very improper; but timber must undergo a change in form, by being sawn or hewn, which takes up some time, before it can be introduced into a ship.

Do you know that part of that timber, so employed in building those frigates, was not left upon the mud, in situations where it was occasionally overflowed in spring tides?—It is probable it was; but I cannot speak to that.

What is the shortest period within your experience, that ships built of English oak have been found subject to the dry rot?—I have known them in so short a period as two years; in some instances, in less time.

Do you think that English oak, used in the construction of those frigates built of fir, being so subject to dry rot within the period of two years, it might not have affected the fir, in place of the fir affecting the oak?—No; I consider that the oak was injured by the fir, as much of the oak timber was good when it was taken out.

Is it the practice in the navy to use the fir plank of Canada for deck plank?—In some few instances the red pine has been applied.

In such instances, has it been found subject to dry rot, or to premature decay from other causes?—It has, more so than the Prussian deals.

Is it the practice, in the King's yards, at all times to keep their fir timber piled dry in the yards, or to keep it afloat?—The timber which is applied to the construction of ships is generally kept dry; that which is appropriated for masts is immersed in the water.

Has the dry rot been found to exist in those French and Spanish prizes (ships of war) that have been captured and afterwards broken up in our dock yards?—Yes; I have seen the dry rot in a very great degree in many French ships, but not to so great a degree in Spanish ships; they have been built chiefly in South America I believe.

In those instances did those ships appear to be of great age, or otherwise?—The ships I have seen it to the greatest extent in, were the Hercule and the Desiree, and many other small vessels, I have seen it as bad as in any English ships.

Have you had occasion to remark, that the yellow pine of North America is in general cleaner and freer from knots than the pine of the Baltic?—Yes, consider-

---
## Page 17

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

ably so; for which reason we generally apply it for carved work, for ships heads and sterns.

You have stated, that you have known of instances, of a ship built of English oak contracting the dry rot in two years; can you name the ship?—I think I can; the Foudroyant and the Bulwark; I name these two, in the King's yards; I could mention others in the merchants yards also.

Is there not a quantity of fir timber always used in what is called an oak-built ship?—Yes, there is.

In those two ships, was the fir timber Canadian or Baltic?—It is not absolutely in the fabric of the ship that the fir timber is used.

Was any Canadian fir used in those two ships?—I cannot say; I should suppose not; if there had, I do not think it would have at all injured the ship where it is applied.

To what do you attribute the early appearance of the dry rot?—I have stated the causes before, when I referred to a ship being built in haste and sent to hot climates, and so on.

You say that Canadian timber is still used in the yards for large masts, yards, and bowsprits?—It is.

Can you inform the Committee what is the durability of Canadian timber when used for those purposes, as compared with Baltic timber?—That will depend much upon the climate in which the ship served; if in a hot climate, the durability of the American timber, particularly the yellow pine, would be very short; if in a cold climate, it would be prolonged.

Upon the whole, is the Canadian timber in masts as durable as the Baltic?—Certainly not.

Why do you use it, if it is not so durable?—Mast timber of proper dimensions is not to be procured, I believe, from any other part of the world.

Is it likely, that in any event, except that of absolute necessity, any fir frigate shall again be built of American or Foreign fir timber?—I should suppose not.

Is the time necessarily occupied in the conversion of the timber sufficient to remove the effect of any moisture which the timber may have imbibed, either by being floated from the place where it was piled into the merchants yards, or from being exposed on the mud at high tides?—I should suppose, if the timber is reduced, which is generally the case, in its dimensions, that little of the wet which it receives by being transported can be injurious to the durability of the timber, because, although the timber is floated, I do not apprehend the water can have penetrated further than a quarter of an inch.

Were the ships that were built in 1796, of Baltic fir, constructed in the merchants yards or the King's yards?—In the King's yards.

Have you ever seen, yourself, the effect of placing a piece of American timber in contact with a piece of well-seasoned European timber?—Oh yes, many times.

What has that effect been?—It has been to inoculate it with the disease.

You mean, that it has communicated the dry rot?—It has. I have seen when a piece of American timber has been added to another, to fashion it out, termed an eaking, which has been of American oak, where fungus has commenced upon it, and has considerably injured the piece that it was in contact with. I have never seen fir so applied.

You speak of English oak, and American?—Yes.

Had they been equally well seasoned?—I imagine so.

Are you aware that the fir timber coming from Canada is improved in quality within the last few years, from the period at which they cut it, or from the different manner in which it is sent over?—No, I am not aware of that.

You are not aware of the contrary?—No, I am not. The timber is very well manufactured in point of shape, and so on.

Do you suppose that any mode of preparing or manufacturing the timber could obviate the existing objections to its quality?—No, I am afraid not. You may prolong the durability of bad timber, but I am afraid you cannot make it equal to the other.

What, in your opinion, are the relative qualities of American and English oak for the purposes of ship-building? I consider the English oak as superior to the American, as the Baltic fir is to the fir of that country. The American oak is very bad; I think of the two, the oak is worse than the fir.

How would you state the result of the experiment between live oak and British oak?—I should think live oak better than British oak.

---
## Page 18

Has it been used in our dockyards?—No, it has not been; I have seen ships that have been built with it.

Can you furnish to the Committee the result of the different experiments which you state to have been made with respect to the strength and tenacity of American and Baltic pine and oak timber?—I will furnish the information.

Is not the proportion of fir timber used in the construction of a ship compared with oak timber very small?—Very small; it being used only for the flats or flooring of the upper and quarter decks, half beams, &c.

You stated, that there were some parts of a ship, the masts, the bowsprit, and other parts, for which you conceive the American fir to be necessary?—Yes, absolutely necessary, as the materials cannot be obtained of sufficient dimensions from any other part of the world that I am aware of.

Does the same necessity apply to the large description of our merchant ships?—I should suppose so.

Do you at all know what the quantity of timber imported for those purposes is? or can you form any conjecture upon that point?—I have not the means of answering that question.

But whatever it is, no reduction or alteration of duties can affect the importation of timber for those purposes?—Certainly not; that is to say, the timber must be procured.

Have you had no experience of the durability of North American timber when used in the construction of buildings?—No, I have not.

Would it be possible for ships to come loaded with those large masts, to which you have referred, without bringing smaller descriptions of wood by way of stowing their cargoes?—Certainly not; for which reason we generally make our contracts so as to introduce a small proportion of smaller articles, for the sake of the stowage of the larger.

Your state, that whatever might be the state of the duties, the timbers for the masts must necessarily come?—Certainly.

And that if the timber for the masts come, a certain quantity of smaller timber must come with it?—Yes, that has been the usage always.

Then, as far as respected that necessary to come for the stowage of the masts, the alteration of the duties would make no difference?—None.

Is the Committee to understand, that you are not acquainted with any distinction in the qualities of timber, as shipped from Canada and shipped from New Brunswick and Nova Scotia?—We have ever given the preference to the timber shipped from New Brunswick, but it is subject to the same disease; I think the timber from New Brunswick is preferable; but I am not sufficiently master of the subject to say which is from the one and which from the other.

It bears a better character?—It does.

Alexander Copland, Esq. Called in; and Examined.

IN what business are you engaged?—I have been until recently a timber merchant and builder; I have lately retired from the latter branch of business.

How long did you continue a builder?—About six or eight and twenty years.

In that time your observation has been directed to the different kinds of timber used in the construction of houses?—Yes, it has.

Have you been in the habit of using the fir timber brought from the north of Europe, and also that brought from North America?—I have used a very large quantity from the north of Europe, but only a small quantity of the American.

Have you used sufficient to ascertain, to your own satisfaction, the comparative value of those two descriptions of timber in the construction of houses?—I can judge more extensively of the timber from the north of Europe than I could of that from America, having used but a very small quantity of that in building.

What has been your reason for having used so small a quantity of the American timber?—The American timber has not been so generally introduced until the latter years, at which time I was not carrying on works to that great extent, and I should not have felt myself justified in using American timber, particularly in public buildings, until it had been proved by experiment that the quality was equal to that brought from the north of Europe.

Then it was not from any experience you had had of the American timber not being equally good, but from a doubt upon the subject, that you did not avail yourself of it?—I have seen American timber used, and have used a small portion myself, where...

---
## Page 19

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

where it appeared to be very subject to the dry rot; and I have also seen, in instances where the American deals have been laid in contact with each other, that they have a great tendency towards encouraging the growth of the dry rot.

Are you enabled to compare the American timber in that respect with the timber from the Baltic?—Only in the particular instances I have named.

In the use of the Baltic timber, have you observed that it was subject in the same manner to the dry rot?—I have not found it so, except in situations where it has been subjected to a damp or stagnant atmosphere, and I have, in those circumstances, seen the Baltic timber also subject to the dry rot; but from what I have seen, I do not think it is so liable as the American.

In the cases in which you have observed the American timber to be so affected by the dry rot, was it placed in situations similar to those in which you have described the Baltic timber when affected with the dry rot to be placed?—No; I do not think the Baltic timber would have had the dry rot in the space of time in which the American timber I have spoken of, had it in the same situation.

Have you ever had an opportunity of observing the use of the American timber in contact with the Baltic?—No, I have not to my recollection; in my former answer, I spoke of the yellow pine.

Is the yellow pine that which is imported into this country for common and inferior purposes?—It is.

You have had great experience of the qualities of the Baltic timber?—I have.

State to the Committee the result of any observations you have made upon the different qualities of the Baltic timber, as brought from the different countries of the north of Europe?—The superior timbers are drawn from the ports of Memel, Dantzig and Riga; that from Norway is generally of a small dimension, although equal in quality; Swedish timber is also considered nearly equal in quality, but not of so large a dimension in general; for some purposes Dantzig is preferred, it being of larger dimensions; Riga perhaps is considered the next in point of superiority, and Memel the next to that.

Have you had an opportunity of observing the comparative liability of the Baltic and the American deals to the disease of the dry rot?—I have seen many instances similar to what I have stated, the American deals being placed close to each other, that the dry rot would be created in the course of a twelvemonth; and I have also seen Norway deals placed in a similar situation for eight or ten years, that have shown no such symptom.

Do you mean that in both cases the deals have been equally well seasoned?—Yes, both recently imported: I do not speak from experience in buildings, for I have never used the American myself; I have seen it put into buildings, but I have not known the result.

In what situations did you observe deals, where the dry rot was, placed?—Lying in piles in the timber yard.

Are you aware that the quantity of deal shipped from the North American colonies is very small?—I have attended to the concern as a timber merchant so little for the last two or three years, that I cannot speak to particulars or to prices, and I would beg to refer to my partner; I have understood, latterly the importation from North America has been very considerable.

Have they not come for the purposes of stowage only?—No; numbers of cargoes have come.

Do you consider the inferiority of the American to the Baltic timber as applicable to timber as well as to deals?—Yes, it is applicable particularly to timber, the inferiority of the yellow pine.

As much in timber as in deals?—Yes, I should think more so in timber than in deals, because deals are not used in contact with walls or under floors, in damp situations.

Do you see any objection, in your branch of trade, to making the duties on the Norway deals equivalent to the duties on the Prussian and Russian deals, by regulating the duties on the whole by the cubic measurement?—I should see no objection to that mode of fixing the duty, provided a scale were made in favour of those countries where the qualities of the timber are not similar; for instance, I conceive that the Russian deals would not sell in the market, if Norway deals were at the same price; there would also be a considerable difference in the value between Norway and Swedish and Prussian deals, in favour of the Norway; therefore, although the duty might be laid on the cubical contents, it might be proper to vary it in favour of the different countries.

---
## Page 20

Alexander Copland, Esq.

You conceive, therefore, the Norway deals to be the most valuable?—Yes, I do.

Can you state to the Committee what difference it would be necessary to make in the duty for the purpose of bringing the Norway deal upon an equality with the deals of Prussia and Russia?—Generally speaking, I should think the Prussian and Russian deals, perhaps, might be worth one-fifth less than those of Norway.

Should you, as a builder, consider the Norway deal to be twenty per cent. better than the Russian and Prussian deals?—Yes, about that.

What advantage do you suppose there would be in laying a tax, so as to induce the people of this country to buy the worst timber in preference to the better?—That is a political question I cannot answer.

Is the difference between the American fir timber and European fir timber distinguishable to the common eye, when worked up?—A professional man would know the difference; but I do not think any gentleman who had not professional knowledge, would.

Do you not conceive that great deception is frequently practised upon persons who have not professional skill, in the value of the timber, by the use of the inferior instead of the better?—I think it very probable that it may be so.

Do you not think, that in consequence of the encouragement that has been given by the legislature to American over European timber, the buildings that have been generally erected in London within the last four or five years, are much less solid than the buildings of former times?—Where the American timber has been used, I conceive they would be very inferior to those where the timber from the north of Europe has been made use of, from the circumstance of their being more liable to the dry rot.

Do you not suppose that the same objection applies to the new buildings throughout the three kingdoms in the same period?—Yes, as being more subject to the dry rot; a great deal of timber of course is used for other purposes than building, for which it might answer.

In the new buildings, constructed within the period mentioned, have not peculiar precautions been taken to preserve the timber from the dry rot, by admitting the air?—I think if that were done, it might have a good effect, but I am not aware of it; I never used any American timber in houses myself; I do not know whether the precaution has been made use of in other instances.

Do you know, either as a timber-merchant or a builder, the comparative value between the fir brought from the different ports of North America, from the United States, the province of Canada, and that of New Brunswick?—I cannot speak very correctly upon that point.

As far as your experience goes, you consider the American timber as very inferior to the Baltic?—Yes; except in the instance which I have particularized before, which is that of the pitch pine, of which a very small part comes to this country; and the red pine, which I have had no experience in using, but which appears to be very fine in quality.

Are you an importer?—Yes; we import timber from the north of Europe, not from America.

Do you know whether the red pine and the pitch pine do not come principally from the United States?—I believe they do.

Independent of its use in building, is not the American fir used for a great many of the interior purposes?—Yes, to a considerable extent, I should think.

What proportion do you conceive that wood used in those purposes bears to the general consumption of wood in the country?—It is difficult to answer that, except in a very general manner; the deals would be nearly altogether used for those purposes I should think; of the timber, probably one-third for such purposes.

Has the American timber any particular advantages, as applicable to these purposes besides its cheapness?—It has some advantages in particular instances; such as for picture-frame carving where soft wood is required, and packing-cases where great width is required.

Then supposing an alteration to take place in the duties, so that both commodities are brought into the market at the same expense of freight and charges, do not you think that a considerable quantity of American timber would still be imported and sold for those interior purposes?—I conceive very little in that case.

Can you state any instance of houses built with American timber, in which the dry rot has broken out?—I am sorry to say I put some of it into my own house, and it had the dry rot in a year and a half; I had it cut out immediately.

---
## Page 21

Do you know that one of the wings of St. Bartholomew's hospital, which was built with the best Baltic timber, has been lately found so much affected with the dry rot, that it was obliged to undergo a very thorough repair?—I am not aware of the circumstance.

Can you state to the Committee for what purpose, in the building your own house, the American timber was used, which you found decayed through dry rot?—For joists, made use of on the ground floor, where there were cellars beneath it.

Was the situation damp and confined?—Not particularly so.

With what timber did you replace those joists?—I put in oak, for the purpose of being doubly secure; that is not so liable to the dry rot, certainly.

Had you used Baltic fir for similar purposes, in the same situation?—I had, in the same house, in a situation where it was equally exposed to damp, and there was no dry rot.

How many years has the oak stood with which you replaced the Canada timber?—About six or eight years; that is perfect.

Have you any reason to believe it has taken the dry rot?—It has not.

In very unfavorable situations, oak will take the dry rot?—Yes, in very unfavorable situations, but not so soon as fir.

Did you use American fir joists on the first floor, in your own house?—No, I did not.

Have you reason to believe that American fir in that situation would have been affected with the dry rot?—I have never tried the experiment, and cannot speak to it.

You have stated, that you made this experiment seven or eight years ago?—Yes.

Are you aware how long the pine timber had been seasoned that you placed in that situation?—No, it was in the timber yard; it had been recently imported.

You state, that the instance where you saw the dry rot, in the course of a year, was in two planks laid together in a timber yard; in your timber yard there is a great quantity of Canada plank and deal so placed?—Yes.

Have you found that generally the case?—We do not place them so, generally; lately they have been so placed as to admit of air between them.

Do you know of more than one instance?—Yes; it was a report of my clerk lately, that they were obliged to move some deals that had been placed for six months; there was a kind of mildew.

You deal very considerably in Canada timber, as a timber merchant?—Yes, I do.

Do not you find mildew and symptoms of dry-rot appear where Baltic planks are laid together in the same manner?—No, we do not.

Is not the American yellow pine in general much cleaner and freer from knots than the fir of the Baltic?—Yes, it is cleaner, and of larger dimensions, and on that account it is preferred for some purposes.

Have you any experience of North American timber being used in house building for a period of ten or twelve years, which you have found either subject to dry-rot, or perfectly free from it?—No, I have not; there has been an opinion against this timber, which has prevented its being used by all professional men in public works.

You state that Baltic deals are generally twenty per cent. inferior to Norway deals, how much then do you consider Canada deals inferior to the Baltic deals?—Any opinion must be very speculative; the present market price would be the best guide for that; according to the present market price it would probably be from thirty to forty per cent.

You would estimate the difference in value between Norway deals and Canada deals at about fifty per cent?—Yes; I am speaking now very generally, for, as I said before, I have attended very little latterly personally to the timber concern; the relative prices in the market at this time would be the best guide for that information.

As Canada deals are used for other purposes than building, and for some purposes are even of a superior value, is not the difference of value therefore still greater in favour of the Baltic deals, when used simply for the purposes of building?—There would be a difference; but upon the scale in which I am speaking, I could hardly make that distinction.

Is not the North American timber equally suitable for all the ordinary purposes of house building, where it is not subject to close confinement, such as floors, skirtings, mouldings.

---
## Page 22

Alexander Copland, Esq.

(12 February.)

Mouldings and doors, window-shutters, and other purposes?—For some of those purposes I think it is, such as mouldings; for floors it is not so durable, for doors and window-shutters I never tried it; I think it might where it was not attached to the wall; in those cases where the air gets round it, it may be equally good, but I have had no experience to justify my observation.

Do you think that the consumption of Foreign timber in this country has been materially diminished by the existing duties?—No, I should not think it was.

If the duty on Foreign timber was not more than a moiety of what it is, would not the quantity consumed be greater?—I should think there would be very little difference in that respect.

Has your importation of timber from the Baltic been considerably diminished since the imposition of the duties?—I should think not; but I beg to refer to my partner for the particulars.

Martis, 13° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

John White, Esq.

Called in; and Examined.

YOU are engaged in the timber trade?—To a very considerable extent.

Are you engaged in it, in all its branches?—In all its branches.

Do you import timber both from the north of Europe and from America?—I do not import it from America.

But you are acquainted with the wood that is brought from America?—Perfectly I believe.

You have been engaged in a course of some experiments, to ascertain the strength and value of different species of wood?—I have been so for the last two or three years.

Have the goodness to state the result of those experiments?—I think a short extract of the experiments which I have made, will probably give that information. Mr. Lack wrote me a note, saying it was possible I might be called upon for the information.

The witness read the statement as follows:

"RESULTS of Experiments on the Stiffness and Strengths of various specimens of wood.

The trials were made upon pieces carefully selected as to quality and grain, and were in substance, two feet long, one inch square; they were all from split portions of timber. The order of Stiffness was,

| No. | Description                                      | Weight (lbs.) |
|-----|--------------------------------------------------|---------------|
| 1   | Long sound timber, bent half an inch in the middle | 261           |
| 2   | Christians white spruce fir                      | 261           |
| 3   | English oak, young wood; suppose 60 years; from King's Langley, Herts | 237           |
| 4   | American pine, yellow or soft; from Quebec       | 237           |
| 5   | Riga oak (commonly called wainscot)              | 233           |
| 6   | White spruce from Quebec                         | 180           |
| 7   | English oak from Godalmin, suppose 200 years; old timber | 103           |

The order of Strength, as ascertained by their being broken by the application of weight, was,

|-----|--------------------------------------------------|---------------|

---
## Page 23

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Other trials of Strength were as follows:

1. Alice Holt forest, full grown timber No. 1 - 455 lbs. avoirdupois
2. Dantzig fir, yellow - 35
3. Alice Holt forest, full grown timber, No. 1 - 95
4. Christiana yellow fir - 370
5. Archangel, ditto - 330

The only two descriptions of American fir to which you applied your experiments, were the yellow and the white pine of Canada?—Yes.

Those experiments applied to the strength of the different kinds of wood?—To their strength and flexibility.

Can you speak at all to the durability of different kinds of wood?—I think I can.

State your opinion of the durability of the different kinds of timber brought from the north of Europe, and brought from America; first of the fir?—In general use I have no doubt that Norway timber is the most durable, because after many years, it does not part with its resinous particles. I consider that American soft or yellow pine is the most durable of the American firs; I have known it last when exposed to the action of the sun and weather, for a great period, by the side of Norway timber, with equal effect, fully exposed to wind and rain; if painted, it does not stand at all so well.

Are you acquainted with the red pine and the pitch pine of America?—I am acquainted with both.

Have you reason to think they are less durable than the pine you have referred to?—I think so. I have some knowledge indeed of the fact. I remember my father supplying to the Hampstead Water Company's ponds at Highgate, about eight and twenty years ago, the camphorating which was taken down last year; it was perished; and I have seen soft pine which does not appear to me so much affected as the pitch pine was. I have not known the red pine for any thing like the number of years that I have known the pitch pine.

The comparison you have hitherto made has been on wood placed in situations where it was exposed to the wind and weather; are you able to make a comparison between the durability of the American and Northern fir placed in closer situations in buildings?—I am.

State what the result of that is?—I supplied myself to a warehouse in the city some of the most beautiful red pine that there ever was; it was placed in a green wall; but two years afterwards the green wall had destroyed all its effects. I speak of the red pine of Canada; fungus had grown out of it, and all the strength of the wood was destroyed.

Was it the end of the beam that was so destroyed?—Yes the end of the beam, which came against the green wall.

Have you seen fir brought from the north of Europe placed in the same situation?—I have.

What was the appearance that exhibited?—I do not believe it would have been affected in such a situation; I have never seen it affected so.

Have you ever seen the yellow pine you are speaking of in that situation?—I have.

What was the state of that?—That has gone also.

Equally soon?—In a building of my own in Bryanstone-square in a shorter period; it was not above a year and a half.

Are the Committee to infer from this, that you conceive the American timber to be much more liable to the dry rot than that which is brought from the northern parts of Europe?—In damp and close situations, certainly so.
