Being a timber merchant to a considerable extent, can you speak to the purposes to which different kinds of timber are applied?—I think I can.

State to what purposes in this country the different descriptions of fir timber are applied?—The timber from the north of Europe almost exclusively to good buildings; of late years American timber has almost superseded the use of northern timber, in contract and slight buildings.

What do you conceive, as far as your trade is concerned, to be the proportion of that inferior timber which is applied to inferior purposes, to that of the better description of timber which is applied to the better purposes?—The market has been so operated upon of late years, that it is hardly possible to answer the question.

Are there any purposes for which the inferior kind of timber brought from America answers

---
## Page 24

answers as well as the superior kind from the north of Europe?—For inferior purposes certainly, and for some superior purposes even better.

Can you state at all the extent or magnitude of those purposes in which it answers as well or better?—The consumption of London for packing cases; for mouldings, not merely mouldings for rooms, but for cast iron work and a variety of purposes, its magnitude and width gives it a great advantage over the smaller and narrower timber of the Baltic and Norway.

Also for the purposes of ships masts?—I am not much in that line.

Would it answer for scaffolding?—No, I apprehend it would not answer for that; it is too short; I mean brittle.

What proportion of the consumption of the timber that is used in this country do you conceive to be applied to those inferior purposes?—The price of the last year has so operated that I should think three-fourths, but I speak merely from conjecture; my consumption is principally for the larger description, and for better buildings; but I should say, speaking generally, that three-fourths used the last year have been American.

What proportion does that wood, applicable to those purposes, and for which it is as good or better than northern timber, bear to the general consumption and import of the country?—A fourth or a fifth I should say; I speak with reference to the consumption of London and its neighbourhood.

Do you conceive that if the timber from the North and the timber from America can be brought into the market at the same expense for those purposes, the American timber would still have the preference?—I think it would, for those special purposes.

Can you state what effect the imposition of the high duties produced upon your trade?—It filled our pockets in the first instance, and of late years has emptied them.

Be so good as to explain in what way this happened?—Our stocks in hand were increased in value by the addition of the duty, and subsequently they have fallen again, by the introduction of American timber, which is without duty, which has brought northern produce in some measure below its proper or natural level.

What effect was produced on your trade by the imposition of the high duties?—Probably it introduced one moiety of American produce; to other timber-merchants it must have done a great deal more.

Did it, in the same proportion, reduce your import of timber from the North?—Unquestionably.

Do you apprehend that the same effect has taken place generally in the trade, that took place in your particular business?—I believe to a much larger extent with others than with ourselves.

State the reason?—Because our demands are principally for the best buildings, and the best purposes.

What effect do you apprehend would now be produced by an alteration or reduction of the duties on the timber from the north of Europe?—I think, generally speaking, a very beneficial effect to the public in general.

In what way?—Timber would not be applied improperly, that is, American timber would not be used where it ought not, as it now is.

Do you apprehend that it would be likely to produce a great increase of the import from the northern ports of Europe?—I have not a doubt of it.

Do you believe that the effect of the imposition of duties has been to force out of consumption a great deal of the timber from the north of Europe, which ought to be applied to the better purposes of the country?—I am quite sure of it.

Do you believe that the consequence of that has been to make the buildings, and other things to which it is applied, less solid and less durable than they would otherwise have been?—Unquestionably.

You have stated, that the present duties on Baltic timber have forced a great deal of it out of consumption; do you know in what proportion the consumption of Baltic timber in this country has been reduced?—No; excepting looking at the tables; the general public tables.

Have you looked; since you gave your evidence in the House of Lords, at the official returns, printed in the Appendix to their Report?—I have.

Have you not found, by those returns, that the imports from the Baltic, so far from having diminished, as you stated, within the two last years, have very much increased?—I do not think that is the fact.

In your evidence before the Committee of the House of Lords, you were asked, "Whether

---
## Page 25

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

"Whether the sale of Baltic timber has continued to diminish within the last two or three years;" to which you replied, "Certainly; when I spoke before, I spoke of the quantity altogether; the American has superseded the Baltic timber, the price is so much lower." Have you read the official returns of the quantities of timber imported from the Baltic, and do you not find the result to be, that so far from having diminished within the last two years, they have considerably increased? —The price was so low, it might, in bulk, have caused a greater consumption; in value, certainly not.

In your answer here, you say you speak of the quantity altogether?—So I do still.

The official returns are as to quantity, not as to price; has not the quantity increased from all the northern powers of Europe, instead of diminishing?—As far as my knowledge goes, I am satisfied it must be quite the contrary; and I am sure those tables must be constructed on some principle of taking either an eight-feet deal as a twenty-feet deal, or the bulk in some way that they must be quite irregular.

Do you not find, that the quantity of fir timber from Norway, imported in the year 1816 was 10,239 loads; in 1817, 15,501 loads; in 1818, 26,257 loads; and in 1819, 24,750 loads?—I am quite sure then, that it must be the entering up of cargoes for the duty, and not the importation of them in the port of London.

You speak of your knowledge with respect to your own trade, and the trade in the port of London?—I speak of my own knowledge principally; of what I myself am concerned in; I cannot speak generally, because I do not know generally.

Has not the price of timber from North America fallen, as well as the price of timber from the Baltic?—It has.

Would not that tend to encourage the additional consumption of Baltic timber, in the same proportion as that of timber from North America?—I am not quite sure of that.

Has not the fall of price produced by the competition between timber from those different parts of the world, been advantageous to the public?—I have no doubt to the consumer, excepting as before stated that it has caused the introduction of American timber to improper purposes.

Is not that the fault or want of judgment of the parties applying it?—People are often deceived; you may have to pay so much a foot for timber which you suppose to be Baltic timber, when in fact it is American; and it is covered up, and you know no better.

Would any builder or surveyor of respectability impose in that manner upon gentlemen who employed him?—It is impossible almost to detect it; who is to watch every stick of timber put into a building? it can be detected by experiment; but if I were to produce the specimens, they would not be known in the first instance.

Is it not the duty of professional gentlemen to see that proper timber is employed?—They are not always employed; and it would be impossible when the timber is converted in the carpenter's yard, for them to know that improper timber has been employed; when it is in the roof of a building they cannot see that each individual stick is Baltic timber.

You have stated in your evidence before the Lords, that you have scarcely ever known a ship built of Norway timber to have the dry rot?—I have.

Have you seen the Danish sloop of war the Little Belt, which has lately been broken up at Battersea?—I have not; but she I believe was not Norway built; she was Danish.

Do not the Danes use Norway timber in the construction of their vessels?—I believe not; very little timber is imported from Norway to Copenhagen.

From whence do they bring their timber to Copenhagen?—Principally from the Baltic.

As you state Canada timber and American timber generally to be peculiarly subject to the dry rot, may not that arise in a great measure from its being floated down the rivers both in deals and in timber, and shipped in that state in the heat of an American summer, and in that heated state crossing the Atlantic in the hold of the ship?—I believe that the first principles of that vegetation are acquired from that circumstance.

You would consider deals which have been floated down previous to being shipped, more liable to infection than timber?—Nearly equally so, for the bulk of timber generally in an American ship, gives it an equal degree of sweating; I have seen two cargoes of pitch pine timber affected to the heart with dry rot as they were discharged from the ship; that is now seven or eight years ago.

May

John White,
Esq.
13 February.

---
## Page 26

May not that have happened from the timbers having been decayed before it was cut, in some instances?—I think otherwise, and indeed I learnt otherwise on making an inquiry, that it was the length of the period it remained from capture or some circumstances; it remained above a twelvemonth heated in the ship.

Do you recollect any instances in which timber has remained a considerable time on board ships coming from the North?—I do.

Have you observed the same effects?—Yes, I have; but not to an equal extent.

Is the effect of the confinement of timber during a long voyage frequently injurious to the timber?—I have no question of that, when it has been taken in with moisture; moisture is, I am satisfied, the principal cause of dry-rot.

Are you of opinion, that the pine of the north of Europe is in general better seasoned before it is shipped than the pine of North America?—I am very much inclined to think it is; but I have no positive knowledge, excepting in Norway, where I have been, and seen the circumstances of its being shipped.

Judging from the relative appearance of the timber of the north of Europe and of America, when landed here, would you be of that opinion generally, that the one was better seasoned than the other before shipping?—I have no doubt as to the Norway timber, because I have known it, but I am not so sure about that particularly at Memel; I think from thence it has come greener, deals especially.

Is not the timber of Memel in general much better squared than the timber of the North American Colonies?—Certainly.

Therefore, is there not reason to suppose, that the inferior part of the timber, which might more immediately introduce decay, is taken away in the squaring?—It comes with very little sap, which more readily holds the moisture.

Then are you of opinion, that if the timber of America was equally well squared and cleaned, it would be less subject to decay?—I do not think that has much to do with it, because the pine has very little sap.

Have you been long acquainted with yellow pine as an article of importation to this country?—Yes, I have.

For what number of years?—Twelve or fourteen at least.

Have you during that period had occasion to know yellow pine used for the ordinary purposes of house building, where it was not particularly exposed to damp, or shut closely up?—Yes, I have.

In such circumstances, have you found it durable and lasting, or subject to premature decay?—Twelve years is a very limited period judged for the decay of timber, but I certainly have observed its failure. It generally comes cleaner than the Baltic timber; but where the knots appear, the knot of the white spruce gets loose, and of the yellow pine it appears through the paint, notwithstanding all the endeavours to cover it with paint, which are two very objectionable circumstances in building.

Have you not found the dry-rot appear in timber so circumstanced, where it was neither exposed to particular damp, or closely shut up?—Where it is used as skirting, or in floors, undoubtedly it is affected very rapidly by dry rot, led to it perhaps from something else; if it gets near to brick rubbish, it is sure to go.

What is the present relative price between the yellow pine of America and the fir of Riga?—The yellow pine is about half the price of the other; say three guineas and six guineas per load of fifty feet.

Has not a very general increase in the consumption of timber in the country taken place of late years?—I am not so sure of that; I have no tables that will enable me to speak to that point.

Do you recollect, that in answer to a question to this effect, which was put before the Lords: "If the duties were the same, do you believe any American timber would be used?" you answered, "Not one stick?"—I repeat, that using it as timber for beams or such purposes, we make a distinction, calling cut timber, deals, and timbers in the log, timber.

Are you acquainted with the quality of the yellow pine of New Brunswick, Nova Scotia, and the islands of the Gulph of St. Lawrence, as well as that of Canada?—I am.

Do you consider, that there is any distinction or difference in quality between the yellow pine of one colony and another?—There are shades of difference.

But no material difference?—I like the quality of the Miramichi rather better than the Quebec.

Are you aware of any circumstance having been found out, by which the dry rot

---
## Page 27

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

in American timber has been prevented?—No; I have heard of various preparations that are said to do it, but I have no faith in them.

Do you know how the timber is generally brought to this country from the north of Europe?—Generally.

You have stated, that red pine as well as yellow, used for beams and placed in green walls in a confined situation, have perished within the short period of two years; are you of opinion, that the timber of the Baltic would not have perished in a similar manner?—I do not think it would, at any rate, so readily; it is a bad thing to put any timber in green walls; it possibly would have perished, but not so readily.

Is not the general character of American timber well known to the trade?—Very well.

Describe the sorts of building you have specified as being of a superior class, for which Baltic timber is generally used in London?—In those, which are under the Act of Parliament known as first and second rate buildings, Baltic timber is consumed; in those of the third, fourth, and other classes, American.

Be so good as to state what are comprehended in first and second-rate buildings?—Churches, chapels, large warehouses, and gentlemen's houses fit for large families, families of opulence, are first and second-rate buildings; the second-rate only respects a difference in the walls, they are carried to an equal height as the first-rate, but are less in dimension.

Under which description do the buildings in the New Street come?—The New Street is all first-rate buildings; they are limited by the Act of Parliament.

outhouses and buildings of that description are not of that class?—No, in those appendage buildings American timber is frequently used.

To all these sorts of buildings the American timber is used as a matter of cheapness?—Generally speaking so; some persons will not have it.

If the duties were in any degree equalized, and the American timber still continued to be cheaper than that brought from the North, do you think it would still continue to be used for those purposes?—To a certain extent.

At what proportionable price do you think the American timber would be used in preference to the Baltic timber?—It may be at one third less.

For all purposes?—No, for inferior purposes; for superior ones they will not use it all.

Is not the scale of durability on which inferior buildings are constructed, as to brick work, such that the American timber is likely to last as long as the building will in other respects?—I think not, it is not a matter of my experience to say so; but my opinion is certainly, that the timber will go before the building; indeed in some individual instances I have known it do so, they have taken it out, but it has been principally from the greenness of the wall.

Will not a builder endeavour so to construct his buildings as to make the duration of one material keep pace with that of the other as nearly as possible?—He ought.

Presuming that a change was to take place in the manner of levying the duties now payable on deals, by placing them on a footing with the duties payable on square timber; and that this change of system led to the diminished importation of deals, and an increased importation of timber, would the facilities be found in this country sufficient for cutting up the timber into deals at a moderate expense?—I think not.

Do not you think, that if the duties were so regulated, establishments for sawing by steam or otherwise would be found?—I think not, because the deals come from the ship directly to the merchants yard, the duty has forced them into the docks; but merchants who can pay their duties take them into their yards in the first instance, and there they are to the consumer's hands exactly the thing which he wants, and there is no waste of slab or otherwise.

Are you not aware that an establishment has been formed under the sanction of government for sawing timber in the docks, where it is landed and warehoused?—A permission has been given for the purpose of exportation, and I have availed myself of it to saw for exportation, but not for home consumption.

If the same facility was extended to home consumption, would not every objection you have stated in the present state of things to the sawing mills here at a moderate expense be done away?—I am sure it would not.

What objections would then remain?—Deals cannot come in a more convenient form than they do; of all classes such as a man can readily handle and use, one of the reasons of the convenience of the Norway deal is, that it is easily carried, it is easily put upon a cart, and there are various other circumstances of convenience about it.

Would

---
## Page 28

Would it not be equally easily carried, if it were sawed in this country? — After it was sawed unquestionably.

Are you aware that the principal sawing establishments at Riga are by steam? — I believe they are.

Are you aware that the Dutch have, for the purpose of encouraging their own saw mills, prohibited the importation of wood in any other shape but of the log? — They have almost always had their timber in the log from Norway.

Is not the importation in deals altogether prohibited? — Not to my knowledge. In France it is very much encouraged, and in the thinnest leaves possible.

Are you of opinion that if the duties on deals were placed on a footing with the duty on square timber, the importation of deals would not be diminished? — I should wish to consider that question before I answer it.

Is the Committee to understand that no timber used in this country for deals, comes otherwise from the Baltic than in the shape of deals? — I do not believe that there does. I imported some myself from Norway for the purpose of sawing, and found it would not answer.

Are you aware that there is a very extensive establishment of saw mills at Leith, where timber is imported expressly for the purpose of being there cut into deals? — I have heard it, but do not know it.

Are you aware of any objection there would be to making the duties equal the duties upon the deals and upon the log? — It would be an eternal trouble.

In what way? — They are none of them so exactly of a size that they could be counted into quantity.

Would there be any difficulty in taking the duty on the cubic contents of deals? — I think that would prolong exceedingly the discharge of a ship; we find it takes a great while to count out five or six hundred deals; that there would be a great delay I am satisfied.

Would not there be less delay in taking the cubic contents than by the count? — No, certainly not; it would in my opinion afford opportunity for cheating; the officer I fear would never be kept in order.

Supposing it to be desirable to equalize the duty upon the log and upon the deals, what allowance should in your opinion be made for waste in sawing the logs into deals? — I think there would be the injury of quality as well as the injury of waste, because many deals turn out bad when they are sawn out of the log and thrown aside, they will come here in a different state of perfection.

Supposing the log to be cut up without any defect, what would be the difference arising from the mere waste? — It cannot cut up without defect, because every log of timber that I have seen is more or less shaken, even supposing it to have no bad knots; it is an impossibility almost.

To what do you attribute the great increase of the importation of timber from America within the last two or three years; is it attributable to the demands of the country, or to any other cause? — The want of employing capital I think has had a great deal to do with it; persons have been sending their capital into all sorts of experiments, many detrimental ones I am sure.

Do not you suppose the want of employment of shipping has had to do with it also? — I suppose it has had to do with it; the last cargo of Miramichi timber sold for 3l. 1s. and the freight was five and forty shillings; that could pay nobody.

Do you mean that the 3l. 1s. includes the 45s. freight? — Yes, and all other charges and expenses, to bring it up to my yard in Westminster at 3l. 1s.; I have had many cargoes offered to me to take them up at the freight.

Lancelot Holland, Esq. Called in; and Examined.

IN what business are you engaged? — A timber merchant.

Are you also a builder? — No, I have never been a builder.

You are acquainted with the timber of the north of Europe and of North America? — Buying and selling considerably all foreign timber.

Can you speak to the comparative quality of the different kinds of timber? — The best timber which comes into London, I believe to be the Riga timber. The Dantzig timber is a larger grown timber; it is of a size which you cannot procure from Riga, and the quality is perhaps quite as good. I think the general prejudice is in favour of the Riga; and that next to those comes the Menel, which is of exactly the same size as the Riga timber, but it is coarser; it is more knotty, and therefore more liable to break. Then comes the Swedish timber next to that, the red pine timber from America. I omitted Norway timber, because there is so very small

---
## Page 29

small a quantity of it used; the duty is so very heavy; there is hardly any of it imported to make it a subject of consideration; the red pine timber is about equal in price, and I believe as good as the Swedish timber in quality.

Where would you place the Norway timber in point of quality?—Perhaps first, certainly not second to any. After the red pine, you come into a large field of different timbers, which come from America, which are all called the yellow pine, and are all of an inferior quality, of a softer wood.

What are the differences?—I think that shipped from Miramichi is rather stronger than what comes from Quebec.

You consider the American timber as greatly inferior to the Baltic?—Not the red pine. I think if the red pine is well manufactured, it is as good as any timber that comes into London. I believe, if the red pine could be manufactured so as to be exactly like Riga timber, there would not be more than 10s. a load, I am sure not more than 5s., difference between the value of that and Memel timber; but in the manufactory, it is to be considered, the timber is all measured here, and if it is not quite square and parallel there is a great loss, the angles being measured as if they were square; this loss might amount to about 10 per cent.

Do you consider the yellow timber as very inferior?—That is not fit for building purposes; it is very good for some purposes; for finishing the inside of rooms it will do, but it will not do for strength; it is a much weaker timber. I speak both of the Quebec and the Miramichi.

In point of durability, how do you rank them?—That is according to the purpose; this table will last if it is made of either, but if you put the American in damp situations, it will perish more rapidly than any other.

Is it more liable to the dry rot?—All wood is liable to the dry rot; but I think the soil is more congenial to the growth of the dry rot in yellow pine; the wood is more obnoxious to it.

Much more than in the red pine?—Much more than any other timber.

Is the red pine more liable to it?—I apprehend it is not more so than Swedish timber.

Do you apprehend the length of the voyage from America contributes to the dry rot?—If wood is put on board a ship in a damp state, which it is nine times in ten, you will see a tendency to dry rot whenever the timber is delivered, that is, you will see the commencement of the dry rot on the surface.

By wet do you mean green?—Not entirely; in Memel it is kept under water, when they wish to preserve it, for a great length of time; they consider that will preserve it better than an alternate exposure to wet and dry.

Have you observed this more in the American timber than in the timber from the Baltic?—The voyage being longer, the dry rot has had more time to grow; but if it is shipped in a dry condition, it will come out perfectly good and sound.

Do you know the different purposes to which the different kinds of timber are generally applied?—The first timbers I mentioned are applied for what is called builders purposes, that is to say, for the frame work of a house; then the yellow pine is not at all applicable to that purpose; the red pine is.

To what purposes is the yellow pine applicable?—The yellow pine is used by coach makers, blind makers; it is better for musical instrument makers than any other; for mouldings, where you want soft wood to carve picture frames; chests and packing cases, to an immense amount.

What proportion of the wood imported into this country is employed for those purposes?—It would astonish the Committee to know the quantity used for packing cases in London alone; if I were to guess, I should say one-fourth of the whole wood that comes into London.

For those inferior purposes this description of wood is as good as the superior description would be?—No, not for the packing cases altogether; a great number of the packing-cases made for long voyages they are obliged to make out of Baltic deals, because the American is so very soft it will not hold the nails; the East India Company have perhaps half, and half, or rather more Baltic than half; they will not admit it except for copper cases; for their stationary, and other things they are very anxious to preserve, where the splitting of wood would be of consequence, they will not admit the American.

Do they use the Baltic timber now for many of those purposes?—The Baltic deals are used for packing-cases to a very great extent; the price is not very different.

Supposing that the freight, insurance and all charges were the same on American and Baltic timber, and the duty equal, what effect would that have upon the sale of American timber?—Almost a total exclusion of the American timber.

---
## Page 30

To what extent could the duty be laid on American timber to bring it on equal terms, considering its relative value to Baltic timber, to market?—I apprehend that the American timber would not bear a duty of more than 10s. or 15s. per load of fifty feet, with a proportion on deals, and I think it would bear that duty, and continue to sell very freely. I should like to lay more upon the red pine, because at this time it sells in London for 2l. a load more than the yellow pine, namely 5l. for the best of it; therefore if they can bring the yellow pine to London, and sell it for 3l. and can obtain 5l. for the red pine, they must in the country receive a price of 2l. a load for their red pine beyond what they get for their yellow pine, therefore I imagine they must have an enormous profit, for all we pay at Memel is about 25s. a load for timber: Now suppose at the present prices, the yellow pine leaves but ten shillings a load for the prime cost, the red pine at 5l. a load would leave fifty shillings.

Supposing the freight upon a load of timber from Memel were 21s. and the freight upon a load of timber from Quebec were 45s. would a duty then imposed of 10s. a load upon yellow American pine admit of its being imported into London?—I think it would.

You think 10s. is as much as it would bear?—From 10s. to 15s.

What is the present price of Memel timber in London?—It has lately risen 5s. in consequence of a small supply in the Autumn, it is about 6l. 5s.; 6l. has been about the price for the last six months.

What is the price of American yellow pine?—From 3l. to 3l. 5s.

Upon the same grounds that these questions have been put, how much do you think the duty on foreign timber might be lowered, so as to admit the Canadian yellow pine into this country?—I think if you lowered the duty on the foreign timber, you would raise the price in the country whence you imported it.

You think the price would remain the same here?—I think it would, or there would be very little difference.

Why should lowering the duty raise the price in the country, unless it augmented the consumption in this county?—It would check the diminution of the quantities of timber imported from the Baltic; the merchants are now in a very ruined state; the merchants at Memel, which is the principal port from which we get building timber, are gaining nothing; they are selling the timber at the price it stands them in, within a shilling or two; there are efforts made every spring to raise it; this year they have met together and combined to raise the price 5s. a load; and I apprehend, that if any amount of duty were taken off, the greater part of it would go to facilitate their object, because the quantities that are imported from Memel are annually diminishing most prodigiously; the quantities imported from America are annually increasing in an equal ratio. The quantity of timber from America, imported into the port of London last year, was double that from Memel; one is gradually going out, and the other is gradually coming in; if the importation of late years be examined, the progress of it will be seen.

Has the importation from other ports in the Baltic decreased in an equal degree?—Yes, quite; I include all the ports of the Baltic.

You have stated that in your opinion the red pine would not be more subject to dry rot, than the timber which came from the Baltic; have you had any experience of the use of the red pine in buildings?—My experience is, having sold it to builders, and having heard their reports; not from any personal knowledge of my own; I have not had sufficient experience of it to speak decidedly from what I have seen; but the red pine deals in the piles do not get the dry rot so soon as the yellow pine deals.

Your answer was grounded on what you heard from builders to whom you have sold the timber, and the experience you have had of the piles in your timber yard?—Exactly so.

Do you know from what part of America the red pine principally comes?—There is a good deal comes from the United States; but they have latterly sent a good deal from New Brunswick, from what are called the lower ports; there is a great deal grows in our own colonies.

Is there any comes from the river St. Lawrence?—Yes; it does not grow near Quebec.

If a duty of 10s. per load were imposed on yellow pine from Canada, sold here at 3l. to 3l. 5s. per load, what net proceeds would remain to the importer after paying freight and charges?—Little or nothing.

If yellow pine at the present market price, after paying the charges, does not leave a sufficient surplus to cover the prime cost, on what principle do you propose to subject

---
## Page 31

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

subject it to a duty of from ten to fifteen shillings a load?—Because the Americans have glutted the English market, and therefore it is unnaturally depressed.

You are then of opinion, that if such a duty was laid on American pine timber, the market price would advance in proportion to the amount of duty, and that the consumption would not be affected thereby?—I apprehend that the consumption would be checked in a small degree; but I think that would be a very good thing, there is a great deal of it improperly used.

Would not the effect of a duty of from 10s. to 15s. on Canada timber be so far to increase the demand for timber from the north of Europe, as to occasion a rise of price in those ports?—If the American timber now bore its natural price, I think it would have that effect; but as it is now unnaturally depressed, I do not think it would; I do not apprehend it would affect, in any way, the price at Memel; the difference at this moment is what it cannot continue to be.

Supposing that a duty of from 10s. to 15s. on American timber was to continue in operation for a considerable number of years, during which it is probable the market would again be in an ordinary and healthy state, do you think that American timber would continue to be used for buildings?—I think it would continue to be used, but not quite so much.

If a duty somewhat higher was imposed than that which you have suggested, might it not have the effect of excluding Canada timber entirely from buildings, and yet to leave it open to the market, which you have stated to arise from packing cases and other purposes for which it is particularly applicable?—It certainly might.

Can you inform the Committee what duty Canada timber would bear, if its quality was equal to the Baltic timber, and if it was only exposed to the higher charges which arise from a longer voyage, and from its being brought down to the water with greater difficulty?—I am not aware of its being brought down to the water with greater difficulty; but setting that aside, 2l. would put it on a level, that is, the difference between the duty and the freight; I take the difference of the freight, and subtract that from the duty.

Are you acquainted with the quarters in America from whence the red pine is brought?—No, I am not; my last answer had reference to the yellow pine only.

Jovis, 15° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

Lancelot Holland, Esq.

Again called in; and further Examined.

BE so good as to state what are the present prices of yellow Canadian deals?—Fourteen pounds, by the Petersburgh standard, which is the usual standard by which they are sold.

What is the price of the Baltic plank?—It would average about 18l. for yellow, by the same standard, and about 15l. 10s. for white wood.

Then do you not from that conclude in the public estimation, that is to say, in the estimation of those who use the timber, the value of the Canadian pine, as compared with the Baltic white pine is as 14l. to 15l. 10s. and as compared with the yellow pine is as 14l. to 18l.?—I think at least that I should say if Baltic deals were to rise it would not make the other rise; I think if Baltic plank sold at 20l. American plank would continue to sell at 14l.; therefore I do not think that it is quite conclusive.

Do you think the circumstance of Parliament occupying itself with the timber trade has had the effect of varying the price of Baltic or Canada timber?—I conceive the importation of deals from America to have fallen short in 1820, and importation of timber to have increased.

Do you attribute this alteration to the apprehension of any alteration in the regulation of the duties?—I think in 1819 there was an increased importation under the expectation of a duty being laid upon American deals, and I think our market was glutted, and that has checked the importation this season 1820; the observation applies to deals.

Do you not think that until the question is in some way or other settled, the importations...

---
## Page 32

portations will take place out of proportion to the consumption of the country?—Certainly.

Are you not of opinion that any alterations of the duties which shall have the effect of a graduated alteration, will be constantly an encouragement to the importers from the Colonies to glut the market with timber beyond what it can carry off?—Certainly; I am of that opinion very decidedly.

Would it not, therefore, be, in your opinion, wiser policy and more consistent with justice towards timber dealers in this country, and those concerned in the importation, for Parliament to regulate at once a permanent system of duties upon that important article?—I am of opinion it would be decidedly for the good of all parties.

Be so good as to state the market price of colonial yellow pine timber per load?—From 3l. to 3l. 5s.

The Russia yellow pine?—The Memel timber, 6l. 5s.; Riga timber, 6l. 10s.

Supposing a duty of ten shillings laid upon the load of American timber, would not that bring the price to 3l. 15s. for the purpose of affording the same freight charges and prime cost which it at present affords?—Certainly.

Do you think, if the price of the colonial pine were at 3l. 15s. while the yellow Russian pine remains at 6l. 5s. to 6l. 10s. that there would be the same demand for the colonial timber, in proportion to the Russian timber, which now exists?—When the ports are open, I think that the price of Russian timber would be more fairly taken at from 6l. to 6l. 5s. that the rise of the price of ten shillings in the yellow and American pine would not materially diminish the consumption of it.

Do you think a rise of fifteen shillings would?—I think after ten shillings it would; but I think it would be used to a very great extent, whilst the difference between the yellow pine and the Baltic timber was thirty shillings per load.

Supposing the relative duties upon the two species of timber to be altered, by taking off duty upon European timber, would that proportion of duty taken off from Russian timber act in the same manner as the same amount of duty laid on the colonial timber?—I think it would not.

What proportion of duty, according to your opinion, might be taken off of European timber, before any effect would be felt in the consumption of the American timber?—The question is difficult to answer; because I think the taking off the duty from Baltic timber would tend to raise the price of that article in the country.

Supposing it to produce no effect, but the markets there were to remain the same, would the effect here, of taking off the duty on the Baltic timber, be, in your opinion, exactly equivalent to laying on the same amount of duty on the American timber?—Certainly.

If in place of laying a duty of ten shillings on American timber, the duty on foreign timber was to be reduced ten shillings, should you be of opinion that would increase the demand of foreign timber?—Not materially.

Are you of opinion, that a duty of ten shillings laid on American timber, would lead to the importation of timber of a superior quality, and better prepared?—No, not to any extent.

Supposing the prime cost of colonial yellow timber to be 1l. per load in the country, the freight to be 2l. 10s. and the charges ten shillings; making together 4l. if ten shillings was added to that, would yellow pine continue to bear a fair demand in the market at 4l. 10s. while Baltic timber was selling at 6l.?—The consumption of it would be considerably diminished I should think, but there would be still a very considerable market for it.

In what proportion do you think the consumption would be reduced?—I dare say nearly half.

Is it your opinion, that supposing the duties remained as they are at present, the trade in Canada timber is likely to continue to the same extent?—I think it would go on increasingly.

How is that consistent with its being carried on to its present extent at a loss?—Because my answer had a reference to the last series of years, and not to the present moment merely.

Would such an imposition of duty as would produce a diminution of one half of the trade in Canadian timber, produce a correspondent increase in the Baltic trade?—Yes, without an increase in the price should in a small degree lessen the consumption of timber.

Do you in giving your evidence before the Committee, speak with a particular reference to the London market, or with a view to the general market of the kingdom?—Entirely with reference to the London market.

---
## Page 33

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

You are aware that the present existing duty on deals and battens is lower than the duty on the same quantity of timber in the log?—I believe that to be the case, with the exception of Norway goods.

What would be the effect in your opinion of equalizing the duty on wood in its different shapes, so as to take it generally upon its cubic contents?—I think that it would produce a great deal of inconvenience; it is a very difficult thing to do; every cargo would be a great deal longer in delivering than it now is; there would be a vast number of officers in attendance, and a great expense on that account would be incurred, besides which constant bickerings about the deals being an inch narrower or an inch larger would take place; and that in general it would create great delay.

Are you aware of any other difficulties?—I think likewise it would not be fair upon those ports which are more distant and have a higher freight to pay; the ship can make two voyages to Norway for instance, while she can only make one to Riga or Petersburgh; therefore Norway can bear a heavier duty than Riga: but I think the difficulty may be got over by circumscribing the present dimensions, and then the duty can be levied in a much more simple manner; the Norwegians have now one advantage and one only, in exporting eight-feet deals; I should therefore say in fairness I should make the present duty on deal ends, to commence at one foot lower; instead of eight feet I should begin to call any thing above seven feet a deal; then I think Norway borne very hardly upon by the Russian ports, by their twenty-feet deals; I should therefore circumscribe their length and limit them to sixteen feet; the average of Norway deals is now about thirteen feet long, three inches thick, nine inches wide; therefore they would still pay a heavier duty than those distant ports which have to pay a heavier freight.

The duty on deals the Committee understand, can now be only collected by measuring the thickness of the deals; are you aware whether that is so?—It can only be collected in that manner; but in consequence of the duty, there is one dimension entirely shipped from each port, for they take the utmost extent to which you limit them; and if you were to say that only sixteen-feet deals shall be imported, you would not then have any fifteen-feet deals from parts which can export sixteen-feet; but if you were to take the duty by the cubical contents, it would enable the merchants to ship all shapes and sizes, and therefore there would be great difficulty in collecting the duties.

If a plan were devised that would obviate the difficulties you have stated, and by which the relative proportion of duties now payable on deals imported from different countries was preserved, would it be advisable, in your opinion, to alter the present duty on deals, so as to bring it substantially to the present rate now payable on the same timber?—I think it would be a very desirable thing to equalize the duties, to let deals and timber pay equally; for now the raw material is paying less than the manufactured one, I should think that would be unobjectionable.

Then if such a system was adopted, do you think it would lead to a decreased importation of deals, and to an increased importation of timber?—I do not think it would to any extent.

Are you aware of any objection why the duty should not be altered to such an extent as to give some encouragement to the saw mills of this country?—If you were to saw your own deals in this country, you would entirely alter the system of the timber trade both abroad and in this country; you would bring over a great deal of wood which would be of a very bad quality, for in opening timber a very large proportion of it is found not to be fit to be converted into deals, and which is kept back in the country where it is cut. Slabs, saw-dust and waste, would be brought over here, duty would be paid upon it, freight would be paid upon it, and it would be of no use to the country.

Do you believe that deals are sawed into shorter pieces, for the purpose of coming in as deal ends?—I think they have got ten and eleven-feet deals left on hand, which they have been obliged to reduce to eight-feet deals, and they have not lately manufactured a great number of that size to bring to this country; but I think if the same system were to continue, it would be so to a very great extent.

Are deal ends used for the general system of building in this country?—Yes.

Do you see any disadvantages that would arise from equalizing the duty between the deal ends and the larger deals?—I do not think that they would come near the same price in the market, and therefore I think there would be a loss to the merchant; every cargo must bring some proportion of deal ends for broken stowage.

Presuming it was the object of the legislature, when it laid on the duty on deal ends,

---
## Page 34

ends, to encourage them being brought as broken stowage, is it not a fact that they are now brought as cargoes?—Yes.

If the present relative proportion of duty on deals was preserved, and a scale advanced to place them on a proportionate footing with the duty on square timber, would such a measure, in your opinion, be practicable and advisable?—Yes; but I think the present duty on twenty-feet deals is not in proportion, and ought to be increased, because at present the duty upon Norway deals, by the load, amounts to £1. 17s. 1d. and upon the Baltic plank to £1. 1s. 8d., a difference considerably greater than the difference of the freight.

Mr. Brodie Augustus McGhie; Called in; and Examined.

WILL you have the goodness to state what you are?—I am at present a shipbroker, and concerned in repairing and building of ships.

To a considerable extent?—Yes; I am concerned in three docks.

Can you speak of the durability of ships that have been built of different descriptions of timber?—Yes; I have seen a great number of ships; I have had a great many go through my hands, which have been repaired; I have seen several that have been built in different countries.

Will you state the result of your observations upon ships which have been built of American timber?—The durability of ships chiefly depends upon the seasoning of the timber; I am of opinion, that ships built with Quebec white oak is very nearly equal to English white oak; and, in many instances, the English timber has decayed first; I know of five or six instances, of ships that were built in this country; one was called the Minerva, 600 tons, built at Lancaster, of English oak, and she was obliged to be rebuilt after six years, in consequence of the dry rot. There was another ship, called the Batavia, of 500 tons, built at Topsham; she was likewise built with English oak. When six years old there was the Alexander; she is now in the river; she was built at Hull about six years back, and she was totally rebuilt about two months back.

All those ships were obliged to be rebuilt, in consequence of the dry rot?—Yes; there was the Atlas, of 1200 tons, an East-Indiaman, built by Staniforth & Co., of Hull; she was nearly rebuilt when seven years old, from the dry rot. There was a 74-gun ship, the Blake, which was built at Deptford, with German and Baltic oak, and was sold and broke up, occasioned by the dry rot; when seven years old, she was sold out of the government service as not worthy of being repaired; she was built with German and Baltic oak; I can speak of this to my own knowledge.

Was there any other timber made use of, in the construction of those ships?—Not in the first; there was nothing but English timber; what the decks were I am not acquainted with; they might be American or Baltic, but that forms an immaterial quantity.

Supposing the decks or the bulwarks, or any other parts of the ship, which are built of fir, whether of America or any other, do you suppose it would be at all likely that they could have communicated the dry rot to the other part of the ship?—Not at all.

You have spoken of several English ships which have been broke up, in consequence of the dry rot; can you speak to any ships which have been built with American timber, and which have lasted as long?—I have seen several ships that have been built at Quebec, which, when ten or twelve years were expired, were none of them so bad as those ships.

Have you seen any vessels built of Canadian timber that have been broken up at as early a period as those you have mentioned, in consequence of the dry rot?—No, I cannot say that I have.

In your answer to the last questions, you have spoken of ships built at Quebec, are you acquainted with any ships built of American timber in this country?—No, I am not.

Are you acquainted with any that are partially built with that description of timber?—We repair ships with American timber, because it comes cheaper.

Can you state how those repairs hold out, in comparison with repairs made upon ships with English or other timber not American?—Speaking from my own knowledge, I have seen no difference at all; we have repaired ships with Quebec timber, and which answered all our expectations; had the repairs been done with English oak,

---
## Page 35

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

oak, I do not apprehend they would have been better. Besides, it is so much more expensive, that few would go to that expense, for the sake of putting in English plank.

Have you observed that those ships have been more liable to the dry rot than those which have been built with the Baltic timber?—No, I have not.

Is it your opinion that American timber is more subject to dry rot than the British or Baltic timber?—They are all subject to the dry rot, if not properly converted.

The question put by the Committee applies to timber under the same circumstances?—I should think not; not the white oak. I never in my life saw a Quebec ship rotten in six years, and I have had a great many through my hands.

Have you seen any instances of ships, where both British and American timber have been used, becoming liable to the dry rot from the mixture of the two?—No, I cannot say I have; I should think they would be likely to contaminate each other, if put in not properly seasoned. I think that the Quebec oak would destroy the English; and the English, if not properly seasoned, would have a tendency to destroy the other.

Then if it should appear to take place, you would attribute it to the different states in which the timber was used, and not to the natural quality of the wood?—I should attribute it to the state in which the wood was in when used.

Is it not the case that any wood which has contracted the dry rot, will communicate it to any other wood with which it may come in contact?—No doubt of it; it is clearly a disease.

From what cause do you think the dry rot to arise, principally in ships?—My own opinion is, that the cause in the English oak is its being cut down in an improper season, the sap being up the tree, which is done for the sake of saving the bark, but which in my opinion causes a vegetation, and produces the dry rot.

Do you not attribute a great deal to the situation in which it may be placed?—If it is not cut in the proper seasons it is no matter where you put it; it will have the dry rot.

Will not the best seasoned timber also acquire the dry rot, if placed in close damp situations not sufficiently aired?—It will injure the timber, no doubt, but it would not have the dry rot in the same period of time that the other would; timber that is well seasoned will last for a great number of years, especially English oak, if got at a proper time.

Are you of opinion that the dry rot originates solely from the want of seasoning in the timber, and never from the position in which that timber is placed, when the timber is well seasoned?—I think it arises from the cause I have mentioned, namely, cutting it at improper seasons; if it is cut at the proper season and not seasoned before it is converted, it will get the dry rot.

What do you mean by proper season?—Cut in the proper season.

Is not all the American oak cut in the winter season?—I do not know; I believe they cut it in the summer; I never was up the country, though I have been at Quebec.

You cannot speak to it?—No.

Are you of opinion American oak and English oak, each felled at the same season, are equally liable to the dry rot, and the same with respect to American fir and Norway fir?—It depends a great deal upon the seasoning and felling; at the same time it depends a great deal on the situation where it grows; oak that grows in a low swampy situation, as for instance in some parts of Lincolnshire, is a red foxy oak, and do what you will with it, it will get the dry rot. With respect to drawing a comparison between the felling American and English oak, I think it would have the same effect on them. The white oak in America is very little inferior to the English oak if seasoned properly; at the same time I certainly should prefer the English oak to the American oak, as I should think that the one would be more subject to the dry rot than the other.

Is there any other description of American oak brought to England except white oak?—A great deal comes into this country both red and white.

Which comes in the greatest quantity?—It is impossible for me to say; perhaps there may be as much of one as the other.

What are the purposes to which it is chiefly applied?—Chiefly applied to the repairing of ships.

Do you allude to the red oak?—Both the white and the red.

Please to describe to the Committee, what in your opinion are the qualities of red oak?—Red oak is a very inferior wood, it is very subject to the dry rot.

Does

---
## Page 36

Does this disposition for dry rot depend very much on the seasoning at which it is felled, or to its position in the work in which it is placed?—I should think that the cause of the Quebec oak timber being subject to the dry rot, very much depends on the manner in which it is converted; it comes over here in logs, it is put in the water, and after laying there some time, it is taken out of the water to the saw-pit; there it is cut up and put into the ship immediately, which is very much against the quality of the wood; let it be what it will, it will sooner decay in that way than if cut into plank, and had remained under a shed to season it, for all the damp is in it which causes it to decay.

Do you think then that a more careful system of management would prevent the red oak from becoming subject to the dry rot?—I think if the oak was to come from America in the shape of plank, as it does from the Baltic, it would be superior to the Baltic plank; the Baltic plank comes converted into plank; it is landed, and there it lies for months before it is sold, and all that time it is seasoning; whereas the Quebec comes in the log; it is cut into plank and converted at once, which is not doing that justice to it which it ought to have, or indeed to any timber so used.

Is there any thing in the state of the laws which prevents the Canada timber from being imported in the state of plank instead of log?—No; if they had mills in Canada to cut them; as a commercial country Canada is as yet only in its infancy.

Are you not aware that in the United States of America great prejudices exist against the northern oak, as well the Canada as the New England oak, and that the preference is always given to the oak grown in the middle States?—I should think the United States have very little knowledge of the Canada oak; the ships that are built at Boston are built of red oak, which are considered very inferior to ships built at New York or Philadelphia.

Is there no white oak grown in the northern States?—Very little.

Will you have the goodness to state to the Committee any observations that you have had an opportunity of making with respect to the comparison of the American fir with the fir of the north of Europe?—The red pine of America in my opinion is equal to the pine which comes from any ports of the north of Europe for masts or for decks of ships; the yellow pine I should think equal to the Norway pine or to the Swedish; but the Dantzie I should think superior to any of the others except the Quebec red pine.

When you say that, do you mean for any particular purposes?—I mean for ship-building exclusively.

Do you not consider the Riga pine superior to the Dantzie?—Yes, I consider it superior.

Have you had any experience of the use of the Norway timber in ship building?—No.

How do you estimate the yellow pine of Canada as compared with the yellow pine of the Baltic?—It depends upon what purpose it is to be applied; for the internal work of a house I should have no choice which I take; but if it was for the repairing of a ship, the Riga and Dantzie are preferable to the American yellow pine.

For what parts in the construction of the ship do you use the yellow pine?—Principally for the flooring of the decks.

Is it used for timber?—Very seldom, the English ship-owners generally prefer the Quebec oak.

Is the Committee to understand that for the flooring of decks the yellow pine is superior to the Norway pine?—It certainly is; the other is not large enough, you cannot convert it, you cannot make a deck of it.

Then when you speak of the quality of the American pine as compared with the Norway pine, you do not mean to give an opinion as to the quality of the wood, but you give the preference to the American over the Norway, in consequence of the latter not being convertible to the proper sizes?—Yes, certainly.

Have you ever seen ships built entirely or principally of American fir?—Yes, I have in New Brunswick; they build those ships of oak at Quebec.

What has been the durability of those ships?—The ships that I have seen built of red pine at New Brunswick, have been superior to almost any ships except oak ships; indeed for myself, I would as soon have a red pine ship as many that are built in this country, at Sunderland for instance.

How long have you known those ships to last?—Nine or ten years.

Are you aware that about the year 1813, several frigates were built of American pine in the merchants yards?—Yes.

---
## Page 37

Those ships were broken up in 1816; had you an opportunity of seeing any of those ships broken up?—Yes; Sir Robert Wigram built four, and the others were built at Hill's or Curling's dock.

Do you know whether those were built of red or white pine?—That I cannot tell.

Had you an opportunity of seeing those ships in consequence of breaking them up?—I only saw them going down the river as I was passing.

If those ships had been of a size which could have been employed for the merchants' service, were they then in such a state that they could have been so employed?—If they had been ships that could have been converted into merchantmen, I have no doubt they would have been in existence yet, or would have been sold to merchants abroad, but these frigates could not be sold to foreigners, as it is a special condition made by the Navy Board.

Why do you suppose they were broken up if they were in a sound state?—I should suppose peace coming, it was not worth the while government to keep them merely to lie by the walls to rot.

The Committee understood you to say you did not see them only upon passing when you were going down the river?—I could see them very well, as part of their sides were open, and I could see that many of their planks were rotten.

Did you examine those ships sufficiently to ascertain whether the dry rot had or had not existed on board of them?—No, I did not.

What is your opinion as to the relative value of the Quebec oak and the Dantzic oak; which is the best timber of the two?—There is very little timber comes from Dantzic. If the Quebec white oak was properly seasoned, it is preferable to the Dantzic, and very little inferior to the English.

Have you ever broken up a ship yourself built of Quebec oak or American pine?—No, never.

At what relative difference of price would you prefer to purchase English oak to American white oak?—We cannot draw a comparison, because Quebec oak comes all of one description; but English plank will vary from 7l. per load to 20l. per load.

Suppose the English oak to be the same scantling as the American oak?—If it was to repair a ship, I should prefer the Quebec timber; but I can hardly answer that question, because the American timber chiefly comes in logs, out of which I can cut a six inch plank, and which does not cost me more than 7l. per load; but if I had to purchase an English plank of six or seven inches thick, I should have to pay 17 to 20l. per load; to get a six-inch plank out of an English tree, the tree must at least have 60 or 70 feet, and there being a considerable scarcity of that size wood, the timber merchants ask a great deal of money for it; but if I were to buy a Quebec log, I should not have to pay more than 6l. or 7l.

Is not Quebec oak used as well as English oak for repairing ships?—Yes; I never knew a ship built of Quebec timber in this country.

For such other purposes as your last answer does not apply, to what difference in price do you think you should prefer to buy English oak at, to Quebec white oak?—I should, as far as it depended on myself, give 20 per cent. more for white English oak.

Can you answer the same question, with respect to the relative value between American white oak and the red; what degree of inferiority of price, between red oak and white oak would induce you to use it?—When we buy a lot of English oak in this market, we cannot tell whether it is red or white; it is all mixed together; we cannot go in the market and select it; if we buy a lot, we cannot afford to throw away the red; we put it in with the white.

Would any merchant employing you to repair a ship, allow you to put in red oak in place of white?—No, if he knew it, certainly not.

In point of fact, if you were to enter into a contract to build a good and sufficient ship for those purposes of trade, where they are employed, such as the Jamaica trade or East-India trade, would you introduce into that ship any of the white oak?—No, not of myself, without permission of the owner.

Why not?—Because, certainly, those who build ships in this country, are generally confined to build them with English oak.

Have you any other reason?—No.

In point of fact, is not the Canada oak more liable to perish than English oak?—Yes.

Is not that circumstance a sufficient reason, of itself, for not introducing it into

---
## Page 38

the building of ships?—There is some English oak worse, in my opinion, than the Quebec white oak, and very little better than Quebec red oak.

Presuming that you have had occasion to purchase oak plank, would you purchase Canada plank in preference to Dantzig?—I should prefer the white oak of Quebec; I should rather give the preference to it than the Dantzig.

As you have spoken of the durability of ships built of North American pine, can you speak of the durability of ships built of the pine of the Baltic?—No, I cannot.

Is the timber of the Baltic or North America, equally valuable for repairing ships with English oak?—It is preferable for repairing ships.

In giving that answer, do you not take into consideration that it is not necessary to last so long as timber which would be used for the building of ships?—Certainly that is one reason why we repair a ship with Canada or Baltic oak, it makes the ship all of apiece with that which is left.

Are there not a great many parts of a ship, as the masts, and other parts, for which American timber is universally used?—They use Riga masts, and sometimes American masts; you are obliged to use that which is in the market.

Is it not used for all the ships of war?—You cannot get such large masts from Riga as from Canada.

The Committee understand that, to apply to ships of war?—To ships of war and to Indiamen.

Does it likewise apply to the higher description of merchantmen employed in other trades?—A merchant always prefers the Quebec red masts, which I look upon to be the best that can be brought into a ship.

Is the yellow pine applied to those purposes?—Sometimes it is.

What do you take to be the amount of the quantity of fir imported from America, with a view to those purposes?—That is a question I cannot answer.

Is it a very large quantity?—It is a very large quantity, no doubt.

A large quantity is necessary for those purposes, and for which the American timber is preferred?—Yes.

Do you think, if there were a considerable alteration of duty, that for those purposes American fir would be still brought to this country?—I should think that for those purposes American deal would be still brought to this country; the difference of freight would prevent the importation of timber from America.

Under what possible alteration of duty do you think that American timber would still come and be saleable in the market of this country?—I think American timber would bear from five shillings to seven and six pence, perhaps ten shillings per load upon it.

Suppose instead of laying on a duty of five shillings or seven and sixpence per load, that amount should be taken off of northern timber, what effect do you think that would produce?—I think the Canada trade would be then reduced to a very great extent, and the Baltic trade would be increased; the price would get up, for the demand for it would be so great from the ports of the Baltic, that they would raise the price immediately.

Why do you think a greater effect would be produced by taking off the duty from the northern timber, than there would be by laying on the same amount of duty upon Canadian timber?—It would be the same to look at it; if a duty were laid upon the timber, there would not be so many importers of it as there are at present, there now being persons who glut the market; they would then be more select; but when it comes duty free, they send all sorts of timber, and the bad establishes the price for the good.

Then do you not think that such an alteration of the duty as would produce the effect you have stated would be for the general advantage of the country?—I should think any thing that would employ the British shipping, of course would be advantageous to the country; if the duty were to be altered so as to prevent the importation of timber from Canada, you would throw about 6 or 700 sail of ships, and more than as many thousand men out of employ; and besides that which comes from America, the freightage forms the most valuable part, that is all spent in the outfit of the ship and the sailors wages, and very little of it goes into the owners pockets; that I can speak to, of my own knowledge. We get very little money; all our freight goes in paying the shipwright and mastmakers, and ropemakers and sailmakers, and all other trades, which are requisite to the outfit of a ship.

Have you any vessel in the Baltic trade?—None; the port charges and expenses are so heavy, that if an English ship owner can get a freight to America, he will not go there. I would observe, in addition to my former answer, that in fitting our ships out

---
## Page 39

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

out to America, we lay in our provisions in this country, such as salt beef and pork, and bread and flour; but the English ships which go to the Baltic, take no Irish provisions, or very little; they may have a single cask or so on board; the reason is, because they can get them cheaper in the Baltic.

Are you of opinion, that the imposition of the duty you have mentioned on the American timber, and which would tend to check the importation of it, would produce a great effect upon the shipping, and the persons employed?—Certainly not; though it might to a small degree, but not to any great extent, because no timber would be imported then, but of sound quality.

You have stated, that if a duty of five shillings to ten shillings per load were laid on the timber of America, it would exclude from the trade a portion of importers, whose conduct is injurious to it, and would lead to the importation of timber better selected and better prepared, being aware that the present freight of timber is 45s. from Canada, that the other charges are not less than five shillings, making together 50s.; are you of opinion, that an increase of those charges, of a sixth or an eighth, would be sufficient to exclude those persons from the trade, and lead to the consequences you have described?—I should think it would.

Then you are of opinion that this advantage would be obtained in this manner, by laying a duty on the American timber, and the Baltic timber remaining subject to the present duty?—Yes.

Is the Committee to understand that you are of opinion that a reduction of the duty upon Baltic timber of from five to ten shillings per load would not produce the same effect?—I should think not.

You have stated the present price of freight of American timber to be about forty-five shillings?—It is forty shillings from New Brunswick and some of the other ports, and forty-five shillings from Quebec.

Is that such a freight as at the present rate of seamen's wages will yield a fair remuneration to the owners?—It is at such a rate that the ship-owner would prefer going there, to the Baltic for twenty shillings; a ship-owner would more readily go to Quebec for forty-five shillings, than to the Baltic for twenty shillings.

Would they rather go to Quebec for forty shillings than to Memel for twenty shillings?—I should think they would; for a ship would make her voyage nearly in as little time as she would make two voyages to Memel, and the port expenses and other charges are so much greater, that I should think he would prefer it.

Do you think he would rather go for five and thirty shillings?—I should think he would in the summer season; he would not in the fall of the year.

Then it is your opinion, that the ships in the Canada trade are now earning such freights as are paying the owners fairly?—We prefer going there to going to the Baltic; it is as good as any employment we can get; but we could not afford to build ships for that trade; the ships employed in this trade are the second-class ships, which would be broken up, if we had not this trade.

Then do you, therefore, consider one of the advantages of this trade to be, that of being able to employ vessels which would not be competent to carry articles of weight, and which were perishable?—They would be broken up, if it were not for that employ.

Veneris, 16° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

Henry Usborne, Esquire; Called in; and Examined.

THE Committee understand you are a merchant?—Yes.

Engaged in what trade?—In the Canadian principally.

You have been also engaged in the Russian trade?—I have.

Are you still engaged in the Russian trades?—In a small degree.

Then you can speak to both the Canadian and Russian trades?—I can speak to both, but more particularly to the Canadian.

Can you state what proportion the exports of wood bear to the general value of the exports from Russia to this country?—No, I have not that information.

Is it a considerable proportion?—I should think so.

Can you state at all, whether any diminution of the duties now imposed upon timber,

---
## Page 40

timber, would be likely to produce any considerable effect upon the Russian trade in this country?—I should think so.

In what way?—I should think the less the duty, the greater the trade.

Do you think that any diminution of the duty would produce a larger export of Russian timber to this country?—Yes, I think it would.

Do you believe that the effect of diminishing the duty would be to raise the price of timber in Russia?—Yes.

Do you think it would be raised in a proportion equivalent to the duty taken off? I think it must depend upon how much duty is taken off; how much it would alter the price.

Suppose five or ten shillings per load of timber, do you think it would rise in the same proportion?—I think it would, or nearly so.

Then what reason have you to think, that if wood came at the same price as it does at present, the import of it into this country would be much increased?—There would be a greater inducement to the landed proprietors to get it out and export it.

What effect do you think an increased export of wood from Russia would produce upon the imports of the manufactures and produce of this country?—I think not much.

Are you of opinion that Russia takes as much now as she would be likely to take?—I think she does.

Upon the supposition of any alteration taking place in the mode of taking the duty, what effect do you think would be produced by taking the duty upon the cubic feet, instead of the manner in which it is taken at present?—It would much more benefit Norway than any other country, provided the rate of duty was the same.

Do you think it would tend to lessen the importation from Russia?—I think it would.

In any considerable degree?—Yes; Norway would have such a decided preference, it would lessen the export from all other countries.

Would not that be confined to deals in particular?—More generally so.

In what ships is the trade between this country and Russia principally carried on?—Both in British ships and foreign.

Do you know in what proportion?—I think in the greater proportion of foreign.

Do you speak from mere recollection, or have you looked to any accounts or returns to confirm your opinion?—No; as there are tables which I supposed were before the Committee, I did not refer to them.

You have resided, the Committee understand, in Canada?—Yes.

How long have you been engaged in the trade of that country?—Nineteen years.

Can you speak to the quality of the Canada timber as compared with that of the Baltic?—Yes.

State to the Committee what is your opinion upon that subject?—Do the Committee refer to oak or to pine?

Of pine, the growth of our colonies, as compared with the pine of Norway?—The red pine I conceive equally good for every purpose.

Is not the principal import in yellow pine?—Yes.

Compare that?—I think for internal purposes it is as good as the Baltic.

Have you found that the pine brought from America into this country is particularly liable to the dry rot?—No, I think not.

Not more than the pine brought from the Baltic?—No, I conceive not; in Canada many of the houses and churches which have been built of it, have I understand stood a hundred years.

The Committee is speaking of the wood brought to this country?—I conceive it not more liable.

Have you had any means of observing its use, either in shipping or in buildings?—I have built several ships in Canada, in which the yellow pine has been used; some of them are now in being; the trade has been so new in this country, that it is difficult to give an opinion as to its liability to the dry rot. I know of no instance where the dry rot has happened, where it would not have happened if wood of other description had been used.

To what description of persons is the yellow timber sold?—To all timber merchants.

Do you know for what purposes it is chiefly employed?—For all purposes, but more particularly for inferior purposes.

Do you know of its being employed in any of what are called the first rate houses?—I scarcely know anything of the conversion of it.

---
## Page 41

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Are not the houses in Canada generally built of that wood?—Yes, invariably of the wood of that province.

Is not ship building carried on to a great extent in Canada?—It has been, but now it is not.

Can you state at all, the tonnage annually built there in any average of years?—Twenty to thirty ships, from two hundred to five hundred tons per annum.

Is a great proportion of the timber trade carried on in those ships?—At first, during the war, they were too valuable when new; but latterly they have been employed in it, as there has been less employment for good ships in other trades; inferior ships are as good as the first class of ships for the timber trade.

Do you know any ships built in that country, that have lasted more than a few years?—Yes; I have a list here of 123, all of which have been built ten years and upwards; it is extracted from Lloyd's book of this year; it contains the number of ships built in Canada, and which are now in existence.

[The List was handed in.]

Have you ever had any opportunity of observing the Canadian timber in union with other timber, such as Baltic?—No, except it has been in ship-building.

Or of its communicating the dry rot?—No; I should say we have supplied government with great quantities; and when the demand in Canada has been large, and the prices high, some oak timber has been cut in the spring and immediately brought down and floated to Quebec, where it has been shipped wet from the rafts, and in this state brought to London; I understand it has been immediately used, and being so used, it has been subject to the dry rot; but I conceive that any other timber would be equally subject, were it treated in the same way.

You attribute it then entirely to the period at which the wood was cut, and not to any peculiar liability to the dry rot in the wood itself?—No, I attribute it to want of proper seasoning.

What is the ordinary employment of the peasantry in Canada?—In winter many are employed in cutting and preparing timber, and in the spring, rafting it and bringing it down to the port of Quebec, and in summer, in farming.

Do you mean by that, that generally speaking, the wood that is cut in Canada, and rafted, is cut and rafted by the people who are employed in farming during the summer season?—Yes.

Do you mean to state by that, that there are no persons in Canada, whose time is exclusively devoted to timber-cutting and rafting of it?—Very few.

Are the persons chiefly employed, natives, or Canadian settlers?—I think more Canadians than others; there are many Scotch settlers or emigrants who are employed, and many of the Americans.

Does the pine timber chiefly come from the Upper or Lower Canada?—The oak timber principally from Upper Canada, and the pine timber, about equally from both.
