Do you not know, that the timber men come from the United States for the purpose of cutting timber, and rafting it within the British colonies?—The Americans are more expert axemen, in consequence of which they are sometimes brought in for the purpose of cutting our timber and rafting it.

Is it not the common and general practice?—No, it is not a very common practice; it is done some times. I should add, timber is procured in many different ways; frequently many farmers procure a small quantity, which he exchanges for rum and clothes and provisions, and all the necessaries that he requires, with the merchant, who, in the country, retails out the different things which are imported into Canada.

Is not a certain proportion of the timber shipped at Quebec, the produce of the United States?—Yes.

What proportion will it bear to the whole quantity exported from that country?—I should think one-third.

What, in your opinion, would be the effect upon the British interests, either in Canada, or in this country, if the importation into the United Kingdom were confined to the timber the actual growth of our own provinces?—I think it would be very advantageous to our own colonies.

Are you aware of any means by which that could be effected?—It might be effected, I think, by having a certain number of custom-houses, and levying a duty when it came in.

At what stations would you suggest the placing of those custom-houses?—At St. John's I would station one.

---
## Page 42

Is there any restriction by any colonial act on the timber trade, between the two countries?—No, I believe not.

No duty exists upon the importation of timber into Canada from the United States?—I believe none.

In what other situation would you suggest a custom-house to be established?—At or near St. Regis.

Do you think, that the growth of the timber should be ascertained at those custom-houses?—I should think that quite impossible.

Do you not think that Chateau-Quai, where every raft is obliged to take a pilot when it passed the rapid, would be a better situation for a custom-house than St. Regis?—No; for much timber is got out below St. Regis.

If the American timber should still continue to be imported into Canada, and not to be exported again under the exemption of duty which now exists, or any reduced duty at which it may hereafter be advisable to permit the importation of American timber into Great Britain, are you not of opinion that the American timber would be consumed principally in the province, and that more timber, the growth of the province, would be exported in consequence of it?—I think in the towns, more American timber would be used in consequence.

Then of course the quantity of Canadian timber that would have supplied that demand, would be sent to this country?—I think it would;

How long were you a resident in our North American colonies?—Seven years; principally in Quebec.

Does not a great proportion of the colonial timber come from New Brunswick?—Yes; a great proportion, especially of the inferior.

What is your opinion of the relative durability of colonial yellow pine, as compared with Baltic pine, for general purposes of building?—Where it is protected from the weather, I think it would last as long, or nearly so: I form this opinion from the buildings which I have seen in Canada.

Should you form the same opinion, from any experience you have had in this country?—I should think it impossible to form any correct opinion of that kind, because it has not been long imported into this country.

Is not the general opinion of builders, of both ships and houses, opposed to yours?—I think they are prejudiced against it; but which is perhaps the case in all new trades.

Are not the relative qualities of the two descriptions of timber best ascertained by the prices which the same dimensions of timber sell for in the market?—I think not; because a prejudice prevails against American timber; the Baltic timber is much better prepared, and therefore better to convert.

Is not what you call prejudice, the real opinion of purchasers?—I can answer, that I have lately repaired a house with it myself. I there used all Canadian timber; therefore I judge the prejudices are unfounded.

But if you should find all other purchasers disposed to give a higher price for the Baltic timber, would you not come to the conclusion, that the opinion of all other persons is in opposition to yours?—I cannot put my opinion in competition to every other person.

Then you conceive that a great deal of prejudice exists on this subject?—Yes, for its use, in many cases.

What is the present value in the market of the colonial pine?—I think from £2. to £1. 19s. depending upon the quality.

What is the present price of Russian yellow pine?—I am not prepared to say, having been absent from town some time.

What difference of price between the colonial yellow pine and the Russian yellow pine would, in your opinion, cause the preference to be given to the European pine in the markets of this country?—I think, for many inferior purposes, the one is as valuable as the other; but the trade is in that unnatural state now, no fair inference can be drawn.

Then are you of opinion, that if European pine was at 6l. while the colonial pine was at 5l. there still would be a considerable consumption of the colonial pine in this country?—Yes; particularly on the west side of this country.

In what proportion would the consumption exist, as compared with the present?—I think it would be nearly equal to the present.

If the American wood were at 4l. 10s. and the Quebec at 6l. that is to say, with the difference of 30s. between the two, are you of opinion that the quantity of

---
## Page 43

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Colonial timber consumed would be diminished?—I think the prejudice would be less; and, in time, the demand would increase, as the timber was better known.

You are therefore of opinion, that the colonial timber will bear a higher proportion of price in the market than it now bears, when we have greater experience of its quality?—I think Canada timber would; I think the New Brunswick much inferior to that of the Canada.

What has the price of yellow pine timber been at Quebec during the last year?—From 20s. to 30s. a load.

Do you think that 20s. a load is a sufficient price to remunerate the cutter and rafter for his labour in bringing the timber to market?—I think, to a certain extent, it is; if there should be an increased demand, it will be dearer.

You do not think however that 20s. would be a higher price than timber may generally be expected to bear at Quebec?—No; on the contrary, I think a lower price.

Are the difficulty and expense of getting good timber increasing both in Canada and in the Baltic?—I understand it is; the expense perhaps, more than the difficulty, as it is further to bring it.

Is there any considerable stock of timber left on hand this year at Quebec?—I understand not.

Do you know in what state the preparations were for cutting timber during this winter?—I think the trade has been nearly stopt since the suspense respecting the duties; I think if the Baltic duty were taking off, the colonial trade would entirely cease.

Then in any event, you do not think so much timber could be shipped this season as has been shipped during the two preceding seasons?—No.

Not in any event?—No.

According to the general habits of the trade, is nearly all the timber cut down in one winter exported in the ensuing season?—It is generally; but it sometimes depends on the state of the snow, which if it goes away, some of it cannot be got out.

Is there generally much timber left at the ports of export?—Generally there is.

A good deal left?—Yes.

Was there last year?—Not so much as in general.

Can you form any conjecture of the stock remaining over, as compared with the annual export?—I should think about one-eighth, but I speak this without knowing positively.

The Committee think you have stated as your opinion, that the cutting this last winter has been less than usual?—Much less.

You speak of the present winter?—Yes.

Has the cutting of the present winter been less than usual?—I understand that it has.

Is the winter far enough advanced so as to enable you to form a correct opinion upon that subject?—I form my opinion from the circumstance of not having sent any orders myself for the private trade; and I understand no houses in the Canada trade have given any orders for wood during the suspense of the duties.

Could any orders now sent affect the quantity to be cut, so as to be imported in the present year?—I think only to a very small degree.

In your opinion what would be the effect of a moderate duty upon the importation of colonial timber?—I think it would be advantageous, because the quality of what would come would be better selected. I would beg to add, that in giving my opinion, I am presuming that no alteration will take place in the Baltic duty; if so, the trade with the British colonies would totally end.

Would not the laying a duty on the colonial timber, or the taking the duty off the Baltic timber of a similar amount, have the same effect upon the colonial trade?—I think not, because now great quantities of very inferior timber come over seeing it is subject to no duty; but should a duty take place, more care would be taken in selecting that which would be exported from our colonies.

Are you therefore of opinion, that adding a moderate duty upon the colonial timber would be less prejudicial to the interests of the Colonial trade, than abstracting the same amount of duty from the Baltic timber?—Yes, unquestionably.

What amount of duty in your opinion could be laid on Colonial timber without materially affecting its consumption in this country?—I think ten shillings might be laid per load, without much affecting the consumption, because I think the quality would be improved of that which came here.

---
## Page 44

What would be the effect of a fifteen-shilling duty?—Still more care in the selection of it.

As to consumption, how would that be affected?—I think the consumption at first in that case would be lessened, but from better timber coming, ultimately it would have no effect to lessen the consumption, provided the duties of the Baltic remained as they were; I take that as the basis of my opinion.

Would a twenty-shilling duty materially reduce the consumption?—I think it would.

To what extent do you think it would?—I think in time it still would make but little difference.

Would not the duty increase the price at which the timber could be sold in this country?—Yes, certainly, I conceive it would.

Would not its consumption depend upon the relative price at which the Baltic timber could be delivered in this country?—Yes, certainly.

But are you still of opinion, as expressed in one of your former answers, that with a relative difference of thirty shillings, as compared with European timber, the consumption of colonial timber would not be materially reduced?—I conceive the quality would be much affected by being better from the duty; but still I think the consumption would be nearly as much.

Would not, for a great many purposes, the colonial timber be consumed at prices nearly equal to that of Baltic timber?—I think for a few purposes.

Have not very considerable establishments been formed at Quebec, for sawing timber, during the last few years?—Very considerable.

You yourself have formed such an establishment?—Yes.

What amount of capital have you invested in establishments at Quebec, for sawing timber?—Our house has an establishment there, in which we have expended nearly forty thousand pounds; I have another establishment of my own, where I have expended ten thousand pounds.

Those two establishments have been formed principally with the view of sawing timber for exportation?—The larger one has; the smaller one has not.

Do you know that several other establishments have been formed at Quebec for the same purpose?—I know there are; near, or at Quebec.

At a very considerable expense?—Yes.

Have any steam-engines been sent from this country, for the purpose of driving machinery to saw wood?—Yes.

Is not Baltic timber at present at a higher price than American yellow pine, by nearly three pounds a load?—Yes, nearly three pounds.

Then when you say American timber would come into consumption, with reference to a relative difference of price of thirty shillings, you look forward to an improvement of quality?—Yes, I look forward to an improvement of the quality, as well as an improvement of the trade likewise, because at present the trade is a very bad one.

Does not the badness of the trade tend to depress the price of Baltic timber, as well as American timber?—Not so much I think; because in the new buildings in and about London, I understand the American timber is excluded, so that there is not the same demand.

In your judgment, would it not be most advisable to commence with imposing a small duty on American timber, and to increase it afterwards, as the improvement of its quality would enable it to bear such an increase?—I think any permanent plan would be better than the present suspense in which the trade is placed, and I am of opinion, that a smaller duty might be more easily borne at first.

Would not a system of gradually increasing duties, induce the importers to be constantly spoiling the market, for the purpose of coming in with a large supply every year, to escape the encreasing duties on the succeeding year?—No doubt of it; if you anticipate a larger duty, you would glut the market.

Do not the duties operate as much as a bounty to the people of the United States as they do to our own colonial subjects?—Yes, to a certain extent.

Not to the same proportion?—No; our own colonies receive an advantage which they do not, because shipped from and brought to our own ports, therefore, in some measure, the advantages are on the side of our own colonies.

Has not the American timber trade in our own provinces, been glutted by the imposition of those high duties?—Yes, in a great measure.

---
## Page 45

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Are you aware of any material advantage derived in a time of peace, from the permission afforded to the United States of sending their timber duty-free through our colonies?—No, I think it is a wrong measure.

Does that practice still continue to any extent?—Yes.

Is not confined entirely to the red pine?—To that and to the oak, but more particularly to deals, which are principally the yellow pine.

Sabbati, 17th die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

John Bainbridge, Esq.
Called in; and Examined.

Will you be so good as to state to the Committee, in what business you are engaged?—As a general merchant, particularly with the United States of America and the British colonies.

Are you acquainted with the trade between this country and Russia?—Generally; I have some general knowledge of the trade between this country and Russia.

Do you know at all what proportion the wood trade of Russia bears to its other exports?—No, I do not.

Do you happen to know the proportions that the exports of Russia in wood bear to the exports of wood from the other countries of the north of Europe?—Upon looking at the various papers that have been laid before the House of Lords, I find the average of importation of the article of timber, previous to 1807; from different ports of the north of Europe, was as follows:—from Norway about one-fifth; Sweden one hundredth part; Russia one-ninth; and from Prussia two-thirds; that was taking the average of nine years previous to 1807: In 1819, I find that Norway was reduced to about one-sixteenth; Sweden had increased to one forty-eighth; Russia was decreased to one thirty-second part, and Prussia one-fifth. The state of deals for the same period was as follows:—Norway enjoyed about two-thirds of the trade previous to 1807; Sweden one-ninth; Russia one-sixth, and Prussia one-fifth; in 1819 I find that Norway was reduced to one-third, Sweden had increased to one-sixth, Russia to one-third, and Prussia one-eighth.

Do you attribute this great change in the proportions enjoyed by the countries of the north in the supply of wood to this country, to the duties that have been imposed?—I consider that the relative proportions of the exports from the different ports of the north of Europe, have arisen from the variation in the market, occasioned by sometimes one portion of timber being required in this country, and sometimes another; no doubt the duty would operate so as to decrease the use of foreign timber, inasmuch as that from the British colonies came in competition with it.

To which should you ascribe the greatest effect; to the state of the market, or the imposition of the duties?—I should think to the importation from the colonies, which has decreased the quantity from the Baltic, and materially reduced the prices of Baltic timber in the market.

What effect do you think would be produced in the trade with the northern part of Europe, if a diminution of the duties were to take place?—I should think it would increase the importation from the north of Europe.

Do you think the same effect would be produced by an imposition of a duty upon American timber?—Yes, I think it would increase the importation from the north of Europe, if the foreign duty remained in the same state as it does now. I think a small duty upon the American timber would produce a corrective upon the state of the market, which, from the facility of bringing it from the colonies, has been glutted, the importations having been unusually large for the last three or four years.

Do you consider those importations have arisen from the facility of importation, or from any other cause?—I think the excessive importation has arisen from the total want of employment of the ships of this country, and from their not having been able to find any other means of employment, but to have recourse to the colonies to bring timber.

Then

---
## Page 46

Then you think that the ships have created the trade, and not the trade the ships?—I do think so certainly; a great part of the trade has been carried on by the owners of the ships, and not by mercantile men connected with the colonies. They were obliged to have recourse to the trade with the colonies to keep their ships and seamen in subsistence.

Do you happen to know to what purposes the American yellow pine is chiefly employed in this country?—I have no practical knowledge upon that subject. We are in the habit of selling it, and I believe it is used for all subordinate purposes; its uses are very general in London, for the purposes of packages, and all inferior works of every description.

What is the proportion of yellow pine, as compared with any other description of pine brought from America?—I should think the yellow pine constitutes nearly the whole of the quantity that is brought. There is a small quantity of red pine brought, but I do not think it amounts to one-tenth of the whole importation.

At what difference of price do you conceive that the American yellow pine would be preferred for those inferior purposes for which it is now used?—At present the market is in a very extraordinary state. We are obtaining only, for the best St. John's timber, and timber from Memelchini, 3l. load; this does not leave to the importer more than about 12s. for its first cost, the expenses of getting out the wood from America being about 15s. or 16s. per ton.

This is a state in which the trade could not continue, if no alteration was to take place?—No, it could not; the importations have been so immensely large, that it would be useless to bring timber here, when it was producing a loss; the increase during the last two years has been excessive.

What effect could be produced on the consumption of American timber by a given duty imposed upon it, say 10s.?—I think it would have one very beneficial advantage, inasmuch as it would correct the importation of a very large proportion of very ordinary timber brought from America, and produce a beneficial advantage ultimately.

Supposing the duty was extended to 15s. what do you think would be the effect of that?—I do not think that the trade of that country would bear 15s.

Do you mean by that, that the consumption in this country would be completely checked?—I think it would, because the quality of the yellow pine of America is not considered good. I do not know whether it is from prejudice, or from what cause, but it certainly will not bear a duty of more than 10s. as compared with the Baltic timber at the present existing duty.

What is the present difference between the prices of American and Baltic timber?—3l. 5s.; the present price of St. John's timber is 3l., and Memel timber 6l. 5s.

Then if the price of American timber was increased to 5l. 15s. do you consider that the consumption would be equal to what it is at present?—I do not think it would be used at all.

At what difference of price do you think the consumption of American timber will remain as at present in the market?—At a difference of 55s. between the American and Baltic timber.

Presuming that the timber cost twenty shillings a load in America, that the freight is 2l. 10s. and that the other charges are ten shillings, making 4l. and to that, ten shillings should be added as a duty, making the price of timber in this market, 4l. 10s. and presuming that no alteration takes place in the duties upon foreign timber, and that the price of Baltic remains at 6l. 5s. do you think at those proportions the consumption of American timber in this market would be equal to the present consumption?—I do not; I think if the price of American timber was 4l. 10s. and the Baltic at 6l. 5s. from the difference of quality (I am speaking of the yellow pine) the consumption of American timber would be considerably less.

To what extent do you think the consumption would be diminished?—It is not possible for me to say; the state of the trade is so extremely unnatural now, that we cannot at all judge of the precise effect that the change would have. I should think that the importations of the last two years must have exceeded the consumption by at least a hundred thousand loads; I should think there is that quantity in the market unsold, the timber of the growth of different countries. I would just mention, that in taking the criterion of timber, I observe it is generally spoken of as Canada; the proportion of timber that comes from Canada bears so small a proportion to the general trade, that I think is a matter of very considerable importance to be understood;—

---
## Page 47

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

understood;—the importation from Canada is only about one-fourth of the whole importation from the British provinces.

Do you include deals in that?—Yes, I include the whole; Nova Scotia and its dependencies, produce about one-ninth, and New Brunswick about six-ninths; it has been stated that much of the timber comes from the United States; I wish to explain, that what comes from the provinces of New Brunswick and Nova Scotia, is entirely cut within the British colonies, and has no American interest in it, in any manner or shape whatever.

Do you not think, that a difference of thirty shillings in the price would be sufficient to protect the consumption of the American timber for the inferior purposes for which it is generally applied?—I do not.

Has not a great fall taken place in the price of American timber within the last two years, but not a correspondent fall in the price of Baltic?—In 1817, the price of Memel timber was 6l. 10s.; in 1819 it was 6l., and has continued from 6l. to 6l. 5s.; the price of timber from the lower provinces in 1817, was 4l. 10s. in 1819, 3l. 10s. and in the present year it is 3l.

Then it appears, that a reduction in the price of American timber during the last three years has taken place, of thirty shillings a load, and of the Baltic not above ten shillings; is not that so?—Yes.

Has not this fact led to a great increase in the consumption of American timber?—I should think it has, very considerably.

Are there not certain purposes for which American timber is now used, that it would be still applied to, such as packing cases, picture frames, window frames, if the prices were equal, or nearly so?—I think it would be always used for those particular purposes, as it is generally understood to work easier, and is therefore always more desirable for persons in those branches of trade.

Have you formed an opinion of the proportion of the whole timber consumed in London, for those purposes?—I have no means of ascertaining that particular point.

Are you not aware, that the consumption of timber for those purposes is very considerable?—Yes, it is very considerable, particularly in all cases where articles are required to be exported, and packing cases of every description; and it is extremely desirable, because it is of such a description that those articles can be made with it at a low rate.

You have stated, that you estimate the stock of all kinds of timber at present in this country, to be about a hundred thousand loads?—I should think it is.

What do you consider to be the usual annual import of all kinds of timber?—Upon looking at the state of the trade for nine years previous to 1799, the whole import of timber was about 220,000 loads; from 1815 to 1817, the importation had increased upon the average of those three years to 243,800 loads, a trifle more or less; in 1819 it had increased to 385,700 loads; in the first period of average 207,700 came from the north of Europe; in the next period 1816 to 1819, it had decreased to 107,000 loads from the north of Europe; in the last year it was 104,865; so that during the last four or five years, the timber trade from the Baltic has remained stationary, notwithstanding the large imports from the British colonies.

Then in point of fact, it appears from your statement, that the present stock of timber in the kingdom is little more than three months ordinary consumption?—Yes, I do think so.

Are you of opinion that three months supply is an excessive stock?—No, I do not think it is a very large stock; it certainly is larger than what we have usually had at this season of the year, but I understand that there remains a very large quantity of timber in the colonies, which was not got home last year, owing to the very depressed state of the trade.

Was the quantity left in the colonies larger than usual?—Much larger.

Are you acquainted with the probable quantity of timber that will be cut in the colonies this season?—I have been informed that it will be very much less than that which had been cut the preceding year.

Then are you of opinion that the surplus left over last season, and the quantity cut in the winter, will form more than an ordinary supply?—Yes, I think it will.

In what proportion more?—Perhaps I am not precisely in a condition to answer that question, but I am informed that nearly a hundred and thirty thousand loads remain over of the last year's cutting, which is about one-half of the whole importation of the past year.

186. Are

---
## Page 48

Are the statements of quantities with which the Committee have been favoured, made from official documents, or from your own observation and information?—They are taken from the documents which have been presented to the House of Lords, and which are added to the Appendix of their Report.

Has your evidence, which has been given before this Committee, a particular reference to the London market, or has it been given with a reference to the general market of the kingdom, and to the general state of the trade?—With reference to the general market of the kingdom, and to the general state of the trade.

Are you acquainted with the relative value of the North American timber and the Baltic, in the Liverpool market?—Not so decisively as to be able to speak to it; I am more particularly acquainted with the London market.

You have stated in a former answer, that the expense of cutting timber or getting it out of the woods was eighteen shillings per load; has not the great demand for that timber caused an increase in the expense of getting it out of the woods during the last two years?—I have understood the loss to the colonies has been excessively great, and I presume it has arisen more particularly from the depressed state of the market, than from any larger expense incurred by getting it out of the wood.

Is the Committee to understand, that there has been no increase of the expense in getting the wood to what it was previously?—I have understood that the expenses of getting timber were about 18s. per load; I cannot answer the question precisely.

Are you acquainted with red pine timber, brought from below the lower ports?—Yes.

Is it equal to the red pine brought from Canada?—Yes.

To what port do you particularly refer?—To St. John's.

Are you acquainted with the red pine exported from the port of Miramichi?—No; our transactions are principally with the port of St. John's.

What were the current rates of freight from the lower ports of America, during the last season?—It varied from 40s. to 45s. all charges included.

What, from the higher ports?—I believe from 45s. to 55s.

What, from Norway or Memel?—I cannot tell, I am not particularly acquainted with the Baltic trade; I have only been in the habit of exporting there.

Do you consider a diminution of the difference of the duty between the Baltic and North American timber would be likely to produce an increased or decreased consumption of timber generally in the United Kingdom?—I think if there was a decrease of duty upon the Baltic timber, it would decrease the use of American timber. I should presume that the wants of the country would remain as before, and I should think the quantity of timber that would be consumed, would be the same.

What effect, do you think, would be produced by the diminution of the duties upon the Baltic timber, or the imposition of a duty upon the American timber, in the exports of the manufactures of the country?—I think it would make no material difference, for if you decreased the duty upon Baltic timber, so as to occasion any decrease of the importation of American timber, the exports would probably increase to the north of Europe, while they would decrease to the colonies.

Then you are of opinion, that a decrease in the amount of the duties upon the Baltic timber, would increase the exportation of our manufactures to that country?—A diminution in the duty on the wood, in all probability would increase the means whereby foreigners would be enabled to take away manufactures from this country.

Do you think it would produce that effect?—No, I do not; I think that the quantity of British manufactures that would go to the north of Europe would not be increased by any further importation from the north of Europe; I think the amount of British manufactures and articles which are usually taken by that country, would not be materially affected by any alteration of the duties on wood.

Do you think, with respect to Russia, Sweden, and Prussia, that the increased means of paying for our manufactures would not induce an increased consumption?—I believe the woollen manufactures in Prussia are in such a state, as to enable them to compete with us completely; I speak of it particularly, because we are in the habit of having transactions with the United States of America, and I find that a very considerable proportion of fine woollens, and of low woollens and stuffs, are absolutely shipping from the Netherlands, and from ports contiguous, part of which I understand to come from the interior of Germany, and from Saxony particularly, so that a portion of the trade which we have been in the habit of transacting with the United States, is finding its way from the north of Europe; I therefore conceive that

---
## Page 49

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

that their manufactures are competing very much with the manufactures of this country, and consequently they would not come to us to receive a supply of those articles which they can produce from their own manufactures at home.

Do you know whether the export to those countries has been increasing or diminishing within the last few years?—I cannot give a direct answer to that question; but contrasting the trade with the north of Europe upon an average of the last six years, it appears that the amount of exports to the British colonies, exceeded the amount of the exportation of British and Irish produce and manufactures to Denmark, Norway, Sweden and Prussia, altogether.

Do you in that estimate exclude Russia?—No.

Do you in that estimate also include those provinces belonging to this country in North America, from which we do not bring any considerable quantity of timber?—I have not included the province of Newfoundland. The trade of Russia in timber has not decreased at all; for upon an examination of the facts, I think that the trade in timber with Russia is pretty nearly as it has been for several years; therefore it occurs to me, as far as the trade of timber is concerned, Russia would not take a larger proportion of manufactures than she does at present.

Do you think this country in the case of a diminution of the duties, would take a greater quantity of the superior wood from the north which we now import?—Yes, I think it would.

Will you state, if you have the means of doing it, what is the comparative advantage derived to the different interests of this country concerned with the wood trade, to the colonies from that trade?—From looking to the amount of importations of 1819, I should state that the British shipping interests would take the amount of about £630,000, that the incidental charges would be £84,000, and that the amount remaining to the British colonies, would be only £168,000, taking the average price of timber at 63 shillings; I merely state that, for the purpose of showing the respective interests that will be affected by any alteration in the operation of the trade.

In the statement which you have just made, the British colonist does not appear to be the person most interested?—No; the person who will be the most affected in whatever way this question may be decided, is the person connected with the British shipping; the colonists certainly will very materially be affected by the question; they have no other resource but this country to derive their supplies from; if that branch of trade is destroyed, they will neither be enabled to take our manufactures, nor to pay us for those which they have already received, nor will our ships find employment (the number of vessels being so great that were left on the hands of the ship-owners subsequent to the war) but by this carrying trade of timber.

In the commencement of your evidence you have stated, that it was impossible to continue the present extent of British shipping in that trade, because it produced an extent of supply of American timber in this country, which rendered the sales very disadvantageous; when you make that statement, how do you reconcile it with the opinion, that this trade can be carried on to its present extent with advantage to the British owners?—I think it cannot be carried on to the same extent with advantage to the British owners; but I think if any sudden stop was put to the operation of the trade, it would affect, very materially, both the colonist and the British ship-owners; I state that, because I think, if a very heavy duty was imposed upon the British timber, it would prevent the importations taking place to this country; and they fear it in the colonies themselves, because they have, in all their letters to us on the subject of this trade, expressed a hope, that whatever was done they should be made acquainted with it, to have an opportunity of placing themselves in a situation so as not to be destroyed by the operation of the trade.

In the present year they have a large portion of timber remaining over the last year's cutting; and they certainly will be placed in a very unpleasant situation if any operation was to occur, that was to put an entire stop to that trade which they had been carrying on. I think if any duty was imposed, or any diminution of duty was to take place immediately, that it would also tend to prevent the usual supplies going out to the colonies; I speak practically, because certainly if any operation was to take place that was to prevent the colonists getting home the timber they have already lying by them, we should not ship the usual supplies we have been in the habit of doing.

Did you send out to the colonists the Report of the Lords Committee of the last sessions?—Yes; it was sent out, I believe, by the colonial agent.

From

---
## Page 50

MINUTES OF EVIDENCE BEFORE SELECT COMMITTEE

From what you have stated, then, it is quite impossible, whatever may be the future views of the British colonists, that they could expect to maintain a trade in timber to the extent that they have maintained it during the last two years?—Certainly, it would be injurious, inasmuch as a much larger quantity was imported than was really wanted for consumption.

If any regulations were to take place, by a diminution of British shipping or by other causes, which produced a more regulated importation of the British American timber into this country, would not the British American timber well squared and prepared for sale, sell at a nearer proportionate price to the Baltic timber than it now does?—If the timber from the British colonies were regulated by a duty, sufficient to prevent the importation of the worst species of timber which comes from the British colonies, I think it would be a corrective of the evil of which we have now to complain; for it has been by the introduction of timber of every description, in consequence of there being no duty at all, that I conceive the present importation has been so excessive upon the market; and I conceive, that a small duty will correct that evil.

If the importation, then, of American timber were confined to the better descriptions, as you now anticipate, at what proportionate difference of price do you conceive that such American timber would sell, with reference to Baltic timber?—I have before stated, I believe, that my opinion is, that there should be a difference of £1.15s. per load, between the relative value of the British American timber and the timber from the Baltic.

Would not that be with a view to give more of a monopoly in the market, than to place the timber from the two countries upon a competition?—I think a small duty would have one beneficial effect; because it would prevent the ships going out to the colonies without the intervention of a regular merchant; it would prevent them from taking that supply of ordinary timber which they now take from necessity; for when they go out for cargoes to particular ports, they are obliged to take a supply of whatever qualities may be there, and that has produced an unpleasant result in the market, and which exists upon the state of the trade at present.

In 1810 a protecting duty was imposed, in favour of American timber, by doubling the then existing duties upon Baltic timber, making the duty at that time £1.4s. a load; if the duties were now reduced to that rate from £2.s. what would be the effect upon the trade with America?—I think if the foreign duty was diminished in that proportion, we should have no trade in timber from the British colonies.

Luneæ, 19 die Februarii, 1821.

THOMAS WILSON, Esquire, in the Chair.

John Bainbridge, Esq.

Again called in; and further Examined.

SUPPOSING a duty of 10s. laid upon American timber, or 5s. laid on, and 5s. taken off Foreign timber, in your opinion, would that make any difference in the consumption in the market?—No, I do not think it would; if 5s. were taken off foreign timber, and 5s. was put on American timber, I think it would produce a corrective in the exportation of timber from the British Colonies, which, owing to its being brought here in a much worse state of manufacture than timber ought to be brought to this country, I think it would prove a corrective to its being brought to this country in an improper state.

Do you mean a duty of 5s. and a diminution of 5s.?—Yes; if that relative proportion was adhered to; I think a 5s. duty on the timber from the colonies would prevent the importation of very bad timber.

Then the Committee are to understand, that it is your opinion, that the same opinion would be produced by 5s. laid on and 5s. taken off, or 10s. laid on the American timber, and none taken off the foreign timber?—I think it would produce no effect upon the general consumption of timber in this country; I think the general consumption would be the same.

---
## Page 51

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Supposing 10s. laid on, or nothing laid on American timber, and 10s. taken off Baltic timber, in either case preserving the same difference of cost, would either result produce different effects?—I think the taking off 10s. from the Baltic timber would produce a different effect to the laying on 10s. on American timber.

State the difference?—The nearness of the Baltic to this market would give them a decided preference, if 10s. were taken off that timber, compared with the distance of voyage, and the expenses that we are put to in bringing the timber from America.

You will recollect, that the question goes to preserve the same distinction between the two, that is, a difference of 10s. a load, than what possible increased facility could be given to Baltic timber, where 10s. is taken off, when no duty is to be laid on North American, compared with the state in which the trade would be in, if 10s. were laid on American, and no duty laid on the Baltic.

I think if you took 10s. off Baltic timber, I should certainly say, as a matter of course, that the price in the London markets would be 10s. lower than it now is, and if so, that it would preclude the importation of American timber to a certain degree.

If a duty of ten shillings was laid on American timber, would not that advance the price of American timber in the same proportion, while the Baltic would undergo no-material alteration in the quantity imported?—I think not; I think it would decrease the importation of the timber from America; because a large proportion of the timber now brought to the country would be prevented coming.

Would not that tend still farther to advance the price?—I think it would advance the price of American timber certainly, but I think it would decrease the importation; I am of opinion that you cannot advance the price of the article here, unless you decrease the importation.

Do you not think that the duty would fall on the consumer?—No, I do not, indeed.

Are you acquainted with the various costs of timber in the ports of the Baltic?—No, I am not.

Can you state to the Committee what was the shipping price of timber in the ports of New Brunswick and Canada, during the last season?—It has varied excessively in the province of New Brunswick; during the last season there has been an unusual scarcity of money in the colonies; during the last season there has been an excessive depression in their trade from peculiar circumstances; in consequence of that depression, timber has been purchased at all prices, even as low, I believe, as ten shillings per load, from ten to twenty shillings per load.

Do you think twenty shillings a load a fair remunerating price to the shipper in the British Colonies?—Yes, I have been so informed.

You mean by that in the New Brunswick colony?—Yes.

Can you state what you consider would be a fair remunerating price for yellow pine timber shipped from Canada?—We have very little connection in Canada; I cannot therefore decisively answer that question; I believe twenty-five shillings per load.

You know that the expenses are much more in bringing timber out?—Yes.

Have you any establishment yourself in the lower provinces?—No; we are commission merchants entirely, both export merchants and importing from the colonies on commission.

Do you not think that commercially speaking, a duty of five shillings on the American timber, and a reduction of five shillings on the Baltic timber, would be more favourable to the trade than the imposition of a duty of ten shillings on American timber only?—I do decidedly; for I think that a duty of five shillings only levied upon the timber of the colonies, would be much more easily arranged, and prove more beneficial to the general interest of the colonial trade and the shipping.

William Smith, Esq.; Called in; and Examined.

WHERE do you reside?—In Liverpool.

What business are you engaged in?—As a merchant generally, and as ship-owner, but principally in the Nova Scotia and New Brunswick trade.

How long have you been engaged in the trade with New Brunswick and Nova Scotia?—About thirty years.

Were you resident any part of that period in Nova Scotia or in that country?—Twenty-two years.

Whilst resident in that country, were you particularly engaged and acquainted with...

---
## Page 52

with the timber trade of those colonies?—Very much during the latter part that I resided there.

Can you state to the Committee, what is the general character given to the timber of Nova Scotia and New Brunswick in the Lancashire market, compared with that of the other colonies, and also the Baltic?—The quality of New Brunswick timber is superior to Nova Scotia, but inferior to the Baltic.

Are you acquainted with the fact, whether red pine timber is shipped from New Brunswick as well as yellow?—There is but a very small proportion of red brought from that country.

Is the red pine of New Brunswick superior to the yellow?—Yes; it is of a superior quality.

Are you acquainted with the purposes for which the yellow pine of the British North American colonies is in general use in Lancashire; if so, will you have the goodness to state them?—I understand it is used for a variety of purposes, particularly for packing-cases; in Manchester, a great quantity of it is used for that purpose; and it is used very much in the inside of buildings.

Are you aware that it is used for other purposes?—It is used for finishing the cabins of ships, and for purposes of that kind.

Is it used for any part of the work connected with warehouses or cotton factories?—I believe for all parts, except for the beams of those in which great strength is required.

Then is the Committee to understand from your answer, that the timber of the North American colonies, with the exception of beams, and for other purposes where great strength is required, is in general use in the county of Lancaster?—I understand so.

Have any instances come within your knowledge of the timber of North America when so used in buildings, perishing prematurely from dry rot or other causes?—No, never.

Have you had occasion to hear of such instances from others?—I have heard of the dry rot appearing in warehouses and other buildings in Lancashire, but when examined it proved to be the timber of Dantzig and not of America.

You are then impressed with the opinion that the timber of the Baltic is equally liable to the dry rot with the timber of America?—So I am informed.

During your residence in the North American colonies, had you occasion to know any instances of the durability of the yellow pine timber of those colonies, when used for the erection of buildings there?—I know there are many houses standing in Halifax, that were erected several years before I went there, which is upwards of thirty-six years ago.

Were those houses built entirely of timber?—Altogether of timber.

Have they undergone any considerable or material repairs?—The shingles and clap-boards which are used, which are outside, require to be renewed once in twenty or twenty-five years.

Is not the lower frame or foundation of those houses so constructed of timber, also of the same material?—The whole frame is of timber, but there is brick or stone for the foundation.

To what height is that raised?—About four feet.

Then has that part of the frame which is immediately laid on the stone or brick wall, been found to decay when the other part has not?—No; I have never known an instance of its decay.

How long is it since you were in America?—I left in 1807.

Have you any buildings of that description in Halifax, which are your own property?—Yes.

Are those built of timber?—Yes; but they were erected after I went to the country.

How long is it since they were erected?—About thirty or thirty-two years.

Is it your belief that those buildings are in good and substantial state?—I believe they are.

Will you state to the Committee, what is the present value of the yellow pine of New Brunswick in the Liverpool market?—About seventy shillings a load.

Can you inform the Committee what is the present price of the Memel or Dantzig timber in the Liverpool market?—6l. 5s. per load.

Are you acquainted with the proportion, that the measure of Liverpool and the measure of London, with respect to timber; bear to each other?—The measure in Liverpool

---
## Page 53

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Liverpool contains a greater quantity, by 10 per cent, than the measure in the London market.

Are both kinds of timber equally in demand at those places?—No; American timber is in much greater demand than the Baltic, in the Liverpool market.

At what reduction in the price of Baltic timber do you think it would be equal in demand with that which comes from America?—I should suppose about 1 l. 5 s. per load reduction.

Is the Committee to understand, that, in your opinion, if an advance in the price of American timber, of 1 l. 5 s. per load, or a reduction in the price of the Baltic of 1 l. 5 s. per load were to take place, both descriptions of timber would then be equal in demand?—It is impossible to answer that question; I think it would increase the consumption of the one, and decrease the consumption of the other. If a duty equal to that were laid upon the one, or that much taken from the other, it would be very injurious, in my opinion, to the shipping interests of this country.

Are you of opinion, that if a duty of 10 s. per load was laid upon American timber, or 10 s. per load taken off Baltic, or 5 s. laid on the one, and 5 s. taken off the other, the demand for American timber would be materially affected by such an arrangement?—I do not think it would be much altered in either case.

Are you of opinion, that were a scale made of 15 s. in the place of 10 s. and applied in either of the ways suggested by the last question, that a material deficiency would be produced in the demand of American timber?—It would no doubt lessen the demand for American timber, and increase that for Baltic in some degree; but I do not think it would have a material effect.

Would 20 s. have a material effect?—I think it would.

You think that the point at which it would begin to have a material effect would be somewhere between 15 s. and 20 s.?—Yes.

At 25 s. of course the effect would be still greater?—No doubt it would.

Would 25 s. have the effect of driving the American timber entirely out of consumption in your neighbourhood?—No.

Would 30 s.?—It is used for a great variety of purposes, being soft and easily wrought, and what difference of price would occasion a decrease of consumption, it is impossible for me to tell.

Can you state to the Committee, at what difference of price, between the Baltic and the colonial timber, the colonial timber would be still used to a considerable extent?—I think 25 s. a load.

Do you think that at a difference of twenty-five shillings it would still be used?—Yes.

At that price do you think that both sorts would continue to be used?—Yes; but the quantity of American timber consumed would be very much lessened.

But it still would be consumed to a very considerable extent?—Yes; I think it would.

Can you inform the Committee what proportion the relative consumption of North American and Baltic timber bear to each other, in the Liverpool market at present?—No; I know the quantity of American timber consumed, but not the quantity of Baltic.

Can you form a pretty accurate opinion upon the subject, without detailing the facts?—No, I have no idea.

Can you inform the Committee whether the importation of timber into the port of Liverpool from the North American colonies, has not gradually and materially increased within the last year?—It has very much increased.

Can you inform the Committee what quantity of timber has been imported into the port of Liverpool from the North American colonies, the last and preceding year?—The quantity last year, was 78,000 tons; I cannot speak to what was the quantity in 1819.

From your impression, was the quantity imported into the Liverpool market in 1819, equal to the last year?—I think from the number of the ships employed, the quantity was about the same.

Are you aware of the present stock of North American timber in the Liverpool market?—It is about 42,000 tons.

About six months ordinary import?—Yes.

Can you state to the Committee, what has been the consumption or the quantity

---
## Page 54

sold in the port of Liverpool within the last year?—The quantity sold in the last year was 81,000 tons.

Are you acquainted with the quantity sold within the preceding year?—No, I am not.

Are you not aware whether it was less or more?—I should suppose rather less; about 23,000 tons less.

Are you acquainted with what was the current shipping price of yellow pine timber in the colonies of Nova Scotia and New Brunswick during the last season?—Seventeen shillings and sixpence per load was about the average price below.

Do you think that price sufficient to remunerate the shippers for the costs and charges, and to leave them a reasonable profit?—The shippers complain of that price, as being lower than they can afford to do it at.

Will you state to the Committee, what in your opinion would be a fair remunerating price to the shippers from those colonies?—I should suppose twenty shillings a load.

From New Brunswick?—Yes.

Has the price ever been at twenty shillings?—It has been as high as thirty shillings a load.

Does the price of cutting timber, and bringing it out to the ports, fluctuate?—No, I should think not, excepting as between a state of war and a state of peace.

Is there any oak timber shipped from New Brunswick and Nova Scotia?—No; or if any, it is very little.

What proportionate price does the red pine bear in the Liverpool market with the yellow pine?—There is so little of the red pine which comes to market, that I almost forget what the price is; from sixpence to ninepence a foot.

Is the cost of the red pine higher than the yellow, or lower?—It is about twelve shillings a load higher?

Is that additional cost in consequence of an additional expense in cutting and bringing it to the shipping ports, or in the first cost of the timber?—In consequence of the superior quality of the timber, that I consider is the reason why they put that value upon it, and the scarcity of it.

Can you inform the Committee what were the current rates of freights from those colonies to Liverpool during the last year?—From fifty-two to fifty-five shillings a load.

Liverpool measurement?—Yes.

Have any ships been engaged for the present year?—Not that I have heard of.

In what manner is the timber so imported paid for to the colonies?—In British manufactures and provisions, Irish provisions, salt, articles used for the fisheries, and for the supply of the people employed in cutting timber.

If the importation of timber from those colonies were materially diminished, would not the colonies be deprived to that extent of those supplies on which they depend?—They certainly would; because they have no other means of making a direct remittance to this country, but by sending timber.

Are the persons that are employed in cutting the timber in those colonies, residents in the colony, or do any part of them come from adjoining districts?—Some of them come from the United States.

Do they return after the timber is felled to the United States, or remain in the colonies?—They consider the United States as their home, and if they could not get employment in the country, they would return.

Has the emigration from this country to those colonies, been considerable?—Yes, it has; but the proportion has been small compared with Canada; I am speaking of late years.

Are the persons who so emigrate, employed in cutting or preparing or shipping timber in those colonies?—The people from this country are not so expert in cutting down timber as the Americans; they are chiefly employed in hauling it out, and squaring it.

Have you had occasion to know anything of the durability of ships built of the pine of New Brunswick?—Yes, I have had a good deal of experience.

Can you give any information to the Committee on that subject?—We have some ships now, which were built in that country, one of them at present is at Liverpool; the Rosina; built in 1802.

Was she built of the yellow pine?—There is a great deal of yellow pine in her; her top sides are yellow pine; there is a little oak in her, which I believe was procured

---
## Page 55

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

cured in that country; but her lower timbers, I mean those which are under water, are birch.

During the period that she has been built, have you had occasion to repair her?—We were under the necessity of repairing her, in consequence of getting ashore once at Liverpool, but the upper works have not been touched. I had a survey upon this ship the other day by a shipmaster and a ship carpenter, and the report is, that the pine timber appears to be perfectly sound, and no appearance of dry rot. The report is as follows:—"We, the undersigned, have this day gone on board of the ship Rosina, built at St. John's, New Brunswick, in 1802, and have carefully examined the pine timber in the vessel; when we found the same to be perfectly sound and fresh. We particularly examined her top timbers and bowlock timbers, also her topside planks, which are all of pine, and there is not the smallest appearance of decay or dry rot. Liverpool, 14th Feb. 1821. William Gaskell, shipwright. William Danson, shipmaster."

For what parts of that ship was pine timber used?—For the upper works, and I believe the beams are of pine, and the fastenings forward; the heads, I suppose, were of pine too, but I am not certain. We never make use of hard wood above the water, because it soon decays when exposed to wet and dry.

Is it within your knowledge, that the timber and plank described in that survey is the original plank of which the ship was built?—I do not recollect that that ship had ever any repairs done to her upper works.

Has she belonged to your house since she was built?—Yes.

Have you any other instances of the durability of ships built at Nova Scotia or New Brunswick?—Yes; we have a ship called the Comet, now returning from India; she was built in 1811, at St. John's; this is her third voyage from India; before she went out we had occasion to open a small part for light, and we found the timbers perfectly sound.

Was she built of the yellow pine also?—Yes, her top sides and timbers were of hackmatack.

Then is the Committee to understand that the timbers and upper works of the ship, as well as the plank, are of pine?—The plank in her are of pine, and the top timbers are built of hackmatack.

Explain what is meant by hackmatack?—It is a species of larch. The next ship is the Caledonian; she was built in 1815, and is now on her fourth voyage to India; she was quite sound when last examined.

Was she built likewise at New Brunswick?—Yes.

Do you know of any ships built in England of the colonial pine?—No, I do not.

Is it much used in Liverpool for the purpose of ship-building?—I should think not.

Have you heard any opinion expressed by ship builders in this country, as to its quality for ship building?—No, I have not; it is so little used; it is not used in the building of ships in this country, except for inside works.

Can you account for the difference of opinion which exists in the colonies and in England, as to its durability?—I should suppose it has arisen in consequence of many ships having been built in British America, for sale, and very little pains taken in preparing the timber; that I believe has occasioned many of them to go to decay very early, and has occasioned people in this country to have a bad opinion of them. Those ships of which I have been speaking, were built for our own use; they were built under proper inspection, and a great deal of pains taken in order to have the wood well prepared and seasoned.

Do the merchants of Liverpool frequently build ships of that timber in the colonies?—There are a good many that belong to the port of Liverpool built in that country.

Are they built considerably cheaper there than they can be built in England?—Very little.

Have they not the advantage of a freight home?—Yes; and during the war that was a very great advantage.

Do you know what proportion, generally speaking, of red pine and yellow pine is shipped from the North American colonies to this kingdom?—I should think the proportion of red pine not more than one-tenth.

The Committee understand you to say, that the American yellow pine is not more likely to suffer from the dry rot than the Baltic timber?—I never heard of the American pine having the dry rot.

In

---
## Page 56

In what respect do you apprehend that American yellow pine is inferior to the Baltic?—It is not so strong; it would not bear so much pressure as the Baltic timber.

Do you think that is the whole reason for the difference of the price?—Yes, I should think so; the Baltic timber has a great deal of turpentine in it.

Would you not also consider in some measure, that difference in the price to arise from the very large supply of the one, and the scanty supply of the other?—There has been a very good supply of the Baltic, but there has been an over supply of the American lately.

From what port in New Brunswick does the timber principally come?—From St. John's, St. Andrew's, Miramichi, and several ports of inferior note.

Is Miramichi in New Brunswick?—Yes.

Does much come from St. Andrew's?—Yes, a good deal, a considerable proportion.

Are there not large importations of timber from the United States, into those parts?—No; from St. Andrew's. I believe some small quantities are brought from the American side, but it is a very little.

From what part of the country do you suppose the timber sent to St. Andrew's to come?—From New Brunswick side of the river principally.

Have not those colonies a very considerable trade in timber and different sorts of wood to our West India islands?—Yes, they have, of small sized timber; which they call scantlings and boards.

Have they not a monopoly of those markets, in consequence of the Americans being excluded from them?—I believe they have at present.

Do you know whether there was any considerable stock of wood lying on hand in the New Brunswick provinces, after the shipping season of last year?—Yes, there is a large quantity; a very considerable stock both at St. John's and Miramichi.

What proportion do you suppose that stock might bear to the quantity shipped last year?—I should suppose about one-half of the quantity shipped last year.

Have not the preparations for cutting timber for this season, been greatly suspended, in consequence of the uncertainty prevailing with respect to the system of duties?—They have considerably; I understand there would not be so many people employed in cutting this last winter, as in the previous season.

Do you not consider it much for the interest of your trade, that a permanent system should be fixed as early as possible?—Yes, I should think so; for people are all holding back now, from making any preparation, not knowing what measures are to be adopted with respect to those duties.

Thomas Tooke, Esquire; Called in; and Examined.

THE Committee understand you are in the Russia trade?—Yes.

Are you very generally connected with the timber trade?—Not much.

You are aware that the trade in timber with the Baltic and with Russia, has fallen off materially of late years?—I am aware that it has declined of late years.

Do you attribute that principally to the high duties on the Baltic timber, or to the facility with which the American timber is introduced into this country duty free?—Both causes operate to produce that effect.

Should it be in the contemplation of Government to put a duty on American timber, say of 10s. a load, should you consider the effect produced by that, or a duty of 5s. on American timber, and 5s. reduction Baltic timber, to produce the same effect?—I should say the reduction of so trifling an amount would afford scarcely any perceptible benefit to the one, or any material influence upon the other; for practical purposes, I think it is too small a difference.

But you should suppose more difference would be produced by levying a duty of 5s. on the one, and reducing the duty 5s. on the other?—The timber trade generally, I conceive, would be benefited by a reduction; but in no case can it be benefited by a mere additional duty upon the American timber, without any reduction upon the Baltic timber.

Should you conceive, that so small a reduction as 5s. on the Baltic timber would give that advantage to the importer of the timber, or that it would be likely to raise the price in the ports from which it would be shipped?—I think a reduction of 5s. would be too trifling to produce any perceptible effect on an article of such very large consumption and importation generally.

---
## Page 57

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Are you sufficiently acquainted with the relative value of the timber of the growth of the two countries, to state what duty on American timber, or what reduction of the duty on the Baltic timber, you should think likely to produce a beneficial effect on the timber trade, without driving the American timber out of the market?—I am not sufficiently acquainted with the difference of quality between the two, to be able to pronounce any distinct opinion on that point.

What, in your opinion, ought to be the alteration of duty, either by a reduction of duty on Baltic timber, or by imposition of duty on British colonial timber, or by combination of both, so as to place the timber trade on the fairest footing, in order that each article should be applied to the purposes for which it is best adapted?—I should conceive that a reduction of the duty upon Baltic timber, or a reduction of the duty on the Baltic combined with an imposition of duty upon American timber, so as to reduce the present protection in favour of the latter by about 30s. a load, would be a material benefit to the Baltic trade, without, as I conceive, removing the competition of the timber from our own colonies.

Can you inform the Committee what are the data on which you rest that opinion?—Although not practically acquainted with the precise difference of the quality of the timber, so as to speak of it technically, or with any degree of positiveness, I have the impression that it is of an inferior quality, which, upon the principle of an ad valorem duty, would justify a smaller duty on the American than on Baltic Timber, supposing the voyages equal in distance.

Is the timber from Russia imported into this country, chiefly imported in deals or square timber, and in what proportion?—I cannot speak from present recollection; I believe the proportion of deals is larger than that of timber.

Is not the duty on Russian deals considerably less in proportion than the duty on square timber?—I am not competent to speak with accuracy on that point. I am perfectly aware that it is less, but to what degree I cannot say.

Is it about one-third?—Yes; I cannot say positively, not having had occasion to make the calculation.

Is not the timber trade at present carried on with very little profit?—Not only is it carried on without profit, but it is, I believe, carried on with a loss.

Is not that trade carried on principally, if not entirely, with British ships?—I believe the Russian timber trade is wholly carried on by British ships.

Has not the system of this government, of discouraging the importation of Baltic timber, produced very great commercial prejudices against us in all the countries in the Baltic?—I believe it has been productive of very injurious effects in the disposition of the Russian government, and probably, next to our exclusion of their linens and corn, more than any other.

Has it not been an inducement to the inhabitants of those countries to press upon their governments a countervailing system of exclusion of articles of the growth and manufacture of this country?—It has; and I believe, that the system in this country of prohibitory duties and exclusion, has been the ground upon which the sugar refiners at Petersburgh have very recently represented the impolicy of allowing our crushed sugars to be admitted upon the same footing as the Havannah and Brazil sugars; and memorials have been presented to the Russian government by important classes of its own subjects, proving the superior advantages of their transatlantic commerce, from the circumstance of so many of their productions being admitted either without duty, or comparatively at very moderate duties, into the ports of America and Havannah, which are wholly prohibited here, or only admitted upon the payment of high duties.

Are you not of opinion, that a country having such extensive and valuable manufactures as this, for which to find a market, should give as little countenance as possible to a restrictive system, where there is not some very great object to be gained by it?—I am of opinion, that every restriction, that is not warranted by the paramount necessities of the revenue, is disadvantageous, and may be proved practically to be so, in every instance in which it has operated.

Can you state to the Committee, any reason why the duties on the Norway timber should not be regulated, so as to give that timber a fair competition with that which comes from the Baltic?—I am not aware of any sufficient reason; I think the Norway trade is entitled to be admitted fairly into the competition.

Is it not true, that the Norway timber is principally imported in foreign ships, while the timber from Russia comes principally in British ships?—I believe that the Norway timber is chiefly imported in foreign ships; but if it should be, on that account excluded, or charged with a prohibitory duty, it seems to me that the

---
## Page 58

same principle would apply to excluding Swedish tar, which comes mostly in foreign bottoms, while the Russian tar comes wholly, or nearly so, in British ships.

Would it be easy, by laying a heavier duty upon the timber imported in the foreign ships, to secure, that the importation from Norway shall take place to a considerable extent, at least in British shipping?—That would certainly beone mode; but I believe, that the British shipping need not fear a competition of foreigners in the long run, and they require no protection beyond a fair countervailing duty.

Are you able to state to the Committee, what amount of discriminating duty would have the effect of transferring that trade to British shipping, or give a fair competition between the two?—I am not competent to give a distinct opinion upon that point.

Considering the fact to be as stated, that the greater part of the timber from the Baltic comes in foreign ships, and the greatest part from Russia in English ships, would you not think it advisable to encourage the latter trade, in favour of the former, by a discriminating duty?—I am rather disposed to question the policy of encouragement upon the grounds stated; because, although in the first instance, or on a superficial view, there might appear to be a disadvantage by the whole difference of freight, yet it should seem that as the freight to the foreigners must be paid by an exportation, direct or indirect, of some of our manufactures or productions, the advantage to the classes connected with that increased exportation would be at least equal to the disadvantages experienced by that class which would feel the diminution of employment of the shipping. And I am not sure that the general extension of our trade, arising from greater facilities, and the removal of the irritation and hostility which exist, and of which we set so much the example, would not eventually afford employment to as much British shipping, as would compensate for the proportion of increase of foreign shipping in the case alluded to.

The present price of American timber being £3 l. 5s. if 15s. duty were added to that, making the then price £4 l. and the present price of Baltic timber being £6 l. 5s. if 15s. was taken from that, leaving the price £5 l. 10s. in what proportion are you of opinion, if such alteration in the duties should take place, the consumption of each species of timber for the purposes for which it is most applicable would be affected?—I cannot speak to the effects upon the consumption, not being sufficiently acquainted with the details of the timber trade; but I should suppose that the present price of American timber is depressed below its fair proportion of quality, compared with that from the Baltic, in consequence of the great importation that has taken place from America, being chiefly brought forward by a speculation on the alteration that is expected in the arrangement of the duties; and the Baltic timber has come in rather smaller quantity from the very same speculation; therefore, when the duties are fixed upon a scale that shall remove some part of the existing bounty in favour of the American timber, the reduced importation of the one, and the increased importation of the other, will bring them to a much nearer proportion of price in the market, and I should think, would still admit of the American coming into fair competition.

Then your opinion of 30s. difference in the present rate of duty, whether laid on or taken off, is not founded upon a calculation on the present prices?—No, it is not founded on a calculation on the present prices; for, in my view of the probable effects, I include the expectation that the quality, and the imperfect squaring of the timber in America, which has hitherto been very much complained of, will be improved, in order to admit its bearing the increased charges.

Are you aware of the present price of freight of timber from Memel to this country?—From twenty shillings to twenty-one shillings the load, through the greater part of last year.

If upon the examination of a price current of timber during the years 1817 and 1818, during which time the shipping of this country had full employment in various ways, and the importation of American timber was reduced to a more fair level than at present, and you discover at that time, that the best sorts of American timber sold at a regular difference of from ten to fifteen shillings, when compared with the Baltic timber, or with the timber from Memel, would you infer, that was a difference in price arising from the comparative estimate of their different value?—If such were the facts, which I cannot state from recollection, it would afford a fair presumption that such was the relative quality.

---
## Page 59

Martis, 20° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

Thomas Tooke, Esquire;

Again called in; and further Examined.

THE Committee understanding that you are desirous of giving a further explanation in reference to one of the questions put to you yesterday, the Committee would be glad to repeat the question to you, which is; "Are you able to state to the Committee what amount of discriminating duty would have the effect of transferring the trade to British shipping, or give a fair competition between the two?"—Although I must repeat, that I am not competent to state what the quantum of that discriminating duty should be, it occurs to me that there is a rule of the utmost fairness, applicable to such a case, namely, to determine, from the competent authorities, the extra expense attaching to ship-building in this country, from the direct taxes on the materials, and to compute the amount on the tonnage of the vessel. An extra charge to this extent on the tonnage of the foreign vessels would be strictly a countervailing duty, and would have nothing of the invidious character which our restrictive and prohibitory system possesses, and which has given rise to endless imitations and counter-restraints. Subject to such a countervailing duty, I have no doubt that British shipping could maintain a successful competition with foreigners; and I am very much inclined to think that we should have no reason to apprehend the throwing our ports generally open to all importation in foreign ships, with this single precaution.

The Committee would be glad if you would state the grounds on which you form that opinion?—I ground my opinion on the evidence before the Lords Committee, confirmed by what has occurred within the range of my own observation. My house is the largest importer of the more valuable articles of Russia produce, and for the purpose of conveying it, I am in the habit of chartering ships to the extent of from 12 to 14,000 tons annually; in no instance, however, has it happened that any Russian ship has been offered to me since the year 1814 on terms so cheap as those of British ships. I likewise annually charter a British ship, in consequence of orders from a correspondent at Dunkirk, for the conveyance of a cargo of hemp from Petersburgh thither, as being cheaper on the whole than by a French vessel, although there is an extra duty of about 2s. 6d. per ton on the hemp, and higher port charges on the ship, in consequence of not being of French origin. In 1817 I chartered a British ship, to convey a cargo of tallow from Petersburgh to Rotterdam, as being cheaper than by a Dutch vessel; and last year, having it in contemplation to ship some wheat from Petersburgh to Holland, I inquired the terms upon which Dutch ships could be chartered in Holland for the purpose, and I found that they were as nearly as possible the same as by British ships; but this equality was merely in consequence of the port charges being lower on a Dutch ship. If the port charges were the same, the freight of the British ship would be proportionally lower. The shipment did not take place; but if it had, it would have been in British ships, as being the cheaper upon the whole, including the lower insurance, and the greater expedition, and other grounds of preference, which are very obvious, in favour of a British ship. I have no hesitation in adding, as the result of the information I have obtained, that if I had occasion to make any shipment of articles of Russia produce from Petersburgh to Dutch ports, I would give the preference to British, at the present relative rate of freights. My house has of late, as indeed in most years, had occasion to ship hemp and flax from hence to ports in France, Portugal and Spain, and cordage to the Brazils, and I have, with scarcely an exception, found the conveyance by British cheaper than by any foreigners, although the British are in some cases liable to heavier expenses in the ports of discharge, than vessels belonging to the foreign state.
