I am now shipping some hemp to New York, and could readily get a British ship to take it at a freight of 2l. 10s. a ton, but the American navigation laws, which are a counterpart of ours, prevent it, and I have been obliged to give 3l. 10s. a ton to a second-class American ship for it. Other houses, I understand, have paid as high as 4l. If, then, the shipping of this country is thus able to maintain the competition

---
## Page 60

competition to foreign ports, without protection in any instances, and with heavier expenses of port charges in most cases in the place of discharge, subject moreover to heavy direct taxes on the materials of ship-building, particularly on timber, there seems to be little reason to apprehend any great increase of employment of foreign shipping in importations to this country, where they are subject to heavier port charges, and the cargoes to extra duties. And it is still less to be apprehended, if, by a reduction in the duty on timber, this most important of all the materials of ship-building become cheaper.

Do not those observations principally apply to ships which are taken to carry cargoes of considerable value?—The articles to which I have alluded, namely, hemp and flax, may be considered to be valuable as compared with timber; but of very inferior value compared with cargoes of manufactured goods and colonial produce.

Are not an inferior description of ships capable of conveying the timber?—Yes.

Are not those ships likely to be found in Denmark, Norway and Sweden?—Not, I should apprehend, in any considerable numbers, to meet any extra demand for timber in consequence of additional encouragement to the importation from the European ports.

Then your observation does not apply to those ships as ships, but in consequence of there not being a sufficient number to meet the demand?—I rely upon the evidence before the Lords Committee to justify the opinion, that no new ships, to any extent, are likely to be constructed, at anything like the present freights, to meet an increased demand for the importation of timber.

If there was an increased demand for the importation of timber from the ports in the North, would not the price of freights rise?—By the supposition of a proportion of British shipping being thrown out of employment by the diminution of the importation from America, it is to be presumed that freights would not advance in any material degree; and I beg leave to observe, in reference to the question of the employment of foreign and British ships in the wood trade from the Baltic, that in 1819, the number of ships declared inwards to Memel were 268 British, 153 Prussian, 270 Dutch, Swedish, French, &c. together 691 ships, cleared inwards. Of these the whole of the British vessels departed with cargoes; but of the Prussians, being as above 153 in number, about 40 laid up for the winter, 80 went to Holland, remained 33; to these add the number which wintered in 1818-19, making 30 ships, leave 63 ships under the Prussian flag which went to Great Britain, or about one to four to the number of British.

Do you think, that while the British shipping is in the situation in which it is at present, that there could be any inducement to foreign states to build ships for the purpose of carrying on any trade that may be created?—I should think not.

Do you happen to know whether, within the last few years, in point of fact, ships have been built in those countries to any extent?—I cannot speak positively to the fact; but I infer from the information of parties concerned in those trades, that there has been a diminution in the quantity of shipping in those parts.

By the diminution in the quantity of shipping, have those ships, which have been worn out, been replaced by ships newly built?—Not fully replaced, as I understand.

You have suggested, as a compensation to British shipping, when in competition with the shipping in Norway, and other nations in the north of Europe, that a tonnage duty should be laid on the ships of those countries equal to the direct taxes in this country on the materials for ship-building; thus taking a part of the first cost of the British ship, as the rule to be applied to the rate to be levied for each voyage on the foreign ship; as for instance, supposing that the difference of cost, from those causes, of a British ship, amounted to £1 a ton, how would you propose to distribute this sum on the various voyages of the foreign ship, which she might or might not be engaged to make in competition with the British ships?—This is a calculation which ship-builders and ship-owners, and brokers may, I should imagine, be able to make; but which I am not prepared with at present. I am willing to admit that there may be complexity, and perhaps uncertainty in such a calculation, but it may afford some approximation to an equitable rate. At the same time there can be no doubt, but that a repeal of the duties altogether, upon the timber, &c. employed in the construction of ships, would be very much preferable; and in such case I should expect, that British shipping would be enabled to obtain very far the greater part of the carrying trade of the commercial world.

Supposing that the principle laid down by you was found practicable and adopted, ought it not to be extended to the additional cost of the stores necessary for the outfit of the British ship on each voyage respectively, whether of British or foreign production.

---
## Page 61

production, as well as the difference in the amount of the seamen's wages?—It is only in so far as the increased cost of outfit can be ascribed to direct taxation that it should be included in the calculation.

The Committee understand you to have stated, that British ships in the trade with Russia obtain a preference over those of Russia in the ports of that country, can you inform the Committee, whether or not British ships so employed, are productive of profit to their owners?—I am concerned as a part-owner in only one ship, which ship has been navigated at the current freights; and I can only say, that by the economy and activity of the managing owner, it so happens that she has made money each season, at the very lowest freights at which vessels have been chartered from this port. We have had a dividend each year, more or less.

When you say, each year you have received a dividend, more or less, can you state to the Committee the average extent of that dividend?—I cannot from memory.

In receiving those dividends, have you taken into consideration the natural depreciation in the value of the ship?—I cannot speak without a reference to the accounts.

Has not this particular ship, from the extensive and important connections of your establishment, obtained advantages in the outward as well as homeward freight, which do not fall to the lot of shipping in general, engaged in that trade?—Possibly she may, but not to any material extent, as the freight has always been at the lowest of the then competition.

Has she not, in such cases of competition, obtained a preference?—A preference at the lowest freights.

And whilst she thus obtained outward freight, were not many other ships going at the same time in ballast?—My house ships very little to Russia, and it has not been once in a dozen voyages that she has had any goods outwards, of any consequence, from my house.

Speaking generally, are there not more British ships that offer for freight in the Baltic, than employment can be found for them?—That is a question of freight; I should observe, that in 1818 I had very great difficulty indeed, in getting the requisite quantity of tonnage; and indeed, it rather fell short of my intended importation, from the inability to get it, without a more considerable advance in freight than the goods would have borne.

Did this circumstance occur at any particular season?—Through the whole season of 1818; there might be short intervals, in which the demand was not so great.

In point of fact, how has it been in 1819 and in 1820?—Neither in 1819, nor in 1820 were the freights of those ships that I chartered lower than they were in 1816, and the early part of 1817.

But in point of fact, has there not been a greater number of British ships seeking employ in 1819 and 1820 than could find it?—That is a question of freight; and the chartered freights were not lower last year than in 1816.

When you were desirous to charter one ship, could you not, with equal facility, have obtained two, had you desired it?—Yes; but not at lower freights than in 1816, and the beginning of 1817.

Is the fact not within your knowledge, that there has been, during the last two seasons, a considerable surplus of British shipping at Cronstadt seeking freight, beyond what could find immediate employment?—Yes; there was a surplus of seeking-ships as they are termed; but I have seldom, with the exception of 1818, known a season of late years, in which that has not been the case.

You have stated to the Committee, that of the Prussian ships employed from the port of Memel, of late years, only a small proportion of them have come to this country, but that the greater part have traded with Holland; do you know, whether the whole of the Prussian ships, belonging to or resorting to that port, were not employed?—I am not, from immediate experience or connexion in business, acquainted with any further detail, relative to shipping in that port; I cannot, therefore, answer that question.

You stated to the Committee, that on a late occasion you were obliged to pay 70s. a ton for freight in an American ship from hence to New York, whereas a British ship might have been engaged at 50s. supposing that the article had been one of British production, and therefore open to be carried in the shipping of either nation, would it not have been in that case taken at equally a low freight in the American as in the British ship?—I cannot say, having had no occasion to make inquiry.

Were there American ships in competition for the freight of hemp at the time you applied

---
## Page 62

applied-for the tonnage?—Of course the terms I have mentioned imply a scarcity of American shipping, in proportion to the demand, compared with British shipping.

Are you aware, that seven-eighths of the trade carried on from Liverpool with the United States of America, is carried on in American ships, and not above one-eighth in British?—I was not before aware of it to such an extent. I knew that the proportion was considerable.

Does it not, in point of fact, follow, where shipping of one country has no preference from additional duties or charges over the ships of other countries, that the American ships must be able to carry a freight on terms more favourable to the charterers to obtain such preference?—There may be peculiarities in the nature of the trade which may compensate to the shippers for the higher rate of freight, such as the proprietor of the cargoes being connected with the ownership of the vessels; at the same time I cannot speak with any precision upon the question of American shipping, having only an impression from general information, that they are not navigated at a lower expense than British.

Do you happen to know, that English ships, seeking for employment in America, are under peculiar difficulties as compared with American ships?—I am not sufficiently acquainted with the nature of the American trade in America, to answer that.

Do not the prejudices against our restrictive system, which you say have produced an unfavourable disposition against us in the minds of the Russian government, extend to other points besides the duty on their timber?—Yes; I believe that our prohibitory duty on their linens is considered in a more invidious light than even our high duties upon their timber.

Was not a great deal of dissatisfaction expressed at the Polish Diet last summer, on the restrictions put by the government of this country on the importation of Polish corn?—I believe that our prohibition of foreign corn, under the present bill, is very obnoxious to the powers of Russia and Prussia.

Would not, then, a reduction of the duty of timber be chiefly valuable, as a proof that we are no longer anxious to uphold our present system of restriction, amounting to a prohibition of the productions of other countries?—As far as relates to Russia that might be the case; at the same time I would add, that there are some of the minor ports of Russia in the Baltic and in the White Sea, where the exportation of deals forms nearly the whole of their trade, and therefore it is an object of more importance, probably, to the government than the mere pecuniary value of the article in question.

In your opinion, would a reduction in the duty on the article of timber alone, remove that unfavourable disposition which you say is entertained against us, unless followed by the removal of the restrictions upon the other articles?—It would contribute to such an effect chiefly as an earnest of a more liberal system.

What is the comparative export of linen, corn and wood, from the northern powers of the Baltic, in point of value, on an average of years?—I will endeavour to obtain it for the Committee.

From the general knowledge which you have of the trade of the country, and particularly of that branch of it, the carrying trade, have you any reason to suppose that the shipping interest has been as successful, generally speaking, as that ship to which you have particularly alluded, as belonging to yourself?—Perhaps not; but with severe economy and activity and prudence in the management, I should suppose that in a great proportion of instances they may have made a bare livelihood.

Do you know the selling prices which northern timber has borne in this country for the last twenty years?—I will endeavour to prepare a statement, to lay before the Committee.

What number of voyages do ships usually make between the principal exporting ports of timber in Russia and London, in the course of the season?—Between St. Petersburgh and London they make generally two voyages sometimes; but rarely three. From Riga they may, if there be any adequate object for it, make three voyages; but I do not believe, in point of fact, that they often make more than two. From Archangel and Onega, one voyage.

What proportion of the timber is carried on from Archangel and Onega, compared with St. Petersburgh and Riga?—I should think it is about one-fifth, compared with a total of the Russian ports in the Baltic.

How many voyages do the ships make, which are employed in the timber trade between Prussia and this country?—I cannot speak from personal knowledge respecting the trade with Prussia.

Can you speak to Norway and Sweden?—No, nor to Norway and Sweden. Have

---
## Page 63

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Have you made out a statement of the rate of freights outward, from the ports of America and the ports of the Baltic to London, during the last year; and also of those engagements, as far as made to your knowledge, in this present year?—I have.

[It was delivered in, and read.]

"In 1820:

The freights from Quebec to London, or the ports in the English Channel, were:—

47s. 6d. per load, for pine timber.
50s. to 52s. 6d. per load, for oak timber.
7l. 5s. to 7l. 10s. per standard hundred, for deals.
Staves, 12l. to 15l. per mille, for broken stowage, and two-thirds port charges, or 5 per cent.

From Miramichi, Picton St. John's, or St. Andrew's, to London, &c. 40s. to 42s. per load, for timber:
Broken stowage, in proportion thereto, and two-thirds port-charges; or 5 per cent.

The Baltic freights to London, were:—

Memel.—For timber, 20s. to 21s. per load; deals, 3l. to 3l. 5s. per standard hundred, and 5 per cent.
Dantzig timber, 22s.; deals, 3l. 10s. per standard hundred, and 10 per cent.
Riga timber, 24s. to 25s. per load; deals, 3l. 15s. to 3l. 10s. per standard hundred, and 5 per cent.
St. Petersburgh.—Deals, 4l. to 3l. 15s. per standard hundred. No timber imported; but the freight would have been 25s. to 26s. per load, and 5 per cent.

"In 1821.

Quebec.—A few ships have been taken up this year to load, chiefly deals, at 7l. 5s. down to 7l. per standard hundred, and 5 per cent.; more could be got on the same terms, or 47s. 6d. per load for timber, and might perhaps be prevailed upon to take 45s. but at a greater reduction it would not pay.
Miramichi, Picton St. John's, or St. Andrew's.—Ships would go at 40s. and 5 per cent. perhaps a few might take 37s. 6d. per load; but that is questionable.
Memel.—Ships have fixed at 20s. 6d. and 20s. per load, and 5 per cent; no expectation that they can go lower.
Dantzig.—The merchants offer 21s. and 7½ per cent. hardly adequate to the expenses of that port.
Riga, or St. Petersburgh.—Yet too early; but the freights cannot well be lower than last year, or at most a trifle.

The ship-owners find one Quebec freight to be made in about the same time as two voyages to Memel, and to pay better, as although the wear and tear may be greater in crossing the Atlantic, yet the difference of expense in only one loading and discharging, besides saving twice port charges, makes the American trade the most profitable. In short Baltic trips, the expenses of the port of London in tonnage dues, dock dues, &c. is severely felt, and deters many from sending their ships to Memel, &c."

19 February 1821.

Can you inform the Committee what was the current price of timber of good quality at Memel, during the last year?—Not precisely. I have, however, understood that it varied according to quality, from 22s. to 28s. and even to 30s.

Can you give the Committee information of what was the current price at Riga?—From 30s. to 40s. on board.

At Dantzig?—I cannot say.

In Norway?—Nor anywhere else.

Are you acquainted with the extent of the exports from Riga, Archangel, and St. Petersburgh?—I have a statement to that effect, which is as follows:
