## Page 66

John Caldwell, Esquire, Called in; and Examined.

WILL you be pleased to state in what capacity you appear before the Committee?—As a landholder in Canada.

Do you not hold some public situation there?—I am a member of the Legislative Council, and receiver general of the province.

Have you been long a resident in Canada?—Yes; I was born in Canada.

And you have generally resided there?—Yes, the greater portion of my life.

You are a proprietor to a considerable extent?—I have, I suppose, three or four hundred thousand acres. I have, besides others, one property opposite Quebec, six leagues by six.

Are you a large proprietor of timber?—I have a great deal upon my estate.

Do you consider the proprietors of land as deriving great advantage from the wood trade, speaking as a proprietor?—Generally, I do. I wish to state, more particularly, the kind of wood in which the proprietors are particularly interested. I conceive, that all the proprietors of land in Lower Canada, have particular interest in the question as respects the spruce timber, as there is scarcely a landholder or farmer who has not more or less of that description of timber on his property; and which is available to the market, either by its being near to the St. Lawrence, or great streams which empty themselves into that river; hence it can be readily brought to the mills near Quebec, erected for the purpose of sawing timber.

What is the quality of spruce timber?—It produces a deal as nearly as possible identical with the Norway deal.

Is it yellow or red?—It would be called a white deal; it is the most like what is called the moss deal of Norway, or some of the coarser kinds; it ranks with about the second deal of Norway.

Is it considered equal in durability and strength to the Norway deal?—I conceive so; some, however, of the groves that we have explored have been considered as affording a deal not so mellow as the Norway deal.

Are there great quantities of it?—Immense quantities.

Has it been the subject of importation to any great extent?—I was the first who imported it in 1808-9.

Is it exported to a very considerable extent?—Yes.

Under what name does it come into the market?—White spruce.

What price does it bear as compared with the yellow pine or the red pine?—It rates rather higher in this market than the yellow pine, and would rate lower than red, if with regard being had to profit we could bring any red pine deals to this market, which we cannot do at present.

Do you know for what purposes it is chiefly applied?—It is applied to the purposes for which second Norway deals would be applied.

Is it exported in square timber as well as deals?—No; the growth of that timber being small it is only susceptible therefore of being sent here cut into deals.

What is its value in Quebec before conversion; as compared with yellow and red pine?—It greatly depends for what purpose it is to be used for; it is seldom obtained of a larger size than fourteen inches square; it is the timber of which the frames of all the houses or nearly so in Canada are made; it is used for frame work, all the roofs and rafters are made of the spruce; it is seldom brought into the market as an article of public sale.

It is used chiefly by the inhabitants of the country?—Yes, every one having more or less of it on his farm.

Are you a proprietor of much of the yellow or red pine?—I have some groves of the yellow, but none of the red.

Is the wood trade of great importance to the Colonists, as giving employment to their people at any particular season of the year?—The cutting of the spruce timber is of particular importance to all the inhabitants of Lower Canada; to begin with the lowest point of the country, that to the northward and eastward of Quebec, upon the banks of the St. Lawrence, there are three or four parishes, whose principal occupation is the cutting this wood in the winter, and sawing it at their own saw mills, and bringing it forward to Quebec from St. Paul's bay and St. Mary's bay, to ship it to this country.

It is the principal business, and not necessary to any other employment which they have?—They look to the sale of their deals to enable them to procure their supplies of West India and British produce.

---
## Page 67

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

In other parts of the country, does it contribute much to the advantage of the colonists, by the employment of their people in the winter, who are occupied in other periods of the year in agriculture?—It does universally on the South side of the St. Lawrence, and within the last year has afforded employment for the new settlers in what are called the Eastern Townships, namely, those on the banks of the St. Francis, and those rivers which fall into it. I may add, that the logs are drawn, in the winter, to the banks of the river, and brought down to Quebec in summer, after the farmer has finished putting his seed in the ground, and before hay-harvest.

Is that a part of the country from which the greatest proportion of the timber trade is carried on?—No, except as respects the spruce timber.

Then with reference to those parts of the country from whence the greatest quantity of wood is brought, is it not usual to cut the wood by gangs, if they may be so described, brought in from the United States for this purpose?—Whenever large groves of timber are found at a distance from any settlement, that is the case to a certain extent.

Does not that constitute a great proportion of the woods from whence the timber is derived?—The yellow pine timber is cut a good deal on the banks of the Ottawa; a proportion of which is cut in the way mentioned, but a considerable proportion is also cut by farmers.

Does the quantity cut by the farmers bear almost any proportion to that cut in the way to which the former question refers?—I should say, it did; but not dealing in square timber myself, I cannot accurately answer that question.

Do not the same persons who cut the timber, usually raft it and convey it down the river to the ports?—I understand that in Upper Canada, in the St. Lawrence and the bay of Quinti, it is the practice of the country merchant to purchase from the farmers different lots of lumber, which he unites into one raft, and which he brings down for sale to Quebec.

Are not the people employed in this business, chiefly citizens of the United States?—I cannot speak to that.

Is the timber trade considered as one of the means of clearing the country, with a view to cultivation, and valued as such?—It affords the means of clearing the country, inasmuch as the new settler is thereby enabled to obtain an immediate subsistence on his property, which he might otherwise be obliged to abandon through want of capital.

Then are you of opinion, that if there were no timber trade in Canada, those lands would not be cultivated?—They would not be cultivated with so much advantage.

Then how do you reconcile that with the cultivation having more rapidly increased in parts of Upper Canada, where there is no timber trade, than in Lower Canada?—Because the soil and climate are better; and inasmuch as it is generally a hard-wood country, the value of the potash will afford the same means of clearing the hard-wood land that the timber trade does to the black timber land.

Does not a great proportion of the wood which is exported from Canada come from the United States?—A large proportion.

Can you state anything near what that proportion is, of the whole exported from Canada?—Perhaps somewhat under a half.

More than a third, and less than a half?—Yes.

Are there the means, in its passing through the country, of distinguishing that which comes from the United States, from that which is the produce of our own colonies?—All that which is cut upon Lake Champlain passes by the custom-house of St. John's.

Is there any other channel by which it comes?—That which is cut south of latitude 45° on the St. Lawrence, must pass by Chateaux Quay.

Through which channel does the largest quantity come?—Hitherto from Lake Champlain.

How long have the people of the United States been in the habit of bringing timber through Canada?—I have known timber brought in that way twenty years ago.

Has it increased considerably within a few years?—Most considerably since 1807.

Do you consider that increase as having been created by the high duties which have been imposed on the foreign trade?—Unquestionably.

What effect upon the colonies, beneficial or otherwise, would be produced by subjecting the timber of the United States to the same duty as that imposed upon the timber of other foreign nations?—It would benefit the landholders and manufacturers.

---
## Page 68

facturers of timber in Canada generally, but would hurt the commercial part of the community, and those who depended upon the arrival of a large number of ships for their trade.

Would it have any effect upon the commercial part of the community, independent of the effect produced in diminishing the demand for shipping in the port of Quebec?—For a few years it would diminish the timber market, but ultimately abundant supplies of timber would be obtained in the country. As the country gets settled, groves of timber become available, which were before out of reach, except at a very heavy expense.

Would not a diminution of the timber market in Quebec be beneficial to the persons engaged in the timber trade within our own colonies?—Speaking as a proprietor I should say it would; as a general merchant, I should say it would not.

Do you think that, in time of peace, the admission of American timber to all the benefits of our colonial timber is productive of an equivalent advantage in the vent of our manufactures through that channel?—I conceive that the purchase of Lake Champlain timber materially contributes to the sale of British manufactures, inasmuch as it enables the timber-cutters to purchase those articles.

Will you be so good as to state in what way that trade has been, and now is carried on?—Formerly, I believe, all the money passing immediately into the hands of the timber cutters, went into the United States; latterly, the payments having been in Canada bank notes, they have been generally exchanged for British manufactures and West India produce at Montreal; this has also arisen from British manufactures being now relatively cheaper at Montreal than they were at New York in former years.

If any regulations were adopted so as to make any difference unfavourable to American timber, in the exportation from Quebec to Great Britain, the growth of the United States and of Canada, is it not probable that the timber the growth of the United States, would be used in the country and shipped to the West Indies, so as to admit of an equal additional quantity of Canadian timber which otherwise would be consumed by those purposes, being exported to Great Britain?—As in that case the United States timber would be at the lowest price, it would be used generally for all purposes to which it was applicable.

Has not the timber trade much increased the population in the British provinces of North America, or has it not much encouraged emigration into that country?—I conceive it has, by giving immediate employment to many persons on their arrival.

Does it form any inducement for persons to emigrate from this country?—I should answer the question by supposing a man in this country to receive a letter from his friend or relation in Canada, stating that he had obtained immediate employment on his arrival; such information would certainly encourage emigration.

Have you ever known emigrants from this country expert persons in cutting and felling timber for market?—Last winter I employed an Irish family in the woods; they being industrious people, obtained a good livelihood by preparing logs for the market; not in cutting the tree down, for which they employed Canadians, but in cross cutting the tree when felled with the saw.

What proportion do the exports of timber from the Canadas bear to the whole exports from those provinces?—I am not informed upon that subject.

Is not the whole of the timber so exported an additional means of paying for British manufactures and produce?—Unquestionably it is.

Have many establishments been formed at Quebec at very considerable expense, for sawing timber for this market?—Yes, there have.

Have you formed such yourself?—I have, very large ones.

Have you sent out steam engines to form those establishment?—No.

Have you formed those establishments, in the expectation that those advantages, which you have hitherto enjoyed with respect to duty in this market, would be continued to you?—I have always felt full confidence in the protecting care of the mother country. The consolidation act, declaring the duties on deals permanent, acted likewise as an encouragement; I have since then cut a canal to a considerable extent in my own property, for the purpose of bringing logs forward to my saw mills.

To what extent does it happen to fall within your own knowledge that those establishments have been formed in Quebec by others, what extent in point of capital?—The capital expended in and about Quebec, is about 100,000l.

Has that amount of capital been laid out in establishments for sawing the timber?—Yes

---
## Page 69

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

—Yes, and the necessary ponds connected with them; there is one establishment where the machinery is driven by steam.

Do you think a moderate duty, imposed upon the trade, would not operate in the way of regulation that might be upon the whole beneficial to Canada?—It would depend on the extent of it. I should conceive, a small duty would check that spirit of adventure which has done so much injury to the trade, and reduced it below its fair rate of profit.

Do you not also think, that the effect of such a small duty would be to induce the Canadians to send their timber home better squared, and in a much more marketable state, than it is sent at present?—I should conceive so.

You have spoken to the amount of capital invested in the establishments at or near Quebec, for the purpose of sawing and preparing timber, will you be so good as to state to the Committee, what you conceive to be the amount of capital so invested, in the whole of the colonies?—About 150,000 £ in Canada.

Mercurii, 21° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

[THE PETITION of certain Merchants of Liverpool, referred to the Committee, was read.]

John Caldwell, Esquire,

Again called in; and further Examined.

In your evidence yesterday, you stated, that a considerable capital was invested in the erection of saw mills and machinery, for the cutting of timber into deals in Canada; are you particularly acquainted with that business?—I believe I am.

Has it been carried to any considerable extent?—It has very much increased within the last two years.

What proportion of the timber (by timber meaning square timber and deals) shipped annually from Canada, may be in deals?—About one-fourth. I should observe of this, nearly one-half, and in former years still more than one-half, had come from Lake Champlain.

They are deals cut in the United States?—Yes; at present we are equal to the supply of that whole quantity ourselves, if our mills were in full operation, and not restricted by a competition, we are fully equal to the supply of any quantity that might be required.

Do you mean a larger quantity than has been hitherto supplied from our own mills?—Canada is equal to the supply of 1,200,000 deals.

Is the Committee to understand, that a supply of deals is regularly received into Canada from the United States?—Yes, to the amount of 5 or 600,000 pieces annually.

What is the practice, in point of fact, in Canada, of cutting those deals? What are the usual lengths, breadths and thicknesses, that they are in the practice of cutting them to?—The deals, which come from a distance, are invariably cut three inches thick, either nine or eleven inches wide, and twelve feet long.

Mention those that are not from a distance?—Those from mills in the neighbourhood of Quebec, are cut in the most advantageous mode for converting the timber; they are generally twelve feet long, nine to eleven inches wide, one and a half, two, and three inches thick.

Then is the Committee to understand, that although the breadths and thicknesses to which deals are cut in Canada vary, the general length is twelve feet?—Certainly.

Suppose that a duty were to be laid on timber and deals, have you considered in what manner that duty might be laid on deals, so as to secure its fair and equal payment, and be attended with the least inconvenience to the importer in this country?—The answer to that question is extremely difficult, and would require a person, perhaps more conversant with the London timber trade than I can pretend to be. I am not prepared to give any opinion upon the subject.

To what ports, in the United Kingdom, are the deals of Canada usually shipped?—

---
## Page 70

shipped?—The first deals come to London, the second quality deals go to Ireland and Liverpool, and other parts; we have been driven out of Ireland by the Norway importers, from the deals being received there by the graduated scale.

Inform the Committee what were the current freights for deals during the last season, from Canada to Great Britain?—About £7.10s. per Petersburgh standard.

Can you inform the Committee whereabouts was the average shipping price for Canada deals during the last season, by the same measure, the Petersburgh standard hundred?—From £5. to £7. I think, according to quality.

Would £6. be a fair average of the whole quantity?—The price of floated deals and of bright deals are different.

Have the goodness to state the difference?—Generally at least 1l. and sometimes 30s. a hundred in favour of the bright deals.

By bright deals are meant those which are carried in craft?—Yes; not floated.

Are you at all acquainted with the stave trade of Canada?—Not, except shipping staves occasionally, always as broken stowage; indeed not sufficiently to give particular information upon the subject.

Do you consider it very probable, that if any alteration were made in the duties, any reduction of the duties on Baltic timber would have the effect of limiting the export of British manufactures to the Colonies, to a considerable degree?—Exactly in the degree that it interfered with the export of our timber.

Do you mean, that the Colonies have no means of paying for the manufactures they receive from this country, but through the timber trade?—Their means must be limited by their exports; if you diminish their exports you necessarily diminish their imports.

What means have they of paying for their imports besides the timber trade?—Wheat and flour, when the ports are open, and whatever potash is made in the country.

What proportion of the export of the manufactures from this country do you conceive depends upon the timber trade?—I should say, that were the timber trade annihilated, and the ports shut against flour and wheat, we should not have the means of paying for any other manufacture than what the bills drawn by Government for the support of the troops, besides the proceeds of its potash and furs and skins, would enable the country to pay. We should be nearly ruined.

The high duties were imposed in the year 1809-10?—Yes.

The timber trade, previous to that time, was comparatively very trifling?—Very trifling.

It appears, by the accounts delivered to the House of Lords, that in the year 1808, which was previous to the imposition of those duties, the exports to the Colonies exceeded £938,000; that in the year previous to that they exceeded £829,000; and in the year previous to that £746,000; and in the year 1802 they exceeded £1,112,000: by what means were those importations paid for?—As to the year 1802, it was subsequent to the largest harvest ever known in Canada, at a period when the price of corn had been high in Europe. As well as I can recollect, 1799, 1800, 1801 and 1802 were years of great scarcity in Europe; the exportations from Canada were very large in all those years of wheat and flour, at very high prices; the Newfoundland trade had also considerable activity in those years. The years 1806, 1807 and 1808, were years also of very large demand for corn; being years of war, the supplies that were required for the army in the country were very considerable; the supplies required for Europe were great, and Government at that time had the policy of obtaining its supplies for the troops at Halifax from Canada, which has been dropped since that period. The export of timber was also getting to be very considerable, and was very beneficial in those years, because, in consequence of the alarm as to the state of the Baltic, we had a very excellent market in this country, without the introduction of the duties. In 1807, 1808 and 1809, the American embargo system, and the Non-intercourse Acts, also caused a large quantity of goods to be passed into the United States through the medium of the Canadas. Potashes, during the whole of the above periods, were likewise exported to a considerable extent, more particularly during the period of the American embargo and non-intercourse.

Did not the same causes operate, in conjunction with the high duties, up to the end of the war, to enable you to pay for the exports from Great Britain?—The large sums expended in Upper and Lower Canada, during the progress of the late American war, contributed for some years after to enable the inhabitants to clothe themselves in British manufactures.

---
## Page 71

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Is it your opinion, that the import of British manufactures to the colonies would be in proportion to the export of their timber to this country?—Yes.

And that as the diminution of the export of timber proceeds, the diminution of the import of manufactures would keep pace with it?—Certainly.

John Hamilton, Esq. Called in; and Examined.

ARE you a proprietor of land in Canada?—I am.

Are you also a merchant, importing timber from Canada?—I am.

Where resident?—At Liverpool.

How long have you been in that trade?—About seven years myself.

Are you in the habit, as a merchant, of supplying a considerable part of the consumption of timber in the part of the country where you reside?—I sell a number of cargoes in the course of a year.

Have you found that the use of the Canadian timber has, in a great degree, superseded that of the Baltic, in that part of the country?—As I am only engaged in the Canada trade, I have no opportunity of knowing the diminution in the consumption of the Baltic timber.

Has the consumption of the Canada timber very much increased in that part of the country?—Very much.

Since what period?—It has increased very much within the last three years.

To what do you attribute that increase?—To the very low price of the timber.

Do you consider the heavy duties imposed upon the Baltic timber as having contributed to that?—I do, very considerably.

Do you consider that any effect has been produced by the experience they have had in that part of the country, of the value of the Canadian timber?—I should suppose so, of its being adapted to the purposes for which it is used.

Do you know to what purposes it is generally applied?—I sell it by wholesale to the timber-merchants, and I learn from them the uses to which it is applied; from my own personal knowledge I am not competent to speak upon that subject.

Do you know from such information, whether it is applied in that part of the country to purposes to which it is not usually applied in London; as for instance, to the higher description of buildings?—I consider that it is employed in the building of small houses in the neighbourhood of Liverpool; an immense quantity is consumed in Manchester, for the purpose of packing-cases; a good deal is employed where timber of a large surface is required, and also in a great number of country purposes, where it is exposed to the air out of doors.

You would consider those as inferior purposes?—I should.

Are there any purposes for which, at equal prices, you have reason to believe it would have a preference over Baltic timber?—I have been given to understand, that for musical instruments, and in some cases for packing cases, it would have an equal demand with the soft wood from the Baltic, but not a superior one.

For the inferior purposes to which you have adverted, would not the circumstance of its being cheaper give it the preference?—I should think so.

For all the inferior purposes to which you have adverted?—I should think it would depend upon the difference of price; upon the relative proportion of the price of each.

Can you state at what relative proportion, the price of American timber would still continue to supply the consumption for those inferior purposes to which it is now applied?—It is a difficult question to answer; I should conceive about thirty shillings a load difference, but it is really a matter that I am hardly competent to give an answer upon, having had no experience as to that immediate fact.

Do you think that if the difference was twenty shillings instead of thirty shillings, that would produce a considerable check upon the trade?—A very considerable check I should suppose.

Can you state any reason for that?—I consider that the yellow pine timber is frequently bought from its cheapness; there is a great prejudice in favour of the Baltic timber, and if the difference of price were not great, I conceive very frequently the purchaser would buy the Baltic timber.

Do you think that for inferior purposes to which the Canada timber is as applicable as that of the Baltic, a difference of twenty shillings per load would not be sufficient to cast the balance in its favour?—For some purposes I think it would be a sufficient difference; but it would materially lessen the present consumption.

Can you state what the proportion of the consumption in those inferior purposes is to the general consumption?—I cannot.

---
## Page 72

Do you happen to know in what estimation as compared to Baltic timber, the Canada timber stands in the neighbourhood of Liverpool?—It is generally considered inferior where strength is required.

Do you know whether it is considered as more liable to dry rot?—I do not.

Are you aware that one of the prejudices against it is on account of its superior liability to dry rot?—I have heard so.

In unloading any of your cargoes, have you observed much appearance of the fungi which precede dry rot?—I am not acquainted sufficiently with the appearance of dry rot to know; I have observed on the surface of the timber, an appearance of dampness, and that it was in places discoloured; and also on the surface of the deals, but that has arisen from their being shipped in a wet state.

Did the timber, appearing in that state, sell at the same price as it would have done if it had arrived in a perfect state?—Exactly the same price.

Have you been resident in Canada?—I have.

Have you property to a considerable extent there?—I have, in conjunction with my brothers.

Does it consist of woodland, or of land in a state of cultivation?—Of both, but principally of woodland; also of mills and wharfs, adapted to carrying on the timber trade.

What is the situation?—The mills are situated on the Atawa river, at the back of the Island of Montreal.

Is your chief property in that part of the country?—We have lands in different parts of Canada.

In what way do you consider the timber trade valuable to you as a colonist?—I have been so much in the custom of considering the trade as a merchant, that I hardly know how to look at it merely as a colonist. I consider it valuable, as enabling us to cultivate our lands, and to employ our people; but that is in a mercantile character.

Do you think the extension of cultivation in that country is at all dependent upon the timber trade?—I think it is promoted by it.

Are the persons who are chiefly employed in cutting and rafting timber, persons employed also in agriculture?—The axemen are not; they are chiefly Americans, from the United States. The labourers, who are occupied with the timber, in hauling it out of the woods, and preparing it for the mills for being sawn up, are employed in the summer months in the cultivation of the lands; the axemen are not beneficial to the cultivation of the country.

What proportion do the axemen and rafters, in point of number, bear to the other persons employed in the timber trade?—They are employed in a smaller proportion than other labourers.

Do you know whether the population of the province has increased much within a few years?—In Upper Canada very much, in Lower Canada not so much, but still it has increased.

Do you consider the timber trade as one of the causes of that increase?—Yes; a material cause, in giving encouragement to emigration.

Has the emigration taken place more into Upper Canada than Lower Canada?—Considerably more into Upper Canada than Lower Canada.

Is not the principal quantity of timber derived from Lower Canada?—I should think not.

Is there much ship-building in Canada?—There was at one time; it has almost ceased at present.

What is the extent of the property that has been vested in mills, and other establishments, with a view to carrying on the timber trade in Canada?—I should think about 150,000l.

Into how many principal establishments is that divided?—About four or five principal establishments.

At what period were those establishments commenced?—Principally about the year 1815; 1815, 1816, and 1817.

Have you any of those establishments belonging to yourselves?—Yes.

Is it one of the most considerable, or have you several?—I should say, it was the third in point of extent in Canada.

In speaking of the period at which they were commenced, do you speak of your own only, or of the whole?—I speak to the whole. They were begun at an earlier period, but they have been much extended.

What led to that extension?—The profit upon the exportations.

Were they established and extended in consequence of the operation of the duties on

---
## Page 73

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

on the Baltic timber?—I should say, they were extended in consequence of the high price the goods bore in the English market.

Was not that, in your opinion, in consequence of the high duties which have been imposed on the Baltic timber?—I think the high price the goods bore in this market was owing to the duties on the Baltic timber; but the immediate cause of the increase in Canadian establishments was, the price obtained here for American timber and deals.

Were not those establishments commenced and extended under the conviction, that the laws imposing those duties were temporary?—That they were temporary to a certain extent.

Was not the original law which imposed the duties a temporary law?—I was not in the Canada trade myself at the time when it was imposed, therefore, perhaps, I am hardly competent to speak to the subject; but in the year 1817, previous to the increase of our establishment, there was a communication from the Board of Trade to the merchants and ship-owners connected with the Canada trade, stating, that it was the opinion of their Lordships, "that an extension of duty, equal in amount to the additional duty imposed in 1813, would be beneficial to the revenue, without prejudicing the interests concerned;" under that impression, we certainly conceived that any alteration which might take place would not exceed that amount at any time.

Have you the letter to which you refer?—I have not it about me, but I could produce a copy of it to-morrow.

Are you sure that that letter did not apply to the whole of the duties that were imposed for the purposes of revenue, subsequent to the year 1813?—I have always considered it as applying to the additional duty of about 10s. imposed after the year 1813; the last duty imposed previous to the consolidation act, I think it was about 10s.

[The witness was directed to produce the letter.]

Do you import deals, both bright and floated?—No, we only import floated deals ourselves.

Are you aware, that they fetch a different price in the Liverpool market?—The difference is very little in the Liverpool market; about 10s. the Petersburgh standard hundred.

Are you aware whether any material difference exists in the London market?—About one pound the standard hundred.

You have stated, that the principal people employed in felling the trees are United States men, and that they are consequently not beneficial to agricultural purposes; do you not still consider, that their residence in the country is beneficial, to the extent of so much of their earnings as they expend in the country?—Certainly.

Are not the persons employed in agricultural pursuits the parties to whom the topping and squaring of the timber is left?—Very much; in Lower Canada, almost exclusively so in the trade of spruce deals.

Therefore the axemen are merely employed for felling the timber?—Yes, and subsequently squaring it.

Do you know what becomes of the axemen during the period of the year that there are no trees felled?—They have the occupation of bringing down the rafts from Upper Canada; there is a period in the autumn during which they have not much occupation, but it is but a small period.

They are United States men?—They are.

Is the trade carried on to a considerable extent, and at the present low prices of timber, advantageous to the merchants who charter ships for the purpose of bringing it?—Certainly not.

Is it advantageous to the ship-owner who employs his ship on his own account?—Decidedly not.

Can the trade be continued at its present extent under such disadvantages, even although no alteration of the duties were to take place?—Not at the present prices certainly.

Can you inform the Committee what proportion of the whole timber annually shipped from Canada is shipped in deals?—I should think at present about one fourth.

Can you inform the Committee what the practice is, as to cutting those deals into certain lengths and sizes?—The Canada pine deals are chiefly manufactured, three inches thick, eleven and nine inches wide, and twelve feet long; the spruce deals which are intended as a substitute for the Norway deals, are nine inches wide, three inches thick, and twelve feet long.

---
## Page 74

Then the Committee are to understand, that although the deals vary in width and thickness, the general length is twelve feet?—The proportion of other lengths is very small.

Should it be determined to lay a duty on North American deals, have you at all considered the manner in which it would be best levied on importation into this country, with a due regard to the protection of the revenue and the convenience and accommodation of the importer?—I am inclined to think by tale would be the best mode of levying the duty, inasmuch as very considerable delay would take place in measuring each parcel of deals, and a considerable charge be incurred thereby.

You are of opinion that to ascertain the duty by tale would be preferable to the conversion of the deals into cubic measure?—I am of that opinion.

Is it the practice in Canada to cut what are termed deal ends for the purpose of broken stowage?—In the manufacture of deals, a considerable quantity of deal ends will always be made, these are shipped for broken stowage.

In the regulation of the duty, to what lengths should deal ends be extended in your judgment?—To about six feet in length.

Therefore if deal ends were admitted at a lower duty, as is now the case, in your opinion that would be sufficient for the protection of the manufacturers?—I should think so, at and under six feet.

In the event of a greater length being so denominated, would that lead to any abuse or improper advantage?—In that case I think it ought to be charged as a deal.

Are you at all acquainted with the stave trade of Canada?—I am in the habit of importing a considerable quantity of staves.

Is it of considerable importance to Canada?—Very great, I conceive.

What are the descriptions of staves usually shipped from Canada?—Pipe staves, hogshead staves, and puncheon staves.

To what markets are they usually shipped?—The longer description of staves, pipe and hogshead staves, principally to the London market; puncheon staves have of late become an article of great import into Liverpool.

Are those the only markets?—There is generally a small quantity of staves shipped to other ports as broken stowage.

Are not a considerable quantity of staves shipped from Canada for our West India colonies?—A very large quantity of puncheon staves.

Are those in the rough, or dressed?—Some dressed, some undressed, principally undressed.

Can you state to the Committee, the dimensions of the different description of staves?—The pipe staves are reduced to the standard of five and a half feet long, by one and a half inch thick; the puncheon staves are generally by count.

Do you refer particularly to white oak staves?—I refer to white oak staves.

Are not red oak staves also sent both to the British and the West Indian markets?—They are.

Is the proportion of red oak staves considerable?—No, the proportion of white oak staves is much greater.

Can you state to the Committee, the prices that were current during the last season in Canada, for the different descriptions of staves you have mentioned?—Yes, I can; pipe staves were from £7l. 10s. to £40l. per 1,000; standard puncheon staves £2l. 10s. per 1,200.

Can you state the rates of freight that were paid for staves as cargo, from Canada to Great Britain during the last season?—I am not acquainted with the rates of freight when brought as cargoes.

Mr. Charles Churchill, Called in; and Examined.

BE so good as to state what your business is?—My business, at present, is that of a broker employed in the sale of deals and timber.

Are you extensively engaged in that business?—Yes, I believe as much as most of those gentlemen are. Twelve years before that, I was engaged in the foreign wood trade, in the Norway wood trade particularly, in the house of the Danish consul.

Are you employed in the sale of all descriptions of deals and timber?—Of all descriptions of deals and timber that come under that head.

Has the trade with British North America, in deals and timber, extended very much of late years?—The trade, within the last four years, I think particularly, has extended very considerably.

---
## Page 75

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

To what cause do you attribute that extension?—I attribute that extension more particularly to the circumstance of deals and timber from the colonies, coming in at greater advantages than those from the North of Europe, in consequence of their exemption from duties, the price at which they have come into the market has not been such as to induce any extraordinary shipment, for within the last two years particularly, they have fallen very much in value, which I attribute to an extensive importation; to an importation beyond the necessary demand. The importation of the soft pine in particular, has been so great as to affect every other article in the market, so that there is scarcely an article which can maintain a fair relative price.

What effect do you conceive would be produced upon the importation of timber from the colonies, provided a duty were imposed upon that timber, or a less duty charged upon Baltic timber than at present?—I conceive, that a regulation of that kind would go to the effect of checking the excessive importation we have, at present, of that article, for which there is not a concurrent demand; the soft pine has come over in great quantities, because there has been no duty upon that, and their peace-freights have been so low as to admit of its coming in much greater quantities than the demand could take off; it has therefore been forced into consumption, at prices much below its fair importation value, and that is still the case.

What was the duty upon Baltic timber, previous to the year 1810?—Previous to the year 1810 the permanent duty was 1 l. 6s. 6d. a load; the war duty, 6s. 10d. making a total of 3 l. 7s. 4d. a load.

What was the duty imposed in 1810?—By the Act of the 15th of June 1810, I see the duties were then doubled, making a total of 2 l. 14s. 8d. a load.

What was the cause assigned for doubling the duties at that period?—I believe the cause assigned was, that the foreigners in general got a much greater value for their goods, and that it would be advisable to allow the importation from the colonies, as a check upon that.

The double duties were then established as a sufficient protection for the British colonies to carry on a trade in competition with the Baltic?—No doubt of it; I believe it was then stated by Mr. Rose, in bringing that Act into the House of Commons, that the tonnage of shipping employed in the wood trade was 428,000 tons, of which 338,000 was foreign; that the measure he then brought in would tend to encourage the colonies, and enable them to take a greater proportion of the home manufactures, in lieu of the timber they would supply; that vessels to the north of Europe generally returned in ballast, whilst they would take out cargoes to the colonies.

What is the present duty upon Baltic timber?—The present duty is 3 l. 5s. per load.

Upon what ground was the additional 24s. imposed, beyond the duty that was considered necessary for protecting the trade in competition between the British colonies and the Baltic?—The only duty that has been since imposed, was in the year 1813, I think, which was an additional duty of 25 per cent. on all duties of customs not relating particularly to the timber trade, and without any particular reference to that trade, simply, I believe, as a matter of revenue, that was by an Act passed on the 15th of April 1813; then the war duty, which should have ceased at the expiration of six months after the definitive treaty of peace, was afterwards continued from something like the same motive, the necessity of upholding the revenue.

If it were now in the contemplation of the Committee to recommend such an alteration of the duties upon Baltic timber, as to bring the timber trade with the British colonies in America on a fair competition with the Baltic timber, upon what principle do you conceive the duty should be so altered, as to admit of that competition?—Provided the qualities of the timber imported from the North of Europe and that from our possessions in America could be assimilated, I conceive it would be a very easy matter; for it would simply be to take an average of the freight from the Colonies, and the average of the freight paid from the North of Europe, and to put such a countervailing duty as would do away with the distance or the disadvantage the Colonies laboured under in that respect; but as there is a very material difference in the qualities of the timber imported from both places, I consider that a duty of that kind would altogether prohibit the importation of wood from the colonies; the duties at present are 65s. per load, perhaps as far as reducing that to 50s. or leaving it as it is, and laying 15s. per load on American timber, would not tend altogether to prevent the trade from the colonies in wood, but it would materially lessen the amount imported.

(0.1.)

To

Mr. Charles Churchill.
(21 February.)

---
## Page 76

To what extent do you think it would diminish the amount imported?—I can only refer to London; looking to the extensive importations we have had the last two years, I think it would lessen it to the amount of half.

Have you any means of ascertaining from your own practice at what proportionate rates American timber will sell in competition with Baltic timber, when there is only a due proportion of each in the market?—I have a memorandum of some particulars in that respect, and I find that in the years 1817, 1818 and 1819, the relative price of the yellow pine with Memel timber was something like two thirds, little more or less, the relative value in the market. I have here a statement of the prices; perhaps I should previously observe that in the years preceding those I have mentioned, the years 1814, 1815 and 1816, when the importation of American pine timber was a good deal interrupted in one way and another, the prices were relatively much higher than in the succeeding years, when the importation was greater. I find in the year 1814 we were selling Memel timber at 10l. a load; we had comparatively no American pine timber in the market. In the year 1815 we sold Memel timber at 9l. per load; red pine at 8l. 10s.; yellow pine at 8l. 10s.; Memel deals at 29l. per hundred standard; and pine deals at the same time at 31l. 10s. per hundred standard.

[The witness delivered in the Account, which was read as follows:]

| Timber per Load | Deals per Standard |
|-----------------|--------------------|
| January         | Memel. Red Pine. Y. Pine. Memel. Pine. Norway. Spruce. |
| 1814            | £10 0             | 29 0    | 32 0   | 34 0   |
| 1815            | £9 0 - 8 10 0     | 29 0    | 31 10  | 34 0   |
| 1816 heavy stocks | 6 0 - 5 10 0 - 5 0 | 20 0    | 22 0   | 25 0   |
| 1817 steady     | 6 0 - 5 50 0 - 4 10 | 21 0    | 18 0   | 24 0   |
| 1818 improving  | 6 5 - 6 0 0 - 5    | 22 0    | 17 10  | 24 0   | 21 0   |
| 1819 full       | 6 15 - 6 2 6 - 4 10 | 22 10   | 17 10  | 24 0   | 21 0   |
| 1820 bad        | 6 0 - 5 50 - 3 15  | 19 10   | 14 0   | 22 0   | 16 10  |
| 1821 bad        | 6 0 - 4 15 0 - 3 3 | 17 10   | 13 10  | 20 0   | 16 0   |

In the years 1819 and 1820, the importation of pine timber had so exceeded the demand for it, that it had fallen considerably below what I consider its relative value to other timber, and we were selling Memel timber at 6l. and pine timber at 3l. 10s. to 3l. 15s.; at the present time Memel timber is worth 6l. and pine timber is not worth above three guineas, owing as I believe to the excessive importation of the one article, and the moderate importation of the other; that I believe will be borne out by the estimates which I have seen of the importation from the colonies in the years 1817, 1818, and 1819, as being particularly heavy.

In which of those years do you conceive the Baltic and the American fir bore their comparatively fair prices in the market?—I think at the beginning of the year 1819, when the price of Memel timber was 6l. 15s. and the price of yellow pine 4l. 10s.

On what grounds do you consider the comparison to be made, as to the quality of the timber, or how otherwise?—As to the quality of the timber certainly.

Without reference to the means of getting it?—Of course I am not looking to what the goods cost before they come here, but what they are worth when they are here at those relative prices; I am of opinion the two articles would circulate together here, without injuring one the other; I am to be understood as speaking of the yellow pine.

Do you speak of the yellow pine of good quality, coming from the British colonies?—Yes, I am speaking of the yellow pine of good quality; at the price I have mentioned yellow pine of inferior quality we are selling at inferior prices; I consider that the red pine timber from the colonies, in the shape we have it, would be at a fair price at the medium rate between the yellow pine and the Memel timber, that would be 5l. 12s. 6d.

There has been much evidence adduced to show, that several descriptions of the American pine are nearly as good in quality as the Baltic fir; why do you suppose that the reduction of the difference of price between American and Baltic timber, by the alteration of duties to the extent of 30s. would destroy the sale of American timber in this market?—I conceive that the opinion, that there is any description

---
## Page 77

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

scription of timber quite equal to the Baltic timber, is not well founded; it may be equal, and might be brought by a little better manufacture to hold upon a par with Norway or Swedish timber; but certainly not with either Memel, Riga, or Dantzig. I refer to the American red pine; with respect to the yellow pine from the colonies, it certainly will not bear competition with any of the timber we get from the north of Europe, and as long as it is brought in, in any quantities of importance, it must be sold at a comparatively less price.

After such a reduction of duties, to the extent of 30s. had taken place, would there not still remain a very considerable difference in the duties between the one and the other?—Certainly, there would remain a difference of 35s. a load.

Do you conceive the 35s. a load is not a sufficient protection to the American timber?—I certainly do not consider it a sufficient protecting duty, looking at the difference in the qualities of the wood brought in.

Can you give any sufficient grounds to the Committee, for the reasons on which you found your opinion, as to the inferiority, from want of durability or other causes, of the American yellow pine?—It appears to me, from the experience that I have had of that timber particularly, that it is not an article which will stand in competition with the timber from the north of Europe, in point of durability or strength, in any respect; there is a total absence of turpentine, or anything like a resisting quality in it, to prevent the wet or damp, or other causes from rotting it; it is soft and porous; there is no strength of fibre; nothing in fact, to make it fit for bearing a heavy weight, or for durability for a number of years; for some particular purposes; for light work, anything of moulding or picture-frames, or piano-forte-making, it is valuable beyond any other wood, from the absence of that strength which is essential for other purposes.

What extent of duty do you conceive might be levied on American timber, so as to give the Americans still the advantage of this market for the red pine?—I think in that way it might bear a duty of twenty shillings per load; but perhaps I am speaking rather hastily on that subject.

Is there a large stock of American timber now on hand?—Yes, there is a very considerable stock.

What proportion do you conceive the stock bears to the importation of last year?—I have a general idea of what our stock is, and what the importation was the last year; I consider, from some little attention I have paid to it lately, that our stock of American timber is about thirty thousand loads.

Do you know what the importation was?—The importation into the port of London about fifty thousand loads of timber; it must be of course a very rough guess.

Do you know the proportion of the red to yellow pine which has been imported into this port?—I should guess something like one-sixth; that the red bore the proportion of about one-sixth to the yellow.

If an alteration, to the extent of fifteen shillings or twenty shillings, were to take place in the relative duties upon Baltic and American timber from what exists at present, what, in your opinion, ought to be the alteration of duties which should take place upon Norway timber to bring it upon a similar competition with the American timber?—I really am not aware that anything particular is required, in that respect; that the Norway timber and the red pine would sell at nearly the same value; and as Norway is nearer to us than the other ports in the Baltic, and the timber might be brought here at a less freight on that account, I am not aware that anything is necessary to give them a particular advantage in that respect; it is certain that the Norway timber, from the smallness of its growth, cannot compete with that from the Baltic, and therefore does not stand on a fair footing, but perhaps with the red pine it would if the duties were equalized.

When you speak of the fair competition of the two different kinds of timber, do you mean that the American timber should enjoy the same consumption in this country, that it does at present?—Certainly not; it appears to me, that it is forced indiscriminately into uses for which it is by no means fit, that the lowness of the prices induces the consumer in many instances to use it without his principal being acquainted with it; and that we shall find in the building of a great many houses, a great quantity of timber that will rot and require to be replaced.

What reduction do you contemplate in the consumption of American timber, at a difference of price between the two kinds of timber of 45s.?—I take it for granted, that the American timber is now used for about two-thirds of the whole consump-

(186.)

---
## Page 78

MINUTES OF EVIDENCE BEFORE SELECT COMMITTEE

tion; and if this reduction were to take place, I should think it would fall to one-third of the whole consumption.

You think that it would fall to one-third of the consumption of the whole, at a continued difference in the price of 45s.?—Yes, of course; I am speaking of this port; to the westward, there are advantages to the colonies.

Do you happen to know the description of persons by whom the American timber is chiefly purchased?—It is purchased indiscriminately by all who use timber; but the first-rate carpenters and builders are very averse to using it, and I believe do not use it in buildings of any consequence.

Do you know the purposes to which it is generally applied?—It is applied in many instances indiscriminately for all buildings.

Do you mean that it is applied to superior buildings?—No, I think not, where the carpenter or builder considers his credit.

Then the general application of it is to houses of an inferior description, as far as buildings are concerned?—Certainly.

State to what other purposes, independent of building, it is used?—It is used to all the other purposes of timber, shelves, boards, packing cases, for anything of inside work. I do not consider but that it is as good as other, except for floors; it would not do for floors, it is so soft; a boot upon it would make an impression.

But for inside purposes, independent of boards, you consider it as good as other?—Yes.

Is it not applied to musical instruments?—Yes.

And to every thing which requires a great surface, and a freedom from knots?—Yes.

For the two last mentioned purposes it is preferred, is it not?—It is superior to any other for those purposes.

Then for those purposes it would be preferred at an equal price?—No doubt.

For packing cases, and other inferior purposes it is as good as the Baltic?—For some packing cases it is; but not where much of strength is wanted.

Generally speaking, for the packing cases it would answer?—Generally speaking, not; it does not hold the nails. I should think two-thirds of the packing cases are made out of other wood, and not American.

Are you at all acquainted with the proportion of timber imported into the port of London that is used for packing cases?—No, I have no idea.

Do you think, that for those inferior purposes, for which it is nearly as good as the Baltic timber, it requires a difference in price of 45s. to preserve a consumption?—It is to be considered, for all those purposes, the most inferior. Wood from the Baltic has generally been used, and therefore there is not the difference between the price of them and those prices I have been mentioning; there is not the relative difference.

The Baltic timber to which you allude is subject to the same duties as the other, is it not?—It is; but for packing cases, deals are more generally used.

In stating the prices, do you make a distinction between the price of one description of timber and another?—No, I take Memel timber; but in looking at the packing cases, deals are more properly the article taken into consideration.

Is the difference of forty-five shillings necessary to preserve a consumption for those inferior purposes to the American timber?—The thing bears more upon deals than upon timber; we have many articles of deals which come to market nearly as low as the American deals; at the present time we have white deals from Riga, which sell in the market at 4l. 10s. 6d. per load, and the American deals will average 4l. 5s.

You were understood to say that to preserve a fair circulation in the market between the American and Baltic wood, it was necessary that there should be an advantage on the side of the American timber of forty-five shillings; were you correctly understood?—Perfectly correctly.

For those inferior purposes, is it necessary there should be that difference of forty-five shillings, to preserve the consumption to the American timber?—I am still fully of opinion that that is necessary, because the bulk of the timber after all is used in buildings; speaking of the timber, the opinion I have given as to the difference in price relates to timber; the duty upon deals bears heavier than it does upon timber.

State what you conceive to be the protecting difference of price that would be necessary upon deals?—The prices vary so much of the deals from the north of Europe, that I can only give it as an average price; the average difference I think would be about 25 per cent of American deals below the average price of deals from the north of Europe.

---
## Page 79

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

That is a quarter?—Yes, if the one should be at 20l. the other should be at 15l.

What is the selling price now?—Of the one about 18l. and the other about 13l. 10s.; but then it is to be taken into consideration that we have deals from the north of Europe, which sell as low as 15l.

When you speak of 18l. and 13l. 10s. do you speak of the same measure?—I am taking the Petersburgh standard.

Supposing the standard Petersburgh deals to sell for 20l. you think the difference in price between the American deals and the Baltic deals should be 5l.?—Yes, I think there would be that difference in the estimation of the buyers as to quality.

Do you conceive, that for those inferior purposes which have been alluded to, 5l. in the 100 of deals is necessary to preserve the consumption for those inferior purposes to American timber?—I conceive that the deals from the North of Europe are preferred, and generally supply the better uses for which deals are required; the deals from North America will still only fill the lower and inferior purposes, therefore, if the American deals did not supply the market, we should have, I suppose, an inferior description brought in from the Baltic, which would supply it; in which there would be, perhaps, nearly as much difference as there is between the price of the best Baltic deals which we import, and that of the American which we import.

The question referred to inferior purposes, and what the Committee wish to know distinctly is, whether that amount of difference is necessary still to continue a consumption of American wood for those inferior purposes to which it is now applicable?—I do think so.

From your general knowledge of the timber trade, do you know what proportion of the consumption of timber is applied to the inferior purposes that you have stated?—I can give but a very inadequate idea of that; I should conceive about one-third of the whole amount; but it is mere guess.

Can you speak particularly as to the trade in staves?—Not so particularly as I can to that in deals and timber generally.

Has any great alteration taken place in the extend of the import of staves from the Baltic of late years?—I am not particularly acquainted with it, but I think there has been to about the same extent which has been experienced in deals and timber.

You cannot speak previously to 1810?—I cannot speak with the same accuracy of the stave trade as of the deal and timber trade; the alteration is generally, I understand, the same, arising from the same causes.

You would conceive, if an alteration were to take place in the duties on timber, that an alteration, upon the same principle, ought to take place in staves, to bring them back to a footing of competition?—Decidedly.

If such an alteration were to take place in the duties on timber, as the Committee have been referring to, what comparative alteration ought to take place in the duties on deals, to place deals from the British Colonies and the Baltic on a footing of competition?—I should consider, that the same proportion which the duty taken off or laid on, bears to the present rate of duty on timber should be the rule to affect deals; in fact, as far as I have been able to give it any consideration, it appears to me, that the war duty, or a proportion of that in one case or the other, would be a fair guide to go by in all the articles. The war duty was 13s. 8d. on timber, and 4l. 7s. 6d. on deals. I am clearly of opinion, that if the war duty had been taken off the foreign produce, we should not have had the immense quantity of inferior timber poured in upon us from the north of America; and perhaps the thing would have required very little adjustment, owing to the excessive advantage the colonies possessed in the total exemption from duties, and in peace freights, they have poured in quantities of goods so much beyond the real demand as to have overset the market altogether.

You are of opinion that 13/8 the amount of the temporary duty either taken off the Baltic timber, or imposed upon the American timber, would be rather beneficial than otherwise to the trade?—I consider decidedly so, that it would be a very great advantage to the trade.

Supposing that was increased to 20s. what effect would it then produce?—If it were increased to 20s. I conceive the importation of pine to this market, particularly of the inferior pine from America, would be reduced to a very small compass indeed.

Supposing it was 15s.?—I think it might bear 15s. without any other than a wholesome reduction of the quantity of that description of timber which comes in.

To what extent do you think the imposition of a duty of 20s. or the adjustment of

---
## Page 80

of a duty between the Atlantic and the American, making a difference of the existing duties to that amount, would affect the import to this country from America?—I do not think that 20s. a load would considerably affect the importation to the western ports; to London and the northern ports I conceive it would cut off two-thirds of the importation; that observation would apply more particularly to the yellow pine; I do not think it would go to that extent in the red pine timber.

Explain the difference you have stated, with reference to the different ports?—I consider the difference of freight between London and Liverpool and the western ports, generally about 5s. a load against the northern ports of the island, and of 5s. a load in favour of a voyage from the colonies to the western ports, making a total of difference as between the western and the northern ports of 10s. in favour of the colonies, against the north of Europe.

In point of fact, can you state to the Committee what the current freights were during the last year, from the lower ports of the Baltic to London?—The freight from Memel, generally, was from 20s. to a guinea, to London.

Do you know what was the current freight from Memel and Dantzig to Liverpool, during the same period?—I do not.

State what was the current freight of timber during the last year, from the lower ports of the North American colonies, that is, New Brunswick and Nova Scotia, to London?—From two guineas to 45s.

Can you state what was the freight from those ports to Liverpool?—No, I cannot.

Then, on what facts do you found your opinion, that such a difference as you have stated, exists?—Upon a general knowledge that a voyage from Memel to London, or a voyage from Memel to Liverpool or the western ports, is generally considered about one-fourth as much more.

According to the mode in which the duties on deals are at present levied, is it not much more advantageous to the importer to bring in deals of large lengths than short

short lengths, and how, in your opinion, ought the duties on deals to be regulated so as to equalize that difference?—It certainly is the interest of the merchant to bring his deals in of the largest possible dimensions, and from the Baltic and from the White Sea they are enabled to send deals which bear proportionally a much less duty than from Norway and Sweden; but as that advantage is counteracted, in a great measure, by the length of the voyage, I am not aware that the duties press so unequally upon any country as to require much of alteration in that respect.

Can you state what proportion of the ships that are employed in the timber trade with the north of Europe make one voyage, two voyages, or three voyages in the season?—To Archangel or the White Sea of course one voyage only can be made; to Petersburgh sometimes two; to Memel, Riga or Dantzig, three voyages; it is a rare instance to make four; from Norway I have known a ship make six, but four voyages is the outside that they can in general make.

Can you at all, by any conjecture, state what proportion of the import takes place in those that make one, in those that make two, and in those that make three voyages?—As the trade is at present regulated, the bulk of the importations certainly comes from Norway, Sweden, and Memel; the other at present is but small, so that there is no doubt the supply is brought chiefly in vessels which make from three to four voyages in the year.

Do you know how many voyages are made by vessels going to the lower ports of America?—Where they are altogether in that trade, I believe two can be made.

To Canada?—To Quebec sometimes more, but not very frequently; I have known ships make two voyages in the year, but it is running it very late.

Are you acquainted with the ships that go from the western coast?—I am but little acquainted with the trade from the western coast.

What is the statement you now hold in your hand?—A Statement of the present value and charges upon deals from different ports of Europe and America, showing what is left to the Shipper of each article in loads for his goods.

## Page 82

Jovis, 22° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

Mr. Bartin Haigh,

Called in; and Examined.

WHAT is your business?—A joiner, house carpenter and builder, at Liverpool.

How long have you been established in business at Liverpool?—Five-and-twenty years.

Have you, during that period, been accustomed to make use of Baltic timber, and also American yellow and red pine timber?—Of American yellow pine, but not of red pine so long as twenty-five years.

Have you had opportunities of ascertaining the durability of each of those kinds of timber after they have been used?—Yes, several.

Have they occupied your particular attention with a view to ascertaining their degrees of durability?—Yes, I have paid attention more particularly to the pine, because there was not the prejudice against the Baltic.

What opinion have you formed of the respective durability of each, when employed in the ordinary purposes of house-building?—The more experience I have had of the pine, the more partial I have become to the use of it.

State comparatively your opinion, which you have found the most durable, and which the most liable to decay, with reference to the particular manner in which they have been respectively used?—I have used the American pine four-and-twenty years back, for gate-posts, and renewed them in 1814; this was in 1796.

You used American pine for making posts of gates in 1796?—Yes; in 1814 we renewed them; they were decayed; that was a period of upwards of eighteen years; we do not consider that Baltic timber would last longer to be in the ground than from eighteen to twenty years; that is a fair trial of Baltic timber.

Were those posts to which you refer, placed in the ground so as to be exposed to the damp of the soil?—Yes, they were buried in the ground at least four feet.

And lasted for eighteen years?—Yes, upwards.

When taken out, in what state did you find them?—They were decayed; it was between the surface and the earth that they were decayed, just where the wet gets to them.

They were decayed just in that part where they are subject to the alternation of wet and dry?—Yes.

They stood well exposed to the air, but in that part where there was an alternation of wetness and dryness they had decayed?—Yes.

Was that part of the post which was placed in the earth, decayed?—Not so much as the other part.

Did the decayed part bear the appearance of dry rot?—No, not at all.

No part of the post had that appearance?—No part; it was just the same as Baltic timber.

You are quite clear they were made of American pine timber?—Quite clear, for they were put down by my directions.

Can you state any other instances of the durability of American yellow pine timber?—Yes; in 1797 we made sashes and frames and shop fronts for three houses, and I examined them on Monday last, and they are in a perfect state.

By shop fronts, you mean the ornamental parts of shop fronts?—Ycs.

Have you known any particular instances of the durability of yellow pine where it has been used for joists or other purposes of house building, where it was placed in a particularly confined state, and in any way exposed to damp or green walls?—Yes; in 1810 I built eight houses of pine timber, and about four months since I opened the floors of two of the houses, and examined the ends, which have their bearings on the outside walls, the joists in the walls, and found them perfectly sound and good, and the boards also.

Were those walls outer walls?—Outer walls.

Do you know of any instances of Baltic timber decaying at any given period, when used in a similar manner?—Yes, in nine years. I built a house of Riga timber,

---
## Page 83

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

timber, and under the wine cellar where the air was kept from it, in nine years both the boards and the joists were completely decayed.

What appearance did that decay assume; was it the dry rot, or otherwise?—A rot that is found arising from the damp.

Was there a fungus?—Yes, it was all over fungus; there was nothing but the skin, of the upper part that appeared free.

Are you of opinion that American yellow pine timber, for the ordinary purposes of house building, is not more subject to decay than the common pine of the Baltic?—Not to my knowledge; I have never found it so in all my experience; having tried both, I do not conceive that it is.

Do you know any instances in which American pine timber, when used for house building or other similar purposes, has decayed prematurely?—Not at all; I do not remember any.

For what purposes is the American yellow pine timber generally used in Lancashire?—There are large quantities used in the manufacturing towns, for boxes, and also for the insides of buildings; in Liverpool the cabins of ships are all fitted up with the pine timber, and the insides of buildings; it is used for mouldings and architraves.

For floors?—Sometimes in floors, but more generally for the doors, window shutters, and all that part of the work which has most labour upon it.

Do you consider American yellow pine equally suitable for floors, with Baltic timber or deals?—For warehouses, it is my decided opinion that the yellow pine will be found equally as profitable and as durable as Baltic.

Is it not of a soft nature and apt to yield to pressure?—It is rather so; it sinks, it gives way, it is more spongy, but it does not wear. I have examined a warehouse on Monday last, the floors of which were laid twenty years back, and are in perfect good order.

Is it within your own knowledge that those floors were made of American pine?—Yes, the boards went from my own yard, and the warehouse doors were of pine, and they are in perfectly good order.

Then, in point of fact, though you find American pine of a softer texture, upon the whole it is not less durable for this purpose than Baltic timber?—No; from my experience of it, though we have not been in the habit of using so much of it, yet by proving the time it has been in use, I am quite convinced that the more it is used, the more persons will be in favour of it. Another reason why I find that the pine timber will wear equally as well as the Baltic, is, that in the warehouses there is generally a grit or sand that is upon the floors, arising from the different uses of the warehouse; the timber receives that by the treading it in, it makes the surface more hard.

Inform the Committee, what number of joiners and house carpenters you are in the practice of employing, in the ordinary course of your business?—Fifty hands.

What proportion of American yellow pine timber, compared with Baltic, are you in the habit of using in the course of your business, as a joiner and house carpenter?—Some years about equal, other years more Baltic than pine; my business is principally in building the best houses, houses of the first class; and there has been a prejudice against the pine, and my employers have preferred using Baltic to pine, and that has led me to use more of the Baltic.

Are you acquainted with the general proportion throughout the country, that is used of each description of timber; that is, the North American pine and the Baltic pine?—Of late years, I apprehend there has been about two-thirds of American pine to one of Baltic; in Liverpool, I believe, that will be found to be about the average.

Can you state to the Committee, the present price of good North American pine in the Liverpool market?—Eighteen-pence is the price now.

Can you state the present price of good Baltic timber, such as that of Memel, Dantzig or Riga?—Dantzig is 2s. 6d. Riga, being scarce, about 3s. Memel, about the same as Dantzig.

The high price of Riga is owing to its being particularly scarce?—Yes.

Is there American timber, good timber, at the price of 1s. 6d.?—Yes; but not what it has been for some time past; at present there is a scarcity of demand.

Is there any abatement of demand in consequence of the price?—No, not at all.

Is there, at the prices you have quoted for Baltic timber, a good demand, equal to the demand for American?—No, there is not the same demand for Baltic as for American.

186. What

---
## Page 84

What approximation, in the prices of the two kinds of timber, that is, coming nearer to each other, would be necessary in order to increase, materially, the demand for Baltic timber, the present difference being about 1s. a foot?—As Baltic timber is stronger bearing timber than the pine, it would make 4d. a foot, the difference from the bearing of the one and the other, I should imagine about 6d.; that is to say, the Baltic timber about 6d. a foot more than pine, would make Baltic timber to be more used than it is.

Then the consumption of American timber would decrease in the same proportion?—Of course.

Then in point of fact the Committee understand you to state, that the proportion, that in your view of the subject, the difference of strength between Baltic timber and American, for the purposes of bearing, is as 24 to 28?—I have made my calculations according to the bearing. When I have used pine timber, I cut my scantling ten by two and a half, for which I should put in Baltic nine by two and a half, so that, supposing pine timber to be two shillings, then it would, to put in the same strength of timber as I should in Baltic, make it equal to 2s. 4d.

Then in the use of American timber, put in, in such a manner as to be of equal strength with the Baltic timber, there would still be a saving of two-pence in the expense?—There would be a saving of two-pence, but the prejudice in favour of Baltic would lead the public to prefer Baltic.

With this difference in the scantling of the timber, would you use for your own purposes American timber as readily as you would Baltic?—For all purposes, with the exception of beams for warehouses, or wherever there might be a great pressure; where we use props under the beams, the American pine being softer than the Baltic, there would be a giving way, which would lead in many stories, that is, in six or seven stories of a warehouse, the beams to sink so much more. For that reason it would not be so preferable as the Baltic; but for warehouse floors, I am fully persuaded it would answer as well.

Is there not a way in which you now make the beams, by constructing them of different species of wood, that prevents that giving way you have spoken of?—That has always been used for ages past; it is what we call trussing a beam.

When the beams made of the American timber are so trussed, are they not as strong as those made of the Baltic?—The American timber I have mentioned must have a great proportion more in it than Baltic, to make it equal to Baltic. If you truss an American beam, and truss a Baltic beam, the proportion is equally the same in the one as the other; to go to truss an American beam, and not to truss a Baltic beam, the expense of trussing the beam would be so much more that no one would ever attempt it.

Supposing the prices of American pine and Baltic pine to be equal, or nearly so, are there any purposes for which you would still continue to use American pine, notwithstanding the equality of price?—Certainly, as I before observed, for mouldings, architraves, and every thing of that kind, the American timber is superior; it receives the paint better than the Baltic; it is cleaner, freer from knots, and for doors and shutters is lighter on the hinge; and the breadth also prevents glueing of pannels; where you require width of pannels on the inside, it is always preferred on that account.

Do you give a preference to any one particular port of the British North American colonies over the other?—Yes.

Which?—The Miramichi timber is what we consider to be the best timber that comes into the port of Liverpool; that is New Brunswick timber.

Better than Canada timber of any description?—It is cleaner and more lively timber, not a dead timber; I am not speaking of the red pine. It is only for the cleanness of it; when we cut the other timber up, either St. John's or Quebec, it is found to be more knotty; we use it more on account of its being cleaner than the other. We give a preference to Miramichi timber; it sells a penny a foot more in the market than St. John's timber.

Have you ever used white spruce deals, or timber from Canada?—Not the deals; I have used white spruce timber balk.

Is that better for any purposes than the yellow pine timber?—We do not approve of it at all in Liverpool, and very little is used; very little comes there.

Inform the Committee what is the usual square of the timber imported from Miramichi?—The size is sixteen, eighteen, and twenty-two inches, and some two feet.

Have you made any remark on the comparative quality of the middle or smaller sizes

---
## Page 85

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

sizes with the larger sizes?—Yes; I will first state, that as to the United States of America, we used to have all our yellow pine timber from thence twenty years back; that was of a softer nature; a more grown timber, a much inferior timber than what we have from the British settlements; it has been my opinion, that that has produced more prejudice against the timber, than the timber from the British settlements. With respect to what comes from the British settlements, there is some that is overgrown timber, and that timber we always found to be the most inferior timber; the timber of large scantling.
