What do you mean by overgrown timber?—That the trees had not been cut time enough; and it is my opinion, that the more the timber is used here the better we shall have it come into this country; as the forests will be first cleared of the old timber, and then that in a fitter state for use in its turn, though of smaller dimensions, will be cut.

Have you been in the habit of using the deals imported from Canada?—Not much.

You do not feel yourself sufficiently acquainted with the quality of them, to give the Committee any information upon them?—The quality is about the same with the timber; but they are often knotty and twelve feet only long; that we prefer cutting up out of the balk, as being more profitable.

That objection applies generally to all short deals, does it not?—Yes.

Are you acquainted with the red pine of America?—Yes.

What opinion do you entertain of the red pine of America?—I have a good opinion of the red pine.

Is it superior in quality to the yellow?—For the purpose of bearing timber, it is superior.

Do you know, from your experience, whether it is more durable or less so?—That I cannot speak to; the time of its coming into the port has not been sufficient to prove.

What difference of price does it bear in the Liverpool market, from other pine?—Four-pence.

Have you been in the habit of using pitch-pine in the course of your business?—Yes.

Have you found pitch-pine more durable than the yellow-pine of America?—The reverse, in some instances.

Point out the instances?—Whenever pitch-pine is confined from air, it will decay sooner than the yellow pine, but not otherwise.

For what purposes has pitch-pine been commonly used in Liverpool?—For floors, we have used more of it since Riga timber became scarce.

Is it not used for ship-building purposes?—Yes, it is, for some purposes.

Can you state, within the last twenty-five years in which you have been in the habit of using yellow-pine, what difference has taken place in the comparative use of Baltic and American timber?—I cannot state, except that the yellow-pine timber has been much more in use every year, for ten, twelve and fourteen years.

Do you conceive it to have been in a state of progressive increase, previously to the year 1810?—Yes.

It has been gradually increasing, subsequent to that time?—Yes.

More rapidly?—When the American pine timber was lower, of course there was more of it used, in proportion to the difference.

Do you think that the increased use of it, subsequent to the year 1810, has been owing entirely to the difference of the price between Baltic timber and American timber, or to the effect of the experience they have had in the value of it?—I believe both, that the more experience they have had the prejudice has been less, and also the difference of price.

In consequence of your having conceived a better opinion of this timber than you had originally, have you been in the habit of using it in better descriptions of buildings?—Certainly.

That you now use it in a description of buildings, in which you would not have ventured to have used it some years ago?—Exactly so; and I mention as a reason of my prejudice twenty years back, my noticing more particularly the gate-posts in which we used it, and also the sashes and frames that we made in 1797.

You have compared the durability of Baltic timber with the durability of American timber, and have given some instances, state what the description of Baltic timber

---
## Page 86

timber was, with which you made the comparison?—It was with Riga of the best quality.

Do you know from the communications you have had with other people of the same trade, whether the same impression has been made upon their minds, by the experience they have had in the use of the yellow pine timber?—It is generally approved of, I believe that the opinion in favour of it is growing.

Do you happen to know whether any other persons beside yourself, have used it recently in the higher descriptions of buildings?—Yes, it is more used in Liverpool by others, than it is by myself.

Do the present prices of timber bring in a great deal of very bad timber from America?—No, I do not perceive that; only as I observed before, and it is my decided opinion, that if some plan was adopted to prevent the large scantling of overgrown timber from coming to market, that would be a very great advantage to the American trade.

That if some measure could be taken to produce greater care in the selection of the timber, it would on the whole be more advantageous to the trade?—I have not a doubt of it.

Supposing by alteration in duty, or from other causes, the difference in price between Baltic timber and American timber, which appears to be now in the Liverpool market about fifty shillings per load, should be reduced to half, or twenty-five shillings, in what proportion would the consumption of each species be affected?—I cannot speak exactly to what proportion, but Baltic would be more used than the American pine.

To what extent do you think it would be more used?—That I really cannot say.

You have stated that you conceive, and that it seems to be the impression of those at Liverpool in the same trade with yourself, that the American timber is as good as the Baltic, for a great many purposes?—Yes.

That for some it is even superior?—Yes.

In those for which you conceive American pine to be superior at equal prices, and for those purposes to which you consider it equally applicable, would not a moderate difference in the price cast the balance in favour of the American timber?—For those purposes it would.

From those purposes, you have excluded all the purposes to which it is applied for bearing considerable weights?—Yes.

What do you conceive in your own trade to be the amount of wood used by you for the purposes of houses, and various other purposes, exclusive of those where it is used for purposes of bearing?—The most we use is for those purposes, not for bearing purposes, it is about equal; about the same quantity of pine as Baltic, sometimes more in one year of Baltic than the other, and sometimes more of American.

There are certain purposes for which you say they are equally good, from those purposes you except those that apply to bearing in the use of the trade; generally speaking, what do you conceive to be the proportion of wood used by them for those purposes which are not for bearing, such as beams, joists, and those things?—I should say that as three is to four, that three parts of it are used for other purposes, and one for bearing.

Would not, in your opinion, a moderate difference of price make the American wood be preferred for those purposes in which bearing is not an object?—I mentioned before, that the preference would be given, provided the price was in favour of the pine; but it should be observed, that though the persons that use this timber would be prejudiced in favour of it, yet the persons that it is used for, the employers, are still prejudiced against it, and there might not be if the price came near the quantity of pine timber used in the same proportion as there is now, because the employers would still be in favour of Baltic timber.

Do you think that a difference of twenty-five shillings in the load, would be sufficient to overcome that prejudice?—Yes; it is my opinion that twenty-five shillings would be a fair proportion; but I think it is as much as ought to be laid upon it.

What do you think would be the effect of a difference only of twenty shillings?—It would still be in favour of the pine.

Would a difference of twenty-five shillings throw out of use, that proportion of the pine timber which is now employed in bearing?—No.

That would still continue, in your opinion, to be as much used in the purposes of bearing as it now is, if the difference was only twenty-five shillings?—I cannot speak

---
## Page 87

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

speak exactly to that; in some instances it would, and in others it would not. I have before observed, that where the better houses are built, the gentlemen who have their houses built would sooner give more to have them made of Baltic, the prejudice being in favour of it, though the price is so much higher.

John Diston Powles, Esq. Called in; and Examined.

WHAT is your line of business?—A ship and insurance broker.

How long have you been engaged in that business?—From ten to twelve years.

Is your business largely in freighting ships to bring timber from the Baltic and from the British provinces in North America?—Very much so.

Have you had occasion to observe what has been the effect of increasing the duties on Foreign timber, and exempting the timber from the American provinces from duty on the employment of British shipping?—It has had the effect of providing a resource for the employment of British shipping, for which otherwise no employment could have been found.

Upon what ground do you state, that no other employment could have been found for that proportion of British shipping?—I say so because all other means have been tried; and there still remains a quantity of shipping for which it is very difficult to find employment.

To what circumstances do you attribute that difficulty?—I attribute it, in the first place, to the demand for transports having ceased; and secondly, to the trade of the country being exposed to greater competition than formerly.

If the timber trade with the British provinces in North America had not afforded this resource for the employment of shipping, would the same quantity of shipping have been engaged in bringing the wood from the northern powers of Europe?—I apprehend not one-eighth part of the quantity of tonnage.

What are your grounds for that opinion?—I say so, because from Russia, ships make upon an average about two voyages a season, from Prussia three or four, from Norway six; from which I reckon, that if the whole had been brought in British tonnage, it would not have employed above a quarter of the tonnage to have brought it; but looking to the fact, that from Prussia and from Sweden one-half of the tonnage employed is Foreign, and from Norway five-sixths of it Foreign, I come to the conclusion, that upon the whole not above one-eighth part of the tonnage would have been employed to bring the same quantity of wood from the north of Europe that has been employed in bringing it from America.

If such regulations in the duties on timber were made as to throw a greater proportion of the supply from the Baltic, and less from the British provinces in North America, do you apprehend that the increased trade with the northern powers of Europe would be carried on in a greater proportion in British ships than it hitherto has been?—Certainly not.

What means have you of forming an opinion upon that subject?—Because I find, looking to the three years ending in 1819, which is the latest date to which I have found any returns made up, that although the Norway ships, in coming here, lose one-fifth of their whole freight by the difference in duty, and although the Prussians lose to the extent of one-seventh in the difference of duty; notwithstanding that circumstance, they have, for the three years ending in 1819, been gradually gaining ground on British ships.

Can you state the difference of expense of ship-building in this country, and in the north of Europe?—Ships may be built in the north of Europe; fir ships for less than half the money they are built for in this country; oak ships for about two-thirds.

Do you speak from your own knowledge, or from information?—From information I am constantly in the habit of receiving, from persons coming from those countries; and from accounts I have actually seen of the cost, the fir ships answer the purpose for the timber trade, though of course they are very inferior to the British ships.

What is the comparative cost of fitting a ship for sea, in the ports of the Baltic and in the ports of Great Britain?—Every material article is considerably cheaper there, for this reason; that it is not subject to the duties to which it is liable in this country; I hardly know one exception.

Do not the duties now, in consequence of the depression of the price of all raw materials employed in the manufacture of articles necessary for fitting ships to sea, bear much more heavily upon the cost of those articles than they did when the

---
## Page 88

price was comparatively higher?—I think in a twofold degree, as nearly as I can estimate it.

What is the duty on hemp now?—Ten pounds a ton, and a fraction more.

What is the price of hemp?—Forty pounds per ton.

What was the duty on hemp in 1813?—I think the same.

What was the price of hemp in 1813?—I will obtain the price, for the information of the Committee; but I think it was at least double the present price, and was so for a great part of the war.

So that the duty upon that article, which was about one-eighth or one-ninth in 1813, is now one-fourth?—Yes.

Are you to be understood, that notwithstanding the duty appears to bear harder in proportion to the price of the hemp, it is, in fact, to the ship-owner in this country not much more than one-half of the price it was in the year 1813?—I mean to say, that in the year 1813, the price of the hemp was, I think, 30l. and now it is 40l.

What proportion do the freights of 1813 and 1820 and 1821 bear to each other?—At the time hemp was double, freights were double.

Does the nature of your business give you opportunities of observing the actual situation of British shipping, and of persons dependent upon it?—Very much so.

What do you conceive their situation to be at present, in relation to capital and profit?—The situation of the British ship-owner I apprehend to be at present just this, in relation to capital; that since the peace, he has sustained a reduction in the value of his property of full 50 per cent. I mean, that a ship of precisely the same quality in all respects, that would formerly have sold readily for 4,000l. will now with difficulty fetch 2,000l.; and that that part of his capital which remains to him is producing to him no income. When I say no income, I mean, that there is not enough left to him at the end of the year to make up for the gradual deterioration in the value of his property.

Have the effects of this state of things shown themselves in any visible consequences among the ship-owners?—They have so, very plainly. Those ship-owners who had property independent of their shipping, have been obliged to have recourse to that property for their maintenance. A second class of ship-owners have been obliged to sell off their ships to pay their debts; and a third, and a very numerous class are at this time gradually accumulating debt with their tradesmen. I speak now particularly of those engaged in the timber trade. I am told, that the quantity of debt due in that way, from ship owners to the tradesmen, in the north of England particularly, is greater than it was ever known.

Would not additional distress be created among the ship-owners, by any such regulation of the duties on timber as should throw a greater proportion of it into the hands of the northern powers of the Baltic, and diminish the proportion of it now carried on by the British colonies in North America?—Certainly to a most serious extent, shipping having now more than ordinary difficulties to contend with; anything that tends to throw it out of employment, must very much aggravate those difficulties; and with respect to some large towns upon the coast, I mean particularly North and South Shields, Sunderland and Whitby, I think the consequences would be fatal to a degree that I can hardly venture to state to the Committee; and I say this from being in constant intercourse with those places.

Has there been any great reduction in ship-building in this kingdom?—There is no return made up for last year; but from all the observation I have been able to make, I have no doubt it will be found, that in 1820 there has been a large reduction in the number of ships built.

Has not that reduction been progressive for some years past?—No, I think not; I think the ships built for the India trade expressively have prevented the reduction that would otherwise have taken place. I do not think there is any considerable reduction; independently of which, persons possessed of ship-building yards are obliged to keep apprentices and other persons about them, so that there is always a certain quantity of ship-building which must go on, whether ships are wanted or not.

Can those ships, when finished, be sold without a very heavy loss?—Certainly not.

Are you able to state the comparative rate of seamen's wages, between British ships and the ships belonging to the northern powers of Europe?—The rate of wages paid in British ships in the Baltic trade, is 50s. per man, per month; the rate of wages, paid to foreign seamen in the same trade, varies from 18s. up to 27s. per

---
## Page 89

per man per month. The victualling, in foreign ships, is about 6d. per man per day; in British ships from 1s. 3d. to 1s. 6d. per man per day.

Can you state, from your own knowledge, how the Norway trade was carried on previous to the increase of the duty in 1809?—Previous to 1809 I was engaged in a house at an outport, which imported pretty considerably from Norway; we had, in the course of four or five years, from 40 to 50 cargoes from Norway, only one of which came in a British ship, which we sent out by way of experiment; but not finding it answer, it was never repeated.

Supposing the consumption of American timber in this country should be reduced one-half, what effect would that have upon British shipping?—To throw out of employ just one-half of the number of ships engaged in that trade.

Supposing it should be reduced one-fourth?—It would then throw out of employ one-fourth of the British shipping at present engaged in it.

Would any measure, which had for its object a gradual reduction in the employment of British shipping in this trade, be less prejudicial to the interests of the British ship owner, than a measure which would immediately considerably reduce the consumption of American timber?—It would, only the difference between dying at once, and a lingering death.

It appears by a paper before the Committee (the deposition of a sworn broker at Memel) that in the year 1819 the Prussian trade from Memel employed 263 British ships, and that there were but 63 ships under the Prussian flag which went to Great Britain; how do you account for that circumstance, if the foreigner can navigate on so much better terms than the British?—I doubt the correctness of that report, because it differs from the official Report presented to the House of Lords.

You state, that if a reduction was to take place in the importation of timber from the American colonies to the extent of one-half, to that extent British shipping would be thrown out of employ; do you not consider, that in such case, a considerable increased exportation would take place from the Baltic to make good the deficiency from America?—I consider, that very nearly the same quantity would come altogether into the market, either from the one place or the other; but as I believe that a great part of that would come from Norway, I find, by the official returns, that the Norwegian has five-sixths of the carrying trade. I find that Prussia has one-half of it; and I will here state the actual tonnage employed in 1819, from the official documents presented to the House of Lords. In 1819 there arrived from Prussia, Foreign tonnage 78,266, British tonnage 80,258; the quantity from Sweden of Foreign tonnage, in the same year, was 20,165, of British tonnage 21,988; from Norway, Foreign tonnage 82,689, British 17,087; on which ground it appears to me, that very nearly the whole of that trade would go into the hands of foreigners.

Then you are of opinion that the quantity of timber deficient in the American importation would be supplied from the Baltic, but that that supply from the Baltic would come chiefly in foreign tonnage?—I think that very nearly the whole quantity would come not quite as much, for we have American pine so cheap, that that produces a consumption in my opinion, which would not take place if it were dearer.

In point of fact, is there not above a moiety imported from the north of Europe to England, imported in British shipping, deals being included in timber?—No, I conceive not.

Is not nine-tenths of the timber and deals imported from Prussia, imported in British shipping?—Certainly.

Is not three fourths of the timber imported from Memel, imported in British shipping?—No, only one half.

You do not refer to Memel in particular?—No, to the whole of Prussia.

In regard to Sweden, what proportion?—One half.

From Norway?—Five-sixths foreign.

May not such a countervailing duty be laid on timber so imported in foreign bottoms, as to lead to a preference of British shipping?—Certainly.

If such a measure was adopted, would not British shipping be in a great measure compensated for the falling off of the American trade?—Certainly not; for one-fourth of the quantity of shipping would do the work.

Do you mean to say that a ship will make four Baltic voyages in the time she will make one American?—I mean to say she will make six from Norway, three or four from Prussia, and two from Russia in a season.

---
## Page 90

In point of fact, what number of voyages do the ships now usually employed between the ports of Russia and London, make in one season?—Two voyages, and often one from a lower port in the Baltic besides.

From the lower ports of the Baltic?—Three or four.

From Norway?—Six.

How many from the lower ports of the North American colonies?—To the west side of the land, it is very common to make two voyages; from the lower ports of America, from Quebec not so usual; and to the east coast generally seldom more than one from either the lower ports or Quebec.

Are you not aware that the ships employed from the port of Liverpool in the trade to Canada, are in the habit of making two voyages?—It is certainly not very common to make two Quebec voyages; they have tried at two for the last year or two.

Have you known any instances of three voyages having been made from the lower ports of America to Liverpool, in one season?—I have never heard of its being done but once; once I have.

Then in point of fact, the average of Baltic voyages is three in the season; the average of American two?—I would by no means say that the average of American voyages is two, that is the extreme number; I think the average would be something like one and a half.

In point of fact, ships sailing in the regular season, is it not customary for them to make two voyages from the British colonies to the west coast of England?—

I said so before.

Is it not also in point of fact, the practice to make two voyages to the east coast?—Certainly not.

Are you acquainted with the rates of freight that were current last year from the ports of the British colonies, to the port of London?—Yes.

State them?—From Quebec to London in 1820, 47s. 6d. to 50s. per load; from the lower ports, 40s. to 42s.

Was the rate of freight from Quebec, generally speaking, nearer 50s. than 47s.?—Nearer to 47s. 6d. than to 50s.; we did business at both prices, but it was done principally at 47s. 6d.

Can you state the current rates of freight during the last season, from the lower ports of the Baltic?—From Memel 20s. to 21s.

Was that about the rate of the average of the lower ports?—Yes, it was.

Can you state the current freights from the lower ports of the North American colonies, to the west coast of the kingdom?—From five to ten shillings a load higher than to London, on account of the different mode of measuring; a different mode of measuring prevails, which makes the nominal freight higher.

Then in point of fact, after making allowance for the difference of measurement, the rates of freight are nearly the same?—I think nearly so; rather less in point of fact to the west coast than to the east.

Then how do you account for the circumstance you have stated, that vessels employed in the trade making on an average only one voyage, or a voyage and a half in a season, carry timber at the same rate of freight as they do where they make two voyages in a season?—If a ship makes a voyage to London, she is more conveniently situated for a voyage to the Baltic, than if she comes to Liverpool, if she comes to the west side of the land she is more conveniently situate to make a second American voyage.

Can you state the current freight of timber from Memel, or other lower ports of the Baltic, to the west coast of the kingdom?—From one-third to a half more than to the east coast.

That fact is within your own knowledge?—Perfectly.

Then the Committee are to understand, that the freight from Memel was from 26s. to 30s. to Liverpool?—Liverpool imports so little from the Baltic, that I should rather say the freight to Bristol was 28s. to 30s.

What relation does the measurement of Bristol bear to that of London?—The difference on American timber, between Bristol and London, will be from 12 to 18 per cent, according as the timber is well or ill squared; from Memel the timber being better squared, the difference will be found to be not above 5, 6, or 7 per cent.

Do you know that the merchants who are in the habit of importing timber from the British provinces of North America have given ship-owners who have applied for freights notice, that if any additional duty is imposed on timber from North America,

---
## Page 91

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

America, they must have a proportionate reduction of freights for the ensuing season?—They have said they will not be able to take any ships at all; they have told me so repeatedly.

Do you happen to know, that in consequence of the expectation of a duty being imposed immediately upon the American timber, the ship-owners raised the price of freight upon the merchants immediately?—On its being understood, that ships would be allowed to make one voyage, the ship-owners immediately attempted to obtain an advance of freight, thinking that a large number of ships would be wanted to make that single voyage; in fact every ship would have gone to sea.

Are you acquainted with the prices of timber in the Liverpool market?—I can give them pretty nearly.

State what was the price of American yellow pine timber three months ago in that market?—It was down as low as fifteen pence per foot.

State what is the present price of such pine?—I think about sixteen to seventeen pence; I know it has improved about a penny or two pence a foot.

You admit an advance of some importance has taken place in that market; to what circumstance do you impute that advance?—To the market being depressed from the extreme importation at one particular moment, and to a sort of re-action which takes place every day.

Is it not, in point of fact, in contemplation of some duty being laid on American timber?—I should not think it is; I am quite certain that in London not an advance to the smallest fraction has taken place on such a speculation.

Is it not a fact, that in the Liverpool market considerable purchases of timber have been made on speculation, with that result in view?—I am not competent to answer that question.

Have you heard that has been the fact in the London market?—I am satisfied that in the London market there has not been an advance of a single fraction; with what secret views purchases have been made, I cannot say.

Have you heard of large purchases in wood being made by dealers lately?—I believe none have been made; I only heard of the purchase of 500 loads, which I thought was purchased with a view to the duty.

You have stated that the causes of the difficulty at present felt by the shipping interest, arise from the demand for transports having ceased, and from more competition existing with foreign countries; are not the Committee to infer from that, that the existing state of the shipping very much much exceeds the natural demands of this country?—If the American trade remained open to us, and we had in other respects a brisk trade, I am satisfied there is not a ship too much in the country, because in 1818, when we had something like briskness in trade, we were rather at a loss to find ships than to find freights; from that time to this it has been always the reverse, but in 1818 I am satisfied we were often at a loss to find ships so quickly as the merchants required them.

Is not the price of freights at the present moment, a proof that at this time the shipping is very much beyond the natural demands of the country?—At the present moment certainly, because we conceive the trade of the country to be very much stagnated at present.

Do you think that the amount of shipping which was employed in transports, and which has ceased to be employed as such, would have been built with a view to the natural demands of the country?—I think the American trade has taken off the whole quantity that was employed for transports. I believe the number of ships in the transport service never exceeded 1,020, and I find that latterly that number has been employed in the American trade, over and above such quantity of tonnage as would have brought the same wood from the north of Europe; therefore I conceive that the American trade has taken off the surplus tonnage arising from the cessation of the demand for transports.

Then how do you account for the depressed state of the freights at present?—Because, as I have said before, trade is very much stagnated at present; if we had ordinary briskness in trade, the American trade remaining to us, I do not believe we should feel that.

Have you any reason to believe that the American trade can be carried on so as to continue to employ its present amount of shipping?—I think the American trade has been overdone within the last year or two, as every other trade has been.

You have stated that the ship-owners are proceeding in that trade, at a loss?—Decidedly.

Is it possible they can continue to go on with that trade at a loss?—They find

---
## Page 92

that their ships get more injury from lying by than from going to sea, and there are a certain number of persons, apprentices and others, employed about a ship, who must be paid whether the ship is employed or not; that the slip goes to sea not only with the prospect of loss, but really with almost certain loss before their faces; they do continually send their ships to sea.

When you spoke of the building of ships in the north of Europe, did you speak of it as applicable to Norway only, or to all the countries in the north of Europe?—I spoke very much with reference to Sweden, which is a large ship-building country.

Not to Prussia or Russia?—Also with reference to Prussia; and having in my view a very recent instance of a ship built in Prussia; with respect to Russia, nothing I apprehend, would tend so much to give her a commercial navy, as encouraging her to build ships in Russian Finland, where they can be built cheaper, perhaps, than in any other part of the world, and very large ships.

Do you know that, some time ago, a certain number of Russian ships were employed in the timber trade, and that in consequence of the cheapness of English ships, they were laid aside?—I know that ships, which had been employed under licences during the war, and were remaining on hand at the return of peace, were attempted to be employed in the wood trade; I believe those ships have ceased to be so employed, and have been disposed of in any way they could be; but I take them to be no criterion for the general state of the country, inasmuch as they were owned, principally in England, and not at all under that sort of economical management, which a ship belonging regularly either to Russia, or elsewhere, has the advantage of.

If the comparative advantages in favour of the ships of the north are so great as has been stated by you, how do you account for the English ships having preserved so great a proportion of the trade as they have?—I can only state the facts, and refer to the official reports, to show that such respective quantities of shipping have arrived in this country; I believe that neither of them are making money.

Be good enough to inform the Committee what quantity of timber in loads was imported from the British North American colonies to this country, in each of the last two years?—Extracting it from the official report presented to the House of Lords, I can give it for 1818 and 1819, if that will answer the purpose; the quantity of timber imported from British North America, in 1818, was 214,102 loads, in 1819, 267,065 loads.

What quantity do you consider may be about the average of each cargo?—Three hundred to three hundred and fifty loads.

Is not the average tonnage of shipping, employed in that trade, nearly 300 tons?—Very nearly, I think, 280 to 300 tons.

Then is not 350 loads a moderate average?—There are many small ships come to the bye-ports.

Is not 350 loads a moderate average to estimate their cargoes by?—Not if you are looking to timber; there will always be at least fifty loads of broken stowage.

Fix what you consider to be a fair average of cargoes?—In the year 1819, there were upwards of 1,500 voyages made in the year; the ships, upon the average of the whole kingdom, do not make more than one and a half voyage in a season. In speaking of the number of ships employed in the transport service, I have stated the extremest number of ships, employed in that service, at 1,020; that was not the number at the conclusion of the war.

How many do you think?—I should think seven or eight hundred.

The Committee are to understand, that your conclusion is founded on your estimate, that the ships so employed do not make above a voyage and a half in a year?—Just so.

Can you form any idea, with respect to the tonnage of the British and foreign shipping bringing timber to this country from Russia?—I have no doubt that nine-tenths of the wood from Russia comes in British ships, and perhaps more; nine-tenths of the whole of the tonnage from Russia is British, by the official reports.

Is it within your knowledge, that in various ports of the country during the last year, ship-building has been continued to a considerable extent?—It is within my knowledge that it has been continued, but not to the same extent as the year before; and it is unavoidable, that, to a certain extent ship-building must go on, or those establishments be entirely broken up and abandoned.

Does the business of a ship-builder require any establishment which would draw him into expense, by abandoning his building for any time, or by reducing his amount of building?—He must always have a number of apprentices; he must always

---
## Page 93

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

always have premises, and always a stock of timber; because he is not merely a ship-builder, but a ship-repairer; and if he could not, while his temporary jobs of repairs are not going on, employ his apprentices, and take off such timber as has not been used for repairing ships, by ship-building, he could not carry on his business.

His apprentices do not cost him much, do they?—I am not practically informed upon that subject; there is a whole establishment which must be kept up; and certainly with respect to the past two years, I do not think a single ship has been sold at a profit to the builder, though he has been obliged to build them.

Have any new ships been introduced into the Canada timber trade, within the last two years?—No, I dare say not; I do not know any instance.

You say you are particularly acquainted with the shipping trade in the ports of North and South Shields, and Sunderland?—Very much so.

Do you know the number of ships engaged from those ports in the Canada trade?—In 1819 there were from North and South Shields, I think 209 ships, entirely engaged in the American trade, large ships.

How many men were employed in navigating those ships?—The number of men and boys employed in those ships in 1819 was 2,900.

You are of opinion, that if any new duties were laid on the Canada timber, a very large proportion of those ships would be laid up, and the ship-owner would be compelled to give up his voyage to Canada?—I am quite satisfied of that.

You are also aware, that there is large ship-building in that river?—Yes.

You are of opinion that a duty imposed on the American timber will tend materially to injure the ship-building trade in that river?—Yes, unavoidably.

Do you mean to state, that any amount of duty laid on the colonial timber would have that effect?—I would beg to say that I think a very small duty on American timber, would have an advantage that would counterbalance the evils to be apprehended; but when I say very small, I lay great stress upon the words very small; I am satisfied it must be very small indeed. I refer to an advantage in respect of improving the quality.

Do you mean 10s. a load?—No, I mean 5s.; but when I find that the freight cannot possibly be lower, but requires an increase, and that the timber now does not leave its cost in the colonies; it is difficult to say where any duty is to be got from; but still I would not conceal that there are some advantages attending a very small duty.

Can you, from your experience as a man of business, suppose it possible that merchants will continue, year after year, a trade which is unprofitable to them?—Certainly not; I apprehend no merchant will continue, year after year, in trade that is unprofitable to him; but a ship owner is in that sort of situation, that even if he sends his ship to sea to lose money, he is obliged to do it from circumstances before stated.

So that you would wish to give to the Committee, as your opinion, that merchants will go on trading to a loss, that ship-owners will go on employing their ships to a loss, and that people will go on building ships to a loss, for any length of time?—Till they are ruined; they have been doing it for the last two years progressively; the ship-owners are so situated, that they cannot help themselves.

What reason have you to suppose that any duty laid on the colonial timber, would not fall upon the consumer of timber?—I mean to say that a duty laid upon colonial timber, which would have the effect of increasing the import from the Baltic, would principally affect the relative import from the two countries, without any particular reference to the consumer.

Are you, therefore, of opinion, that if the colonial timber were 10s. a load higher, the Baltic timber remaining at its present price, the consumption would turn from the colonial timber to the consumption of Baltic timber?—Undoubtedly, to a certain extent.

With a difference of 10s.?—To a certain extent; if it were above that, it would throw the consumption wholly on the Baltic.

What other interests do you apprehend would be affected, besides the shipping interest, by any measures which should throw British shipping out of employment?—In the first place, the Foreign trade, particularly with Russia, by very much decreasing the demand for hemp, flax, and other articles which are consumed, and particularly hemp, wholly by shipping; also the home trade, by diminishing the demand for oak, fir, elm, iron and copper; a ship bound to the British colonies, spends her whole freight either in this country or in the colonies; a ship bound to the Baltic, spends a very small portion of it here, and often goes to sea without taking

J. D. Povesh, Esq.
(22 February.)

---
## Page 94

taking a single cask of Irish provisions; a ship bound to America, will take from eight to ten casks, but a ship to the Baltic, can get everything cheaper as soon as she gets to Elsinore.

Can they get a cask of provisions in Norway?—I am speaking of the Baltic trade; I have no doubt the American trade has occasioned a consumption of some thousand casks of Irish provisions annually, which would not otherwise have been consumed.

From what documents do you get that fact respecting Irish provisions, when going to America?—From my own personal experience; they get it in the port of London, or wherever they fit out, before they go; a ship bound to the Baltic, gets all her groceries and spirits at Elsinore, where she gets them free of duty; there has been about a million of money within the last two or three years paid annually for freight in the colonial trade, the whole of which has been circulated either throughout England, or the colonies.

You have stated, that a considerable number of ships, to the extent of nearly 1,000, were employed in the transport service during the war, and that have since been engaged in the merchant service; were not many of those ships built for that service? and was not the remuneration they received, whilst employed in it, an inducement to the building of those ships, they considering it a compensation for the risk the owners took upon themselves, of not finding beneficial or regular employment for them on the return of peace?—The transport service was very profitable; and I have no doubt many ships were built with a view to it.

Mr. William Wilson, Called in; and Examined.

In what office are you?—Searcher in the Customs at Quebec.

How long have you resided in that part of the world?—I went out there in the beginning of the year 1802, and have resided there till last November, and am about to return.

Are you acquainted with the durability of timber of that country?—I have had a knowledge of a number of buildings of wood of that country; houses that were standing at the time that I arrived in the country, and are standing now; to all appearance I should suppose they had been standing many years prior to my going to the country, and I left them standing, many hundreds.

Have you heard of many houses there being infected with the dry rot?—No, never in my life. I never heard that mentioned in the country.

Has the population of Canada been much increased of late years?—Very much increased, chiefly from the Irish and Scotch emigrants; there have been some English, but chiefly Scotch and Irish.

Has the timber trade furnished considerable employment to those emigrants?—Undoubtedly, a great deal of employment.

In what way?—In cutting, and about the wharfs, and about the town, in different ways.

Do you confine their occupation to the town and its neighbourhood?—No, not a twentieth part; I am told that they are employed up the country, but I have not been up the woods.

Do you know whether they are employed in agriculture, or in the woods?—I am told considerably in both.

Do you happen to know that it is usual to cut by gangs of Americans, who come down for that purpose?—There is a great deal cut by both; an immense number of logs are cut by the Canadian farmers themselves, where there are no Americans employed.

Do you know the fact, that there is wood cut by any Canadian farmer, with a view to exportation?—Yes, a great number by Canadians in the neighbourhood of Quebec.

Did you ever hear of any Scotch and Irish emigrants employed in cutting trees in the forests?—Yes, Scotch and Irish; not that I have seen them, not having been up the country; there is a person of the name of Kennedy, a Scotchman, who brings down immense rafts.

Is Kennedy a farmer or a woodcutter?—He is both a farmer and a lumber merchant; he brings it down from the woods; he cuts it down, and brings it to Quebec; there are a father and three or four brothers.

Do you know anything of the wood from the United States, which comes through Canada?—I have heard it mentioned that there is some oak and pine that comes from the territory of the United States.

---
## Page 95

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Do you know to what amount?—No, I do not; I have not any knowledge of the quantity.

Do you know any means that there is of distinguishing that which comes from the United States, and that cut within our own provinces?—No, I do not; at the present time there is a Mr. Campbell here, who is in the lumber trade at Quebec, and he is a cutter of timber, and also a merchant.

Would the farming population of Canada, who are at present employed in the timber trade, find any other employment, if that trade were discouraged or diminished?—I should suppose an immense number of them would find very great inconvenience, and would suffer very much, because the timber is cut in the winter season, when they have no other employment but that cutting; when there are perhaps six or eight feet of snow on the surface of the earth, it is impossible for them to till land or any thing of that kind; they have nothing to do but to cut the lumber; there is no other occupation they can attend to but that.

Would the inhabitants of Canada have the same means of paying for British manufactures, if their exports of lumber were diminished?—No, certainly not.

Do you think any considerable emigration from the province would be the consequence of any material diminution of that trade?—It is very probable; I think that a great number of people would go to the States; a great number of the Scotch.

Are you not aware that at present the tide of emigration sets very strongly in from the States to Upper Canada?—No, I am not.

Are you acquainted with Upper Canada?—No, I am not much; no further than I have heard mentioned; I have a knowledge that a great number that come to Quebec as emigrants, go through directly into the States.

Does not the timber form a material object of exportation from Quebec?—Yes, it is in fact the staple commodity of the country, that if that trade is cut off, the country will be totally ruined.

Were you acquainted with the country before the existence of that trade, so as to state to the Committee whether those provinces were not then in a state of tolerable prosperity and affluence?—No, it was a very poor place indeed at the time, there were but about 100 ships went there in the course of a year, the country was not a tenth part in affluence what it is now; the province has become a powerful country since that period, since the years 1807 and 1808.

You ascribe that principally to the timber trade?—Yes.

Is it not a natural consequence that it is from the means of that timber trade, that the inhabitants are enabled to derive their supplies from this country?—Yes, I consider it in that light.

Veneris, 23° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

John Diston Powles, Esquire,

Again called in; and further Examined.

ADVERTING to the evidence you gave yesterday, as to the number of voyages made in a season by ships employed in the timber trade to the British colonies in North America, state to the Committee the result of your further investigation upon that subject?—I have looked particularly to the ships which I have had to do with for the years 1819 and 1820, and I have this morning communicated with two or three principal brokers in the same trade for the same purpose, and I find, that of ships coming to the east coast, not more than one in five has made two voyages in a season; to the west coast they most generally make the second voyage; I dare say, perhaps nine out of ten, or nineteen out of twenty, may have done it upon an average; therefore I am satisfied, that when I said yesterday a voyage and a half was made on the average by every ship in the trade, I rather overstated it than understated it.

Those ships that have made only one voyage from the east coast, at what period of the season have they generally sailed from hence?—I speak of ships sailing in the early part of the year, in the month of April. In the very early part of April; the ship owners, especially in the north of England, have found a second voyage...

---
## Page 96

so destructive to the materials of the ship, that they have many of them determined not to make it all, and have, in their club-regulations, discouraged a second voyage being made.

In point of fact, is it not usual, particularly on the west coast, when ships contemplate a second voyage, that they sail from this country early in March, or not later than the middle of that month?—That entirely depends upon the port at which they seek to load; if they go to the bay of Fundy, they may sail in the middle of March to all the other ports; it is almost useless attempting to sail before the last week in March, or the first of April.

Do you include Miramichi?—I include Miramichi; the timber is seldom ready there for ships sailing before the first of April.

Then it would appear, it is more from an apprehension of expense, or disasters attending a second voyage, that ships are deterred from attempting it from the east coast, than that the time from the middle of March to the conclusion of the season would not, in the ordinary course of events, be sufficient for that purpose?—It is partly from the first of those causes, and partly from the second.

Do you not consider that ships sailing from the port of London in March, for the North American colonies, and meeting with ordinary dispatch there, and the same on the return here, have sufficient time in the ordinary course of events to perform two voyages?—I will state, that from actual experience in the years 1819 and 1820, there were not more than from 150 to 200 ships returned to Great Britain from their first voyage, before the first of September, which is full as late as a ship ought to sail, to make her second voyage safely and conveniently.

Can you speak to when the ships that arrived after the first of September departed from this country?—I can only say, generally, that they departed at the usual time. I take the course of the trade for two years.

Then the Committee are to understand, it is a more convenient arrangement for ship owners trading with the North American colonies from the east coast of the country to make one American and one Baltic voyage than two American?—Not only more convenient, but in very many cases it is utterly impracticable to make two.

On the west coast it suits them better to make two American voyages than to adopt any other system?—Certainly.

Can you inform the Committee what proportion of the timber of North America is brought to the markets on the east coast, and what proportion to the markets on the west coast of the kingdom, including Ireland in the last description?—I am not at present competent to answer that question.

Do not ships belonging to the eastern ports principally carry on the trade between America and the western ports of this kingdom?—They do so to a large extent; the Shields and Whitby ships constantly return to Liverpool.

Has the present system of duties on wood had the effect of decreasing the import from Russia?—I apprehend not, because I find there has been a progressive increase in the import of wood from Russia, which I am prepared to show to the Committee. I have extracted from the Lords Report the whole import from Russia, and brought it all into loads, for the more convenient observation of the Committee, and I find, that from 1800 to 1806, several years before the present system of wood duties was adopted, and during which the intercourse with Russia was perfectly free (I have carried it no further, because in 1807 the passage of the Sound was interrupted) the average import from Russia was 105,344 loads. I have then taken it from 1814 to 1819, and I find the average for those years to be 121,837 loads; and I find the import for the last three years, 1817 to 1819, to be greater than that of any preceding three years, 1818 and 1819 being also two years in which the largest import took place from the British colonies.

The first of those comparisons is between a state of peace and war?—In war we import generally from Russia for government, which demand has almost totally ceased now.

In the statement now made by you, is the import for government in the preceding years included?—Certainly in both cases.

How long has it ceased?—During the peace the import for government is comparatively very small; there is some.

Are you aware of any objection to such an alteration of the present duties as should have the effect of decreasing the import of deals from Russia, and increasing it from Norway?—As respects shipping, nothing I apprehend could be more fatal.

What other objection do you see?—The other objection I apprehend to be, that Russia is an infinitely better customer to this country than Norway.

---
## Page 97

Do you apprehend that the same injurious consequences would ensue to British shipping if the duty on Baltic timber were reduced, as if a duty to the same extent were imposed on timber from the British colonies to the north of Europe?—If the duty on timber from the north of Europe were reduced, I apprehend the consequences to British shipping would be beyond all comparison more injurious than by laying a duty on American timber.

What extent of reduction of duty do you contemplate in that answer?—I believe that any reduction would produce that effect; the greater the reduction, of course the more decided and the more instantaneous the consequences.

Do not you believe, that any small reduction of the duty on Baltic timber would have the effect of raising the price of it in the country to the same extent?—I think that a small reduction of the duty on Baltic timber would have the effect of raising the price of it abroad, but would, at the same time, lead to such an abundant consignment of it to this country as would continue the present depressed state of the market, and be bad in its result both for themselves and the colonies.

Would not the demand depend upon the consumption in this country?—That has not been found to be the case in the wood trade for the last three or four years.

Has not that been found the case as referable to that part of it which comes from the Baltic?—Certainly that has not been the case with respect to that from the north of Europe, for the Norwegians will send here at all events if they find the least opening to do so.

Do you think that some arrangement of the duties for the protection of British shipping might have the effect of preventing any possible injury that might arise from an additional facility of import from Norway?—There would still be the circumstance before pointed out, of the capacity to bring the wood in so much smaller a proportion of tonnage than from the colonies.

In other respects it might be effected?—In other respects it is practicable of course.

Are you acquainted with the state of the timber trade of Memel?—Only generally.

Can you inform the Committee, what was the current shipping price of timber there during the last season; good timber?—The price of middling timber in the last season at Memel, I think, was from 25s. to 28s. a load.

Are you aware that the shippers of timber at Memel, have intimated to their correspondents, that they cannot afford to supply timber for the present year, but at an advance of 5s. per load?—They have offered it for this year at 30s.; and generally as to the Prussian ports, they have recommended their correspondents to buy early, as, if the duty were reduced, an immediate advance of price would take place there.

To what circumstance do you impute the advance that has already taken place?—To the former price being too low.

Do you mean lower than the shipper could purchase, or afford to supply it?—Of that I cannot possibly judge, I can merely state the facts; if the price last year were 26s. to 28s., their now asking 30s. is a proof they thought it then too low.

Then in point of fact, whether a reduction in the duty on Baltic timber should take place in this country or not, the price for the present season is likely to be higher, particularly at the port of Memel during the present year than last?—All I can say is, they are asking more, and will get it if they can.

An advance in the price could only arise from an increased demand?—Of course; if the duty is lowered there would be more wanted, and that would immediately cause an increase of price, the effect of which would be just to put into the foreign merchants pocket the difference of duty.

Admitting that the trade with Sweden and Norway is dependent upon the control of Great Britain, inasmuch as it chiefly consists in a trade of import, what evils in your opinion might be calculated from such duties being laid on goods imported in foreign ships, as might give a decided preference to the British flag?—There is no evil in laying a duty on foreign ships as regards British shipping, certainly on the contrary, it is in so far a protection to the British shipping.

Are there any other evils that might result from such duty being laid, in your contemplation?—As far as regards Sweden, almost every thing British is prohibited at present, and therefore we can hardly be upon a worse footing with that country; as far as respects Norway, our exports to it are so inconsiderable, that I cannot contemplate any evil worth consideration.

Therefore it would be your opinion, that it would be good policy to confine our import

---
## Page 98

import trade with Sweden and Norway, to our own ships?—That is a large question; as to general policy, it would be decidedly advantageous to British shipping.

You have stated that you are of opinion, if a protecting duty is laid on timber imported in ships of Norway and Sweden to this country, so as to give preference to British ships, it would be productive of benefit to British shipping, might it not be expected in such case, that a countervailing duty would be laid on the shipment of such timber by British ships in Sweden and Norway?—No; because their great object is to sell their timber.

You think they would submit to it?—I think they would.

Mr. David Bellhouse; Called in; and Examined.

WHAT is your business?—Generally a builder and timber merchant and carpenter, at Manchester.

How long have you been engaged in business there?—Three and thirty years.

During that period, have you been accustomed to make use of Baltic timber, and also American yellow and red pine timber?—Up to within the last twelve or fifteen years we had not made use of much American yellow-pine; since that, we have made use of a great deal of it.

Have you, in the course of your experience, had opportunities for ascertaining the durability of each of those kinds of timber, after they have been used?—With respect to American pine, it has not been long enough in use, I fancy, to ascertain; I have never seen any that has been at all injured by time.

For what purposes have you been in the habit of using the American yellow-pine?—The general business, what it was first used for in Manchester, was cotton machinery; and after that it obtained almost universally for packing cases; and then it is used for building a great deal in cottages almost wholly, and in middling buildings it is used frequently for small scantlings, on account of its being freer from knots; we conceive it not quite so strong, but approaching very nearly, because a large knot in the Baltic timber would break a small scantling, when it would not be an object in a large one; the Baltic timber being more knotty than the American pine, we can get smaller scantlings out, to be as strong or thereabouts.

By small scantling, is the Committee to understand that you mean joists?—I mean joists, and single rafters, and ceiling joists.

Is it the practice to introduce the ends of such joists into the walls?—Yes.

Have you had an opportunity of observing whether, in such cases, the appearance of dry rot has been found to exist?—I have never known an instance of it.

Have you known the existence of dry rot in the use of any other description of timber?—I have, in the Baltic timber, both Dantzie and Meuel.

Will you mention the particular instances to which you refer?—The first, which was several years ago, was in the cellar floor, what we call a ground floor, of a house of the first class, built by me, and it was a sandy situation, and the ground is very nearly as high as the floor, but not quite; it appeared that air had been in that particular instance excluded, by making a potatoe cellar; there was no air got into that place, in consequence of which, the beam-end rotted; that was very good Dantzie timber.

Do you know how long that timber had been in?—I should think nearly forty years.

Was it the dry rot?—I think not; we cut it off, and I believe it has not gone further.

The question had a particular reference to dry rot?—With respect to dry rot, it is very little known; I think it appeared to me to be rot coming from damp and want of air, and by no means the disease called the dry rot; though I have seen the dry rot, where it has taken one side of a house, and the other side has been completely free from it.

What appearance, in your opinion, does the dry rot assume?—There is always a fungus grows upon it.

Have you known any instance of dry rot being found where no timber had been used?—I have; there is an instance where I found the dry rot in a wall which had been an outside wall for thirty years, and when we broke into it, it had strings of this fungus, like little bits of leather, running all along in the joints; we did not know at that time it was the dry rot; we built a dining-room to the end of the house; in twelve months, the ends of the timbers were decayed, and we found it was the dry rot, and it has kept spreading ever since.

---
## Page 99

What kind of timber did you introduce?—Dantzig.

Are you then of opinion, that for the purposes of house-building, which you have enumerated, the yellow pine of America is equally durable, and as eligible as that of the Baltic?—With respect to being equally durable I have no means of ascertaining, except from not having been called in or seen any decayed; as to its eligibility, it is very eligible for small buildings; I think the prejudice in favour of Baltic would be a very long time in subsiding, supposing it was as good, because people who have been used to the use of Baltic do not like to use American pine, in a great measure from prejudice, but not altogether so; were I going to build a good house, I should not advise any but small scantlings of American to be used.

As American pine has been more generally introduced, has that prejudice been diminishing?—I rather think it has.

Can you inform the Committee, what quantity of timber is annually used in Manchester, for the purpose of making packing-chests and boxes?—As far as I can form an opinion about it, I think about 500,000 feet at least, for packing-boxes in Manchester, in a year.

Is that wholly of American pine, or part American and part Baltic?—There is very little Baltic, if any; there is a little white deal from the Baltic used sometimes; but that has been when it has approached very near; it was very low once last year.

Will you state what is the present price of good American yellow pine, in the Liverpool market?—The best yellow American pine is eighteen pence.

State the current price of good Dantzig or Memel timber?—That is half a crown in Liverpool.

Is the demand for the last considerable at that price?—It has not been considerable of late on account of there being very little building going on in Lancashire; there have been no heavy buildings going on, generally speaking; therefore the demand is not so good as it used to be.

What reduction, in the price of Baltic timber, would lead to a considerable increase in the consumption?—I cannot say, I am sure; where people want Baltic timber, I think they have it without reference exactly to the price, where the difference comes within what it is now.

If a reduction of sixpence a foot was to take place, would that lead, in your judgment, to a material increase in the consumption?—For what Baltic timber is used for, I do not think that would make much difference, except that it might be used for cases or common things; where people would so use Baltic timber, they will use it in public jobs, and things of that sort.

Is it in your recollection, at any former period, that the difference in price between the two descriptions of timber was materially less than it is at present?—In the time of the war they were both a vast deal higher; American pine I have known as high as 4s. 6d. when the Baltic was 5s. 6d. and 6s.

Was the consumption of American pine considerable at that period?—No, nothing like what is now.

Was that because the supply was small, or from the want of demand?—From the want of demand, in a great measure; and I conceive that the exportation trade has so very much improved in Manchester, within the last seven or eight years, that there is more than twice as much used in the packing-cases.

Can you inform the Committee, what proportion the consumption of American pine with Baltic timber bear to each other in Manchester, at present?—I can only form a guess; with respect to my own consumption, we use about 100,000 feet of timber a-year, and I should think 10 to 15,000 a-year is Baltic; but that always depends upon the public jobs or heavy jobs we have; sometimes we use twice or three times as much as at other times. Of late, heavy jobs have been very scarce in the county.

Have you made any experiments on the comparative strength of Baltic and American pine timber for bearing?—Yes, I have.

State what proportion you find the one to bear to the other?—There is a vast deal of difference in American pine itself, but I think the average is about four to five.

That is, five of Baltic is equal to six of American?—Yes, or rather under that.

Is the Baltic timber more equal in strength than that of the American colonies?—Yes, much more.

Inform the Committee what the common scantling, or size of the American timber is, from the lowest to the highest?—The lowest is down to as low as twelve inches.

---
## Page 100

inches, which is the Picton, and the St. John's runs up to two feet; but we call the best size from sixteen to twenty inches.

Have you had occasion to find that the large timber has in some instances been approaching to or showing a system of decay?—We find loss in all sizes more or less, and we always consider there is more risk in the timber of large scantlings, than of the small, but I think that is rather more in this way, that a large log, if it proves bad, is a larger loss than a small one; I do not know that there are more rotten logs in the large than in the small, but the large logs are more apt to be shaken at the heart than the small are.

Do you ever find the logs rotten in the centre?—Yes, we do.

Without any outward appearance?—Sometimes without any outward appearance, but not often; we can generally see something in the end which indicates age.

Does it imply it to be timber of older growth?—We look to the size; with respect to the growth, we apprehend that the largest is the oldest.

Have you any means of ascertaining the difference?—No; we have a saw mill, worked by steam, and we find an amazing difference in the sawing of it; sometimes a log of twenty inches square will stop the engine, when another time it would cut two such logs; merely from the toughness and badness of it, its being very hard and knotty and rough.

Do you ever see any spruce fir?—Yes, we get that sometimes.

Of what quality is that?—The American spruce is very bad.

Do you mean the Quebec?—I mean the spruce from New Brunswick; it is very cross, and after it is sawn into boards, and put on the horse to dry, it goes into cross shakes.

Have you observed the same result on spruce from Canada?—I think there is no spruce comes from Canada, except in deals; in deals it is very good, but I speak of balk.

Are the spruce deals which come from Canada of as good a quality as the yellow pine deals?—We should not consider them so good as the yellow; they always fetch rather a less price.

You have mentioned that American pine is generally used in Manchester for making chests and packing boxes for all packages; is it not found to be rather soft, and that the nails do not hold?—No, we think it holds nails as well as the other timber; but we do not depend upon the nails, we have hoops round, we use a great deal of iron in hoops now, round the ends.

Are you acquainted with the quality of the red pine of Quebec?—Yes.

What opinion do you entertain of it?—It is very good; the red pine of Quebec, if it was as well manufactured, would be very nearly equal to the Baltic, but it is not well manufactured.

What do you mean by its not being well manufactured?—It is not squared both ends alike like the Quebec; it is left round at the angles; it does not convert so well; we have a great deal more loss in sawing it up.

What advance in price above yellow pine does it bear in the Liverpool market?—There is a great difference in the quality; Quebec would be from twenty-two to twenty-four pence.

Are you acquainted with the red pine of New Brunswick?—Yes, the Miramichi.

What opinion do you entertain of it?—The Miramichi red pine is very good, but it is still worse manufactured than the Canada, and a vast deal more variety in it, some exceedingly sappy, and other very good.

The St. John's?—We consider that pretty nearly equal to Miramichi; but there are some other countries that are still worse.

Have you been in the habit of using the yellow pine timber for floors in any situation, either of houses or warehouses?—It has not been in good buildings recommended for floors; we have never used it for floors, except for inferior buildings.

In warehouses?—No, not in warehouses.

You have stated, that the present price of good American pine, such as Miramichi, and St. John's, and Quebec is eighteen pence a foot in the Liverpool market; can you inform the Committee what was the current price for such timber in November and December last?—It was sixteen pence; rather under if anything.

To what circumstances do you impute this advance?—It is pretty evident to me, that the advance is in consequence of an expected duty, the speculators going into the market, and others holding there is an immense stock, and yet there has always been but little on sale; there is a double stock, or was at Christmas.

Should a duty be laid equal to the advance, may that advance be expected to be maintained?—

---
## Page 101

maintained?—I should think it would; unless parties that are holding quantities are obliged to bring it into the market, that would drop it.

In that case the duty would fall on the consumer, would it not?—Yes.

Supposing no duty was to be laid as expected, would that advance in price, in your opinion, be maintained?—I think there is no doubt it would lower directly.

Would it in such case, in your opinion, fall back to the former price?—Yes, probably they may be losing money by it; but I think the quantity in the market would bring it back to the former price.

Are you acquainted with the extent of the importation of American pine timber to Liverpool in the last year?—Yes.

What might that extent be?—I am not certain; I think it was about three million of feet.

Are you acquainted with the extent of the present stock in Liverpool?—At Christmas it was, with what was expected to come in, about 2,500,000.

In point of fact, has 500,000 come in since?—No; but I should think at Christmas there was 2,000,000 in stock.

In your opinion, what may be the extent of the present stock?—I can hardly say it has been reduced since more than the import, but not much.

At what would you estimate it?—I should think at present it must be from fifteen to eighteen hundred thousand, but I cannot speak to that positively.

Do you know whether the quantity sold during the last year was equal to the quantity imported?—I think it was not; there is timber in Liverpool two or three years old now.

Would not that timber have been kept at all events for seasoning?—No; it is worse every year by at least a penny a foot.

Is that the case, generally, in yellow pine timber from America?—Yes, it is worse; if there are both old and new, the new will always be bought at nearly a penny a foot more than they will give for the old.

By what period of the present year may a further supply of American timber be expected?—Early in July, we used to reckon the 10th; but it was last year the first, and it is expected the first this year.

Then, in your opinion, there is now seven months supply of timber in the Liverpool market?—I should think more.

And a period of nearly five months must elapse before a supply can be received; therefore it is probable there will not be a surplus of above three months supply, when a fresh importation takes place?—I should think about that, supposing the same demand to be continued.

What is your idea, looking at the circumstances of the times, of the probable demand that may be expected for this timber?—Except that I can see there is a great deal doing in our country, I should think things looked very bad, that the demand would fall off; there has lately been considerable demand, but it was contrary to my opinion.

What do you mean by a great deal doing?—I mean that there is a vast deal of goods sending off a great deal of shipping, and the trade of Manchester strikes me to be reviving; but it is only from facts I see that I judge.

Do you allude to building?—No, not building, but the trade of the town; we have been making more cases; the town generally seems to be more alive; but one part of the trade is exceedingly bad, that is the spinning part, the cotton part, and it appears to me very odd the thing should go on so well as it does.

Is the building at all suspended, or the use of timber for the building of houses?—There are immense quantities of cottages building; but no general good houses building.

In this cottage-building, the American timber is principally used?—It is.

Are you of opinion, that as far as relates to the present stock in hand, the holders of that stock would not lose by the imposition of the expected duty?—I fancy, the holders of the stock have no idea that their present stock is to have the duty imposed upon it.

Then they would gain by it?—Yes.

Supposing any alteration in the duties to take place, so to reduce the difference now existing between the prices of American and Baltic timber very considerably, what effect, in your opinion, would be produced by that alteration in the consumption of American timber?—I consider, that if the American pine should approach any where near the other, there would be a very great diminution in the use of it.

Do you suppose, that an increased demand for the red-pine timber in this country,

---
## Page 102

would be likely to promote an improvement in the manufacture of the article in the country in which it grows?—I cannot say, I am sure.

If it were to come into this country in an improved state of manufacture, would it be more capable of meeting the timber from the Baltic?—It would.

Mr. Charles Augustus Dalmer; Called in; and Examined.

IN what business are you?—An agent for James Moir and company, of Memel.

In what line of business are they engaged?—They are chiefly engaged in the timber trade.

How long have you known the trade of Memel?—I have known the trade of Memel several years; but chiefly from my having been engaged in houses in this country, which have been in that trade.

Since what year?—Since the year 1805.

Has the timber trade of Memel increased or decreased much since that period?—It has decreased very considerably.

Since what year has it decreased?—The decrease has been most evident since the end of the war, since the conclusion of peace, since 1813 or 1814.

To what do you attribute that decrease?—To the decline of demand for this country.

Have the prices in Memel fallen since the year 1818?—They have fallen most considerably since the year 1818; there was a temporary advance of price in the year 1818, since that time, again, they have fallen very considerably.

Can you state the prices in 1813 and 1814?—I cannot.

Can you state the price of timber in Memel, in 1817?—I believe, to the best of my recollection, the price might have been about 33s. in 1817, per 50 cubic feet; but I would not be positive as to that.

What did it rise to in 1818?—About 45s. I believe; I cannot be positive; but that is so far as I can recollect.

What is the present price of timber in Memel?—Thirty shillings; and, I believe, as low as 25s. last year.

Has the diminution of the timber trade at Memel been, in any degree, owing to any regulations which have taken place at Memel?—No.

Have any regulations of any kind taken place at Memel, regarding the exportation of timber, since the period you have known Memel?—Yes.

What are those regulations, and to what extent have they gone?—Those regulations have been, that the Prussian government have taken off the whole of the export-duty, and the Russian government have reduced the frontier duty, which was about six per cent, to about 3 per cent.

Are you acquainted otherwise with the trade of the port of Memel?—I have a general knowledge of the trade.

Have the exportations from that country increased or decreased?—Decreased.

In what extent?—That I am not so well able to say, as a person who has been in Memel during that period personally.

Mr. Jacob Hagen; Called in; and Examined.

IN what occupation are you?—A merchant, in the stave-trade generally.

You are not a broker in staves?—No; an importer and dealer.

You do not employ staves for any purpose?—I do not manufacture staves.

What is the duty at present existing upon Quebec staves?—Twenty-five shillings per 1,000.

Is that duty levied upon all staves indiscriminately, whatever may be their breadth or thickness?—Exceeding 50 inches, it is 1l. 5s.; not exceeding, 12s. 6d.

The 50 inches is the length of the stave?—No; they are divided into four different descriptions.

The length only is estimated?—The length only, without reference to the thickness.

Then a stave twice the thickness of another, pays the same duty?—Yes, it does up to a certain point; I believe three inches and a half; but supposing it only three quarters of an inch thick, it would pay the same duty.

What duty is paid on foreign staves?—Upon Baltic pipe staves (we take them generally,

---
## Page 103

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Generally, the others are in proportion by a British ship) 47l. 18s. 4d. and in proportion for other staves.

Has the importation of Baltic staves much decreased of late years?—Yes, it has.

In what proportion?—I really cannot answer the proportion, but it has very considerably decreased.

Has the importation of staves from Canada very much increased?—They did very much increase at first, but from the decreased demand, they have not I think for the last year or two very much increased.

Was that increased demand at first occasioned by the state of the war, when we could not obtain staves from the countries in the Baltic?—I think it was in some measure.

Was not it principally so increased by the demand for the government, and the victualling office?—The heavy duty on Baltic produce was not laid on till we had got over the worst of it; till the continent was in some degree open again, so that it had not the operation it would have had at an earlier period; the ports have been open ever since.

Are you acquainted with the difference in freight and charges in bringing Canada staves to market, and bringing staves from the Baltic?—This year particularly, there have been very few staves come any otherwise than as broken stowage; and on account of the demand being very small, government not being customers as they used to be, they have come at the very moderate freight of fifteen to eighteen pounds per thousand.
