From what country?—From Canada.

What has the freight been from the Baltic?—From 11l. to 13l.

What has the freight been usually of staves from Canada?—Staves on freight would be 30l. with five per cent premium, 31l. 10s.

What would staves be from the Baltic in the same way, on freight?—From 11l. to 13l.

Is the quality of the Canada stave as good as that from the Baltic?—It is not esteemed so good, it is not used for particular purposes, such as for oil, and for beer particularly, the brewers will not have a Canada stave in their casks if they knew it.

Is it not as much used in casks for wine?—I think it has been used as much for spirits, but not so much for wine; but for spirits it is approved, and it is thought not to absorb so much of the spirit as the Baltic stave does.

In what respect do you conceive the Baltic stave to be superior to the Canada stave for the purposes of the brewer?—For all purposes; I should think the Canada stave is more liable to decay, it does not last to any period except for spirits; the spirits I understand, appear to nourish it, but for any other purpose it decays sooner; for small casks, if persons want cheap casks, they will have Canada, but they decay sooner.

What is the difference of price between Canada staves, and Baltic staves, in the market?—The Canada stave, the standard stave of an inch and a half thick, is worth about 77l. 10s. to 80l.

What is the price of the Dantzie staves?—The Dantzie and Baltic staves are worth about 140l. but then when we speak of Quebec staves, we take them at the standard of an inch and half; now to bring them up to the size of Baltic staves, for since this increase of duty the Baltic merchants abroad have sent the stoutest staves, stouter than they used to send them, to bring them up as near the limitations as they can, that we get now very few slight staves from the Baltic; therefore to make an equal quantity of timber, it would be necessary to take the two-inch Quebec staves, which two-inch Quebec staves will be as large, and take up as much room, be as bulky as the finest and largest Baltic pipe staves.

What is the price of two-inch Quebec staves in the market?—£96.

Are they selling at 96l.?—Yes.

What proportion do the two-inch Canada staves bear to the proportion of one and a half generally sold in the market?—One fifth at Canada, and one fifth freight for every half inch.

How long has the present duty of 47l. 18s. 4d. existed?—Since the year 1813, when twenty-five per cent was added.

What was the duty previous to that?—In round numbers twenty pounds; then in 1810, it was doubled to forty pounds; and then the remainder put on.

If it was proposed by this Committee to recommend an alteration in the duties upon staves, either by imposing in part a duty on Canada staves, or by deducting 186.

---
## Page 104

from the present duty on Baltic staves, so as to place the staves coming from both countries on a fair footing of competition; how do you think that duty ought to be regulated, to what extent ought the alteration to take place?—To put them on a footing, I think a duty of something like twenty-three pounds should be taken off from the Baltic staves; what you choose to put on more as a bonus to the Canada trade is so much the better for them.

You conceive at the difference of duty between the existing duty and the duty you recommend, that the trade with Canada would still exist, although twenty-three pounds was deducted from the present duty?—I think the trade with Canada would not exist to the extent it does now, with so large a duty as that.

To what extent might an alteration take place in the duties without interfering with the consumption of each article in the market?—I think it might be done in three ways; either fifteen pounds taken off the Baltic, or fifteen pounds put on the Canada, or seven pounds ten taken off the Baltic, and seven pounds ten put on the Canada; it matters not which way.

Upon what grounds do you make that calculation?—Because the Canadians would be benefited by ten pounds a thousand; I think three or four and twenty pounds, or some such sum, would make it exactly even.

Do you calculate the difference of freight in making that estimate?—Yes, I do.

Do you conceive, that the heavy duty upon Baltic staves, is a great impediment in the way of the coopers employed in making casks for exportation, as no drawback is allowed upon staves when manufactured?—For the Baltic staves, it certainly is very material; it does not make much difference to the Canada staves, because there is no duty on them; supposing the duty was laid on the Canada staves, it appears to me, that a drawback on the cask might be taken off, equal to that duty, without reference to whether the cask was made out of Canada or Baltic, because the Baltic having paid a larger duty, would only have the same drawback; and if that principle was admitted, I think a very nice calculation might be made, so as to take it off each sort of cask.

What difference do you think it would make in the consumption of staves generally, taking off or laying on any duty; do you think it would increase?—I should think it would.

What are the grounds for your opinion?—I can hardly answer that question, without a little more consideration what the operation of it would be.

Do you think that the same quantity of pipes or casks would be manufactured in this country for the use of the wine countries, if an additional duty was laid on the American staves, which would add to the cost of the manufactured article?—I should think it doubtful, whether there would or not.

On the contrary, do you think that if a duty was imposed on Canada staves, which would increase the price of the cask in this country, that the wine countries might be supplied direct from the States of America with the casks now supplied from Great Britain?—As far as I am a judge, I think, from our ingenuity, and the way in which we get up our casks, we could work cheaper than they could, notwithstanding that.

As much cheaper as to countervail any duty?—Yes, as far as my opinion goes, not being myself a manufacturer.

You suppose, that in the present state of the cooping trade, additional duties imposed upon it would not in the least interfere with its prosperity?—No, not on the Canada staves; I think it would not.

Is there now a larger consumption of Baltic staves in the market, or of Canadian staves?—I think they must be very nearly alike; I should think of Canada rather the most, but not quite equal to the importation.

You are of opinion from your experience, that such an alteration of the duties, would be a general facility to the coopers?—Yes, and to all of us, as far as I am able to judge; I do not think that would be felt by the coopers.

You are a dealer in staves, buying from the merchant, and selling to the manufacturer?—I am an importer of staves, not much of Canada staves, but in some measure so.

If a duty were laid on the Canada staves, would it not prove a direct tax upon trade, for so much as casks are used for packages for exportation?—It would make the casks come to as much more certainly.

---
## Page 105

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Mr. Thomas Simson; Called in; and Examined.

IN what line of business are you engaged?—I am at present a broker in the timber trade.

You are acquainted of course with the qualities of timber in the different countries?—I consider that I am.

State the order of their value?—The timber from Riga and Dantzig certainly is the best; next follows that from Memel; then the Swedish and Norway timber; the latter in point of quality is inferior to none; but from its smallness in size, and the bad way in which it is squared, its value is reduced considerably; then the timber from North America, of which the red pine is considered the most superior, but very little of that comes, compared with the quantity of yellow pine.

Have you found timber from the British provinces in North America, peculiarly subject to the dry rot?—I am aware that such is the impression on the minds of many men; I have not perhaps sufficient experience in the Canada trade to form a correct judgment on that, but I believe all timber to be subject more or less to dry rot, more particularly if improperly treated. I imported a large cargo of timber and deals from Norway in 1815, nearly the whole of which was destroyed by dry rot; a circumstance that is still fresh in the minds of many men now in the timber trade; Mr. George Norman was agent for the parties who shipped it, and he was so convinced of the bad state of the cargo, that he made me an allowance of £600 off the invoice; I have also met with many instances, where Norway ships have been dreadfully infected with the dry rot. I sold two last year, one not more than six years old, and the other not more than nine, the timbers of both of which were entirely destroyed by the dry rot; I have a sample of the timber now, and I am convinced that the bad state of the timber of those ships, arose from the improper treatment it had received at the time the ships were built.

Is the prejudice which you say exists against North American timber in that respect increasing, or is it decreasing?—I certainly think it is decreasing, and that the consumption of the article becomes more general.

What circumstances have led you to that opinion?—The general demand there is for it in our market, more than there was sometime since, as well as the conversation I have had with different builders on the subject. The Commercial Dock Company have lately erected a very large building, entirely of American timber, I believe, under the superintendence of an eminent surveyor; and many of our most respectable timber merchants and builders are in the habit of purchasing large quantities of it. The house of Copland, Holland and Rowles, to my knowledge, purchased many hundred loads of pine timber last year, besides large quantities of deals.

Are there many purposes for which the timber from North America is considered to answer, as well as the timber from the north of Europe?—Undoubtedly there are many.

State them?—I consider it to be adapted for the general purposes of building. There are great quantities used in agricultural buildings; and, from the large quantities that go from London, as well as from Liverpool, to the manufacturing and other districts, I conclude that the use of it has now become general in most parts of the kingdom. There are also very large quantities cut up for fire wood in the Metropolis, and round its suburbs; the consumption of fire wood is immense; now, that ship-building has so much fallen off in the port of London, if it was not for that, I cannot tell where the fire wood would come from, to supply the inhabitants of the Metropolis.

Do you mean by fire wood those small logs of wood that are carried about in carts?—Yes; the quantity is immense; the cutting of that wood employs numerous poor families in the winter months.

Are the deals from North America considered to answer for many purposes, as well as the deals from the north of Europe?—Most assuredly.

State for what purposes?—They are used for floorings of buildings; the box-makers use vast quantities of them for packing-cases; the coopers also use great quantities for heading for dry casks; the turners and brushmakers also use great quantities of them.

Are you aware of any circumstance to which the falling off of our timber trade with Norway is to be attributed, besides the extension of the trade with the British provinces of North America?—So many other channels having been opened of late years,

---
## Page 106

years, from whence deals are now brought, to what there formerly were, it is not more than twenty years ago since deals were brought from Memel and Petersburgh in no other way than merely by the way of stowage, but now we have considerable quantities from thence, many large cargoes annually; and it is a much less time since deals were brought, in very small quantities, from Gottenburgh and many Swedish ports, from whence there come now large quantities; and on the other hand, the use of Norway produce is discontinued in the naval department altogether, with the exception of a few small spars. Formerly the Navy Board used scarcely any thing else but Norway goods; I have been myself in the habit of supplying them with hand-masts (small masts); but now they will not have them at any price; they consider that they get better supplied from the Russian and Prussian ports, as well as from Canada.

Have you ever resided in Norway?—I have; altogether I believe about three years, or a little more.

At what period of time?—I was last there in 1815.

Did you travel much through the country?—I travelled a great deal in it; I believe there are few Englishmen who have seen so much of Norway as I have.

What appeared to be the state of the population of that country?—In 1806 it was stated, I think, to be 725,000, but when I was last there, I was led to understand, that the population had decreased, which I have every reason to believe was true; for in 1808 and 1809 I was there myself; many thousands died for the want of food, occasioned by the blockade of their coast at that time.

Is there reason to expect, from what you have seen of that country, that any increased importation of timber from thence, would lead to a larger exportation of British-manufactured goods from this country?—I do not conceive it would.

State your reasons for that opinion?—They certainly might take a few more articles than they do at present, but they do not stand in need of so much from this country as they were formerly in the habit of taking, in consequence of having many manufactories recently established there.

Of what description are those manufactories?—They have established a woollen cloth manufactory; they have paper mills, glass-houses, and they have a wire and several nail manufactories; some of which are conducted by gentlemen from this country; they have a great many distilleries, and several other manufactories of minor consideration.

Do they generally use their own woollen manufactures?—Generally they do now, except their fine cloths; they make their coarse cloths now nearly equal to our second cloth in this country, but they are still deficient in the art of dying.

Can the Norwegian ships, in your opinion, navigate at lower rates of freight than British ships?—Unquestionably they can.

In what respects, and for what reasons?—The manner in which the seamen are fed and paid, and the very low rate at which they can build their ships.

Can you state the proportion of Norwegian-built and British-built ships that came to this port from Norway last year?—I think we had not more than one-sixth of British tonnage in the port of London last year from Norway.

You have not the exact proportions?—I have not; but there were sixty-two sail of ships, only eleven British; and I think the tonnage is something less than one-sixth.

Do you know whether they have any coal in Norway?—They use charcoal.

Do they use charcoal in their manufactories?—Yes; their offal timber is consumed chiefly in making charcoal.

Do they make charcoal from fir timber?—Yes, from the slabs and tops of the trees and branches.

Has not the excessive duty on European timber operated with peculiar severity on the small timber of Norway?—No, I do not consider that the duty on Baltic timber has operated against the Norway timber.

You do not ascribe any part of the distress that has existed of late years in Norway, to the heavy duties on timber?—I consider the distress to have originated in consequence of the neglect of agriculture.

Was it not considered, some years ago, that the Norwegians derived a very great profit from their trade in timber to this country?—The prices of timber in Norway were more than double what they are now; the first cargo of deals I bought in Norway in 1805, I gave £24 per hundred for the best red deals; they are not now worth more than £10.

---
## Page 107

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Were not the people in a state of comparative riches at that time?—They enjoyed the whole monopoly of the deal trade at that time; there were very few deals came from any other country.

Mention the contents of the hundred you speak of?—One hundred and twenty pieces of twelve feet-three by nine inches.

If Norway were in that state of opulence you have described it to have been in some years ago, do you not think it would still import various commodities from this country, although it might not import those commodities which you now state it acquires from its own manufactures?—They would immediately forsake agriculture, and with the produce of the mountains would buy corn abroad, rather than cultivate the valleys to obtain it at home; such has been the conduct of the Norwegians for ages past, and which I consider as the chief cause of their present wretched state.

When you state that those sixty vessels which came here from Norway last year, arrived in a so much larger proportion than the number of British vessels employed in that trade, are you of opinion that those sixty vessels were employed in a profitable trade?—I should conceive that the Norwegians thought it profitable, otherwise they would not have sent them.

From your particular acquaintance with that country, you are of opinion that the Norwegian ships are now employed very beneficially for the ship-owners of that country?—More to the benefit of the ship-owners of that country, than the British ship is employed to the British ship-owner most assuredly.

Are not the Norwegians carrying on a very extensive export of timber to Holland, and other countries?—They are; they always enjoyed a large timber trade with Holland.

Mr. Edward Patzcker; Called in; and Examined.

WHAT are you?—A merchant at Memel.

In what firm do you carry on business?—Under the firm of C. B. Patzcker, my father.

Has there been a great alteration in the timber trade between Memel and this country, of late years?—Since the war, a great alteration; before the war, we used to have 950 to 1,100 ships in one year, and since the war we have had from two to three hundred only, English ships.

When you talk of nine hundred ships, do you mean nine hundred ships trading between Great Britain and Memel?—Yes.

Do you mean that number of cargoes was loaded in the year for England?—Yes.

How many cargoes were loaded for Great Britain during the last year?—About 270 or 280; there have not been more.

To what cause do you attribute that great diminution in the trade?—To the high duties here; for before the peace, the duties were only 16s. and some pence, and now they are 3l. 5s. and 3l. 8s. in a foreign ship.

Has that diminished trade in timber produced a great alteration in the circumstances of the people in Prussia?—Yes, for it is the only trade now which we can carry on; wheat and all the rest of the articles cannot be brought here; timber is the only one that can be brought, and that leaves no profit at this time, and the trade from Poland has ceased very much in consequence of it; the people cannot sell their goods, and we cannot take such quantities of timber as we used to do, and therefore they cannot take English goods from us.

Nor any other?—No, we have no other goods but English goods on our part; we get our other goods by sea from England, and the rest that comes there are very trifling, and the people in Poland do not like them; they take nearly all English goods.

What markets in the interior do you supply?—Warsaw; Berdeshoff, not far from Odessa; Broddy, on the frontiers of Austria; Kivof, and there are many little places which take the goods again to transport them to the Russian frontiers, and many places on the Russian frontiers, where we carry on the trade, and they get it into the country from thence.

What may the distance be from Memel, of the most distant of those places?—The most distant place is Kiolshoff, which is about twelve to thirteen hundred English miles.

The rivers are the Niemen, the Oder, and the Vistula?—They are.

What are the description of English goods to which you particularly refer?—All sorts

---
## Page 108

sorts of manufactured goods, earthenware, and colonial produce, but most rums. Rum we took great quantities; as that is for the Polish market, it is the most useful.

If such an alteration were to take place in the duties on timber in this country, as to give the Prussians a larger share of the trade than they at present enjoy, do you think that that would produce increased friendly feelings on the part of the people of your country to the people of this country?—They would certainly take far more goods from hence, as they could get better rid of them; the Poles would take more from them.

At what price was timber in 1806, previous to the war?—We buy it in Prussia by the shock; the load, free on board as we sell it, would have been 50s. in 1806.

What is the present price?—The present price is 30s.

What duty was imposed by the Prussian government in 1806?—Five per cent, and a duty at the frontiers, which was about 3-per cent, which made it about 8-per cent; but the export duty was taken off entirely last year, and the frontier duty made only about one half.

Was not the duty on the frontier 6-per cent before it was reduced?—No, it was not so much as that.

Does the timber sent from Memel to this country, at present leave a good profit?—None.

Does it leave a loss?—Yes, it leaves a loss.

Does any article of export from Memel to this market, at present leave a profit?—At present, none.

Have the excessive duties upon timber, operated during the whole time of their continuance to render the trade in timber from Memel to this country unprofitable?—Yes, always; and to show you more clearly, in those times we used to get down from Poland five or six thousand shock, and now we get down only a thousand, and last year only four hundred.

Has the export of grain from Memel much decreased?—There is none exported now; only very little, at least.

Was the export great, formerly?—Oh yes, it was great.

Which was the most valuable article of export from Memel, grain or timber?—Grain.

Have you much acquaintance with the article of staves?—We deal in staves as well as timber.

Is that a considerable branch of trade in Prussia?—Yes, it is a considerable trade, for we cannot get oak plank without buying staves; the oak plank of our country is reckoned to be the best for building ships, and is in general used by the British navy; we are obliged to buy some staves with them, or we cannot get them.

Has the high duty on oak in this country impeded the importation into it since the war?—There are no importations of oak only by government.

And there never have been any?—Yes, there have been formerly, but since the duty, they cannot be imported without a loss.

What is the price of Oak timber in Memel?—A load of oak timber will be about twelve pounds.

And of plank?—That is plank; we ship no timber to England; that is twelve pounds for fifty cubic feet.

Were you in the habit before the war, of exporting that to England?—There was none before the war; it used to be sent from Dantzig, but since that it has come to Memel. There are two canals, which lead, one to Memel and the other to Dantzig, and that which leads to Dantzig has no sluices, therefore they cannot bring it so easily as they can to Memel, the canal to that having sluices, and being a very nice canal indeed.

Is the exportation of staves from Memel to this country very much impeded by the high duties existing here at present?—Yes, very much; we cannot export staves without a loss.

What is the price of staves at Memel?—£. 60 a thousand in Memel.

State the dimensions?—They are about 60 inches length, from 60 to 70; and they are five and a half inches broad, and two and a half inches thick, these we call pipe staves; but the duty on such staves would with charges excepting freight, amount to 52 l.; the freight would be 12 l.

What proportion of the 52 l. is duty?—Nearly 49 l.

What will they sell for here?—They sell here for from 110 l. to 112 l.

Is there any duty upon the exportation of oak timber from Memel?—None.

---
## Page 109

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Is there any duty on the exportation of staves from Memel?—None; all the duties are taken off from wood articles.

From what countries do oak and staves come to Memel?—From Russian Poland, which is called Wohlynia.

What may be the expense of bringing staves and oak timber from the place of its growth to the port of Memel?—It will be about half of that price.

What loss is at present sustained upon the importation of staves from Memel?—The freight is lost.

It has been stated previously to the Committee, that during the years 1817 and 1818 the wood trade was in a much more favourable state with Memel than at present; do you remember whether or not the trade in staves was at that time profitable or not?—No, there was no profit upon that more than the timber trade, for it was said the duties would be altered in those years, and therefore the prices got up; but when the advices came out from England, that there was no alteration, the price went down as rapidly again.

What would be considered a fair remunerating price in Memel for bringing timber there for exportation?—At 35s. a load.

The present price is 30s.?—Yes.

What was the current price during the last year?—It was 25s. and 26s.

Then, in point of fact, there is an advance of from 4s. to 5s.?—Yes; but there is no timber bought yet. The people in the country say, we will not buy any before we know what the duties will be.

Then is the advanced price required in consequence of the expectation of a reduction of the duty here?—No, that cannot be; for we do not know whether there will be a reduction or a duty put on.

Is it in consequence of an expectation of change, either by reducing the duty on the Baltic timber, or by laying a duty on the American, that this advance has taken place in Prussia?—No; but the stock is so small.

Then whether any change takes place here or not, the shipper in Prussia will not accept of less than 30s.?—If they remain as they are now, people from England will not import, and we shall be obliged to get rid of our timber as we can, at whatever loss.

Has there been great dissatisfaction expressed in Prussia at the high duties exacted in this country?—Yes; and our Government has promised us, that there shall be an alteration in the duties here, and that they will petition the government here about it.

If an alteration were made that would reduce the duty from 65s. to 45s. a load, would that materially benefit the timber trade of Prussia?—It would materially benefit the timber trade.

And render it advantageous?—Yes, if we got better prices; but we must always go by this market; I think the demand would then be greater for our wood, and we should get a profit.

What were the rates of freight from Memel to London, during the last season?—21s. and 22s.

With ordinary dispatch, how many voyages do you expect a vessel to make during the season, from Memel to London?—You may reckon sometimes two, sometimes three voyages; in Memel, we give always very good dispatch; a ship of 300 tons never takes but five or six days loading.

With such dispatch, may not a ship be reasonably expected to make three voyages in a season?—Yes.

You have spoken of places on the Niemen, the Vistula, and the Oder, being supplied with rum, and various articles of manufacture from this country before the war, have they been supplied with similar articles from any other country since?—No, they take some articles from the Prussian manufactories, but they are not so liked as English manufactures; in Poland you cannot sell them so well, they will always have the English if they can.

Have they done without those articles?—Yes, they have done without them, for they have no money to pay for them, therefore they were obliged to do without them.

What was the current freight for staves during the last season?—From 10l. to 12l. and 13l.

186.

---
## Page 110

Sabbati, 24° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

Mr. Bartin Haigh;

Again called in; and further Examined.

YOU have stated in your former examination, that the price of New Brunswick yellow pine is at present eighteen-pence a foot in the Liverpool market; can you inform the Committee what was the current price of the same wood in November and December last?—In November, I bought 3,724 feet at sixteenpence halfpenny; I have not any account for December, but it was about the same.

To what cause do you attribute the advance?—I have reason to suppose it is under the expectation that there will be a trifling duty laid upon the timber.

Should a duty be laid equal to the advance, do you think it probable that that advance will be supported?—Yes.

Should no duty be laid, as those buyers expected, are you of opinion that the price will fall again to the rates current in November and December last?—I have no doubt of it.

Then if a duty is laid, the advance in price will be equal to that duty, and thus fall on the consumer?—Certainly.

If the present price of eighteen-pence is supported, will that cause a diminished consumption in your opinion?—No.

If the Dantzig pine falls to 2 s. 3 d. and the American pine is at 1 s. 6 d., will that, in your opinion, cause a diminished demand for American pine in the Lancashire market?—No, I believe not.

Do not the prices of commodities depend rather on the proportion that the supply bears to the demand, than on the duty they pay, or on any other circumstance?—The quantity of timber at present in the market, and the less demand, of course has been one reason of timber coming down in the Liverpool market; at the present price of timber, sixteen-pence halfpenny, and an advance, so as to make it eighteenpence, equal to what it is now, I am still of opinion it would make no difference as to the consumption of the article.

Does not the price depend upon the proportion the supply bears to the demand, rather than on the duty the commodity pays?—Certainly, the price must make an alteration in the consumption of the timber, whether pine or Baltic.

Does not the price depend upon the quantity in the market, and the proportion the supply bears to the demand?—There is not a doubt that the scarcer the article is in the market, of course it will be the more liable to be advanced. I think I mentioned that in my examination before; as it refers to Riga timber, that Riga timber being more scarce, it is higher in price, and that must be the same with every other timber.

In the offers which buyers make for timber, do they consider the amount of the duty it has paid, or whether it pays any duty whatever?—The importers of timber, and the merchants who buy it from the importers, can speak better to that; that is a business of their own. I buy mine generally from the purchasers, and sometimes from the importers, for the purpose of consumption; but with respect to what they do in the market, in point of speculation, that is a business I am perfectly ignorant of.

Do not you always endeavour to purchase timber at the lowest price at which you can obtain it, without inquiring into the charges made upon it by the importer?—Yes, certainly.

Supposing, from any alteration in the duties, the importation of pine timber should be reduced two-thirds of what it has been of late years, and the Baltic timber increased two-thirds what it has been, would not that contribute to advance the price of the North American timber?—Then it comes, probably, to the question before put to me, that it will depend entirely upon the quantity in the market.

If there were only one-third of the quantity of American timber in the market, would not the price be increased?—There is no doubt of that.

---
## Page 111

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Mr. Thomas Simson; Again called in; and further Examined.

There was an account you were requested to deliver in this morning, have you prepared that account?—I have; it is a list of the names of the Norway ships and their tonnage, and the names of the British ships and their tonnage that entered the port of London last year.

From what document is this account made out?—From the Custom-house Report lists.

What duty do you think might be imposed on the timber from the British provinces in North America, without materially diminishing the consumption?—I think that a duty of not more than 10s. a load, the American timber would bear, but that any thing beyond that would most materially affect it; we should then get the article better from America than we do at present; I think it would bear 10s.

Supposing 10s. was put upon the American timber; and 10s. taken off the Baltic timber, what effect would that have?—That would occasion a large importation immediately from the Baltic, and would be a most serious injury to the holders of Canada timber now in this country, who cannot possibly get rid of their stocks of it on hand.

Why would that occasion an importation of a large quantity from the Baltic?—That is my opinion, that there would be immediately a large importation from the Baltic, and that the holders of the present immense stock of American timber would not be able to get rid of that which they have on hand.

What

---
## Page 112

What is your reason for supposing there would be that immense importation from the Baltic?—The Baltic timber would then be brought in at so much lower rate than it is at present.

Do you happen to know at what time the wood for exportation from the Baltic is cut and prepared?—In the fall of the year; in the month of October.

Then all the wood that can be exported from the north of Europe this season, was prepared during the winter?—Yes.

Then upon what ground do you think a variation of duty can produce that immense immediate export?—Because there are always large quantities of timber lying at the shipping ports in the north of Europe, ready for shipping.

What proportion of the annual export is in that situation?—Nearly the whole of the exportation is down, ready for the spring ships.

Do you mean that there is the amount of two years exportation in the ports at one time ready for exportation, if that could be exported?—No, not two years exportation.

Is it more than one?—Of deals it is more than one.

How much more do you think?—Not having been in the Baltic, I cannot speak as to the quantities, I only speak from Norway.

But the timber that is to be exported in the spring, is cut in the winter?—That is the case in Norway; not having been in the Baltic, I cannot answer to that.

If that is the case in Norway, it is impossible that there could be an excess to any very considerable amount beyond the expected exportation of the ensuing season?—I have no doubt of the stocks being immense on hand in Norway; the quantity of timber cut and ready for shipment in Norway is very great.

What knowledge have you of that?—From my general knowledge of Norway, having travelled so much in it.

Have you any correspondents there?—I have in most parts.

Have you any accounts from correspondents, of the actual amount of stock on hand?—No.

Is it usual in Norway, when the timber is at a low price, and the export is limited, to continue to cut and prepare a vast deal of timber beyond the probable demand?—The farmers keep continually bringing the timber down to the shipping ports, so that the timber is continually accumulating.

And that whether there is an expectation of sale or not?—Yes, for unless they bring their timber, they cannot get corn, spirits and salt, and other things which they are obliged to take up into the country in return.

Do they find persons ready to buy it?—It is bought for the purpose of the people on the sea coast getting rid of their commodities; they barter for their timber.

Then the Committee are to understand, that the people on the sea-coast import commodities which they are in the habit of giving for wood, to an amount that they have no great expectation of selling?—They make their bargains accordingly.

Are there not large shipments of timber from Norway, sent to other parts of Europe, as well as to Great Britain?—They ship large quantities of timber to Holland.

If the timber from Norway was admitted here on more favourable terms, with respect to duty, would not that circumstance decide the holders to send a larger proportion of it to this market, and less than usual to other markets?—Most assuredly.

Do you know what the duty is upon Norway timber, in Holland?—No, I do not; I only know that the Hollanders enjoy the privilege of carrying it; there are very few Norwegian vessels go with timber from Norway to Holland, they are principally Dutch vessels; to this country it is chiefly brought in Norway ships.

Do you know whether Norway timber is received in Holland on better terms than it is in this country?—No, I cannot answer that.

Then, not knowing upon what terms it is received in Holland, how can you take upon yourself to state that a given alteration of the duty here, would produce a diminution of the export to Holland, and an increase of export to this country?—I beg leave to state, that I do not mean to say that the quantity that is taken to Holland would be less than is taken at present, but Holland cannot take all the timber which Norway produces.

In your opinion, would deals from the British provinces in North America, bear any augmentation of duty, without a material diminution of their consumption in this country?—Any duty placed upon deals from North America beyond thirty shillings, or two pounds for a hundred and twenty pieces, would drive them out of the market;

---
## Page 113

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

the white deals from Riga and Petersburgh, are now brought in nearly on the same terms as the Canada people can bring their deals, that if any thing beyond two pounds was put on American deals, it would keep them out of the market altogether, and we should be supplied entirely from Riga, Petersburgh, and other northern ports.

In your opinion, would staves from the British provinces in America, bear any, and what augmentation of duty?—Staves from the town of Quebec certainly might bear a duty, but there would be great difficulty in levying the duty on staves generally from Canada, they vary so much in thickness; staves which we get from the lower provinces, Nova Scotia, and those places, the value of them in our market is not more than from seven to eight pounds a thousand, whereas the staves we get from Quebec are worth seventy-seven, or from that to eighty pounds.

What is the present price of British oak per load?—That depends upon its size, in a great measure; small oak is worth about eight pounds.

What is the present price of British fir or larch?—There is very little of that comes to the London market at present; what has been here lately has sold at from £1. to £1. 5s.

Would not any diminution in the present duties on Baltic timber reduce the value of British timber?—Undoubtedly it would.

Do not you consider the timber-market in Great Britain, at present, as very much overstocked?—It certainly is overstocked with all descriptions of goods.

Then if it is overstocked, has it not already, in consequence of that, reduced the price of British timber as low as it can be well reduced?—If the oak timber from the Baltic was brought in upon lower terms than it is at present, there would be no sale whatever for British oak used in ship-building; the grower of British oak plank for ship-building now can scarcely get rid of it at any price.

Do not you know that in the present state of the country, with a view to ship-building, it would be very desirable to preserve the English oak growing in it, and that there is actually a great scarcity of it?—That is a question I cannot take upon myself to answer.

The account you have delivered in contains a list of the individual ships employed in the trade, and not of the voyages?—Of the individual ships.

What number of sailors are those Norwegian ships usually navigated by?—About one man more to one hundred tons, than a British ship would take.

Do you know what the insurance is on cargoes on board Norway ships, in proportion to that in British?—It is nearly the same; the premiums are at this time so very low, that there is very little difference.

Do you know whether they are now continuing to build ships in Norway?—I believe not.

Do you know how long it is since they have ceased to build them?—About two years.

Do you think it would be worth while for them to build at the present freights?—Not at the present freights.

Mr. James Haylock Hill; Called in; and Examined.

WHAT are you?—I am a merchant in the Russia trade, partner in an establishment at Riga, and partner in an establishment at Petersburgh.

Your business lies in the wood trade?—Very much in that; not exclusively so; that forms a material part of our business.

What proportion does the export of wood from Russia bear to the other produce of the country?—I really cannot state it in point of value; the value is small, compared with that of other goods.

In point of giving employment to ships?—There were 566 British ships employed last year in the trade between Petersburgh and the United Kingdom; of that number, there were about a hundred British ships employed in the wood trade.

What proportion of the ships employed in the Russian trade do you conceive, upon the whole, are employed in the conveyance of timber?—I should think in the proportion of one-fifth or one-sixth; my information is entirely limited to the ports of Petersburgh and Riga, I have no information as to other ports; there are ports, such as Onega and Narva, where the trade is almost entirely the wood trade; of those ports, I have no means of giving any information, not having establishments there.

In vessels of what country is the timber trade between Russia and this country carried on?—Entirely in British vessels; when I say entirely, the amount in foreign vessels is so small, as to make it not worth mentioning.

---
## Page 114

Do you speak to the two ports only, or to the whole?—Always as to those two ports; but I have no doubt it is the same to the other ports also.

If any increased importation of wood were to take place from Russia, do you conceive that would continue to be imported in British vessels exclusively?—Yes, I conceive so.

State the reasons for that opinion?—The quantity now imported, being brought entirely in British ships, I conceive any increased quantity would be brought likewise in British ships. The difference of duty between a foreign ship and a British would operate in inducing importations in British ships; and there are not many Russian ships that could be so employed; the commercial navy of Russia is very limited.

Do you know any Russian ships that were employed in that way some years ago?—Yes, I know two or three ships that have occasionally brought a cargo from Petersburgh to England. During the interruption of the trade, the trade was entirely conducted in Russian and other foreign ships; but since the peace, it has been almost exclusively carried on in British. We have had one or two cargoes in the course of that time, in Russian ships.

Do you apprehend that a diminution of the duty upon foreign timber, would increase, to any considerable degree, the imports from Russia?—Yes, I do.

As far as your opinion goes, would that be attended by an increased consumption of British produce, and export of our manufactures to Russia?—I think, on a general view, it would have that effect, certainly.

You have resided in Russia?—I have.

Are the Russians disposed to take our manufactures?—Very much so.

In preference to the manufactures of other countries?—Decidedly so. They get them generally cheaper, and therefore it is their own interest to take them; I do not know that there is any other predilection than that.

Are you of opinion, from your knowledge of Russia, that the duties imposed upon timber and upon linens have had an injurious effect on the intercourse of this country with Russia?—With respect to linens, it has had the effect of entirely annihilating that trade; with respect to wood, the importations, as far as I know from Petersburgh, have been upon the decrease for the last three or four years; and regularly on the decrease, as I can show by lists which I have brought with me, for the years 1817, 1818, 1819 and 1820.

State the result?—The exportation of deals in 1817, from Petersburgh to Great Britain, was 2,230,595 standard deals; the exportation in 1818 was 1,564,110; the exportation in 1819 was 1,237,268 standard deals; and the exportation last year was only 1,099,015 standard deals; so that the exportation has been, for the last four years, constantly and regularly decreasing.

What do you apprehend would be the effect of such an alteration of the present mode of levying the duties on deals, as should make them pay by their cubical contents?—I apprehend the effect would be throwing such an advantage into the hands of Norway as to give her, in point of fact, a monopoly of the trade.

Are there any saw mills or other establishments formed in Russia, with a view to the timber trade of this country?—Yes, several.

Are they considerable?—Yes; very large.

Where most of the deals that come into this country are cut?—Yes, where they are all cut.

What proportion of them are under the direction of subjects of this country, or carried on by British capital?—I know of only one mill, whose proprietors are English; there may be more; that one is at Riga.

Do you know whether any British subjects have an interest in any of the others?—No; I do not know that they have.

What is the price of deals at Petersburgh and at Riga?—The last price of redwood deals at Petersburgh was £4.19s. per standard hundred on board, and the price for the same article at Riga, by the last advice, was £4.10s. per Petersburgh standard hundred on board.

What is the freight?—The usual freight varies; I should think about 4l. per Petersburgh standard hundred; we have paid four guineas last year; and had some ships at 3l. 15s.

Are you aware of any regulations in Russia, which may have been, in part, the cause of the diminution in the export of timber?—No, I am not.

The account you have given of the diminished export of timber from Russia, is

---
## Page 115

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

confined to particular parts?—To Petersburgh only; the exportation of deals from Riga was less last year than it was the year before.

Have you any statement to give of the export from all the ports of Russia generally to this country?—No, I have not.

What number of voyages do the timber ships from Petersburgh and from Riga usually make in a year?—From Petersburgh two.

Never more?—There may occasionally have been instances of three, but two is the number they generally make.

You have stated that the price of Petersburgh deals is £1. 19s. per standard hundred, have you any calculations, either of the same quantity, or any greater quantity, to show what the proceeds of the deals are in this country at the present time, compared to their cost?—Yes, I have a calculation of that sort, which, if it is the pleasure of the Committee, I will give in. I see that the proceeds of a hundred of Petersburgh deals, reduced to the Norway standard, is £5. 6s. 9d.; the same sort on board, reduced to the Norway hundred, would cost £11. 9s. on board.

Consequently the trade from Petersburgh is now carried on at a loss?—At a very great loss.

What were the prices of deals at Petersburgh two years ago?—I cannot state that accurately, except from memory.

How long has the trade been in the state in which you have now described it, with respect to the proceeds?—I should think, for the last eighteent months, the prices have been falling here so considerably.

To what do you attribute that fall of price?—I attribute it to the extreme low price at which American deals are sold, and the large importations.

If there was no duty on Russian deals, at what price could they be sold in this country, with a fair profit to the importer?—I think they might be sold at about £3. per Petersburgh standard hundred.

At what price could they be sold under the present duties, with a fair profit to the importer?—One or two and twenty pounds the Petersburgh standard hundred.

The cost price, in 1820, of red wood deals on board at St. Petersburgh, was per Petersburg standard hundred, £. 10s. to 7l. St. Petersburg standard hundred of 120 pieces, 12 feet long, 1½ inches thick, and 11 inches broad, contains 165 cubic feet. A Norway hundred of 120 pieces, 12 feet long, 3 inches thick, and 9 inches broad, contains 270 cubic feet; so that such deals cost in St. Petersburg, per Norway hundred, at the above prices, no less than from 10l. 12s. 8d. to 11l. 9s. 1d. on board.

Have you any grounds for your reason, why the establishment of the duties, if it should so take place by the cubic measure, would prove so detrimental to the Russian trade, and beneficial to the Norway?—Yes; I infer so from the progress of the trade as it has hitherto been conducted; I find, that as the duties have been hitherto levied, Norway has her full share of the trade; and that she obtains, in fact, a better price for her deals than we do, and more net proceeds; and consequently, if they have such an advantage given them as to reduce the duty from 21l. which they now pay, to 12l. which would be the effect of the alteration, they would have such an advantage over us as to drive us completely out of the market.

Mr. Robert Banton Roxby; Called in; and Examined.

YOU are a ship-owner at Shields?—I am.

How long have you been a ship-owner there?—Thirty years.

Have you a considerable number of ships in your own possession at Shields?—Three or four.

Where have those ships generally traded to?—Since the year 1808 they have traded nearly altogether to North America, Quebec, New Brunswick, and Nova Scotia.

They have been employed in bringing wood from thence?—They have entirely.

Can you state what number of ships belong to the port of Newcastle?—Eight hundred and thirty-one were registered in the port of Newcastle in September 1819.

What is the amount of their tonnage?—One hundred and eighty-three thousand, some few hundreds.

What number of seamen do they employ on board those ships?—Upwards of 9,000.

Your observations now extend to the port of Newcastle generally?—Yes, including North and South Shields, Blyth, and Hartley.

In what trade are the ships of the port of Newcastle generally employed?—The coasting and the foreign trade; I am speaking of the year 1819. In the year 1819 there were 475 ships in the coasting trade, and 356 in the foreign trade.

What proportion of that number of ships were employed in the North American trade?—Two hundred and nine.

What is the amount of their tonnage?—Sixty thousand tons.

What number of men are employed in the ships in the North American trade?—Nearly three thousand.

What is the state of the shipping trade at present in the port of Newcastle?—Very much depressed; I never knew it so much so.

What are the particular causes which have led to the depression of the shipping-interest in that port?—The very low state of the freights last year.

Have you not received an intimation, that if any additional duty should be laid on Canada timber imported, that must be deducted from the freights to compensate the grower of the wood in Canada?—The ship-owner is aware that if any duty is laid...

---
## Page 117

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Haid-on the American timber it must come off the freight; the freight was extremely low last year, and of course that will make it less this.

Then your opinion is, that any alteration of the timber duties will very materially affect that part of the shipping interest connected with the port of Newcastle?—It is.

Could those ships, that are now employed in the North American timber trade, be transferred to the Baltic trade?—A few of them might, but not many; the ships employed in the American trade, are too large for the Baltic in time of peace.

Are there not considerable ship-building yards and docks in the port of Newcastle?—Very extensive; but the former are almost given up, for want of employment.

You are of opinion, that any additional duties on the importation of the North American timber, will probably lead to the total giving up of the whole of those building yards?—We conceive so; indeed, if the shipping is any more injured than it is at present, they must give up the trade altogether.

Have you yourself had much business in the trade with Norway?—Not lately.

At one time, a good deal of business was carried on from the port of Newcastle, with the ports of the Baltic?—A good deal.

Has that trade diminished much lately?—It has almost ceased.

You think it would be impossible for the ship-owners of that port to transfer the vessels they now use in the North American trade, to any extent to the Baltic trade?—They cannot, they are too large.

From the knowledge you have of that part of the country, are you not of opinion, that any duties on the North American trade, would be equally prejudicial to the shipping interests of the port of Sunderland?—Equally so.

There is a very considerable interest in shipping, in the port of Sunderland?—There are six hundred sail belonging to the port of Sunderland, and their tonnage is about 84,000.

Do you know the number of men employed in the port of Sunderland?—Upwards of five thousand seamen.

The observations you make as applicable to the port of Newcastle, relative to the injurious effect of a duty being laid on American timber, will apply equally to the port of Sunderland?—Not equally; the shipping belonging to the port of Sunderland are of a smaller class, and many of them do not go to America.

What proportion of those in the port of Sunderland, are in the American timber trade?—I should think, not more than one-sixth; the rest are employed in the trade to the Continent principally, and some few to the Mediterranean, and a very little West India trade.

You are not aware that there is any complaint made of the badness of the timber imported from North America?—Not any, that I know of; there was at first, but that has nearly ceased, in my opinion; in the ports of Sunderland and Newcastle, they find the British American timber so much larger, and so much better adapted for the purposes in the collieries, where almost all the timber is consumed, that it is perhaps the best timber they ever had, or can have, for that purpose.

Do you conceive, from your knowledge of that part of the country, and of the trade in which that country is concerned, that any additional duty laid upon the raw material employed in the collieries would materially affect them in their business?—I should conceive so.

You have stated, that the freights are very low at present?—They are.

Are they low in all trades, as well as the timber trade?—Lower in proportion in the timber trade, we think, than any other, in my opinion.

Is ship-building going on now in those ports?—It has almost entirely ceased, very few ships are building.

Since what time?—Since the year 1819.

Did it continue up to that time, as usual?—In the years 1818 and 1819, some few ships were built at Newcastle, and several at Sunderland.

What were the usual number built at Newcastle?—That it is hard to say.

What should you take to be the annual average number built at Newcastle since the peace?—I should think not more than twelve or fifteen upon the Tyne; the number at Sunderland is very considerable.

What do you conceive to have been the annual number built at Sunderland?—I can hardly give an answer to that question, but I should think fifty or sixty.

What number of ships do you conceive are now building on the Tyne?—I am told only five; I believe that is the utmost.

186. G g Do

---
## Page 118

Do you know the number of the ships now building within the port of Sunderland?—I am told fourteen or fifteen.

Are the ships sailed at a loss now?—At a considerable loss.

What is the reason then for the building being continued?—The builders sometimes have very large establishments; a great number of apprentices, that they cannot get clear of until they have served their time; their building-yards and docks are taken on lease at a high rent, and they are under the necessity of building, and taking their chance what they can get for their ships. They find it, I suppose, cheaper to do that, and to pay the establishment, and the poor rates and other rates attaching to the premises.

You state that there are some ships now building in the port of Newcastle, is there not a large proportion of American timber employed in building those ships?—Not a great deal; they equip them in the flooring or lower decks, and bulkheads and bulwarks, and so on, with American timber.

You have no reason to complain of the nature of American timber for those purposes to which you apply it in building your ships?—Not the least so; I have laid one ship's decks with American timber, and I approve of it before the other. The ship I speak of had two decks, a quarter-deck and a maindeck: the maindeck was laid with American yellow pine, and part pitch pine; the quarter-deck was laid with wood from the Baltic. The ship has been at Sierra Leone, which was a very trying place for ships; they pine and rend there with the heat of the sun more at that port than any port which I know. On her arrival at home, I examined the decks very minutely, and I found the American decks had not rent or pined so much as the Baltic decks; the ship is here, and can be inspected.

You have stated that the ships have been principally employed in the American wood trade since the year 1808, in what trade were they employed before that time?—In the Baltic trade in some degree, but mostly in the coal trade before that period, and in the transport service, but not constantly employed in any way.

Why were they put into the timber trade?—They were ships of large tonnage, and they suited that trade much better than any other trade I could find for them after they were discharged by government.

Were they originally too large for the Baltic trade?—Yes, excepting in time of war; they might perhaps have done then, but in time of peace they were always too large for the Baltic trade.

Does the Baltic trade require larger ships in time of war than in time of peace?—The merchants cannot get small ships then, and are obliged perhaps to take the large; they always prefer small ships to large ones, except for carrying masts.

The Sunderland ships are smaller ships?—Yes, much smaller.

Then the objection to turning the Sunderland ships into the Baltic trade, does not apply in the same way to them, that it does to the Newcastle ships?—It does not; the port of Sunderland has very few ships comparatively speaking that go into the Baltic trade.

When you speak of low freights, does that apply to all trades, the coasting trade and the coal trade?—Yes.

Are they all low equally?—Not equally; the American freights are lowest I think.

To what do you attribute the lowness of the American freight?—Principally to the over-trading in the year 1819, when such an immense quantity of timber was brought into this country.

Is it not true that the ships from the east coast, go to Liverpool and to the west coast for employment?—They in part supply all the kingdom of Great Britain.

Is not the American trade from the west coast, principally carried on by ships from the east coast?—I cannot say principally; but a great number of ships go from the port of Newcastle to the west coast, Liverpool, Bristol, to Ireland, and to Scotland.

Do you happen to know whether a great proportion of those ships which carry on the American timber trade, do or do not belong to the eastern ports?—A very great proportion of them belong to the eastern ports.

How many voyages do they usually make in the year from the western ports?—From the western ports, generally two.

From the eastern ports?—Seldom more than one; I never had a ship that made a second voyage except one, she made two; but I should never send another, it puts them so late into the season, they get torn to pieces in coming home.

In the neighbourhood of Newcastle, have you latterly been much in the habit of using American timber?—Yes.

Do you use it for the frame of the ship in ship-building?—Never; English oak is preferred in that country.

---
## Page 119

Do you build much with Baltic oak?—No, very little.

Is that owing to the high duty?—Perhaps so; but the quality of the wood is not preferred in that quarter.

You prefer the American oak?—Yes, to the Baltic oak; but there is very little comes to that port; there was formerly a good deal of Hamburg wood went to that port.

Have you been in the habit of using a good deal of the American fir?—Yes.

For what purpose is it generally used in the country?—It is used for all purposes; both for building houses and in ship-building, excepting, as I said, in the frames and planking of the ships; they do not use it for that; I have used it myself in building houses.

What sort of houses do you apply it to; to the better sort of houses, or the inferior sort of houses?—The houses, perhaps, of the second or third description. The house I built was of the first description; a shop in the town of Sunderland.

And you used the American fir?—For part of the house; not altogether.

Is it generally found as good for all inside work, and those common purposes?—I should prefer it, for my part, for inside work, from what I have seen of it I have used it for twenty years.

Is there much consumption for packing-cases and things of that kind, in your part of the country?—There is a good deal used, but not like the quantity used in Liverpool and Manchester.

What do you conceive to be the proportion that the American fir used there, bears to the whole consumption of timber?—Full two-thirds of American; the collieries consume the most of the timber that is imported there, and they find the American timber so much preferable to the other, that they use it in preference; it is at a cheaper price, and being much broader, it answers their purpose better at the bottom of the pits.

Is the consumption of timber in the collieries very great?—Very great.

On the whole, for those purposes, the American timber is rather preferred?—Yes, I think for some purposes they might give the preference; as the timber is broader and better adapted than other wood; but for general purposes, I presume, they would give the preference to the wood that could be bought cheapest, as the quality of the wood they use under the ground is not of much consequence.

Do you think they would prefer the American at equal prices?—I think they would not, at equal prices, for the collieries.

Do you think they would prefer the American at an equal price, for the two purposes for which it is generally used, independent of ship-building, inside work, and so on?—I think so, as far as my opinion goes; I should myself: It works much better; it is clearer from knots, and does not rend so much when it is fairly seasoned; season it equally with Baltic timber, and I conceive it would not rend so much.

If it was considerably lower it would, of course, be preferred?—No doubt.

Then supposing it to continue considerably cheaper, what reason have you to believe that any alteration would take place in the importation of it to that part of the country?—I should believe it would not, if it continued considerably cheaper.

What degree of cheapness do you think would still preserve it in full consumption there, do you think thirty shillings a load would be sufficient?—I think in the ports of Sunderland and Newcastle, that might do, for the prejudice is partly done away with against the American timber in those two places, but I cannot answer for other places, as I know there is yet a strong prejudice against it.

Then what reason have you to state that any alteration that is made in the existing duties, must have a considerable effect upon the shipping of the ports of Sunderland and Newcastle?—Because in my opinion, if any duty at present should be laid upon the American timber, the merchant would expect the amount of that duty to be taken from the freight.

How would that follow, if the consumption and the demand remained the same?—If the duty were laid on now at the present depressed state of the market, whatever duty was laid on, the merchant would expect so much to be taken from the ship's freight.

If the consumption remains the same, and the price rises in proportion to the alteration of the duty, what reason would there be for any deduction from the price of freight?—At the present state of the timber market, the depressed state it is in, I do not believe the merchants can afford to give bigger freights than they did last year; and I do believe if a duty takes place immediately, the merchant cannot afford to give so much freight, in proportion as the duty may be; the depression of the market is now very great.

---
## Page 120

When your ships go to America to fetch timber, do they go in ballast or with a cargo?—In ballast mine go generally; there are many ships take cargoes out, but the merchants select the small ships for the purpose of taking the valuable cargoes out to America; they do not often take the large ones for that purpose.

Has the importation of oak plank from the Baltic, fallen off considerably?—There is very little oak plank imported from the Baltic into the merchants yards in the north, it generally comes to the King's yards in London.

Is the oak plank from Stettin, of great importance for the building of ships?—I do not conceive it is, for they can get American plank in the room of it.

Do you conceive American plank to be as good?—As good, if it is of the quality of white wood; there is bad plank comes from Stettin, and bad plank from America, but if it be what they call the white wood from Stettin, and the white wood from America, they are considered as equal.

Mr. William Tindall; Called in; and Examined.

IN what business are you?—A ship builder and ship owner at Scarborough.

How long have you been employed in that trade?—About eighteen years.

Where have your ships generally traded to?—To all parts of the world.

Have you ever traded to British North America, for the purpose of carrying wood from thence?—Yes; we have had three ships in the trade last year.

To what amount of tonnage?—One ship was of 556 tons; one 514; and, one 420.

What number of seamen do you employ in those ships?—About twenty-four hands each; they vary, of course, according to their tonnage, but that is the average.

What number of ships belong to the port of Scarborough?—There are from twenty-five to thirty thousand tons.

Do you know the number of seamen employed in the port of Scarborough?—I do not.

Is there a considerable traffic from the port of Scarborough, in the British North American trade, carrying of wood?—A very small one to Scarborough; they do not receive above four cargoes a year.

In what state is the shipping of Scarborough at this moment?—In a very distressed situation indeed.

To what do you attribute that?—The principal cause is, the present stagnation of trade occasioned principally by the over trading of the previous year?—Would an alteration of the timber duties affect the port of Scarborough?—It will affect the port of Scarborough, as far as respects the shipping interest.

Have you much trade with Scarborough to the Baltic?—We have very little; about three cargoes a year.

Of what build are those three ships which belong to yourself?—They were built by ourselves at Scarborough.

What timber do you generally use in the construction of your ships?—Principally English oak, English elm, and English beech; fir is used for the decks, and for the accommodations.

Do you use Baltic fir, or fir from the British provinces in North America?—For the decks we generally use the Baltic fir, but mostly the North American fir for the cabin and the light work.

Have you ever used American fir for upper decks?—I used American fir about nine years ago; we fitted some ships with half American and half Baltic, partly owing to the price of Baltic timber being very high, and partly to try how the American would last.

Have you lately compared the different state of these two descriptions of timber?—I saw one of the ships about two months ago, and I examined her particularly; I found the decks were generally sound; but where the iron which fastened the deck came in contact with the wood, the American deck was the best; it was less rent by the sun, the ship having been in warm climates most of the time.

What sort of timber do you use for masts?—The timber we use for masts for the larger ships is Quebec yellow pine; for the top masts and yards, the red wood of the Baltic and Canada; for the small spars, the white spruce of Norway and of Canada; we prefer the Norway.

Have you any reason to complain of the want of durability of the lower masts made from American timber?—No; I have found them last rather better than

---
## Page 121

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

than the Rigā, in warm climates, and where red pine has been used it has been much superior.

Can you state the different expense of ship-building in this country, and in Norway and Prussia?—Yes, I can; I have made a statement, which I will submit to the Committee.

In making that statement, what means have you used to ascertain its correctness?—I took the quantities of timber and other materials that were necessary to build a ship with; to that quantity I put the price in England, the price in Norway, and the price in Prussia; the result is a very great difference in the cost of building.
