What means have you of ascertaining the cost of those particular articles in Prussia and in Norway?—Partly from the Prussian and other merchants, and partly from captains of ships who have been in Prussia and Norway, and have bought the articles.

From no authentic document?—There is no authentic documents other than the price currents of merchants, and what we really purchase goods at.

You believe that statement to be as correct an estimate as can be made?—I believe it to be perfectly correct; some things are the same in one country as another, others are much lower.

It appears that the shipwrights account for the hull in England is 5,162 l.; that in Norway it is 1,309 l., and that in Prussia it is 2,187 l. what proportion of the 5,162 l. arises from the high duties imposed upon timber in England?—There is a very small proportion of that, because the ship is principally built of English oak, and the Prussian is built of Prussian oak, and the Norwegian and Swede of fir.

Then the comparison is made, with respect to Norway and Sweden, between fir ships and oak ships?—Yes, because these are the materials they generally build with.

If you were to buy a ship of 514 tons, built of English oak, or of Norway fir, what would be the difference of value in this country?—That is a question I am not capable of answering, because we do not build of that article in this country; there is no such thing for sale in this market as a Norwegian ship.

During the war many Danish prizes were taken, which afterwards obtained British register; what would a Danish vessel, having obtained a British registry, having been built of Norway fir, sell for, in competition with an English vessel of the same size, built of oak, taking them both as new ships?—The difference would be as two is to three.

Would not an estimate of as two is to one be a more correct value?—No, certainly not; I am taking the hull only. Supposing a quantity of Norway fir was to 186.

---
## Page 122

be imported into this country, and put into a ship, then it would be as two is to three.

To make this a fair comparison between the Norway and Swedish ship, and the English ship to the price of the Norway or Swedish ship, a great addition ought to be made?—By adding one half of the £1,309 l. to it, making it £1,950 l.

Are you engaged in ship-building at present?—I am as far as our apprentices go; we have discharged all our men; we cannot continue the trade any longer; we cannot sell our new ships for the prime cost of them.

But you still find it necessary to continue ship-building in some degree to employ your apprentices?—We should have to pay them their wages whether they did anything or nothing.

Did you build these three large ships of which you have spoken, for the purpose of carrying timber from America?—No; one of the ships was built the last year; she cost us £12,100 l., fitted for a voyage to Canada, but she was not intended for that trade; our object was to send the ship to the East Indies, but we could not get employment for her.

State the objects of the other two?—They were old ships, both built for the West India trade, and had gone as long as they were fit for that trade.

Now they are put into the North American trade?—Yes.

Is that the case generally?—It is; in fact the old West India ships are fit only for the North American trade; this trade takes the old West and East India ships.

If those ships were thrown out of the North American timber trade, could you employ them in any other?—It would be a very disadvantageous thing to send them to the Baltic, on account of their size.

Before a ship of that description is put into the American trade, has not she already fulfilled all the services that she was expected to fulfil when she was built?—By no means.

You state them to have been built originally for the West India trade?—Yes.

What length of time do you reckon a ship ought to continue in the West India trade?—About twelve years.

How long had those ships continued in that trade?—One of them had continued twelve years; one has been in the American trade some time; she continued about ten, or twelve years in the West India trade, then went into the transport service, and continued about four years, and she then went into the North American trade.

Having speculated upon her life at twelve years, and she having fulfilled that term in the West India trade, or the West India and the transport trade, were not the owners completely remunerated for the building and expense of the ship?—In answer to that question, we never expect a ship to have fulfilled her object of building until she is either lost or destroyed in some way or other.

But when you build a ship, you always calculate upon her lasting a certain time, do not you?—No; the durability of a ship is very uncertain.

When a ship is built, do not you, in forming your calculation of the price that she is to cost, speculate upon a certain number of years which she ought to last, if she meets with no accident?—If she meets with no accident, we calculate upon her lasting twelve years without material repairs, and as much longer as she will after she is repaired.

Do you mean that you calculate upon a ship lasting twelve years, without undergoing any repair?—Without undergoing any substantial repair; I mean without a new top, &c.

Is not that beyond the usual calculation?—Some ships will not do it, they have to be pulled down to their foundations at five years old.

What ships have you known go twelve years, without undergoing a substantial repair?—A great many of the river-built ships are considered fit to carry the best cargoes till they are twelve years old, and north country ships, ten years.

Previous to the trade with British America being carried to its present extent, how used you to employ your worn-out West India ships?—We used to send part of them to Riga, where they loaded in the bay; sometimes it happened that there was transport service that we could put them into, sometimes they went to Omega for deals, and Wyburgh; but the navigation of the Baltic is rather dangerous for a very deep ship.

Has not the cessation of the war, and the consequent want of demand for ships for the transport service, been the cause of the great number of large ships which are now on hand, without adequate employment?—That was in the first instance the cause of the great depression in shipping, but we got over that depression, and freights rose; we had the rates we have now, about four years ago, and they rose...

---
## Page 123

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

to 3l. and 3l. 5s. for a voyage to Quebec; that was a remunerating price; but there was an overtrading in the year 1819, and that caused the very great flat in 1820.

When you talk of overtrading, what do you mean by overtrading: overtrading in one particular branch, namely, to America, from there not being an encouragement to send ships to other quarters?—There was an expectation there would be a duty laid on American timber in the spring of the last year, and the American merchants anticipated that.

If you had not sent your ships to America, where must they have been sent to?—We must have broken them up, there would have been no alternative; we must have lost all the difference between the price for use, and the price for breaking up.

Was not the overtrading you mention, the cause of that rise in the freights which you mention for one year?—I think it was the cause of the odd five shillings taking place; we had 3l. in the year 1818, and in the next year we had 3l. 5s.

Was not the year 1818 a year of very great demand?—It was not so great as 1819.

If no alteration were to take place in the duties on American timber, would it not be for the interest of the ship-owners, to diminish the number of the large class of ships engaged in it?—It is not in their power, unless by breaking up, and that takes place every day.

It is quite impossible to continue to employ the present number of ships of that class beneficially?—It will be, if the duty remains as it is; if there is no alteration of the duties.

The alteration of duty will tend in your opinion to diminish the number of ships that are sent to North America, and therefore to make the trade more beneficial for the remainder of the ships?—The natural losses tend to reduce the number, and the breaking up together; I would observe that the cause of the freights being so low last year, was not altogether this (with reference to overtrading) there was another cause, the trade to the East Indies has been nearly discontinued; there were two years ago, about eight hundred sail of ships employed in the corn trade, of the smaller class (perhaps averaging 100 tons each) and about three years ago, we had a very good trade in the Mediterranean, for small vessels to bring corn from Odessa to the lower ports in the Mediterranean, and to do other parts of the business in the Mediterranean, but after the battle of Algiers, foreigners were allowed to trade as they pleased, because there was no impediment; the Swedes came to the Mediterranean in great abundance with lumber, they sold the lumber in the lower ports, and then chartered to go up to Odessa, and they did the work for less money than the English ships could do it for, we were therefore obliged to withdraw those ships from the Mediterranean; I had three ships myself in the trade, the last two of them went up in the year 1818, under an expectation of getting the usual employment; when they got there they could not get freights.

Then you are of opinion, that if circumstances were to occur to give general activity to the trade of Europe, such circumstances would be more beneficial to your shipping, than having only the alternative of sending them as you are obliged to do at present, from the depressed state of trade, to America?—It would withdraw part of the tonnage from the American trade, particularly of a smaller class, that would give impulse to the rest, and they would have better freights.

Why do you prefer Quebec yellow pine for the masts of your ships, to Baltic pine?—Because the heat of the warm climates has not so much effect in rendering them; the water cannot get admittance.

Do you consider the yellow pine of America, equal in quality for general purposes to the Baltic pine?—No, I do not; I do for two-thirds of the uses to which it is put.

For what purposes do you not think it so preferable?—I would not put yellow pine into a damp wall; I would not use it as a post in the ground.

For what purposes do you think it preferable?—If I wanted to lay the floor of a house I should prefer it, because it is clean; or for doing any in-door work, making a door, because it is light.

Are those about two-thirds of the purposes to which it is applied?—No; I think there are a great many purposes of the inferior kind, and that they together constitute about two-thirds.

Do you think that in your part of the country, it would have a use at equal price?—No, it will never have the same price in our part of the country, because it does not come home so well squared.

If

---
## Page 124

If you could have the fir at equal prices for those particular purposes for which it is preferred, do you not think it would be used?—There is a very considerable prejudice against this wood, which prejudice will be some time before it is worn off.

Do you think that prejudice is wearing off in your part of the country?—I think it is gradually; but one way in which the prejudice wears away is because it comes in cheap, and persons are induced to make the trial; and until time has given it a fair trial, I think there would always be a great difference in price.

Do you think the preference would go to the difference of 30s. a load; do you think, if the price of the American was 30s. a load cheaper than the Baltic, that it would not be preferred?—No; I think it would take 40s. difference to lead to the general use of it.

You yourself believing it to be equally good?—I only say for certain purposes that it is equally good.

You state that it is used in the ships you usually build, for masts and for certain other parts of the ship; is that the common practice in ship-building throughout the eastern coast, that they use the American wood for masts?—Speaking of masts, it is not the universal practice; but it is the general practice for large ships; I make that distinction, because the red pine is superior to it for smaller masts; but we cannot get the red pine of large size enough.

What do you call a large ship?—I call a ship of 400 tons a large ship, to make a mast of a single tree.

For every ship of 400 tons they are obliged to use the American?—We are glad to use it; we prefer it. I do not mean to say that; every one would prefer it; because if they could get the Riga spars, many people would prefer them; we can get them of that size, but the first cost in Riga is an amazing price. A large mast costs more in Riga than the American mast will in England, although there is the freight and duty to pay upon it.

Have you ever seen Stettin oak plank imported for the purpose of ship building?—Yes, Stettin, Dantzig and Memel.

Are they useful for any purposes in ship building?—We use a large proportion in the planking of the inside only; it is too dear; we get the American timber now for less money.

If you could get the Stettin for less money, should you prefer it to North American?—No, I think I should not, but it is all matter of opinion; the Stettin timber is brought in in planks, it is well seasoned in consequence of being brought in in planks; the American is brought in in logs, and it is not seasoned, and it requires a long time to bring it into the same state, on account of the American being cut up immediately, and put into ships immediately; on its being cut it is very apt to decay; consequently there is a great prejudice against the use of American timber in ship-building, which does not exist against Stettin to that extent; but if I were to give an opinion as to the intrinsic value of the two articles, I should say one was equal to the other under the same circumstances.

The preference then would be a mere question of price between them?—It would, provided they were placed in the same circumstances.

Have you built any ships lately?—One of the ships sent to Quebec was built last year.

Was there any Stettin oak plank put into that ship?—None; I believe there was African oak.

When did you last employ any Stettin oak plank in any of the ships you have built?—I think it must be seven or eight years ago; I do not recollect to have used any since then.

What was the cause of your not using any since then?—The price was too high.

What has occasioned that high price?—The duty is one great cause of it; the duty on plank is about five pounds sixteen shillings and sixpence per load, which is as much as the first cost of the American, or nearly so.

Do you get any oak timoer from Hamburgh?—Never, there is none imported to our port; we use principally English oak for the timbering, some foreign plank occasionally for the outsides.

Are not the lengths of the Stettin oak plank, very favourable for ship-building?—Very favourable.

You state that you have but three ships employed in this trade from Scarborough?—It is not from Scarborough they are employed, it is from England generally, only one of them came to Scarborough, there are others employed in that trade besides mine.

How many other ships from Scarborough are there employed in that trade?—As nearly as I can recollect, ten or twelve sail.

---
## Page 125

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

They are employed from the west coast?—From any port where they happen to be, either London or the west coast; sometimes they come to Plymouth, sometimes to Portsmouth.

Do you know the number of voyages they make in a year?—To the east coast generally one; sometimes a ship will make two voyages to the east coast, but it is a very rare circumstance; there were six ships made two voyages to Quebec from London last year; from the west coast perhaps two thirds of them will make two voyages; there is a risk in making two voyages, for if they do not get a good run out the first passage, they never can make two voyages.

What is the present cost at which the hull of a ship can be built in the out ports at present?—We cannot get what the cost is; the price varies very much, according to the size and quality; it runs from about nine pounds to twelve guineas.

Mr. John Hill; Called in; and Examined.

IN what business are you engaged?—A merchant residing in London, but having connexions at Prince Edward's island, being a proprietor of lands in Prince Edward's island, and carrying on business there.

Have you lately returned from that island?—Yes.

Can you state the present situation of that country with respect to its agriculture and population?—The agriculture of the country is daily increasing; the population during the last seven years, I think, has nearly quadrupled, at least it is tripled.

Is the timber trade carried on there to a considerable extent?—According to the size of the colony, to a considerable extent; it is the principal trade, almost the only trade on which returns can be made; I think four-fifths of the returns are made in timber.

Are the population of that colony principally employed in felling and preparing that timber for market, and bringing it down to the shipping?—The population, during the winter-time, are almost wholly employed in cutting and squaring timber.

Are not all agricultural operations suspended during that season?—Most assuredly; the severity of the weather is such that very little can be done in agriculture.

Are the operations of felling and preparing the timber carried on by the inhabitants of the island, or by subjects of the United States?—There is no subject of the United States ever comes there to cut timber.

How many vessels do you load in a season at Prince Edward's island?—I believe, in the season of 1819, there were about 1,900 tons of shipping.

Is that trade rapidly increasing?—The trade has rapidly increased, and I believe is increasing; I cannot state the amount within the last year.

What effect, in your opinion, would the imposition of a duty on the timber from that colony, have upon the continuance of the trade?—I think it must knock up the importation. I have, at this moment, four cargoes of timber imported into this port: Upon the first cargo that I sold, though I had it at a very low freight, I sunk fifty per cent; upon three other cargoes, at the highest price I can at present get, I shall sink 75 per cent, reckoning the cost of the timber at from 15s. to 18s. per load; that is, the simple cost of the timber, without the attendant expenses of clerks and people to conduct it. I should suppose, reckoning all expenses, it must stand me in 20s. delivered alongside.

What net proceeds will remain per load upon the sale of this timber, at the present market price?—Not 5s.; not more.

Are the exports of timber the principal means the inhabitants of Prince Edward's Island have of paying for their imports of British manufactures?—Certainly; the import of British manufactures: these I take to be from 40 to 45,000l. annually; four-fifths of which have been paid for in timber, and at present they have no other means of paying for it.

If the timber trade should be discouraged, what resource will the population of that colony have?—In the present infant state of the colony, they have no other; if it were not for the timber, I could not support the settlers I have upon my lands; I have placed forty families upon my lands: those families all require sustenance for at least two years, before they can produce sufficient to live upon from the cultivation of their lands. They have first to cut the trees down, clear the woods, and afterwards to bring the land into cultivation, build a house, and they must be sup-

Mr. William Tindall.
(24 February.)

Mr. John Hill.
(24 February.)

---
## Page 126

ported by a proprietor or merchant, and the timber is the only thing they can depend upon.

Is not there a considerable cultivation of grain in Prince Edward's Island?—Very little more than supports the inhabitants.

Used not that to be considerable?—No; the accession of inhabitants took up the surplus which could be spared, and the new inhabitants have not yet had time to cultivate their lands.

Did they not use to supply Nova Scotia with grain?—It is said so in the French account, but that is not the case.

Have any establishments for ship-building been formed in Prince Edward's Island?—Yes, a good many; the establishments for ship-building have very much increased, particularly in small schooners, for Newfoundland and Nova Scotia, and the coasting trade.

In your opinion, does the prosperity of this rising colony depend upon the continuance of the timber trade?—I think entirely so; I must give up my concern if the timber is no longer to be brought here, and meet something like the prime cost.

What duty, in your opinion, would the American timber bear without discouraging the importation of it into this country?—When I state to the Committee that at present we are losing from 50 to 75 per cent upon the article, I suppose a duty upon the present state of the trade cannot be borne.

Would not a moderate duty lead to a better selection of the timber, and to its acquiring a better character in this country?—That is a speculative question I cannot answer; it might in time. I have now five thousand load of timber down, which I should conceive to be a total loss in case there is any further obstruction to the import of timber; I have lost already 50 per cent upon one cargo, and at the present prices I must lose 75 per cent upon two others.

Should you be discouraged from importing the quantity of timber of which you speak, if any duty were imposed upon it in this country?—Most assuredly; I am now attempting to sell the timber I have down at thirteen shillings a load, and I cannot get it.

Is the timber of Prince Edward's Island of particularly bad character?—There has been bad timber sent from Prince Edward's Island, but that perhaps is owing to the want of a good selection.

Is it not considered generally inferior to the rest of the American timber?—I am not aware of its being so.

Was not the timber trade with Prince Edward's Island created by the high duties on the Baltic timber?—It first originated there.

And if those duties had not been so high, all the bad timber that has come from Prince Edward's Island probably would not have come here?—Perhaps not; mine is select timber, cut on my own lands by my own tenants, sent home by my agents, and is perfectly select timber.

Did you enter upon this line of business depending upon the protection of the British government?—I most assuredly did; I sent out emigrants at a considerable expense; I have 80,000 acres of land there; I have already established forty families; the only return for this I can receive at present is in timber, and they can no longer have supplies from me if the timber trade is destroyed.

When did you send out those families?—I have been sending out and encouraging others that would come out of themselves, every year for these three years past.

That is from the year 1817?—Yes.

Had you in the year 1817 any reason to believe that the duties on Baltic timber were considered as other than temporary?—I ever considered them otherwise, seeing them to be so beneficial to the colonies; I considered that the government would always protect them on two principles; in the first place, for the encouragement of British shipping; and in the next place, for the consumption of British manufactures, in which the whole is paid for; that induced me to think the government would never suffer the timber trade of America to be interfered with.

Had not those engaged in the timber trade, received intimation from government, previous to that time, that they were not to calculate on the permanency of the present duties?—I never received an intimation of the kind, nor heard of it; but if I had, I should have considered that a promise given by government could be only conditional, and that if the government found the trade beneficial to the country at large, they would still continue that trade, notwithstanding any previous understanding upon the subject.

---
## Page 127

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Do you happen to know whether there was not a letter from the Board of Trade in the year 1817, which was intended for the information of the trade generally, that it was their opinion that an alteration ought to take place?—I never heard of such a letter; and if I had, I should have conceived, after the benefit the country derived from the trade, that the government would have withdrawn such notice, on the principle of encouraging British shipping, and the exportation of British manufactured goods.

Lunæ, 26° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

Edward Solly, Esquire;

Called in; and Examined.

Will you be so good as to state to the Committee, what business you are engaged in?—I was formerly in the timber trade in Dantzig.

Are you at present in the timber trade?—I am not.

How long have you ceased to be so?—I have been connected with my brothers, as agent principally, abroad, and have ceased for about three years.

Were your brothers resident at Dantzig?—No; I was formerly resident there.

Have you, since you left Dantzig, been conversant in the timber trade of Dantzig and the other ports of the Baltic with this country?—I have.

Are you acquainted with the general occurrences that have taken place in that trade, during the last three years?—Yes, I am.

Will you state to the Committee, whether the timber trade from Dantzig to this country, is carried on in Prussian or British vessels, or in both?—In both, but principally in British.

Is that at present the case?—It is.

Are you acquainted with the timber trade of Memel, and the other ports of Prussia?—Yes, I am.

Is the trade from those ports carried on in the same proportion?—In the port of Memel, the proportion of British is larger than at Dantzig.

Is there the same proportion at Stettin?—The import trade at Stettin is entirely in British vessels.

Are you aware, that in the year 1818, the quantity of tonnage employed in the import trade of Prussia, was nearly equal as to foreign and British; and that in the year 1819, there were one forty-eighth part of British?—That arose out of the circumstance, that in this year there was corn imported.

Then, although a great proportion of the timber trade was carried on chiefly in British vessels, in this year, is the corn of Prussia, when imported into this country, brought chiefly in Prussian vessels?—When corn is imported from Prussia in foreign vessels, they do not pay a higher duty than British vessels do; but in the case of timber coming in foreign vessels, they have to pay a duty of 3s. 8d. a load more.

Does that circumstance give a preference to the bringing of corn in Prussian vessels over British?—No, certainly; but they can sooner compete with British vessels in the carrying of corn, because there is no difference in duty.

What proportion of tonnage, in Prussian vessels, might be employed in importing corn to this country, during this year?—I cannot speak to that.

Then, in point of fact, the corn of Prussia was in this year nearly or wholly imported in Prussian ships?—I cannot say that was the case, but it will account for the circumstance of Prussian vessels bearing a greater proportion than usual.

Is there an export duty paid in Prussia on timber or corn?—The export duty on timber has been lessened.

And on corn?—There is a small duty on corn.

Is it the same in British and Prussian ships?—Exactly the same.

Can you inform the Committee why the duty was reduced on timber, and in what proportion it was so reduced?—I cannot say exactly the amount that it was reduced, but it was reduced in consequence of the complaints of the timber mer-

House of Commons Parliamentary Papers Online.
Copyright (c) 2006 ProQuest Information and Learning Company. All rights reserved.

---
## Page 128

chants of Prussia that the trade was decaying, and the government wished to contribute all in their power to continue the trade.

Are you aware of the proportion of the duty that was so reduced or taken off?—I am not aware of it.

Is the freight of timber from Prussia to the port of London, the same in British as in Foreign vessels?—No; the shipper of timber cannot afford to give the same freight to the Prussian vessel that he can to the British vessel, in consequence of this difference in duty.

Is the difference of freight proportionate to the difference of duty?—It is not always exactly that difference.

But nearly so?—It is.

Will you inform the Committee what the current price of freight in British shipping was, and what it was in Prussian vessels during the last season; that is, from the ports of Prussia to London?—In English vessels about 22s. in Prussian vessels 19s.

Are you acquainted with the current shipping price of timber at Dantzig during the last season, if so, please to state it?—I am better acquainted with the shipping price of timber, of good middling quality, at Memel.

Are you acquainted with the shipping price of timber at Dantzig or Stettin?—It generally regulates its price by the price which is obtained at Memel, and I believe it is about the same as at Memel.

Is not the timber of Dantzig more estimated in this country than that which comes from Memel?—There are a variety of qualities; for some purposes the timber of Dantzig has been preferred, as for instance, for the use of the navy.

Are the relative prices of Dantzig timber generally higher than those of Memel?—They are.

In what proportion?—They vary according to the demand; there are periods when there is a greater demand for the Memel, and at other periods there is a greater demand for the Dantzig.

Can you not state an average proportion how many shillings per load the average may be?—Sometimes as much as 5s.

Are you acquainted with the expense of building and fitting out Prussian ships?—I have been a good deal conversant with Prussian shipping; I was a considerable owner of Prussian ships myself in the year 1809; I was owner of 20,000 tons.

Have you had occasion to know what British shipping cost in building and fitting about the same period?—I have heard what the cost of British shipping was at that time.

Will you state to the Committee what Prussian ships cost fitted for sea, built of the best materials, and fitted in a sufficient manner in 1809, per register ton?—I can speak to the fact, that I had a vessel built of 900 tons, which cost 15,000l.

Have you had no instance of the cost of smaller vessels, 600 tons being an excessive size?—The smaller vessels cost equally as dear, and rather dearer in proportion.

Will you state what British vessels of the same description, built and fitted in the same manner, cost at that period?—I cannot speak to that; I should think a British ship-builder or ship-owner would speak more to the point than I am able to do.

Then you are not prepared to state the comparative cost of Prussian and British ships?—If I may be allowed to state as a general rule, ships are never built for the timber trade, the exceptions are in time of war; ships are built to carry valuable cargoes which must be kept dry; they gradually descend in their employ, and when fit for nothing else, still they are sufficiently able to swim a cargo of timber from the Baltic to this country. Thus between the American and French war, there were only two ships of any size built at Dantzig in the ten years, this I know as a fact; and for the same reason not one ship of any size has been built in their Prussian ports since the peace. The ships built in the late war have been wearing out fast, those remaining may continue in competition with British; but while a ship of the lowest value will serve for the timber trade, it is not likely any one would build for that trade at 10l. a ton in a country where money is so much wanted for other purposes, and where discount is often at two per cent per month.

Can you tell the relative price at which Prussian and British ships could be purchased between the American and French war?—I cannot tell the exact price, but I presume that British ships for the timber trade could be afforded cheaper from the circumstance that no Prussian ships were built.

---
## Page 129

Can you tell whether the British ships were not at that time, relatively with the Prussians, cheaper than at present?—I cannot speak to my own knowledge.

Can you state to the Committee, the present comparative charge of navigating Prussian and British vessels in the Baltic trade?—When I had formerly an opportunity of observing the British shipping that came to Prussia, I observed that they were navigated cheaper than the Prussian vessels, from the circumstance that they were navigated in a great measure by apprentices; that they had fewer men, arising from the circumstance that the Prussian ships were much more heavily rigged, their cables were much heavier in proportion; they required, therefore, more men at the anchor. With respect to provisions, we sometimes found it for our advantage to have them laid in in an English port; but if they were cheaper in the Prussian ports, the British captain had the option of laying in his stock there. With respect to the expedition used in the voyage at that period, an English captain and an English crew showed greater activity, and performed their voyage in less time than a Prussian crew.

In point of fact, at present, are not seamen's wages per month higher in the English than in the Prussian service?—The wages of Prussian seamen are about 30s. per month, but I am ignorant of what is the present rate of wages in British ships.

In point of fact, do not the necessary stores, such as cordage, sails, salt, provisions and articles of that nature, cost more in British than in Prussian ports?—The foreign ship-owner, who has been obliged to buy cordage, or any of those articles in a British port, has to pay for them at a higher rate, and the English captain, who has purchased them in the Prussian ports, perhaps will have found them cheaper.

Then in point of fact, they are more expensive in the British than in the foreign ports?—I would beg to point out, that the English captain, if he chooses, may supply himself in a foreign port.

Do you know that British shipping in the Baltic trade are chiefly navigated by apprentices at the present time?—I do not know they are so at the present time.

Presuming a British ship supplies herself with cordage and sails in the Baltic, on her return to a British port, is she not liable to pay a heavy duty on those stores so provided?—I am not aware of the fact.

You have stated, that Prussian ships at present are under certain disadvantages in their navigation, from the construction of their rigging and the size of their cables; is it not to be expected, that such improvements will be made in their system as will, in a short time, remove those objections?—As there is no inducement to build Prussian ships, I do not see how the alteration is to take place.

Is the Committee to understand, that although Prussia possesses within herself facilities for building and navigating her shipping at a less expense than British ship-owners can, were the most to be made of its advantages, that she is not likely to avail herself of those advantages to that extent?—Yes; from the circumstance that ships are not built for the timber trade.

In point of fact, are not a number of first-class British ships now employed in the deal and timber trade?—Certainly; but not the first class, that I am aware of.

Will you inform the Committee, whether there is now a considerable exportation of oak plank from Dantzig to this country?—There is no encouragement for it, though there has been oak plank imported here.

To a considerable extent?—Not by any means to an equal extent with former exportations.

What is the cause of the discouragement to which you refer?—The high duty; the duty is equal to the prime cost.

If the duty be reduced materially, in your opinion, would the importation increase considerably?—It would increase, but to what extent I cannot say.

Is there a considerable exportation of staves from Dantzig to this country?—There has been, but it has not been equal to what it was formerly.

What has led to the reduction?—The high duty.

By what means has the deficient import been supplied in this country?—By importations from Canada.

Is the quality of the staves of Canada equal, or not, to the staves of Danzig?—I have understood that Canada staves are very carelessly converted, and I have heard that the quality is not equal.

What is the comparative price in this market?—I cannot speak from my own knowledge of the exact price.

---
## Page 130

Do you know whether the dimensions of the pipe staves of each country are similar?—They are similar in length, but the Canada staves are very unequally converted, both as to thickness and breadth.

Are they used for similar purposes in this country?—In many instances.

You have stated the freight of timber from Dantzig to London during the last year; can you state to the Committee what was the current freight of timber from Dantzig to Liverpool?—I believe as low as twenty shillings.

Have Dantzig ships resorting to Liverpool any advantage over ships resorting to London, in their return cargoes?—They carry salt back; the salt freight back compensates for the low freight there.

Are you not of opinion, that a ship of a better description is necessary to carry salt than might be employed to carry timber?—Certainly.

Then is it to be presumed that the better description of Prussian shipping are employed in that branch of the trade?—Certainly.

Do you happen to know whether the duties imposed upon Prussian timber have been the subject of great complaints in Prussia?—Very much so.

Have you any reason to believe that if those duties were altered, there would be an increased exportation from Prussia to this country of timber?—I am of opinion that it would increase considerably.

From any information you have, are you led to believe that that increase of export would be followed by a corresponding increase of import of British manufactures into Prussia?—I am of opinion that it would lead to an increase.

Do you happen to know whether any regulations have been imposed in Prussia, touching British commodities, in consequence of the high duties imposed in this country on their timber?—It is only since the year 1818, that all prohibitions of the import of British manufactures have been removed in Prussia, and a complete new system of duties, on a moderate scale, has taken place. The Prussian government at the same time declared, that if the duties on Prussian produce were not lowered, that they should double the duties upon the import of British manufactures.

Are the people of Prussia much disposed to the use of British manufactures, if they could get them?—Very much so.

Did not the same duty on Prussian timber exist in the year 1818, when this more favourable rate of duties on British manufactures was established as exists at present?—The present high duties did exist at that time, and it was in consequence that the Prussian government made this proviso, that if the duties on Prussian produce were not lowered, they intended to double the duties on British manufactures.

Do you know at all what was the effect produced in Prussia upon the importation of British manufactures, by the high duties imposed on timber in the years 1809 and 1810?—The price of timber here in those years was not affected by the duties, the freight, and the expense of bringing timber from Canada at that price, was so much higher than it is now.

Are not the restrictions of the importation of corn from Prussia an object of very great importance to that country?—They are.

Are they also the subject of complaint?—They are not so much the subject of complaint as the high duties on timber; the high duty on timber is considered merely as a premium to the Canadian and to the ship-owners; the restriction of the corn trade is considered as necessary to the existence of the farmers in England, in consequence of the taxes.

Have you a general knowledge of the timber trade between Russia and this country?—No, I have not.

What is the rate of insurance paid on Prussian compared with British ships?—

The British ships that used to come into Memel were mostly insured in clubs, they insured one another, and the rate for a twelvemonth amounted, generally, to about five per cent; now a Prussian ship-owner cannot get his ship insured for a twelvemonth for ten per cent.

On what authority do you state that the premium of insurance in the clubs is five per cent?—I state, that it has amounted to about five per cent, of course it varies; my information is from Mr. Sharp, the ship-broker.

You do not know it of your own knowledge?—No; I am speaking from the information which I have received.

Can you state whether there are any local objections which prevent the Prussian ship-owners from associating in clubs, in the manner as practised in the north of England?—There is no local objection; it is not their practice; they have not attained to that improvement.

---
## Page 131

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

James Borthwick, Esquire; Called in; and Examined.

IN what business are you?—A general merchant, but principally in the timber trade at Leith.

Can you state to the Committee, what the effects of the present duties are, as it relates to deals and timber?—The effect, so far as regards Scotland, has been this; it has altogether excluded, or nearly so, the timber of Norway; it has greatly increased the importation of battens from Norway and the Baltic: it has in some degree diminished in number the deals imported from the Baltic, but those deals have come of a much larger size; on the whole, however, I believe that the cubical quantity of the larger sort has not increased, but a much greater quantity of battens have been imported than formerly.

To what effect is this owing?—It has been owing to the great increase made in the year 1810, upon the duty on Norway timber. In the year 1810, the duty on Norway timber, not above ten inches square, was only 9s. 3d. per load of 50 cubic feet, while the duty on other timber was 1l. 7s. 4d. per load, and on battens, 4l. 9s. per hundred pieces; by an Act passed in that year, the duties on wood in general were doubled, with the exception of Norway timber of the above description, which had been so much favoured, that it never had, like other timber, been subjected to the war duties. In 1811, however, that exception was repealed, and also war duties for the first time were imposed on that sort of timber, whereby the duty thereon was increased six-fold; and in 1813, the duty on all kinds of wood having received an augmentation of 25 per cent, the duty on the above Norway timber, which prior to June 1810 was only 9s. 3d. was now raised to 3l. 4s. 11d. being more than double the cost in Norway. I have mentioned this circumstance, because it accounts for the small deals called, in the language of the customs, battens, having been an article of manufacture in Scotland, while the larger sized deals were not. So long as Norway timber was admitted on a lower duty than the manufactured article, there was one species of timber from which battens could be made in this country with advantage; but when the alteration on the duties above alluded to, took place, the British sawyers were deprived of the only kind of wood which they could manufacture with advantage; the consequence of which has been, that the Norwegians, who used formerly to send great quantities of wood in the form of logs, or timber, now send it in the form of battens.

Are you extensively concerned in the sawing timber into deals at Leith?—Yes.

Are you acquainted with the relative expense of cutting deals in Russia and Prussia, as compared with the expense incurred at your own establishments?—No, I am not.

Presuming a duty per cubic foot was laid on deals, so as to be equal with that paid on square timber, would such an arrangement enable you to cut foreign timber into deals at your establishment, on such terms as you could sell them at the price of deals as imported?—I hold that to be morally impossible.

You could not do it?—No; because it is clear that any man importing a log to cut it into deals, pays a duty upon the cubical contents, a considerable quantity of which is lost in conversion, being either turned into dust by the saw, or lost in the diminished value of the slabs.

Then in order to enable you to manufacture foreign deals at such a relative price in the market, you must be protected by such a proportionate duty; will you state to the Committee, to what extent that protection would be necessary?—I conceive that what would be a necessary encouragement to the sawyers in this country is 30 per cent on the existing duty, and upon the freight and charges.

What do you ground that opinion upon?—I conceive that necessary, because there is an actual waste of 30 per cent in the manufacture of deals and battens.

Do you mean to that extent upon the timber?—Yes; the waste upon Norway log, not being die square, is 13 per cent, and the waste occasioned by the saw-draft is 8 per cent. The slabs contain 26 per cent of the cubical contents of the log, and as slabs may either be imported as broken stowage at half freight and duty, or if not worth freight and duty, would not be shipped to this country, the loss by this is 26 per cent, of which deduct one-half, leaves 13 per cent; that is, 34 per cent is the actual loss to the manufacturer, upon manufacturing battens. By a similar calculation, the loss upon deals is about 30 per cent; but it is believed, the manufacturers would be satisfied with 39 per cent, as an additional duty

---
## Page 132

James Borthwick,
Esq.
(26 February.)

upon battens as well as deals; for they would then be able to compete with foreigners in the manufacture of them.

Does not also some loss arise from logs upon being sawed, being found defective?—It sometimes happens; but I wish to make my estimate within bounds; in Norway logs there is little risk.

Then in point of fact, are the Committee to understand, that if the duties on deals and timber were put on an equal footing per cubic foot, the sawing of timber into deals, in this country, could not be carried on to advantage?—Certainly not; if no extra duty is to be charged in consequence of the waste.

Still, would not the mills of this country be employed in cutting timber to a very large extent into other scantlings, for general purposes?—It is not an advantageous way of employing mills to cut for general purposes. The loss of time in shifting the saws is so great, that saw-mills can only be employed with advantage when a great quantity of pieces of the same size is required, such as deals and battens for flooring, &c.

Then timber cut into scantlings, so as to be fit for the ordinary purposes of house-building, cannot be cut to the same advantage in saw-mills as by manual labour?—Not to so great an advantage as by manual labour; besides there would be comparatively little demand for sawing, even for general purposes, as there would be a great saving of duty, by importing deals in preference to timber in the log, and they can be applied to almost every use.

Would not the effect of laying a duty of 30 per cent more on deals than upon timber, be to make deals 30 per cent dearer in England?—Not exactly so; the deal would be dearer by 30 per cent on that duty, but government would lose it.

How do you mean that government would lose 30 percent of its duty?—Because the manufacturer must import 30 per cent more wood, which is lost by the waste in the conversion.

But would it not, in point of fact, be 30 per cent dearer to the consumer?—No, not quite 30 per cent of the freight and the duty, not upon the prime cost; it would be 30 per cent on the freight and the duty.

Taking all the allowances into calculation, would it not be brought to the consumer at 30 per cent dearer than if imported in deals?—Supposing it would be so, it would not only be a gain to the consumer, it would also be a loss to the revenue to that extent.

How comes it, that if there is that difference stated by you against sawing in this country, that any establishment should be formed for the purpose of sawing foreign timber under the existing disadvantages?—Because when there was a less duty upon Norway timber than upon battens, there was then a raw material for the employment of saw-mills, cheaper than the manufactured article.

When was your establishment formed?—There has been a saw-mill on the spot for upwards of a century, but the present mill was renewed in 1810 and 1811, and was in process of erecting when the act of Parliament alluded to was passed.

What number of establishments are there, which you know of, besides your own house?—I know of few others but my own, most of the saw-mills in Scotland having been abandoned since Norway timber was excluded from our market, by the high duty.

How many were there?—There were three in Leith alone, besides several others.

How many men do you employ?—Six men when in full work; the object is to employ as few men as possible.

Yours is one of the largest?—Yes.

Are there any in England?—I have heard there is one in Liverpool, and one in Newcastle.

Are you acquainted with the contents of that paper? (showing a paper to the witness.)—I am.

Did you draw it?—I did.

Do you know the facts stated in that paper to be true?—I do.

[It was delivered in, and read.]

"REMARKS on the System of levying Duties

"on Deals and Battens.

"BY the hundred of 120 pieces, and at so low a rate, that the merchant finds it his interest to import deals and battens rather than unsawn timber, whereby not only the saw mills of this country, but likewise the sawyers, a valuable class of labourers, are deprived of employment; with calculations showing how much the revenue..."

---
## Page 133

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Revenue would be increased if deals and battens were charged with duty according to their cubic contents, and (as being manufactured articles) at a higher rate than timber, the raw material, as is practised in the case of oak deals, commonly called planks.

[Note.—In the language of the Customs, wood is the general term comprehending timber, deals and battens; when in the form of a hewn tree or log, it is called timber; when sawn into thin pieces, it is denominated batten, if not above 7 inches broad, and deal when exceeding that breadth. In this paper these terms are so used.]

According to the present tariff, European fir deals may be said to pay duty by number alone; for the scale by which they are taxed is so wide as to have scarcely any reference to their value. Deals, whatever be their breadth, 8 feet and not above 20 feet in length, and not exceeding 3½ inches in thickness, pay 20l. 15s. 8d. per hundred of 120 pieces; hence it follows, that 120 deals 10 feet long, 3 inches thick, and 8 inches broad, which contain only 4 loads of wood, pay at the rate of 5l. 3s. 11d. per load, whilst 120 deals 20 feet in length, 3 inches in thickness, and 12 inches broad, which contain 12 loads of wood, pay at the rate of only 1l. 16s. 7½d. per load. A more unequal mode of taxing deals than this, it is hardly possible to imagine; it is prejudicial to the merchant, because it obliges him to confine his purchases to the markets where large sized deals are to be had, and because it makes too much depend on the activity of the foreign agent in securing those of the most suitable dimensions; it is prejudicial to the country, as it enables the merchant, by a judicious assortment of his cargo, to evade the duty, and to import deals much cheaper than they can be manufactured here, thereby depriving the sawyers and saw mills of work; it is still further prejudicial to this country, as it has nearly annihilated the trade with Norway, the deals of that country being of so small a size as to be almost prohibited by the unequal mode of levying the tax; and the trade with Norway was peculiarly valuable, because the Norwegians never drew their returns in money, but took in exchange colonial produce and British manufactures, to the full value of their timber and deals. Any one of these considerations is sufficient to entitle the subject to the serious consideration of government, and if the inequality in the rate of duties on timber and deals, so beneficial in its effect, be found to exist, they will no doubt apply the most effectual remedy. The pernicious effects of the present mode of taxing fir wood, is but too apparent from the returns of customs, which show clearly that two-thirds at least of the European fir wood used in Britain, are imported in the shape of deals and battens, and that consequently large sums of money are annually remitted to foreign countries for the saving of timber; but it is still more forcibly demonstrated by the fact, that the whole of the saw mills which used to be employed in sawing foreign timber, are now without perhaps a single exception, altogether abandoned, or worked merely to keep the machinery in order until such time as an alteration on the duties shall take place.

In calculating the amount of loss sustained by the revenue, in consequence of deals and battens being charged by the hundred pieces, instead of their cubic contents, the great difficulty is to ascertain with accuracy the average contents of the deals imported into Great Britain. It is not enough to discover that deals usually imported, vary in length from 12 to 20 feet, and in breadth from 9 to 11 inches, and that the average of these dimensions is 16 feet in length, and 10 inches in breadth, for there may be, perhaps, 50 deals imported of 20 feet in length, for 1 of 12 feet, and vice versa. The custom-house records can tell nothing, for it is enough for the custom-house officers, that the deals imported do not exceed 20 feet in length, and 3½ inches in thickness, they have no occasion to inquire how many there are of the different lengths between that and 8 feet; their ideas on the subject must, therefore, of necessity, be extremely vague. The only way in which the custom-house can give information that may at all be relied on, is by an actual survey of the deals in bond, and that, not by a cursory glance, but by actual measurement; unless the whole are measured, and an average of their cubic contents struck, there is a risk of falling into the same error; for the officer of customs who inspects the bonded yard, finding that there are in it deals from 12 to 20 feet in length, and from 9 to 11 inches in breadth, may naturally enough report the average length to be 16 feet, and the average breadth, 10 inches; whilst, perhaps, the average length of the whole deals, if taken collectively, might be 18 or 19 feet, and the average breadth, 10½ or 10¾ inches.

---
## Page 134

The importations into Leith of late years, to which the memorialist has paid particular attention, will, he is confident, average little less than 10 loads of timber per hundred deals. He has not access to know, with equal accuracy, the dimensions of deals imported into other ports; but it cannot be denied that it is the interest, and will be the endeavour of every intelligent British merchant, to import deals and battens of such dimensions, as to come under the lowest rate of duty; nor can it be doubted, that great exertions will be made abroad to manufacture deals suited to the British duties, since each foot in the length of a deal, makes a difference upon the duty with which it is charged, of from 10 to 12 per cent of its first cost, and every difference in its breadth and thickness, has a similar effect. It may, therefore, safely be concluded that the deals imported into this country, will average 9½ loads of wood per hundred, but to make ample allowance for the difficulty of overcoming habit and prejudice, 8 loads of wood only per hundred deals, are taken as the basis of the following calculations.

"If a hundred deals, which pay a duty of 20l. 15s. 8d. contain 8 loads of wood, then the duty which they pay per load is 2l. 12s.* and the amount of revenue from deals, divided by 2l. 12s. gives, in loads of 30 cubic feet, the quantity of wood imported in the shape of deals.

"In the parliamentary returns of revenue, deals and deal ends are classed together, and battens and batten ends together; and as in proportion to their cubic contents they pay nearly the same duty, and their number being comparatively small, we shall put the whole together. The whole revenue from European fir deals, deal ends, battens and batten ends, in the year 1818, amounted to 659,751l. 2s. 7d. which, divided by 2l. 12s. (the duty on deals per load, reckoning 8 loads of wood to the hundred deals) gives 253,750 loads as the quantity of wood contained in the deals and battens imported in the year 1818. The revenue from fir timber that year amounted to 407,652l. 19s. 1d. which, divided by 3l. 5s. (the duty on timber per load) gives 125,432 loads, as the quantity of fir wood imported in the shape of timber. The whole importation, therefore, of European fir wood, in the year 1818, amounted to 379,182 loads, whereof fully two-thirds were imported in the manufactured state of deals and battens; and deals and battens, the manufactured article, paid 20 per cent less duty than timber, the raw material; instead of which, to afford reasonable encouragement to the sawyers and saw-mills of this country, deals and battens ought to pay at least double the rate of timber, considering that they contain the most valuable part of the tree, and that there is a very considerable waste in converting timber into deals and battens. Indeed, if deals and battens paid according to their cubic contents, it would follow, as a matter of course, that they would be charged at a considerably higher rate per load than timber, for it could never be the intention of government, that the raw material should pay a higher duty than the manufactured article; and that timber is now charged with a higher duty than deals, can only be accounted for, by the supposition that at the time the duties were first rated, deals of small dimensions were usually imported. So long as the duties bore a small proportion to the cost of the deal, the merchant would continue to import the most convenient size, without regard to its cubic contents; but when the duty came to be double the cost of the deal, his attention was necessarily directed to the saving of duty, and deals of large dimensions were consequently introduced. The most effectual way of making deals pay the proper rate of duty is to charge them (by the load of 50 cubic feet) according to their cubic contents: it is preferable to a graduated scale of lengths, because it equalizes the duty in respect to breadth and thickness as well as length. The annexed states show, in three different views, the probable effects on the revenue, if deals and battens were charged with duty according to their cubic contents, as is at present practised in the case of oak deals, commonly called planks, by each of which it appears the revenue would be considerably improved.

"The first State supposes the duty on timber to remain as at present, at the rate of 3l. 5s. per load, and deals and battens to be charged at the same rate, according to their cubic contents.

"The second State supposes the duty on timber to remain as at present at 3l. 5s. and deals and battens to be charged at 6l. 10s. (double the rate of timber); and that one-third only, instead of two-thirds of the whole importation of fir wood, will then consist of deals and battens.

"The third State supposes fir timber to pay only 2l. 10s. per load, and deals

* The exact duty is 2l. 11s. 11½d. but 2l. 12s. is taken for the ease of calculating.

---
## Page 135

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

and battens to be charged 5l. being double the rate of timber; and that only one-third, instead of two-thirds of the whole importation of fir wood, will then consist of deals and battens.

"The first view is intended merely to show how much the revenue suffers from deals and battens not being charged according to their cubic contents, even on the principle of their paying nothing more than timber, the raw material. The second view shows what the revenue would gain by charging deals and battens as manufactured articles, at a higher rate than timber, the raw material. The third view is meant to obviate the objections of Russia and Prussia, who might (however unreasonably) complain of a rise of duty on deals and battens, unless accompanied by a reduction of the duty on timber. No objection is anticipated from Sweden, as any change on the duties must be favourable to her by which deals and battens are made to pay according to their cubic contents. Nor can either the Canadian or the shipping interest complain of a measure, which may benefit but cannot possibly injure them, for even if the duty on timber should be reduced to 2l. 10s., as proposed in the third view, yet it would not tend to increase the importation of European fir wood, if, as proposed, the duty on deals and battens were at the same time increased to 5l. per load; for the importation of deals and battens would be lessened as much as the importation of timber would be increased by the change.

"The advantages that would result to the country, from the proposed alterations, are as follow:

1. The duty will be more equally collected, and the revenue considerably increased.
2. Employment will be afforded to the sawyers and saw-mills of this country.
3. British manufactures will be again admitted into Norway and Sweden, from which markets they have been excluded, in consequence of their small deals paying as much duty per hundred pieces as the large-sized deals of Russia and Prussia; a circumstance which has proved ruinous to the trade of Norway, and highly prejudicial to that of Sweden.

The only objection that can be urged against the proposed alteration, is the expense and trouble of ascertaining the cubic contents of deals and battens; but considerations of expense or trouble should not be allowed to stand in the way of an improvement, in which the best interests of the country are so deeply involved. Besides, where a high duty is imposed on an article, government ought, in fairness, to be at the expense of levying it equally. Before the year 1809, Norway timber 8 to 10 inches square, paid only a duty of 9s. 3d. per load. A piece of timber of that description contains, on the average, about seven cubic feet; so that at that time, the duty on such a piece of timber came to about 1s. 3½ d. Deals, at present, pay 20l. 15s. 8½ d. per 120 pieces, being 3s. 4½ d. each. If Government did not grudge the expense of measuring the cubic contents of a piece of Norway timber, in order to levy correctly a duty of 1s. 3½ d. surely they will not now hesitate to incur a similar expense, for levying the larger duty of 3s. 4½ d.; and it cannot be denied, that from the uniformity of the size of deals and battens, a cargo of these will be measured at one-half the expense necessary to measure a cargo of Norway timber; at any rate, indeed, any objection on the score of expense, can have no weight in this case; for whatever additional expense may be necessary, in order to collect the duty on deals and battens according to their cubic contents, it will be nothing in comparison to the increase that will accrue from it to the revenue; and the difficulty of ascertaining the cubic contents will, in a great measure, vanish, when the necessary regulations on the subject are adopted; for it is well known, that deals and battens are measured before being shipped, and both the invoice cost and freight are paid according to their cubic contents, the Petersburgh standard hundred being a cubic measure, and containing exactly 105 cubic feet. The merchant has, therefore, a regular specification of his deals and battens, which he may be required to produce, as is done by the importers of other goods; and as there would then be no inducement to import one description of deals in preference to another, cargoes would consist of regular assortments, and the officer of customs would have little trouble in ascertaining their measure, especially as there are already published tables, by which, at one glance, deals may be converted into cubic feet.

The perfect practicability and even ease of taxing fir deals and battens, according to their cubic contents, is put beyond a doubt, by its being in fact, the manner

Jame Botthwick,
Esq.
(26 February)

REMARKS
On the System of levying Duties on Deals and Battens.

---
## Page 136

manner in which the shore-dues of Leith are at present charged. The Memorialist has been favoured with an extract from the books of that office, specifying the number and cubic contents of the deals and battens imported into Leith, in the year 1818, which is herewith inclosed. This document is very important, as it fully confirms the annexed calculation, regarding the revenue from deals and battens; for it shows, that the deals imported into Leith that year contained on the average $9\frac{1}{2}$ loads of timber per hundred pieces of deals; and in the annexed calculations of revenue, the average contents of the deals imported into Britain is taken at 8 loads per hundred, which is making a very ample allowance for the possibility of less care being taken by the merchants in other parts of the country, to accommodate their importations to the actual state of the duties.

"The loss to the revenue from the present mode of taxing deals, cannot be better illustrated than by reference to the above-mentioned document, for it appears from it, that the importation of deals and battens into Leith, inconsiderable as it is, paid £11,674. 2s. 8d. less than would have been paid upon an equal quantity of wood imported in the shape of logs or unsawn timber.

"In the annexed State No. 3, a deduction of 15s. per load is supposed to be made from the duty on European fir timber, as a sort of equivalent for the proposed rise of duty on deals and battens; and it appears that even under that supposition, the revenue of the country would be increased at least £275,530. 18s. 4d. by the change. If it be desirable that a still larger revenue should be drawn from fir wood, it could be done by imposing a duty of 15s. or 20s. per load on American fir timber, leaving the duty on European timber as at present. This, in so far as European states are concerned, would have the same effect as reducing the duty on their timber 15s. per load, namely, that of preventing the importation of European fir wood from being materially diminished; nor could the Canadians with any propriety, oppose the measure, for they will not surely deny that their trade is at present more than sufficiently protected; and it is very obvious, that as two-thirds of the fir wood imported from the continent come in the shape of deals and battens, the rise of duty on these articles would fully counteract any effect a duty of 20s. per load on American timber would have on the importations from that country. An additional duty on European fir deals and battens compensated by a duty on American fir wood, is further recommended by the consideration of its being a tax from which a very large revenue might be drawn, without being sensibly felt by the country, whilst it would meet the wishes of all those who have erected buildings during the war, when wood of every description was extremely high priced; and it is almost superfluous to add, that the shipping interest must be benefited by any change whatever, which induces the importation of unsawn, in preference to sawn timber, as it requires a considerably greater tonnage to bring home the requisite supply of wood when it is imported in the shape of timber, than is necessary when it is manufactured abroad, and imported in the shape of deals and battens.

"The memorialist states these circumstances, to show that relief may be afforded to the sawyers and saw-mill owners of this country, without prejudice to any other party, and not only without loss, but with very considerable benefit to the revenue.

"He humbly conceives therefore, that as a saw-mill owner, he is warranted in contending, that such an alteration should be made on the tariff, either by reducing the duty on timber, or adding to the duty on deals and battens, as shall make it the interest of the merchant to import the raw material timber, in preference to the manufactured articles, deals and battens; but he is willing to confine his demand to this only, that fir deals and battens be taxed according to the quantity of wood they actually contain, or in other words, that large deals may be made to pay more duty than small ones: This can be done in two ways, namely, by taxing fir deals and battens, either according to their cubic contents, or according to a graduated scale, comprehending breadth and thickness as well as length.

"The memorialist is satisfied that the present extraordinary state of the timber and deal duties is owing solely to government being misled in regard to the actual dimensions of the deals and battens usually imported into this country; and he is confident, that if they were taxed according to the quantity of wood they contain, His Majesty's ministers would not fail to regulate the rates of duty so as to afford reasonable protection to the sawyers and saw-mill owners, for he cannot imagine that it is the wish of government, either that the owner of saw-mills should be ruined, or that the sawyers (a valuable class of labourers) should be deprived of employment; yet that such is the effect of the duties as they now stand, is made manifest by the circumstance already alluded to, namely, that more than two-thirds

---
## Page 137

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

of the European fir wood annually imported into Great Britain, come in the shape of deals and battens, and that immense sums of money are annually remitted to foreign countries for sawing timber, and that too whilst our own saw-mills are unemployed, and multitudes of labourers are daily emigrating.

Leith, 3 May 1820.

Ja Borthwick.
