DO you know the extent of capital generally employed in saw-mills?—No, I do not.

Calculating from your own establishment, how many men do you suppose were employed when they were all going; the Committee allude to Scotland?—I dare say there were not above twenty men employed; but I would add, that a great quantity of battens was manufactured by hand in Scotland.

William Price, Esquire; Called in; and Examined.

HAVE you resided in Canada?—I resided in Quebec for eleven years.

When did you leave that country?—In November last.

In what situation were you?—As a merchant I transacted business myself there for six years.

What effect did you find the timber trade produce with respect to the advantage to the country and the landholder?—It is now of great benefit to the landholder, especially to the farmer of Lower Canada.

State in what way?—In the employment it gives them in cutting and hauling their timber in the winter season, when they cannot occupy themselves on their farms; and in many instances, from the real value of the timber standing; likewise from the demand which this trade creates in the cities for agricultural produce; for the supply of the ships, and the large body of workmen employed in the trade.

Is the timber cut by the labourers who are employed in agriculture at other seasons of the year?—It is entirely so in Lower Canada; it is generally cut and hauled by the farmers, their sons or people.

To what timber do you allude?—It is principally 12-feet saw logs, which they get out in Lower Canada for the supply of the large saw-mill establishments at Quebec.

What description of wood is cut in this way, in Lower Canada?—White spruce; almost entirely in Lower Canada.

The Committee understand, that this timber is principally used for the internal consumption of the country?—No, for exportation.

Is it exported to any extent to this country?—Yes; to a great extent.

As compared with the yellow, to what extent?—At least to one-third.

Are you a proprietor of land in that country?—No; I have mills.

Are you much acquainted with the interior of the country, and the manner of conducting the timber business?—I am.

Have you been much through the interior of it?—I have been through every part of it.

In what part of Lower Canada are the chief woods, from whence the pine timber is exported?—From the Ottawa, or Grand River.

Is that timber usually cut by the farmers?—Yes, it is usually cut by farmers.

Is not it a common practice to bring in gangs of American axemen, for the purpose of cutting the timber?—It was so many years ago, but it is not so now.

How long since?—Nine years ago; I should say it was the custom before the last American war, but it is not so now.

Then the business is now carried on by the farmers?—Yes; I should say so decidedly; and more particularly so in Lower Canada; they cut the timber, and haul it out themselves.

Who rafts it?—In Lower Canada the farmers raft it, and bring it down themselves, or people are hired to do it by the merchants.

What proportion of the timber comes from Lower Canada?—I should say one-fourth.

You mean to state, that one-fourth of the timber comes from Lower Canada?—Yes; which is exported from Quebec.

Do you know how it is cut in Upper Canada?—Principally it is cut and hauled to the bank by the farmers in the winter season, who make their payments by this timber to the country merchants, who supply them with goods.

---
## Page 140

What description of people are employed in cutting? — The farmers themselves; the Upper Canada people are excellent axemen, as good as the Americans.

That practice of bringing axemen from the United States, the Committee understand you to say, has ceased? — It has ceased entirely; I have seen nothing of it; on the contrary, I know that the inhabitants of the country cut it themselves.

Do the same people raft it from Upper Canada? — No; it is the country merchants, who hire people to do it; it is generally rafted by French Canadians, who are hired and sent up from the Lower Province; the timber is rafted at the expense of the country merchants, and brought down by them. They employ the French Canadians to work upon their rafts.

Has the timber trade given much occupation to the emigrants, who have gone out from this country? — I do not think it has.

Then you have been speaking of those who are settlers in the country? — Yes, the residents of the country; the emigrants are generally settled much higher up the country, where they do not lumber; I have no doubt, however, that many emigrants from this country are employed in the timber trade.

The objects of the emigrants then, chiefly, are settlement and agriculture? — Yes; though I have frequently seen them working on rafts.

What number of men does a large raft require to bring it down? — A very large raft would require about 50 men.

What proportion do you consider the timber shipped from Canada, to bear to the other exports of the province? — I should say it was full half.

How are the proceeds of that shipping paid for? — The timber is generally paid for in British goods, either directly or indirectly, at Quebec or Montreal.

What proportion of the timber comes from the United States? — I should say about one-third.

Do you mean from one or both of the provinces? — Altogether; I speak of the whole.

The whole of which has been paid for in British goods? — In all cases, a very great proportion of it is laid out in British goods; I can state, that two-thirds of the American timber, purchased last year by me for shipment, was paid for in British goods.

Can you state what are the net proceeds of a load of American yellow pine timber, at the present market price? — I have not shipped any lately on my own account, I cannot therefore speak correctly to that point.

What in your opinion would be the effect of laying a duty upon timber from the British Colonies? — I am not prepared to state anything upon that subject.

What has been the effect of the expectation of a duty being laid on the American timber, or some alteration taking place in the amount of the duties upon the Baltic timber, on the exportation of the last year or two? — I do not see that it has had any effect; it has always been considered in Canada, that that measure never would be adopted.

How do you account for the great increase that has taken place for the last three years in the exports of America? — From the encouragement which the markets gave in that country, in the year 1818, by a great number of ships going out; it was the ships coming out that created the great demand.

Has the exportation of timber been profitable to the Canadian merchant, in 1819 and 1820? — I have understood in most cases it was a losing trade.

Then would they have continued this trade as a losing trade, without some expectation of the kind to which the Committee has alluded? — It has been considered, that the great loss in the trade has been caused by the great number of ship-owners who have entered the trade.

Then the trade has been created by the shipping, and the shipping has not been called for by the natural demand of the trade? — In some measure it has; but many ship-owners have gone out to seek for freight, or to purchase, which has occasioned an increased demand.

Is there not a great deal of timber that would not be exported, except at the present low rates? — No, I think not; I think we are getting more and more particular in the manufacturing of timber; I think we were more particular in our timber last year than ever we were before.

Then you do not think, that any measure which led to more care in the selection, and the squaring, and the preparing of the timber, would be beneficial to the trade? — I really am not prepared to answer anything that relates to the market on this side.

The Committee is speaking of the Canada market? — I was told, when I came here

---
## Page 141

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

here, that the red pine timber, which I shipped last year, was as good as Memel timber.

Is there much red pine timber shipped from Quebec?—The proportion of red pine timber shipped from Quebec is very large.

Can you state in what proportion?—I should think in the proportion of a fourth; I have been in the habit for some time of shipping for the London market, and I have very seldom shipped any white pine timber.

Has it not been in the habit of your house, to confine itself to the shipping of the highest description of timber from America to the London market?—I was ordered to ship that which would be most advantageous in the London market.

Has the timber trade contributed much to clear the country?—It has certainly.

Mr. Thomas Morris; Called in; and Examined.

WHAT situation do you hold?—I am surveyor general.

Were you not directed to prepare a report for the customs, respecting the manner of taking the duty upon deals?—Yes.

Was it exclusively on deals that you were to report?—On deals and battens.

Not on timber?—No.

Have you that report with you?—I have not yet finished it; but I have the tables of the calculation with me.

Have you found it to be practicable, without any material difficulty in the collection of the revenue, to adopt the principle of taking the duty on the cubical contents?—There would be some difficulty in it at first, I think.

Can you suggest to the Committee any change in the present system of collecting the duties on deals, that would make those duties bear more equally on deals of different descriptions than they do at present?—None other than by cubic measure, or by a scale of lengths and thickness and widths.

Have you thought of such a graduated scale, as you could submit to the consideration of the Committee?—I have not.

If a graduated scale was made in the following manner, that no alteration was made in the width of the deals, but in place of all deals under three inches and a quarter being considered in one class, they were divided into two; and that all deals from eight feet to twenty in length being considered as a class, and divided into three, would such a system be attended with much increased difficulty to the revenue officer, or inconvenience to the importer?—Certainly not to the revenue officer; and I should think not to the importer.

Could you suggest to the Committee any better mode of collecting the duty, so as to make it bear more equally than it does at present, upon the different dimensions of deals?—I do not apprehend that the system of charging the duties by cubic measurement would be very troublesome; and I do not know you can hit upon a more equitable mode of charging the duties.

Could you charge it equally so upon what you call deal ends?—Yes.

Are you aware of any objection there is to its being so charged upon deal ends?—I am not aware of any; so far as regards the revenue certainly not.

Will you state to the Committee the system you would propose to adopt, in measuring the cubic contents of deals?—I apprehend I can answer that question only, by saying that deals of certain dimensions must be piled together, so as to enable the officer to ascertain how many there are of each description; when that is done, the thing is as simple as possible.

After the piles when so measured were taken asunder, no means would then remain to check their contents, in the event of any fraud being practised with the connivance of the officer?—After the deals are delivered out of the custody of the officer and separated, there would be no means of identifying them, but the same difficulty occurs now, after the account is taken by the officer and the deals separated, under the present system.

Are you not of opinion that it would materially facilitate the collecting of the duty, if a certain scale were established with more gradations than at present exists?—There would be the same difficulties if the duties were laid on by scale, as if by cubic measure.

Then it is your opinion that no material inconvenience would arise, either to the trader or to the revenue, from a system of collection founded strictly upon the principles of cubic measure?—No material inconvenience certainly.

William Price,
Esq.
(26 February.)

Mr. Thomas Morris,
(26 February.)

---
## Page 142

Do you think it would be advantageous to the revenue, if deal ends liable to the low duty, were reduced from eight feet to six feet?—Certainly, it would be advantageous to the revenue.

Do you not believe that in consequence of the length being fixed at eight feet, many cargoes consisting of deal ends exclusively, are brought to this country?—Yes, I believe so.

Do you believe that those are cut to that size, for the purpose of coming in under the low duty?—I have reason for thinking so.

Martis, 27° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

Mr. James Young;

Called in; and Examined.

In what line of business are you engaged at present?—As a broker, in the disposal of timber from the north of Europe and the Colonies.

How long have you been engaged in that line of business?—Upwards of thirty years in the timber, from the north of Europe particularly; from the Colonies probably not more than fifteen years.

Have you any information to give to the Committee on the state of the timber trade generally?—I have: The supplies from the north of Europe, considering Russia, Prussia, Sweden, and Norway, as included, and from the Colonies, at the same time, are much larger than by any possibility can be consumed in this country; the competition, therefore, that is constantly excited is destroying both trades, I conceive, by the result of a long experience, that if the supply was more interrupted than it is from Norway, a good trade in British ships might be supported from Russia, Prussia, Sweden, and our Colonies; it is the contiguity of Norway, with its capability of supply, that constantly keeps our market in a feverish and depressed state. A great prejudice has existed against the timber imported from the Colonies, particularly the yellow pine timber; that prejudice is gradually subsiding; and with respect to the red pine timber, it is, by many competent judges, considered as good as the timber from Prussia; the conclusion that I draw from these premises is, that our Colonies would be equal to a supply of all the useful and necessary wood that is wanted in this country in the progress of time. I am not aware that I can give as to general information, more than I have now stated; the timber from the north of Europe, undoubtedly, may be had cheaper and better; but the two trades cannot exist together in their present state, the one or the other must be given up.

What is the general price of Baltic timber in this market at present?—Of Memel timber, from £6 l. 5 s. to £6 l. 10 s. per load.

What is the price of the yellow pine timber from the Colonies?—From £3 l. to £3 l. 5 s.

What is the price of Riga timber?—£6 l. 15 s. to £7 l. There is an unusually short supply this season from Memel and from Riga.

The duty upon Baltic timber being at present £3 l. 5 s. and there being no duty upon American timber, if 5 s. was taken off the duty upon Baltic timber, and 5 s. laid on the importation of American timber, would that, in your opinion, produce any alteration in the state of the market?—I think it would go to the annihilation of the timber from the Colonies; I know that it does not upon the average produce 5 s. a load the proceeds.

Is not that state of the market produced by an overtrading which has taken place within the last two years, and in some degree by a greater importation, in consequence of an expectation that a duty would be laid on American timber?—A large decrease of consumption has taken place in the timber trade, and fewer stocks have been kept on hand from the uncertain state of the duties; it has been usual for the timber merchants to hold a large stock of timber, but from the uncertainty with respect to the duties, they have constantly bought from hand to mouth.

Is there a large stock at present on hand?—There is a large stock of American timber, and it is in the hands of the importers.

---
## Page 143

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Can you inform the Committee what is the extent of the present stock of North American timber in the kingdom?—I believe about 30,000 loads in the London market.

Can you speak to the stock in the kingdom generally?—I cannot.

What proportion does 30,000 loads bear to the consumption of that species of timber during the last year in the London market?—I suppose about two-thirds.

At present there is eight months supply of that timber in the market?—I should imagine so.

Within what period may a fresh supply be expected from America?—Within from three to four months.

Then when the fresh supply arrives, there will be a stock remaining in the market equal to four months supply?—Yes, I apprehend there will be.

Do you consider that an excessive stock?—I do, because the supply is capable of continuing up to the month of December; and I am now stating what would come within four months; we should have three or four times that quantity in all probability before December.

Do you think the stock at present in hand excessive, it appearing that it will last for four months beyond the commencement of the new supply?—I do.

You state that the prejudice against the American yellow pine timber in this market is diminishing?—It is.

You have stated that the price of Baltic timber is at present double the price of American yellow pine?—It is so.

How then do you reconcile the opinion, that when the prejudice is subsiding, and where the difference of price is double, a duty of 5s. and a reduction of 5s. would annihilate the American trade?—Chiefly, I believe, from the apprehension entertained, or the expectation which may be entertained, of a reduction of duty on the timber from the north of Europe.

The question is put upon the supposition of 5s. being taken off the one, and 5s. put on the other?—There is a loss now equal to fifteen shillings a load on the import from America; if a further prohibition is put on of 5s. it will make a loss of 20s.

You state that you have been acquainted with the timber trade for the last thirty years; state to the Committee what were the relative prices of Baltic and American timber in the years 1809 and 1810?—As nearly as my recollection enables me to state it, one-third.

Was not the price at that period of Baltic timber from twelve to thirteen pounds a load, and the price of American yellow pine from ten to eleven pounds?—My recollection only gives me about nine pounds; I think the American timber was about nine pounds, but I cannot speak to particulars.

Then if the price of American timber was then 9l. and the price of Baltic 13l. and at that time there was a considerable consumption of American yellow pine; now that the difference is double, ought not the consumption to be greater in proportion?—I am persuaded, that it would be greater in proportion if the trade believed that the duties from the north of Europe would remain the same, then they would be induced to purchase largely at the present prices of this article, but as it is they will only take the smallest quantities which they can dispose of, which necessarily leaves in the hands of the importer such a stock as he is incapable of sustaining, which we are constantly pressing in the market for sale.

Has not the depreciation which has taken place within the last three years, in the price of American timber, been in a great measure occasioned by the excess of importation beyond the consumption?—The principal occasion has been the decrease of consumption.

What do you mean by the decrease of consumption; do you mean to state that the consumption of timber in the country generally, has decreased?—In London and the neighbourhood, as far as it has come to my knowledge, it has generally decreased within the last twelve months.

Upon what do you found that opinion?—The difficulty I have found in procuring sales for the timber committed to my care.

Is the stock of timber of all descriptions, now in the port of London, greater than it was at this period in the last year?—I should think it was.

In what proportion?—Not largely.

Then it is to be presumed, that the sale of the last year has been nearly equal to the importation?—I suppose it has.

---
## Page 144

Are you aware that the importation of the last year was remarkably large?—It was remarkably large from the Colonies, but remarkably small from the north of Europe.

If the question of duty was set at rest, would not the over-import of timber from the British colonies, which has been excited by speculating on the expectation of an alteration in the duties, be discontinued?—I am induced to believe that large purchases would be made of American timber at the present price, if the belief existed that no decrease of duties would take place from the north of Europe; I conceive if the question of duty was set at rest, that no expectation existed of a decrease of duties on timber from the north of Europe, a much larger consumption of the colonial timber would take place, and much larger purchases be made.

Would not the imposition of a small duty on timber from the British colonies, lead to a more careful selection of it, and tend to improve its value?—Undoubtedly; but that would lead to lessen the quantity most considerably.

In what proportion do you think that a duty of ten shillings per load would diminish the relative consumption of American timber in this country?—More than one-half.

Would not the taking off a small part of the duty on the Baltic timber have the same effect in producing a better selection of American timber, as the laying a small duty on the American?—I do not think it would have the same effect, I think it would have some effect; but I am quite sure that it would destroy the colonial trade one half, if any reduction were to take place of the duty on the timber from the north of Europe.

That amount of duty do you think might be beneficially imposed?—None; most certainly the trade cannot exist under its present disadvantages, it is righting itself; they lose so much in the freight, and so much in the measurement from their bad squaring of the timber, that it is very much improving in its state to what it was; it comes in a much better state this year than it did in the former years; it has bettered itself every year, finding the advantage of bringing it in a state better squared, for they lose so much upon the dead freight, and so much upon the measure, it is evident to the parties interested in the trade that they must take more pains in making that timber square.

Do you mean that the whole of the immense consumption within the last two years has been of a better quality, and better squared than that which was imported before?—I do, generally it has.

And in this immense quantity consumed, there has not been a greater proportion of lower and worse description of wood?—I do not think there has; but the two chief causes which have occasioned the reduction of price, has been the decrease of consumption, and the unwillingness of the timber dealers to hold any stock, under the expectation of an alteration of duty, which has obliged the importers to press upon the market beyond the capability of taking it off; the sellers have been obliged to have sometimes three public sales in a week, from the inability of the importers to keep it.

Has not that been owing to the increased importation?—No; chiefly to the decreased consumption, and the unwillingness of the merchant to hold.

What description of individuals are the importers of that timber, merchants or ship-owners?—Merchants are the largest importers.

Has not a considerable quantity of timber been brought from the Colonies to this market during the last and preceding seasons, for the account of the owners of the ships in which it was brought?—Certainly not to my knowledge a large proportion.

What proportion do you consider not a large proportion?—Not one-tenth.

Do you believe that independent of the state of the shipping, which was in want of employment, the importation of timber during the last two or three years would have been carried to the extent to which it has?—I believe it would not quite, but it would not have made a large difference; the causes of the weight on the market are the decrease in the consumption, and the expectation of an alteration of duty; I may be allowed to state as a fact, that the timber I have been chiefly compelled to force on the market for sale, has been for merchants, and not for ship-owners; I scarcely recollect an instance of a ship-owner compelling me to sell; we have now a great deal of timber which is held by assignees of merchants.

Mr. Thomas Forrest; Called in; and Examined.

YOU are a ship-owner at South Shields?—I am.

Where have your ships generally traded to?—I have had them mostly in the coal trade, the Baltic trade, the North American timber trade, and the West India trade.

---
## Page 145

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

What number of ships belong to the port of Newcastle?—Between eight and nine hundred.

Do you know the amount of their tonnage?—About 190,000 tons.

What number of ships belonging to the port of Newcastle are engaged in the British North American trade conveying wood to Great Britain?—I believe between two and three hundred; the ships of the largest tonnage go upon those voyages; the smaller ships keep in the coasting trade.

What is the amount of tonnage of those ships?—It will be nearly half the tonnage of the port.

What is the present state of the shipping interest in the port of the Tyne?—It is very much depressed, the same as the general shipping interest of the country; I do not know any voyage on which a ship could be sent, that would pay the current expenses of the voyage.

Has the North American timber trade been attended with loss lately?—Yes, it has been attended with loss.

If any additional impediments were thrown in the way of that trade, would it be materially injurious to the shipping interest?—It would very much injure it.

Can you give the Committee some information as to the actual expenses of a voyage to the North American colonies?—I have made up a statement at large, and I have an abridged one.

ARE you acquainted with the first cost of Prussian and Norwegian ships?—I have made inquiry of captains of those ships, and have had conversations with them. I find, that the Norwegian ships can be built for less than one-half of the British, and the Prussians at about one-half.

Is not the expense of navigating the foreign ships much less than that of the British?—It will be about half, or not quite so much; the wages and victuals will not be one-half.

Are there not some very large establishments in the Tyne, for building and repairing of ships?—There are large establishments, which have cost considerable sums of money, on the Tyne, for building and repairing ships.

In what state are those establishments at present?—They are partly given up; and those which are not given up, are merely going on with their apprentices, in building or repairing.

Are there many ships built in the port of the Tyne?—There are not many building in the port of Tyne just now; those establishments which have apprentices perhaps may be building; but they build as little as possible.

You conceive the reason why the ship-builders in the port of Tyne still keep their yards and docks there, is for the purpose of employing the apprentices they have?—I know that to be the fact.

Acquainted as you are with the peculiar interests of that part of the country, do you not think that the imposition of a duty on the North American timber would have a very materially injurious effect upon the port of Tyne?—I should conceive any thing, which throws the shipping interest out of employment, would have an injurious effect upon the port of Tyne, which so much depends upon the shipping.

Your opinion is, that an alteration of the timber duties would have that effect?—In my opinion it would lead to that effect.

Do you think a greater importation from the Baltic would take place in consequence of an alteration of the duties?—I think it is very probable that it would.

Is it your opinion, that a smaller quantity of tonnage would be employed from the port of Tyne, in the consequence of the proposed alterations?—I should conceive half the tonnage would bring the same quantity from the Baltic in all probability, and part of that tonnage would be foreign.

Are the ship-owners of North and South Shields engaged as merchants as well as owners?—They are merchants in the coal-trade, but seldom in any other trade; it very rarely happens, that they engage in any other commodities.

Could the ships that are now engaged in the British North American trade be converted into the Baltic trade?—Part of them might.

What would prevent the whole of them being employed?—There are some of them too large for many of the ports in the Baltic; for instance, Memel is a bar-harbour; and at Dantzie there is but little water.

You have stated, that there are between two and three hundred belonging to the port

---
## Page 149

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

port of Newcastle employed in the timber trade; they are not employed from that port, but carry on that trade from the western coast principally?—I apprehend there are ships belonging to Newcastle trading to all the ports of the kingdom.

Do not the greater proportion of those ships go to the western coast of the kingdom and get employment from thence, by the merchants in the timber trade?—Part of them do.

Is not that part a great part?—I should not apprehend it is the greatest proportion. How many ships do you apprehend carry on the trade, bringing their cargoes to Newcastle?—I apprehend a very small proportion come to Newcastle; a great many come to London.

Have you much Baltic trade from Newcastle?—There is not a great deal of Baltic trade from Newcastle.

Do you know whether it is increasing or diminishing?—I cannot state that.

Is it chiefly in Newcastle ships that the cargoes come?—I think it is mostly in Newcastle ships or Foreign ships; the reason I cannot speak so particularly to that is, that I have been a good deal from that port within these few years.

You have spoken to the expense of Foreign ships, and to the great advantage they have over us in point of expense, do you happen to know that there is a great proportion of the Northern trade carried on in English ships, with Prussia and Russia?—Part of it is carried on in English ships, but they are trading to a loss.

A great proportion is carried on in British ships?—Yes.

And always has been?—Yes.

Can you account for that being the case if there is so great an advantage to Foreign ships?—I account for it in this way, that the ship-owners in England at present have larger capitals than they have in Norway or Prussia, that the ships are sailing to a loss which the Prussian ship-owners cannot sustain, and that that is the reason why they are falling off now.

Can you account for the proportion of British shipping, when they were not sailing to a loss?—This country has the largest proportion of shipping at any time.

When the shipping was sailing to advantage, how do you account for so large a proportion of the trade from the North having been carried on in British ships if all the advantages you state are in favour of Foreigners?—The Foreigners have not so many ships, nor ever had; all the ships they had in Prussia or Dantzig were employed at that time; all the shipping they had was engaged in trade.

If there was any increase of the trade at this time would it not then be carried on in British ships?—I have already stated that the British ship-owners are losing money, and that they have outcarried the Foreign shipping by that means; but it is impossible for me to state how long they will continue to sail at a loss.

If the advantages you state were decisive in favour of Foreign ships, how is it the English ships were not driven out of the trade when it was carried on to advantage?—I apprehend that the whole of the Norway and the Dantzig ships were kept trading, and that they were not laid up in their ports.

Then it arose from the want of ships in those countries?—I conceive so.

Have there been many ships built in those countries lately?—I do not know, I have a certificate of a ship built in 1819, at Pillau.

Was there much ship building carried on at that time?—I am not aware.

Can you inform the Committee of what classes the shipping are that are generally employed from Newcastle in the timber trade of our North American Colonies?—I should conceive ships of different classes, from old ships (say twenty years old) of about four hundred tons; and others which are new of the same tonnage; there are likewise some smaller vessels down to perhaps 250 tons.

Are ships of the first class employed in that trade?—There are many ships of the first class that have been provided for other trades which, owing to the general depression of shipping, have gone into the North American trade.

Is the depression the same in the Baltic trade?—It is much the same in the Baltic trade, but the ships in that trade are generally smaller.

There are ships of the first class belonging to Newcastle, employed in the deal and timber trade in the Baltic?—I think there are; but some ships that go to Petersburg in this way are loaded with a part deals, and a part hemp and tallow, and other articles, which they generally bring in first class ships.

Are you acquainted with the present price of American yellow pine timber at Newcastle?—No, I am not much acquainted with the price of timber.

Then why should you, being ignorant of the price of timber in the markets of this country, be of opinion, that a moderate duty on American timber would throw a considerable

---
## Page 150

a considerable proportion of the shipping now employed in that trade out of employment?—I know so far of American timber as to know this, that the freight has been 50s. per load upon it, or was last year; that the charges are about five or six shillings, or perhaps seven shillings; and I understand it has been selling in the market for about £1. or £1. 5s. per load.

May not that state of things have been produced by an excess of trading, or over-importation?—I think it is very likely that it has.

And that that state might exist whether the duty existed or not?—I cannot speak to that point.

Are not the ships in the northern ports principally insured in clubs where they insure one another?—In the general way they are.

What has the premium of insurance per annum cost, being insured in that mode during the last year, or the year before?—In a general way, I conceive they average from seven to ten per cent; but the last year I understand some of them got up as high as fifteen per cent, on account of the great losses which they sustained the former part of the last year.

Mr. Francis Treadwell; Called in; and Examined.

WHERE do you reside?—In London.

Have you formerly sailed in a Prussian vessel?—Yes; I commanded a vessel belonging to Elias Roupel, esquire, of Memel, from the year 1810 to last October.

In what navigation was she principally employed?—Principally between Memel and London, and Memel and Dublin.

In the timber trade?—Yes, constantly.

Was this ship in which you went built for the timber trade?—Yes, she was.

Can you state the comparative expense of the building and fitting of a ship in Memel and in Great Britain, at the present time?—There is nearly one half difference; a ship and her materials may be purchased ready for sea at Memel for about £1. 15s. a ton, that is British registered tonnage.

What are seamen's wages at Memel?—Memel is the most extravagant port there is in Prussia; that is no criterion to go by; in general in Prussia the average of seaman's wages are 25s. per month; I have never paid more than 30s. during the last four years.

Are not sails and cordage and every description of necessaries for the outfit of a ship during her voyage, and for her supplies, much cheaper there than in this country?—Yes, more than one-third cheaper; the highest price we ever paid for beef was fourpence a pound.

Are not biscuits and flour also cheaper in a greater proportion?—Yes, only 12s. per cwt. ship bread.

What is the present price here?—I believe one or two and twenty shillings per cwt.; I am not at present in employ, so that I am not aware of the current prices in London.

Is a much greater number of hands necessary to navigate a Prussian ship than is necessary to navigate a British ship of the same tonnage?—No; I have commanded a Prussian ship now for seventeen or eighteen years, and I never had a man more in the one than I should have had in the other; I never had more than sixteen men and boys in the Maria, a ship of 375 tons.

Have you known instances of British ships of that tonnage being navigated by a smaller number of hands in the Baltic trade?—No, we went in those ships entirely on the British plan.

Was the owner of that ship a British subject?—No he was not, he was a subject of Prussia.

Was any part of the crew British?—Only myself.

Was there any ship-building going on in the last Autumn, when you left Memel?—No not in Memel.

Do you think the shipping of Prussia is increasing or decreasing?—It is much the same I think.

What proportion of the Prussian timber trade is carried on in British ships?—I am sure I cannot say.

There are a good many British ships employed in it, are there not?—Yes; and there are a great many Prussians, but I never entered into the proportions.

What is the reason if there are such advantages in favour of the Prussian ships, that

---
## Page 151

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

that the Prussian timber is not all sent in Prussian ships?—I cannot say that; it is a matter I never studied.

Have you had occasion to know whether ship-building is going forward in the other parts of Prussia at present?—No, I have not had any occasion, for I have not had any communication with the other parts lately.

You do not know whether the Prussian ship-owners are in the habit of building ships to replace those that may be worn out?—No, I cannot say.

Mr. John Lampson; Called in; and Examined.

WHAT is your situation?—Landing-surveyor in the customs in the port of London.

Have you been engaged much in the landing of timber?—Considerably some years ago, till within the last four or five years, from the year 1811 to 1816.

You are aware of the manner in which the duties are taken at present on timber and deals?—I am.

Do you see any official difficulty there would be, in taking it both upon one and the other on the cubic content?—It is a thing I think practicable, but which would be attended with considerable difficulties, particularly in its origin, and perhaps for a considerable time, and also with additional expense to the revenue.

What are the difficulties which you contemplate?—That of ascertaining it by ships that are delivering their cargoes afloat in the river into lighters.

Is that the only difficulty that occurs to you?—I see no other.

Would it not require the measuring of a great proportion of the deals, owing to the difference of the lengths in which they come at present?—The principal part of them must be measured; but in time it would become such a system, that they would bring them in a more uniform manner than they do at present, and the necessity for measuring them would be less; in the first instance, it would be attended with greater difficulty than it would afterwards.

What additional number of officers would such an alteration require?—I do not suppose many; there is but one boat officer (the delivering officer on the river); there were originally two. It has been found expedient to discontinue one of them, from the great number of ships that have gone into the docks to deliver; it would require at least another boat officer. His salary would be, if one of the juniors, 400l. a year; if senior, 500l.

Would any difficulty attend a change in the scale by which the duties on deals are taken, if it was thought expedient to introduce one which would contain a greater number of gradations than exists at present?—That would depend, in a great measure, upon the extent of gradations; if they were very numerous, they would be equally difficult with the cubical measure.

Do you think, from what you know of the timber trade, that any advantage would be derived from any alterations in the existing mode of taking the duty which you can state?—I am not aware of any change that would be advantageous to the revenue.

Are you of opinion that were the duty on deals to be paid by the cubic contents, not only difficulty would attend ascertaining it, but it would lead to a greater risk of frauds being committed on the revenue?—I am not aware that it would, if under proper regulations, but it would be more expensive.

You have stated that there would be considerable difficulty in cases where ships discharged their cargoes in the river?—In all such cases.

Are not all the deals now landed in bonded premises for the security of the duties?—All bonded deals are landed and those in docks by law; in that situation I consider it is perfectly practicable without any risk and without any increase of expense; but on the river I doubt it.

Are any deals landed in the river that are subject to duty at all, or where any examination of their contents is necessary?—They are not landed in the river; they are delivered into craft from on board the vessel lying in the stream, where they are surveyed by the proper officer afloat; they are finally delivered and put into the charge of the merchant to take them where he pleases.

By the present system all which is to do is to count the deals, whereas under such an arrangement as that proposed to you, they must be measured?—Yes; deals are not put alone into a craft, but a variety of other articles, so that there is a very close inspection now.

---
## Page 152

The only measurement you now have is, whether they exceed eight feet and are below twenty?—Yes, that is done by the eye generally.

Are you not in the habit occasionally of landing cargoes of deal ends?—I never saw a cargo entirely of deal ends.

Has not the importation of deal ends increased of late years?—I cannot say within the last four or five years; previous to that time there was not an unusual importation of them.

They were considered as broken stowage?—They generally were; I have not been on the river for the last four or five years.

Mercurii, 28° die Februarii, 1821.

The Right Honourable THOMAS WALLACE, in the Chair.

John Henry Pelly, Esquire;
Called in; and Examined.

YOU are a merchant in the trade with Norway, resident in London?—I am.

What time have you been in this trade?—About five or six years; the house in which I am engaged, has been established for a great number of years.

Is it a house that carries on business to a great extent?—It did, to a large extent.

Can you state what has been the effect of the imposition of the high duties imposed in 1809 and 1810, upon the Norway trade?—The imposition of those duties has almost excluded Norway from the market; now that a peace is come, it will certainly do so.

State in what way?—There is only one port, Christiana, from which we have imported deals this last year; all the other ports, Moss, Dram, Longsound and Christiansand, from their deals being inferior, are excluded, and timber completely so. The duty presses particularly on Norway deals and timber, partly on the account that Norway cannot produce either of large size or lengths, and the duty being the same on 8 feet 8 inches wide, as on 20 feet 11 inches wide; and partly from the very high duty which affects Norway more than the other northern countries; and from wood from Canada, which is allowed to be imported duty free, interfering more with Norway than with any other country. To elucidate my observation, that Norway is particularly affected by an increase of duty, I beg to refer the Committee to Mr. Norman's evidence before the House of Lords, in his statement of the number of deals imported by him, and the duty paid when it was £1. 13s. 4d. per hundred:

| Average quantity imported per annum | C. Duty |
|-------------------------------------|---------|
| from 1793 to 1796, both inclusive    | - 10,777 | £17,992 |

The duty was then increased to £1. 9s. per hundred;

| Average quantity then imported from | C. Duty |
|------------------------------------|---------|
| 1793 to 1796, was reduced to        | - 4,837 | £21,525 |

Not more than 20 per cent increase of revenue, although the new duty was 170 per cent on the old. This may fairly be taken as the criterion of the whole trade, our own books showing a similar result.

In 1797, the duty was increased to £1. 1s. In 1811 it was doubled, and became £1. 2s. The Appendix, No 6, showing the quantity of timber and deals imported from all the northern ports, as well as Canada, furnishes me with a Statement in corroboration of the opinion I have expressed.

By this it will appear, that on the doubling of the duty in 1811, the average importation of timber from Norway and Prussia decreased nearly half, whilst from Sweden and Russia it rather increased; but in the article of deals, the diminution from Norway is immense, whilst Sweden Russia and Prussia, taken together, import nearly the same as before the advance of duty: Sweden rather less, Russia rather more; but Norway, from C. 24,961, the average annual importation of the preceding 13 years, has not been enabled, from the effect of the duty, to import for the 7 years on an average above C. 8,580 per annum, consequently a deficiency of revenue, from the increase of duty, of 70,209 l. per annum; the consumer paying at the same time double the amount. For C. 24,961, at 9 l. will produce 224,649 l. and C. 8,580, at 18 l. 154,440 l.

From the same Statement it will appear, that the average importation from the north of Europe has diminished, both in timber and deals, very considerably in the last 7 years; but that, including the importation from Canada, the consumption of timber has been more; but not above two-thirds of the quantity of deals have been imported into England. It may easily be supposed, that in the last 7 years the average consumption should be more, from the increased wealth of the country, but the high duty alone can cause a diminution of expenditure; and I think it will appear, if there had been no increase of duty in 1811, that it would have produced as much revenue.

I have

---
## Page 154

I have omitted fractional parts, and have not taken any notice of the further increase in 1813, which must be first deducted from the amount in the Exchequer, to ascertain the above. The calculation is sufficient to elucidate my view of the subject; and I am confident that, with respect to Norway deals, the Committee, on reference to the Exchequer, will find, that if the year 1820 does not show that the revenue from this article, at 20l. 15s. 8d. per hundred, produces less than the average of the years 1785 to 1792, when the duty was 1l. 13s. 4d., that the year 1821 will show that result.

How far do you attribute that to the mode in which the duty is taken upon the deals?—In consequence of Russia being enabled to bring deals of a longer length than Norway, the mode of taking the duty favours them so much, that they have been enabled in the course of the same period to increase their importation, while Norway has diminished.

Is not the importation from Norway confined to a particular length?—It is.

The Russian deals are much longer?—They are.

May not that increased importation have arisen from the superior convenience of having the deals of a greater length than Norway could supply?—From being enabled to import them of a longer length, certainly she has an advantage over Norway. Suppose the prime cost 10l. the hundred, when the duty was 1l. 13s. 4d. and now 2l. 10s. 8d., the difference in duty between long and short deals is about as two-to-five, but I will take it at half; half 1l. 13s. is 10s. 6d.; only 8½ per cent on prime cost; half 2l. 10s. 3d. is 10l. 15s. 1d. and is 107 per cent on prime cost.

Does not the contiguity of Norway, and the difference of freight, nearly compensate for the difference of duty on the different lengths of deals?—Certainly not; if it had, they would have retained their relative proportions of import; but my answer to the preceding question will show an advantage beyond three times difference of freight.

It being stated that the duty on five loads and 20 feet of Norway deals is the same, by the practice which now prevails of taking the duty with that on nine loads and 45 feet of Russian deals, to what extent would the difference of freight between the two countries compensate that difference?—Not being acquainted with the rate of freights to the Baltic, I cannot answer that question at present with accuracy.

What is the price of Norway timber now in the market?—There is none; we have not imported a single load the last year, and very few the preceding one. Our importation has been, in some years, very considerable.

What was the price, compared with Memel timber, of the last import?—From 25s. to 30s. under the price of Memel. There is always this difference, Memel being now 6l. a load; we could not obtain 4l. 10s. the duty being 3l. 5s. would leave only 1l. 5s. for cost, freight and charges, at which it cannot be imported.

What difference in the existing regulations of the duty on deals would, in your opinion, place the Norwegian deals and the Baltic deals on a fair footing in this market?—I think nothing short of a graduated scale, or taking them upon the cubical contents.

Then that would, in fact, be nearly doubling the present duty upon Russian deals?—It would but only make it equal to that from Norway.

Do not you think that the effect of that would be to throw the great importation of deals into Norway, instead of bringing them from Russia and Prussia?—Certainly not.

Explain why?—Upon the average I have given, Russia has increased in so very trifling a degree, that it would only put Norway into the situation in which she was from the year 1790 to 1811.

Do you know the comparative quality of the wood imported from Norway with that imported from Russia and Prussia?—For deals, those from Christiana are superior, but the Dram deals and the Longsound deals, and Moss deals, fetch no more in the market than the Russia deals do.

Upon the supposition of the duties being equal, would not that give a preference to the Norway deals?—No doubt; but when the duties were less on referring to the average, it would only place Norway in the same situation that she was with respect to Russia before.

Is there not a considerable difference in the number of voyages that can be made between Norway and Russia?—Certainly.

---
## Page 155

Does not that make a considerable difference in the freight that is paid?—Certainly.

With those advantages, and the quality, is it not calculated to throw the chief importation of deals into Norway, rather than to divide it, as it is now divided, between Norway, Prussia and Russia?—I can only state upon the average, that it would not have that effect; the importation from Norway might be considerably increased, but it would reduce the importation from the other parts very little. I found my opinion on the statement, that when the duty was doubled, in 1811, although the importation from Norway was considerably reduced, it did not create a correspondent increase from the other ports on the continent.

What was the last price of deals at Christiana?—We do not buy any there.

Are not deals sold there?—I believe not many for London; they are sent generally to the houses of agency.

In what ships does the importation from Norway usually take place?—Sometimes in British and sometimes in Norway; but the larger proportion in Norway, certainly.

Do you think that proportion is increasing or decreasing?—Decreasing; they have sold almost all their ships.

Are they building fresh ones?—Not that I know of.

If the trade from the North should increase, do you think the proportion of British ships employed in that trade would increase or decrease?—Increase, certainly.

Are you at all acquainted with the cost of building ships in Norway?—No, I am not; but, they wished so much to get rid of their ships, that I had several to sell last year, if I could have got five pounds a ton for them, masts, yards, and all their stores; they were fir ships.

Do you know what is left to the importer, after all the expenses and charges are paid upon the price at which Norway deals are sold?—On one hundred and twenty deals from Christiana, at the present price, it would leave him about seven pounds a-hundred, but from any other port in Norway it would not leave four pounds, consequently the deals from any other port but Christiana are excluded.

Can you state what proportion that bears to what would be left to the importer upon a hundred and twenty deals brought from any other ports in the north of Europe?—No, I cannot.

Do you happen to know what are the ships in which the timber is usually conveyed from other ports of the north of Europe besides Norway?—Not being engaged in that trade, I can only give hearsay evidence; I believe principally British.

Has not the importation of deal ended, since the imposition of the high duties, very much increased from Norway?—No, not very much increased; it has been stated there is a particular advantage to Norway, which is not the case.

State how?—Thirteen pounds ten shillings being the price of Christiana deals independent of duty, is at the rate of one shilling a foot; on one hundred of eight-foot deals clear of the duty, it would be one and a penny a foot, consequently an advantage of three halfpence a foot on the eight-feet deals; but as a great many of our nine, tens, and elevens are cut down to eight feet, taking the average at ten, it would only produce eleven pence a foot.

Then the deals of nine, ten, and eleven feet are cut down for the express purpose of escaping from the heavy duty?—They must be, or they would not pay freight and charges; the heavy duty would overcome freight and charges. And the reason why Norway has so many more than any other country is this, that the wood in the other countries will enable them to cut their wood to make deals of sixteen feet; upon an average we cannot cut above thirteen feet, therefore, if any accident happens in the falls, the butt end is spoiled, and every deal that does not run the twelve feet must be reduced; but a sixteen-feet deal from the other ports would make a fourteen, thirteen, or twelve. But all our smaller deals must be cut down at once to eight feet. The sacrifice is to the shipper, and the consumer gets the advantage of it. The Norway merchant gets no advantage from it, because, if you will divest the two articles of duty, the net proceeds to him are only 1s. 1d. All the advantage of the difference of duty is to the consumer. Two eight-feet deals are sold always as one twelve-feet, and produce, as above, 1s. 1d. per foot. Had we been able to have imported the same quantity in sixteen-feet lengths, it would have produced 1s. 4d. per foot.

If no alteration should take place in the scale by which duties are now levied on

---
## Page 156

deals, what effect would the continuance of such a state of things produce upon the trade of Norway?—It would annihilate it, unless the duty was generally reduced, and then we should stand, if it was reduced to what it was before 1811, in a better relative situation with respect to the other ports on the Continent, and our importation would increase; but theirs would not much diminish.

Do you think, that if any increase were to take place in the export of Norway timber, there would be a considerable increase in the export of British manufactures?—Certainly.

What reason have you to think that?—Because the Norwegians always have taken a very large quantity of British manufactures; they use British manufactures in every way.

Are there any laws prohibitory of British manufactures at present operating in Norway?—None that I know of.

The laws that operate in Sweden do not extend to Norway?—Certainly not.

And none have been enacted for that kingdom?—None that I am aware of.

Do you know whether Norway has been in the habit of taking to the extent of her imports?—I believe to the full.

Is there any great debt in Norway to British merchants?—A very considerable debt, and which they would readily pay in wood.

In what way has that been accumulated?—The debt has been accumulated under a variety of circumstances; advances upon exportations; advances upon the produce of Norway; and part of the debt has been in consequence of the confiscation of British property, in retaliation for the seizures at Copenhagen. The Norway merchants that were indebted to the British merchants were obliged to pay the money to government.

It was not incurred by any excess of export of British manufactures or British commodities, beyond the import from Norway?—I cannot say positively that that was the case.

Has it not been the fact, that the imports from Norway have very much exceeded the exports?—Directly, I believe, they may have done so; but I do not think it necessary to trace the direct importation to Norway, inasmuch as our bills go through the continent, and may be traced home, in some instances, to the British manufacturer here; and bills that are drawn for the produce of the country, hardly ever come straight from Norway here, but the importation of timber from Norway is now principally confined to what we call debenture timber, which is allowed to come in at a very small duty, for the use of the mines in Cornwall, and which, by the last accounts we have, was about 32s. a load, delivered in Cornwall. The Memel timber, independent of the duty would be 3l. 5s. and they cannot deliver it there, but the consumer has it from Norway, at the above rate.

Is it not the practice with the merchants who trade to Norway, to keep their correspondents, or the persons from whom they draw their wood there, in debt to them, with a view to securing the importation from particular parts of the country, or a particular estate?—Certainly not, we would rather they would not owe us a farthing; they can only pay the debt in wood, and unless we are allowed to import that wood at a moderate duty, the whole of that money must be lost to the British merchants. If the government would allow a certain quantity of wood to be brought duty-free, to pay that debt, the Norway merchants have no objection to give up the trade altogether; for I believe every one of us shall close our concerns this year, and we must set down with the loss, unless we have some means, in some way, of getting the money back again. The last cargo we sold from Moss, was 2,094l. 4s. 10d. the charges were 2,309l. 12s. 2d. leaving a loss of 214l. 18s. 4d.; the duty was 1,566l. out of it.

What rate of duty, in your opinion, on Baltic timber would give the largest revenue, considering the interests of the different trades?—The largest duty would be produced by reducing it half, or to 1l. 2s. 6d.

Do you think that could be done without the complete destruction of the North American timber trade?—I think it certainly might; the difference of freight, I think, is about 1l. 5s. and that would place Canada, if the goods were of an equal quality, on a par with the north of Europe.

That is, assuming the goods to be of an equal quality?—Yes; but if you allowed them the whole freight, which averages on deals and timber about 2l. the load, that would be placing Canada on the banks of the Thames; and if they cannot compete then as to quality, the consumer is only paying the difference.

State what you conceive would be the amount of duty that would place the

---
## Page 157

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

timber of the respective countries upon an equal footing, so as to give a fair competition?—I have before stated 25s. difference; but as I do not think the article so good, although her advocates say it is, I therefore think it would be on a fair competition in less than 40s. difference of duty.

William Stewart, Esquire; Called in; and Examined.

IN what line of business are you engaged?—A merchant, and engaged in the colonial timber trade, as an importer on commission.

You have of course directed your attention to the question of the duties upon timber?—I have, particularly since the publication of the Lords Report, directed my attention to a consideration of the subject, on finding, from the published evidence, that however high the character of the parties examined, their sphere of observation was too limited to enable them to afford accurate information; as will appear by extracts I have made from official documents, contrasted with the statements of witnesses first questioned.

A Norwegian merchant states, pages 11 & 12,—THAT in consequence of the present system of duties on foreign deals, and the exemption of colonial wood from duty, that "the exportation from Norway, during the last five or six years, has decreased very much," and "during the last year has amounted to very little." It has likewise been stated, that "more than one-third of the ships engaged in the trade with Norway are British; that the Norwegians ships are decreasing very fast, and that as they are decreased, and lost, they do not build them again."

The following Extract from the official returns, page 131, will, however, serve to refute the opinion, that the deal trade with Norway has been declining during the last six years; and although the importation of 1815 (which was generally excessive) is included, shows, on the contrary, that the total quantity imported in the last three years, exceeded that of the preceding three years.

|------|-------|-----------|---------|

| 1815 | 17,797| 5,680     | 3,336   |

Which figures prove, that the representation is equally erroneous in regard to the shipping employed in the trade with Norway.

## Page 158

A Swedish merchant states, (page 24) that "he has observed the exports to Sweden have very much diminished, in consequence of the heavy duties rendering the import of timber and deals unprofitable to the Swedes, which prevented them ordering goods in return in a great measure."

The exports to Sweden have not however diminished in consequence of the duties on timber and deals, for it appears by the official returns (pages 120 & 121,) that our exports from 1799 to 1802, previous to the existence of those duties, were

The erroneous conclusion that there has been a diminished trade with Sweden in consequence of the duties, arises probably from having compared the periods of peace

peace with that period of the war when Sweden was an entrepôt, and when it is well known not only that the bulk of the shipments from hence were for the consumption of other countries, but that Prussian wood was continually imported under the denomination of Swedish.

An importer from Norway states, page 28, that "The present duty on deals is not only a protecting duty to the British ship-owners; but that it will have the effect of a prohibition from Norway, and that the present duty gives the Canadian merchant a monopoly."

It appears, however, from the official returns, pages 131 and 137, that there were the following importations from Norway during the last four years; viz:

which proves that the Canadian merchant has not a monopoly of the market.

What has been the operation of those duties upon the timber trade?—Their effect has been to diminish the importation from the Baltic, and to encourage it from the Colonies.

Has that diminution of the timber from the Baltic been progressive?—It was progressive to a certain period; but it is no longer progressively diminishing, as will be seen by a Statement I beg to submit.

## Page 160

Has not the proportion of the importation from North America greatly exceeded the diminution of the import from the north of Europe?—It has. I will present to the Committee a Statement of the particulars.

Average Annual Import of Fir Timber and Deals into Great Britain.

| Years | Fir Timber Loads | Deals Hundreds of 120 Pieces | Deal Ends Hundreds of 120 Pieces | Battens | Fir Timber Loads | Deals and Deal Ends Hundreds of 120 Pieces | Battens |
|-------|------------------|-----------------------------|---------------------------------|---------|------------------|--------------------------------------------|---------|
| 1790-1802 | 174,385          | 49,591                      | 4,388                           | 5,225   | 2,479            | 370                                        |         |
| 1803-1806 | 218,857          | 45,938                      | 5,111                           | 5,291   | 10,519           | 803                                        |         |

Do not you conceive that a consequence of this has been, a great excess of the importation beyond the demands of the country?—There has been a considerable excess beyond the consumption.

Do not you attribute to this excess the very low price at which timber is selling?—Yes I do.

Do you happen to know, whether the effects produced by the high duties on the wood trade in Russia have been the cause of much dissatisfaction to the Russian government?—I should apprehend that they have not, because I do not consider that the Russian government attach much importance to their wood trade. I conceive that they would wish to encourage the exportation of their more valuable commodities, as tallow, iron, hemp, flax, and other articles. There was a period, about 1798, when the Russian government prohibited the exportation of wood, unless a certain quantity of iron was shipped along with it.

Do you think that a reduction of the duties on Foreign timber would lead to a greatly increased demand for that article?—A reduction of the duties would lead to an increased demand for that article, certainly.

Do you think that an increased import of that article, in consequence of the increased demand you have stated, would be followed by a correspondent exportation of our manufactures?—I should think not, because the imposition of the duties did not occasion a diminished export of our manufactures, but the contrary. An average of the years 1817, 1818, and 1819 shows there was a great excess above the average of 1799 and 1801, prior to the imposition of the high duties; as will be seen by a statement I will beg to submit to the Committee.

The witness delivered in a statement, which was read, as follows:

Mem.:—To the above may be added the portion of Manufactures said to be introduced through the channel of the German Fairs.

IS not the fair inference from that fact, that the inclination of the Russians to receive our manufactures and produce was such, that if their means were increased of paying for them by an increased import from that country, it is probable that those manufactures would be sent in still greater quantities?—The increased export of wood could contribute to it, but to a very limited extent from its very considerable value.

But in proportion to that influence you think it would contribute to it?—That is a problematical question, but I conceive the existing state of the trade enables them to take as much of our manufactures as they require.

Do you not conceive, that the Norway deals being all of small dimensions, have laboured under an unfair disadvantage in the British market, in consequence of the present system or duties, and the exemption from duties of the Colonial deals?—No, I do not think they have.

State your reason?—I conceive Norway has its full share of the deal trade, as will appear by a paper I beg to submit.

Mr. Stewart. In answer to their trade labouring under unfair disadvantages, I have brought another statement which contains the contrast of the Norway deal trade, and the Canada deal trade, which under the existing system would leave 1 l. 9 s. 1 d. per load of fifty cubic feet to the importer of Christiana deals; which a graduated scale of duties would advance to 2 l. 19 s. 4 d. while the Canada trade which they contend, has the monopoly of the market, leaves only 1 l. 6 s. 7 d.

---
## Page 163

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

According to the present system of duty, the standard hundred of deals pays less than would be chargeable on the same quantity of timber in the log; would it not be expedient to approximate those duties?—The effect of that would be to give an additional trade to Norway. Long deals now pay a less duty than timber in the log; short deals, a higher duty. The effect of equalizing the duty between deals and timber in the log would be to cause a reduction of duty on the short deals imported from Norway, and to increase the duty on the deals imported from the Baltic, in the proportions which appear in the paper I beg to submit.

[The witness delivered in the paper; and it was read, as follows:]

| Pieces | Length | Duty |
|--------|--------|------|
| 120    | 12 feet 3 inches nine-inch deals contain 5 loads 20 feet, and pay duty | £ 20 15 8 |
|        | 5 loads 20 feet timber, in the log, pay duty | £ 17 11 — |
|        | REDUCTION OF DUTY on 120 pieces 12 feet 3 inch nine-inch deals (the dimensions usually imported from Norway, agreeably to the Report of the Commissioners of Customs) if charged upon the cubic contents, at the same rate as timber in the log | £ 3 4 8 |
| 120    | 16 feet 3 inch ten-inch deals contain 8 loads, and pay duty | £ 20 15 8 |
|        | 8 loads of timber, in the log, pay duty | £ 26 — |
|        | INCREASE OF DUTY on 120 pieces of 16 feet 3 inch ten-inch deals (the dimensions usually imported from the Baltic, agreeably to the Report of the Commissioners of Customs) if charged upon the cubic contents, at the same rate as timber in the log | £ 5 4 4 |

Is not the effect of the present regulation, as to the duties upon deals, to render it very disadvantageous for the importer to bring into this country any deals under the lengths of from 16 to 20 feet?—Of course upon the present principle of collecting duty, the longer lengths a merchant can import the more wood he brings for the same duty, and he necessarily derives a proportionate advantage.

Therefore in all the lengths of deals, at present the duty upon deals from 8 to 15 feet is so excessive, that they cannot be imported into this country, excepting at a very great increased duty to deals of a greater length?—The shorter deals certainly pay higher in proportion than the long deals; but the short deals are imported from Norway, which, from its proximity, is able to bring them at a less expense of freight.

Might not many deals from Russia, Prussia and Sweden, from whence the long deals are at present sent, be brought of shorter lengths but which are at present excluded, in consequence of the scale by which the duties are regulated?—The timber of those countries is of larger dimensions, and I am not aware that it would be of any importance to them to bring short deals; the Norway timber is of small dimensions.

In point of fact, do you know that before the late great increase in the duties on deals, a very large proportion of short deals used to come from those places?—I am not aware, whether that was or was not the case.

Looking to the interest of the consumer in this country, is it for his advantage that the short deals should be excluded from coming to this market?—The short deals are not excluded, for they come in immense quantities.

But at an increased price from the higher proportion of duty?—Certainly.

What effect would be produced, in your opinion, on the consumption of colonial wood if a duty of 20s. per load was imposed upon it, without altering the present duties on foreign timber?—I do not believe the colonial timber could be brought, except to a very limited extent, if such a duty were laid.

What effect would be produced by imposing a duty on the colonial wood of 10s.?—The effect would be to reduce the consumption from the colonies, and in consequence of communications I have lately had with persons from thence, I am disposed to think that the reduction would be in the article of inferior quality, consequently the general character of the importation would be improved.

What would be the effect of imposing 10s. on the colonial wood, and taking 10s. off the existing duties on foreign wood?—An almost total exclusion of the colonial, I should think.

State your reasons?—There is a loss upon the market price at present; and my reason for thinking the duty which would be sufficient to correct the evil of shipping large quantities of an inferior description, might be of service, would be, that the market

---
## Page 164

market price would in consequence be advanced; if the market price did not advance, the colonial could not bear any duty at all.

What, in your opinion, would be the effect of laying 5s. on the colonial timber, and taking 5s. off the foreign timber?—I do not think it would produce the same beneficial effect as laying 10s. on the colonial timber, because I think that to whatever extent it may be possible for the colonial wood to bear a duty, it will effect a greater reduction in the export of inferior quality, and will therefore tend more to advance the general character of the colonial wood; because also I think that 5s. taken off the foreign duties would be immediately laid on in the shipping price on the other side, and become a benefit to foreigners at the expense of our revenue.

What would be the effect, in your opinion, of taking 10s. from the duty on foreign wood without imposing any duty on colonial wood?—I think that there would be no improvement in the character of the colonial wood, and therefore I think the measure would be injurious both to the revenue and the colonial trade. My reason for thinking it is possible the colonial wood might bear a duty, is the expectation that it might improve the general character.

What effect do you think might be produced by taking 20s. off the duty on foreign timber upon the consumption of colonial timber?—I conceive it would scarcely be used at all; I think taking 20s. off the present duty would nearly exclude it from the market. I am of opinion, a reduction of the foreign duty is not likely to produce the same result as a duty upon the colonial.

Then your opinion is, that 45s. which would then be the difference in duty, between the importation of foreign and colonial timber, would not be a sufficient protection to colonial timber?—The difference which would be a protection is entirely a matter of speculative opinion; and I would rather answer that question by reference to figures, showing what have been the relative importations and the relative prices, leaving the Committee to draw their conclusion; I have brought a statement of the relative importations, and the relative prices for six years.

[The witness delivered in the same, and it was read as follows:]

| Timber per Load in London; the Import of each preceding year. | Imports into Great Britain, extracted from the Official Accounts, page 130 to 140. |
|---|---|
| Years. | Memel. | American Yellow Pine. | Difference per Load | Years. | Foreign Timber. | Colonial Timber. |
| --- | £. s. d. | £. s. d. | £. s. d. | --- | Lands. | Lands. |
| 1816 January | 6 — — | 4 15 — | 1 5 — | 1815 - - | 107,889 | 107,552 |
| 1817 — | 6 5 — | 4 12 6 | 1 12 6 | 1816 - - | 60,158 | 131,842 |
| 1818 — | 6 12 6 | 5 — — | 1 12 6 | 1817 - - | 80,608 | 140,282 |
