| 1820 — | 6 — — | 3 15 — | 2 5 — | 1819 - - | 102,864 | 207,065 |
| 1821 — | 6 2 6 | 3 5 — | 2 17 6 | 1820 - - | Returns not yet published. |

From your knowledge of the timber market here, to what extent do you think the consumption of colonial timber would be reduced, if the difference which now exists in price between Memel timber; the price of Memel timber being 6l. 5s. and colonial timber, the price of colonial timber being 3l. 5s. The existing difference being therefore 3l. should be reduced to 30s.?—Judging from the previous relative differences which have obtained in the market, I should say the colonial would be reduced one half by being brought within that compass.

Is not the colonial timber as valuable for some uses as the foreign timber?—Quite as suitable I believe for some purposes, and more suitable in many instances because it is wider.

Then if a difference of 30s. were to exist between them, would it not be used in preference for those particular purposes?—Certainly, for those particular purposes.

You stated, that laying on a duty of ten shillings on the colonial timber might be advantageous to the trade of the Colonies, and that taking off ten shillings from the duty on foreign timber would be very disadvantageous; why do you make this difference in the two modes of bringing the prices of the two sorts of timber to the same relative difference?—I explained in a former answer, that I understood it was probable the effect of laying on a duty might be to improve the general character of the

---
## Page 165

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

the importation, and by improving the general character an advance in the market price might arise.

Why do you think that taking the ten shillings off the Baltic timber would not have the same effect?—Because the character of the colonial wood would not be improved by that measure; but the imposition of the duty would prevent the same speculative importations.

Would not the effect be to make the duty in either case, as a distinction, ten shillings less between the two timbers than it is at present; that if a duty to the extent of ten shillings were taken off, the person shipping American timber must ship it under the impression of that duty being taken off, and therefore that he must prepare for the increased competition, does not it approximate in the same way?—There is only the same difference in the figures, but I consider the effect would be different. I do not know how to assign any other cause than I have already expressed for that opinion, but I conceive from my communication with colonists and others, that the result would be as I have stated.

Would not the effect of the imposition of 10s. in the duty be to raise American timber 10s. or more than 10s.?—If it improved the character of the wood and reduced the supply, the market price would advance, I think that 10s. duty would reduce the supply, and then an advance in the market price might take place.

Is not the timber trade at present very much carried on by the colonial merchant and the ship-owner?—The trade is not now confined to merchants, but the ship-owners have latterly been large importers.

Do you consider that the natural state of the trade?—I consider that it is not the natural state of the trade.

Can you state in what years you conceive the wood trade to have been in its most natural state?—That is a question I am not prepared to answer.

Would you say it was in its natural state about the year 1817?—I do not feel myself able to answer the question at present.

You have adverted to the loss sustained at present upon the importation of wood; do you attribute that to the excessive importations that have taken place for the last two years?—Yes; I consider it to arise from the redundant importations.

You have stated, that there are some purposes to which the American wood is preferable, and many to which it is equally applicable with the wood from the north of Europe?—I do consider, that for purposes where a large surface is required, it is preferable for a great many purposes; the preference is always given to the cheapest article that can be procured.

Do you not consider those, generally speaking, the inferior purposes of the country?—I do.

Do you know what proportion the consumption of wood, in these inferior purposes, bears to the general consumption of wood in the country?—It constitutes a very large feature in the consumption.

Do you think, that for those purposes a comparative cheapness, to the amount of five and thirty shillings, would be sufficient to cast the balance in favour of the American timber?—As a speculative opinion I should say yes, for some purposes.

Are you acquainted with the nature of the timber trade between the United States and Canada?—I have some knowledge of it from report, not from a residence in the colonies, for I never was there.

Is there much timber, or any great quantity of deals brought into Canada from the United States, which are exported and received in this country on the same terms with Canadian timber?—I should conceive a considerable quantity.

What regulations would, in your opinion, be best adapted to ascertain at Quebec what timber and deals were of the produce of Canada, and what was the produce of the United States?—Certificates of origin.

Would it not be expedient, that the custom-house at Quebec should themselves inquire into the truth of the origin, and grant those certificates only upon being satisfied that such proof was correct?—I should conceive there would be no other way of obtaining correct certificates of origin.

Then such examination at Quebec, and such certificates, would, in your opinion, secure the revenue against any attempt at imposition by shipping timber, the produce of America as the produce of the colonies?—If proper precautions were observed by the officers granting the certificates.

Have you any account of the annual imports from Russia, extracted from the official

William Stewart,
Esq.
(28 February.)

## Page 166

William Stewart, Esq., official returns, and the average exports from Great Britain to Russia, from the official returns?—I have.

[The witness delivered in the same, and it was read as follows:]

Average Annual Imports from Russia, extracted from the Official Returns.

| Year       | Timber Loads | Deals Hundreds | Deal Ends Hundreds |

The trade in timber from Norway being at present, in a chief degree, carried on in Norwegian ships, what would be the consequence if the duty on Norway ships were so laid as to give a preference of the carrying-trade to British ships?—If would be advantageous to the British shipping interest. I do not see any objection to it; for I do not think Norway has any claim, upon the ground of reciprocity, to the benefits she now enjoys. I will submit a statement which shows that Norway has annually a large balance to receive from Great Britain; therefore, her alleged debt is not the natural result of the trade between the two countries.

[The witness delivered in the Account, which was read, as follows:]

Imports of Wood into Great Britain from Norway, agreeably to the Official Accounts, pages 130 and 131; in which Paling Boards, Spars and Lathwood are not included.

| Year | Fir Timber Loads | Oak Timber and Plank Loads | Masts Number | Deals Hundreds of 120 pieces |

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

Official Value of Exports from Great Britain to Norway, in the years 1817, 1818, 1819; extracted from the Official Accounts, pages 120 & 121.

| Year | British Produce and Manufactures | Foreign & Colonial Merchandises | TOTAL |
|------|---------------------------------|---------------------------------|-------|
| 1817 | £41,453                         | £31,975                         | £73,437 |
| 1818 | £76,466                         | £54,306                         | £130,772 |
| 1819 | £60,172                         | £29,174                         | £89,346 |

Total Exports: £293,485

To which may be added the amount of goods allowed under a Treasury Warrant to be exported to Norway without entry, viz., 5l. for each of the crew, and 20l. for the captain; although the collectors of the Customs can prove they seldom avail themselves of the privilege.

Balance payable to Norway in money, being more than one-half the cost of the Imports from that country into Great Britain and Ireland: £496,515.

You have stated to the Committee, that you are intimately acquainted with the timber trade from our North American colonies to this country, as a commission merchant resident here, receiving consignments?—I am.

Can you inform the Committee, for account of whom the timber so imported in the years 1816, 1817, and 1818, generally, were shipped?—Partly for account of the persons having establishments in the colonies, partly for merchants resident in this country.

In 1819 and 1820, has the trade undergone any particular change?—Yes; the reduction in the rate of freights has induced ship-owners to import on speculation.

During those years, has the merchant resident in this country continued to order shipments of timber, on his own account, from the Colonies?—The yard keeper, or timber merchant, has not imported so largely as in the preceding years, owing to the unnatural and depressed state of the trade.

Can you state, whether the operation of the present duties has not been to produce an importation of cargoes of deal ends?—The present regulations, with regard to deal ends, have been the means of introducing entire cargoes of that description, to the injury of the revenue, which will be illustrated by a Statement I beg to submit to the Committee.

[The witness delivered in the same, and it was read, as follows:]

The duty payable on 120 pieces 12 ft. 3 in. Deals, by a Foreign ship, is £21. 10. 8.

Loss to the Revenue:

Which on 185 pieces 12 ft. 2½ in. Battens, being the same cubic contents is £16. 13. 6. only 4. 17. 2.

180 pieces 8 ft. 3 in. Deal Ends, d° d° - 11. - 11. - 10. 9. 9.

120 - - - 12 ft. 3 in. Paling Boards, d° d° - 3. 17. 8. - 17. 13.

The evil might be remedied by admitting at the low duty, as Deal Ends, those only not exceeding six feet, which would confine the import to the limit intended by the legislature, viz., such proportion as is necessary for storage; and the article lately introduced under the denomination of Paling Boards being of the same substance and dimensions as Deals, and applicable to the same purposes, instead of being admitted at £3. 17. 8.

Ought to pay the full duty of £21. 10. 8.

Under the present regulations that apply to paling boards, has not a great deal of wood, applicable to all the best purposes, been introduced under that name?—A great deal; and I should venture to recommend to the consideration of the Committee, that the duty on deal ends should therefore commence from six feet, and that measures should be taken entirely to exclude the article now denominated paling boards, which is not that which was originally in the contemplation of the legislature.

ON THE FOREIGN TRADE OF THE COUNTRY.

N° 5.

An Account of the number of Loads of Timber, with the expense per load, required in building a Frigate of 46 Guns of Canada Fir, and Baltic Fir, respectively.

FOR the building of a frigate of 46 guns wholly of fir timber, which can be done with very few exceptions, by the present method of constructing ships, about 1,100 loads will be required. The contract prices of Baltic fir, with the duty added, and Canada fir, exclusive of the duty, are the same, viz. 6l. 12s. 6d. per load; this for a frigate built of Canada fir, will amount to 7,387 l. The duty on the Baltic fir is 3l. 4s. 11d. per load; deducting this it will cost the government for timber, 3,717 l. The workmanship and other materials will be the same in each case. When sold out of the service, the value of either will be the same or nearly so, as the wood is generally used for fuel.

Navy Office,
19th February 1821.

Rt SEPPINGS.

N° 6.

To the Right honourable the Lords of His Majesty's Privy Council, for the Affairs of Trade.

Memorial of the RUSSIA Company, by their Governor Sam' Thornton, Esq.

THE period fixed by Act of Parliament for the importation of wood duty-free, from our North American colonies, being about to expire:

Your Memorialists beg leave to represent the grievance which they feel from the present system, and to submit the following considerations, which may show, that while it is ruinous to the trade in which your Memorialists were, till the introduction of that system, beneficially engaged, it is injurious to the general commerce of the country and to the interests of the community at large, and unproductive of public revenue in any proportion to these disadvantages.

When the present high duties on Russia and Baltic timber, deals, &c. were first laid on, the difference between the Baltic and Canada freights in favour of the former, was equivalent to the exemption of duty in favour of the latter. Thus, in the instance of fir timber, the freight in 1812 from the colonies, was 6l. to 6l. 5s. per load, while that from the Baltic was 2l. 10s. to 2l. 15s. and the duty on the latter 3l. 5s.; making the aggregate of freight and duty on Baltic timber, somewhat less than the freight alone from Canada.

But since the peace, freights having declined to 2l. 5s. on timber from Canada, and to 1l. from the Baltic, the comparison will stand thus;

| Freight from the colonies | £. s. d. |
|--------------------------|---------|
| No duty                  | £2 5 -  |

| Freight from the Baltic  | £. s. d. |
|--------------------------|---------|
| Duty                     | £3 5 -  |

making the aggregate of freight and duty on Baltic, exceed the expenses of importation on American timber, by 2l. per load, or equal to about 100 or 150 per cent on the prime cost.

The effect of this difference, which is in fact an enormous bounty on wood from America, has been to force the exportation from thence hither, to a very extraordinary extent; and to reduce the price in this country below what similar descriptions from the Baltic can be afforded at, assuming the lowest possible cost of production.

The tendency of this measure, however, was not fully anticipated, and the Russia and other Baltic merchants were accordingly not early enough aware of the impossibility of maintaining the competition. They therefore for some time after the peace, continued and even extended their importations; but these being attended with ruin to many, and loss to all the parties concerned, a sensible diminution has of late taken place; and if the present scale of duties is to be continued, there can be no doubt that as soon as the few remaining capitals can be disengaged (a process always requiring some lapse of time) from this branch of business, it must be abandoned wholly, or continued only for specific and very partial purposes, such as the use of His Majesty's dock yards or other public works.

The detrimental effects arising from the present scale of duties, are not confined to the Baltic timber trade, but will be found to extend to important branches of the export trade of this country; inasmuch as the power of consumption of British manufactures and colonial produce, by very numerous classes of the inhabitants of Russia, Prussia, and Sweden, is very much reduced by their inability to make payments to the extent that they otherwise

---
## Page 172

otherwise would do in the article of wood, to which the present duties, nearly prohibitory, apply; while on the other hand, there is every reason to presume that a reduction of those obnoxious duties would be viewed with particular satisfaction, by the governments of the above mentioned states, and might probably be followed by important concessions and facilities on their part.

With a view too to the general interests of trade, it is clearly desirable that our merchant vessels should be built as cheaply as possible, consistent with their durability, in order to encounter the competition of foreign shipping; but this object is counteracted in an eminent degree, by the high price at which every description of wood is artificially kept by the present system of duties.

To the community at large it is a serious evil, arising out of the present system of a forced and almost exclusive importation of American timber, to be confined to use at a comparatively high price; a description of wood which, independently of its inferiority of quality in other respects, is supposed upon apparently strong grounds to have spread, to an enormous extent among ships and houses, the previously rare disease of the Dry Rot; and the public is thus taxed in the worst possible way, without any corresponding benefit to the revenue of the state, or rather to the detriment of that revenue; as there is every reason to believe, that if the duty on Baltic timber were reduced to what it was prior to 1810, the total receipt of customs on that branch of trade would be increased.

Your Memorialists therefore presume to hope, that the opportunity which now legitimately presents itself, may be embraced, for the purpose of revising and altering a system, which is not more injurious and unjust to the importers of Baltic timber, than disadvantageous and impolitic in a national point of view.

March 1820.

N° 7.

REMARKS on the Trade of Memel, particularly as regards the Article of Timber.

This branch of trade was for many years, the only one carried on to any extent at Memel; it was the chief source of opulence to a great portion of the people resident in the interior of Poland and Prussia; it afforded them the means of living in affluence, and of providing themselves with a variety of articles of luxury, which were principally the produce of Great Britain and her colonies. The ships by which this trade was carried on, were chiefly British, and to this day they have by much the greatest share of it; but the difference of tonnage required to convey timber from Memel to Great Britain, previous to the year 1807, as compared with that now employed in this way, is very considerable indeed, as will be shown in the sequel.

Whilst the duty on Baltic wood was at the former low rates in England, the quantity of timber exported annually from Memel, was from 150,000 to 200,000 loads; and for the twenty years from 1787 to 1807, it may be computed at a yearly average of 180,000, reckoning 50 running feet to the load, as is usual there. Whilst the continental system was in force, little business was done. During that period the rates of duty were raised in England, on wood from the north of Europe, which remaining in force after peace had been restored, served greatly to depress the timber trade of Prussia; and so much to reduce the quantity required for the British markets, that the intercourse between the Prussian sea-ports and the interior of that country and Poland, and the sale of foreign commodities to the inhabitants of those districts, were greatly diminished.—The decrease of the trade will be shown, by the following Statement of the supplies that have come down the rivers to Memel, for the last seven years;—Namely, in 1814 about 108,000 loads, of 50 running feet;

| Year | Loads |
|------|-------|
| 1813 | 75,600 |
| 1816 | 36,000 |
| 1817 | 20,500 |

about this period, a demand arose for the British markets, and produced a temporary re-animation of the trade. At the same time measures were resorted to by the governments of Russia and Prussia, which were intended to alleviate in a degree the difficulties with which the trade had to contend, and which were principally caused by the high duties levied in Great Britain. To counteract in part the latter, the Russian government reduced the frontier duty of 6½ to about half as much; and that of Prussia entirely sacrificed the export duty of about 5½ on the value, which had previously been used to be paid. These trifling facilities, though they were insufficient to effect the purpose for which they were intended, yet, combined with the improvement in the British market, they served to afford the contractors encouragement again to visit the coast, with an increased supply;—So that in 1818 about 40,000 loads and in 1819 76,000 were brought down the rivers to Memel; but the demand did not continue. The immense supply from Canada and from the United States (the latter having had the indulgence afforded them of introducing their red pine, which approaches nearest to the Baltic red fir, into Great Britain as Canadian wood,) and the low rate at which the timber of those countries could be imported, with which that from the Baltic could not compete in point of price, went far to exclude the latter from the British markets altogether. Consequently the arrival

---
## Page 173

arrivals from Poland in 1820, fell considerably short of those received in the former year; only 39,000 loads having been brought to market. And there are strong reasons for believing, that the supply this year will not exceed half that quantity, since only such of the contractors will come down as have made advances to the proprietors of the forests, and are compelled by necessity to realize their property at any rate.

During the more flourishing period of the Baltic timber trade, 600 to 700 British ships, (and even more,) of 250 to 500 tons, were annually loaded at Memel; but as the trade dwindled away, the number of vessels employed in it decreased; and in the years 1815 to 1820, only from 200 to 300 British ships have been loaded there, and even of these a part have taken seed, flax, grain and other articles, which formerly were less attended to. Although from the foregoing, it appears that the number of British vessels now trading to Memel, compared with former years, is so greatly decreased, yet they have a decided advantage over foreign bottoms, there and in all the ports of Prussia; which advantage would still be enhanced, if some facility were given to the trade. It is a fact, which experience has confirmed, that British ships are navigated at a less expense than foreign at this time, for not only that the charges which the former have to pay in Prussia are no higher than those levied on Prussian vessels, whereas in this country they are very considerably less, (pilotage for instance, where foreign ships pay 25½ more than British;) but it is also well understood, that ship-owning is a business which is better known, and conducted with more system and economy, here than there.

At this time, better than four-fifths of the trade between the port of Memel and this country, is carried on in the ships of the latter; which the following Statement, taken from the report of the sworn broker, will serve to demonstrate.

| Year | Ships Cleared Inwards |
|------|-----------------------|
| 1819 | 258 British           |
|      | 153 Prussian          |
|      | 270 Dutch, Swedish, French, &c. &c. |
|      | **Together** 691 Ships cleared inwards |

Of these, the whole of the British vessels departed with cargoes; but of the Prussians, being as above

| Laid up for the winter | about 40 |
|------------------------|---------|
| Went to Holland, &c. &c. | 80 |
| Remain                  | 120     |
| To these add the number which wintered 1819 | 30 |
| Jeaves                  | 69 Ships under Prussian flag, which went to Great Britain, or about one to four to the number of British. |

The Prussian shipping has since the war, been so much reduced, that not above one-half the number of serviceable vessels at most that were owned there at that period, are now in existence. The increase of this species of property in Prussia at a former time, had its origin in political events, not likely to recur again; nor will its value there be sufficiently enhanced by any alteration in favour of the timber trade as regards the duties in this country, to encourage the building of ships, since money has there become too scarce and valuable to be thus invested; and the regulations adopted by the Prussian government since the peace, are particularly detrimental to sailors, and such as will inevitably tend to diminish the number of that class of people. So that it may be justly inferred, the British ship owner would reap the whole advantage of an increased exportation from the ports of Prussia.

The decline of the timber trade has had a very visible effect upon the state of Prussia and Poland, and the imports from England have gradually diminished, in the same proportion with the former branch of commerce; for as the difficulty of disposing of the produce of the country became gradually greater, so were its inhabitants by degrees impoverished and deprived of the means of encouraging importations. And who ever has known the ports of Prussia, previous to the war, especially those situated at the mouths of the three great rivers, the Oder, Vistula and Niemen, and of these in particular the two former, must be forcibly struck with the difference between the extent of their commerce, in imports as well as exports, at that and at the present time.

If the new existing restrictions remain in force, it is greatly to be apprehended, that both the timber trade from Prussia, and that with British manufactures and colonial produce to that country, will gradually dwindle away to nothing. For the proprietors of the forests being prevented from realizing their property, will be compelled to circumscribe their expenditures still more than they have already done; and the numerous body of labourers employed in the preparation of timber, deals, &c. being thrown out of employ, will be entirely precluded the use of those luxuries, in which they would indulge at a more favourable time, and which form no inconsiderable part of the consumption.

On the other hand, an improvement in the timber trade, would no doubt materially facilitate an exchange of commodities.

London,

18th February 1821.

Ca. Dalmer of Memel.
